# Burn Rate

# Chapter 1

The farmer's shin was broken in two places. Bone showed through the split skin above his left ankle. Infection had already climbed from the wound, a band of heat and redness running up the calf, the flesh shiny with fluid that smelled sweet and wrong. Three days, his wife had said. A wall collapse in the grain shed. Six hours under timber and stone before anyone got him out, and the settlement had nothing better than boiled rags and willow-bark paste.

Vel knelt beside the cot on the packed-earth floor and set one hand above the break, one below. Hael made a noise through his teeth but held still. Forty, maybe. Hands built by soil and lumber, nails cracked dark with ground-in dirt.

"Still," she said. "Don't move the leg."

The ability came when she reached for it. The Ledger shifted. Behind her sternum, something changed weight. Thirty-three became thirty-two.

Heat moved from her palms into the ruined leg. Damage resolved itself under her hands: two slant fractures through the tibia, splinters loose in the swollen tissue, infection gone deeper than she'd judged. It had reached the marrow. It had reached the exposed bone.

"Shit," she said.

Hael's wife jerked her head up from the window. "What?"

Maret had been wringing the same cloth since Vel arrived. Her knuckles stayed white around it. Missed meals had pared her down. The dress she wore had been let out once and taken in again, the seams visible where somebody had redone the stitches by hand. That morning she had offered Vel bread anyway, and it had been the best thing in the house.

"It's deeper than it looked. I'm pushing again."

"Does that cost you more?"

Vel put both hands flat against the bone and didn't answer.

Thirty-two became thirty-one.

The infection resisted with the blind stubbornness of anything that had been eating flesh for three days. Vel drove through it. Bacteria died in hot waves. Swelling dropped under her hands. Redness drained back down the calf. Fracture lines knit. Splinters pulled inward and fused. By the time she let go, Hael's breathing had gone from measured pain to startled relief.

Her hands shook when she sat back. That tremor always came after ability use, the body's way of registering permanent loss. She wiped her palms on her trousers and looked at the leg.

The leg was functional now, scarred but usable. He'd limp for a week and ache longer than that, but he would walk. He would work. He would haul water from the well and brace his own roof the next time it tried to kill him. The settlement needed that more than it needed almost anything.

"That's it?" Hael said.

"That's it. Stay off it today. Tomorrow you can move around. Nothing heavy for a week."

He touched the scar, pressed too hard, winced, and pressed again. Gratitude came over his face slowly, as if pain had to clear room for it. Vel wanted out of the house before it finished landing.

Maret crossed to the cot and laid a hand on her husband's forehead, checking for fever. The trembling hadn't left her yet. When she caught Vel looking, she tucked the hand behind her back.

"What do we owe you?"

"Whatever you can manage."

Maret went to the tin over the stove. With the care of someone who knew the number before she opened it, she counted out seven marks. The marks made a thin sound. It was thin money. Then she brought down a jar of preserved cherries, cloudy glass, fruit still a hard dark red.

"The cherries are good," Maret said. "I put them up last autumn, before the —" Her mouth closed. "They're good."

It had all been before the fever, before eleven people died and the Hoarded sent no one, before the grain contract got cut down to match the settlement's new usefulness. Everybody in the room knew the sentence she had stopped inside.

Vel took the marks and the jar. "Thank you."

Her pack waited by the door, canvas worn soft, straps mended with cord and old leather. Vel checked the main pocket from habit and stepped out into late spring sun.

Turned earth and hedgerow bloom rode the air. Twenty houses sat around a well with a crumbling stone lip and a winch held together by rope and a bent nail. South of the houses, fields ran toward an untrimmed tree line. Wheat and barley had been planted in alternating strips, but whole patches had failed where the ground had gone mean or the workers had gone missing.

The grain shed stood half-collapsed on the edge of the road. Hael's wall had folded inward. Roof beams split along the grain. Nobody here had the levels to Shape it back together. Nobody had the spare labor to rebuild it by hand. Their grain sat in a lean-to behind the meeting hall instead, open to damp and rats. Vel had seen settlements die in increments like that.

Kids played by the well with a length of rope, tossing it back and forth after the game had already gone stale. One stared. One waved. Vel raised a hand back. Both had the same narrow shoulders Maret did. Farther down the road, a woman reset a fence post with a flat stone because the settlement's only hammer had broken and nobody had marks for another.

The Hoarded had once kept this place upright with Fertility Charms and a grain contract mean enough to be useful. Rust-fever took eleven people in six weeks. The next contract came back thinner and brought no extra labor or timber. No one from the Vault came to look at the caved-in shed or the wet grain slumping under canvas.

Vel sat on the low stone wall by the well and ate one of the cherries. It was tart, brandy maybe, or something trying its best to be brandy. Maret had put up cherries before the fever and traded the jar for a leg. Syrup stuck to Vel's thumb.

Thirty-one levels remained.

The number lived behind her sternum with the steadiness of an old injury. At three hundred she had barely noticed it. At two hundred it had been background. At one hundred she had started counting on purpose. Below fifty the count kept time with her pulse.

Forty-one years of spending had brought her here through healing jobs, fights, shelter work, flood rescues, fires, bones, and people dragged out of water or rubble because nobody else would spend on them. The hard part still lay ahead, south and eight years late.

Vel had meant to use one level here, set the bone, clear the infection, repair the tissue, and get back on the road. At thirty-three, one level could still be filed under margin. At thirty-one, it sat in the account like a missing tooth.

Her hands were still shaking. They would stop in an hour.

---

The settlement gave her a room at the back of the meeting hall, really a storeroom with the sacks shoved out and a cot dragged in. Burlap dust hung in the mattress. The window never closed all the way. Olla had said the north trader might come through before dark, and this place sat on the south grain loop out of Cairn Hill. If news broke near the Vault in the morning, it hit Breck's yard by noon and kept moving with whichever trader bought feed there. Vel had taken the room instead of starting for the next village with one level missing from her plan because this was one of the first poor places south to hear rich people panic.

Supplies spread across the floorboards: water skin, dried beans, knife, needle kit, two rolls of bandage. Vel knew the pack by weight already. The other count mattered more.

She was down to thirty-one now. Breck's yard, the road beyond it, and anything that went bad on the way would have to come out of that.

Each night for eight years she had run through what those levels could still buy. Healing came first. Three good applications, maybe four, before the ability degraded into something fit only for shallow cuts and fevers caught early. A few defensive tools remained on the combat side: Redirect, Perception, a short Conceal if she needed half a minute to disappear. Utility had held up better. Endurance still covered the long marches. Nightsight and Weatherskin remained. She could still travel. Carelessness would strip the rest off her.

What remained narrowed everything. It left her with a kit that still held the expensive tools and no room for mistakes.

Eight years ago she had watched a door open at zero and Maren go through it. Since then every private calculation had bent toward the Scour. Its location was clear enough. Route notes sat in her pack. What waited there was clear too. Zero was passage.

The math remained ugly.

One person reaching zero could trigger the threshold. That much she had seen. The crossing itself had been brief and ugly, a flicker more than an opening. To hold it steady, the descent needed more force than one exhausted body could produce: two people spending down together, a cascade, enough draw to keep the door open long enough for a clean crossing. Her side of the equation was already thin. The other side needed height.

What she needed was a partner who started far above her and was willing to burn everything.

That was the impossible part.

Years had gone into careful questions in settlements, in Spent Towns, at trader fires, at the edges of the Margins where old explorers still remembered names. Any Hoarded defectors? Any lordlings missing off a north road? Anyone at two-fifty or higher who had walked out and stayed out? The answer had always been no or something close enough to no to kill a week. The Hoarded did not leave. They rotted in place and called it discipline.

Time had narrowed while she searched. Every job took levels. Every emergency took levels. Every stop where she was the only person within a day's walk who could do the hard thing took levels. At thirty-three she had still had room to absorb the world. At thirty-one, the world was beginning to eat the plan faster than she could protect it. Below twenty, her side of the cascade would fail.

Now mattered more than a better plan.

If Sorren brought nothing tonight, she would leave at first light for the trading post two days north and keep listening there. But Cairn Hill was better than listening blind. Breck's yard sat where the Vault road, the east ferry road, and the south grain road still touched each other before separating into lies. Couriers changed horses there. House servants got drunk there. Stable boys watched mud dry on wheels and learned more than their employers meant them to. A report that still held up in Breck's yard was worth weeks of secondhand stories from trader fires farther out.

Vel had just cinched the pack closed when Olla knocked on the open frame. She held herself in the doorway with both hands because the palsy got worse when weather turned.

"Rain before dark," Olla said. "We've got three grain sacks taking damp. If you've still got enough of yourself left to lift one, I could use the arm."

Vel stood. "I've got the arm."

Behind the hall, the lean-to smelled of wet straw, old mice, and the slow spoil of grain that should have been under a proper roof. Canvas hung low on one side where the rope had stretched out. Water already sat in the dip.

Olla handed Vel a bent iron hinge somebody had turned into a pry bar. "Get those boards under the back row first. If the bottoms go, we may as well hand the whole harvest to the rats."

They worked without speed. Vel levered sacks high enough for Olla to shove broken fence rails under them, then shifted the next. Grain weight dragged at her shoulders. Her hands had mostly stopped shaking, but not all the way. Every time she braced and lifted, she could feel the place where the two lost levels had gone. It wasn't pain. It wasn't weakness exactly. It was subtraction.

Olla watched her over one of the sacks. "You're staying just for Sorren, then."

"For the road he brings."

"Same thing tonight." Olla pushed a rail into place with the toe of her boot. "Breck loads him last on market days. Sorren buys lamp oil, salt, and whatever gossip he can flip south for free. If a courier came through Cairn Hill in a hurry, Sorren'll have heard before the horse cooled."

"Breck's yard still the first change point out of the Vault road?"

"Only real one before the east fork. Water, spare tack, enough oats to rob a house servant without apology. If somebody blows a horse and keeps going, that's where they do it."

Vel set down another sack. "And if somebody wants to ask after a missing rich boy."

Olla glanced at her, sharp despite the tremor in her hands. "That too."

Maret came around the corner carrying two warped planks against her hip. Her children trailed after her with armfuls of kindling and the solemn concentration of kids doing grown work because there was nobody else to do it. Maret lowered the planks beside the lean-to and looked at the sacks, then at Vel.

"He came on that leg?" she asked.

"Not far," Vel said.

"Far enough." Maret wiped her wrist across her forehead. "If Sorren's got nails on the cart, tell Olla before he sees me. She'll get a fairer price."

"I always get a fairer price," Olla said.

"You get a louder argument."

That was close enough to a joke that one of the children smiled before remembering not to. Vel slid the planks under the last row. The ground back there had already gone soft. Another rain like tonight's and half the sacks would start turning from the bottom up.

When the work was done, Olla stood with both hands on her hips and looked at the lean-to as if order alone might keep it standing.

"That buys us a night," she said.

"Maybe."

"Confidence like that is why people pay for your company."

Vel left the pry bar by the wall. "People pay because I don't talk much."

Olla snorted. "That too."

They walked back around the hall together. The south road showed a long stripe of late light between hedges. Vel looked at it once out of habit. The plan in her head still ran south. The day did not.

Olla slowed at the pump and dipped one hand in the cold water, letting the tremor shake itself out. "If Sorren has heard something real, don't waste time asking what people think it means. Ask who saw what, where the horse changed, and which yard boy got stepped on. Meaning goes bad first."

Vel nodded.

"And if it's only rumor?"

"Then you lost half a day and a room with bad bedding."

"I've lost more for less."

By the time the light in the window had gone gold, Sorren's cart had come into the yard.

---

Vel stayed for supper because that was what she had been waiting for.

The meal was boiled grain with root vegetables and enough rosemary to disguise how thin it all was. Four settlement adults sat with her at the meeting-hall table. Sorren took the fifth place and set his spoon down like a man already tired of holding it.

He looked the part of a route trader. He was compact and fifty-something, with one shoulder drawn a little high from decades of leading a loaded cart with the same arm. Dust sat in the lines of his neck. His mule looked no fresher than he did.

Road talk opened the meal: crop failures, mud at the eastern fork, which settlements still had lamp oil. Olla handled most of it, walking him back up the road in useful order by asking what Breck charged for horse water, whether the east ferry still took carts after dark, and which Cairn Hill smith was drunk enough to sell nails straight from the back room.

"The eastern road still passable?" Olla asked.

"Passable enough," Sorren said. "Mud past the fork. They had rain."

"And Breck?"

"Charging triple for oats and pretending surprise anybody objected."

"He usually saves that for funerals and harvest."

Sorren gave a dry little breath through his nose. "Vault courier hit the yard at midday blowing a horse to foam. Whole place stopped moving long enough to listen."

Vel put her spoon down. "What did he say?"

Sorren looked at her over the bowl. He heard the speed in the question and filed it away. "Said a Hoarded lord had walked out."

Nobody spoke for a beat.

"Walked out," Olla said. "Out of the Vault?"

"That's the report."

"What does that mean?"

"Means he used an ability at the gate. First one in his life, if you believe the courier. Blew stone loose in the gate hall and walked through the dust." Sorren took another bite, chewed, swallowed. "House Aldraste. The son."

"The preservation house," Olla said. "Three hundred for six generations."

"Six that they'll admit to."

"How long ago?" Vel asked.

Sorren's eyes came back to her again. The question had come too fast, with too much interest in it. He heard that too.

"Today," he said. "Morning, maybe. Noon by the time it hit Cairn Hill. The courier changed horses in Breck's yard and kept going before the foam had run off the saddle leather."

The room stayed quiet in a different way now, with less disbelief in it and more rearrangement.

"Last clean sighting?" Vel asked.

"Breck's yard," Sorren said. "Not because anyone knows he stood there. That's the last place the report still has names on it. After that it turns into mouths."

"And after?" Vel asked. "Which road?"

Sorren lifted one shoulder. "Depends who was lying. Courier shouted north. A carter swore he heard east. One of Breck's boys says two house riders came after and asked whether anybody soft-handed had gone toward the ferry road. Breck says the house man didn't know and picked directions that sounded expensive." The spoon tapped once against the rim. "What everyone agrees on is that the boy burned levels doing it. Ten, twelve in the first stretch. Maybe more. And every fool in Cairn Hill started calculating what a Hoarded runaway might be worth."

"Worth to who?" Olla asked.

"To the Hoarded if you hand him back. To the rest of us if you hide him just to spite them. Breck sold feed to two riders before I left, and another man was already asking how much reward a house name was worth if delivered with both hands tied." Sorren scraped the bowl clean. "Fresh news turns a road ugly fast."

Vel said nothing.

Olla leaned back in her chair. "If it's true."

"If it's true," Sorren agreed. "Could still be courier drama. House Aldraste trying to make a smaller scandal look like a larger one so the small facts disappear inside it."

"What small facts?"

"Maybe he didn't walk out. Maybe somebody carried him. Maybe he got hurt. Maybe he got farther than they want people thinking. Maybe he got ten miles and they're calling it two so nobody asks why he had that much road." Sorren shrugged. "All I know is the courier was riding like he'd been promised his own hide back if he was quick."

That beat the usual smoke. There was a courier behind it, a house name, a last verified report point, and riders already moving.

"Why the interest?" Sorren asked Vel.

"Not often you hear about a Hoarded man leaving on purpose."

"Not often you hear about one doing anything on purpose." He tapped the spoon one last time and set it down for good. "If he is out there, he won't last clean. Boy's never used an ability before today. He'll waste levels, panic, and every house rider north of here will smell it."

The conversation slid back toward lamp oil and wheel grease. Vel let it go. Her meal sat finished in front of her and she didn't remember eating half of it.

The Aldraste boy had what she needed. He also had half the north road looking for him already.

Breck's yard was still the best move because it was the last place where the story had bodies attached to it: Breck, the yard boys, the courier's blown horse, the men who bought feed in a hurry. By the time the rumor reached the next settlement it would be all direction and no mud.

If Sorren had heard by supper, house riders had heard sooner. By morning every trader between here and the Vault would be carrying a version of the story, and every version would pull the trail a different way.

Vel thanked Olla for the meal and the rosemary. She thanked Sorren for the road talk and followed him outside before he could climb onto the cart.

Evening had gone cool. The yard sat blue with last light. Sorren's mule stood hipshot between the shafts, chewing with the kind of patient contempt animals reserved for human trouble.

Sorren set his bowl on the cart rail and started checking the rear lashings. "You've got another question."

"Same one smaller."

"That usually costs extra."

Vel took one of Maret's marks from her pocket and set it on the rail. The coin looked mean in the dusk.

Sorren glanced at it. "Road's gone up today."

"Then say what it buys."

He took the mark and thumbed it once against his knuckle before pocketing it. "Courier came in on a chestnut with white stone dust ground into the knees and a cut under the girth. Vault road dust, not quarry dust. He wanted a fresh horse and the Aldraste posting rider. Didn't get all the way down before he started shouting. Breck's youngest boy caught the bridle and heard the family name plain."

"The boy heard north?"

"The boy heard the courier shout north. An hour later two riders out of the same house came asking whether anyone had seen a soft-handed young man take the east ferry road. Breck swears they were fishing, not tracking. Could be true. Breck lies for money, but he lies carefully."

"The yard boy. What's his name?"

"Jemin."

"Will he remember?"

"He remembers every horse that bites him and every man who tips badly. If the runaway came through there on foot, Jemin will remember the boots, the mud, whether he was limping, whether he bought food, whether he looked like he knew one road from another. Breck will remember who paid. Jemin will remember what happened."

Vel kept her face still. "How many riders were moving when you left?"

"Two on house money for certain. Another three men talking bounty over their feed. By dawn there'll be more. That's your trouble. Breck's yard is the right place because the report still touches the ground there. It's also the wrong place because everybody knows that."

"Any sign the runaway was hurt?"

"Only sign is the level burn." Sorren tightened the last lashing. "If he'd never spent before today and dropped ten or twelve in the first stretch, he'll be shaking by now. Maybe sick. Maybe half blind with panic. Maybe smarter than all of them and already gone where a horse can't follow. But if you want anything truer than rumor, you want Breck's yard before the third retelling."

Vel looked past him toward the north road darkening between the fields.

Sorren swung himself onto the cart step and gathered the reins. "Go early if you're going. By noon it won't be a yard anymore."

Back in the storeroom, six marks sat in her palm and one had gone into road talk. She set the rest beside the cot and pulled the journal from her pack.

The cover was soft leather with worn edges and a spine restitched with thread that did not match. The pages were thick enough for weather and crowded with two hands. One was small and precise, brown with age. The other was looser and quicker, layered in later over the first. They held distances, water sources, settlement counts, and warnings about ground that looked stable until it wasn't. On one southern route the older note said *passable in dry season.* The later hand had answered beneath it: *barely, and the creek crossing will eat a day if you're loaded.*

Vel turned pages carefully. The journal made her careful in a way almost nothing else did.

The southern routes took the most space, page after page of paths toward the Margins and the Scour. The notes were old now. Settlements moved. Roads washed out. People died and took their landmarks with them. The bones of the route still held.

Near the back, the quicker hand cut across a mileage correction in the margin: *not five miles. seven. Vel guesses short when she's hungry.* The words leaned downhill as if written on a bent knee. A brown thumb smear crossed the edge of the note where the page had once been turned with greasy hands.

Vel could see the night it came from without trying. Wet wool steamed by a fire that wouldn't draw right. Maren sat cross-legged with the journal propped against one thigh, hair half out of its tie, stealing the pencil back every time Vel tried to amend the distance in her own favor. Vel remembered burnt beans and damp leather, and she remembered Maren laughing with food still in her mouth and nearly choking on it.

Farther back, where the route narrowed toward the basin and the notes stopped being about weather and started being about the ground itself, Maren's hand crowded the page harder with reminders about water under the west shelf, wind off the basin bad enough to kill a fire, and shelter cut into black stone on the south side. One line ran half sideways across the margin because she'd written it in a hurry: *keep the journal dry this time.*

Maren had shoved the book into Vel's hands at the edge of the Scour with grit on her lips and blood worked into one knuckle. Vel remembered that more clearly than the sky. The door had opened white and wrong, dust lifting toward it instead of falling. Maren had turned once in that light, close enough for Vel to see the crack in her lower lip, and then the opening took her and the world sealed like nothing had ever been there.

North would come first. South had waited eight years already. But north had its own cost. Breck's yard by dawn or the report dissolved into bought stories, and bought stories always favored the people with better horses and more money to spend on other people's memories.

The runaway might refuse her. He might already be half caught. He might never have wanted zero in the first place. Worse, he might want it for some reason stupid enough to make him reckless with the only thing that made him useful. If the Hoarded reached him first, he went back into a clean room and never came out. If bounty men reached him first, he became a tied pair of hands sold north for house coin. If nobody reached him first, he could still burn himself down wrong and make the whole chance useless.

Vel wrapped the journal back in oilcloth and repacked for speed. She put the spare shirt at the bottom, bandages high, and the knife where her hand could find it without looking. She poured the cherries into a rag and left the empty jar by the wall. Glass on the north road was a fool's burden.

Before she cinched the straps, she opened the journal one more time and used a blank strip near the back for north-road notes she hated writing there: *Breck's yard. Jemin. Chestnut with white stone dust. House riders asking east. Start there.* Her hand looked blunt beside Maren's quicker slant. She blew on the ink, checked the old mileage table, and ran the north math one more time.

Eleven miles by the cart road to Cairn Hill. A little under ten if she cut the fallow strip east of the settlement, took the mill track through the alder stand, and forded the creek below the broken footbridge. The shorter line bought her hours and kept her out of sight if house men had already started asking after lone travelers. It also meant wet boots before sunrise, no shelter if the weather broke hard, and a bad ankle turn if she put a foot wrong in the creek stones. Wet feet were better than a dead lead.

Her thumb stayed on the basin page longer than it needed to. Maren was all over that sheet in ways nobody else would have seen: the hard downstroke on numbers when she was cold, the shelter note squeezed into the top margin because she never left enough room for the important thing until it arrived, the grease crescent from dried fish eaten sitting on their packs because the ground had been all thorn. Vel let the page go and shut the cover.

She lay down fully dressed but did not let herself go under. When the room thinned from black to iron gray, she got up, shouldered the pack, and crossed the hall.

Olla had fallen asleep in a chair by the cold stove with a shawl over her knees and yesterday's account slate in her lap. Vel slid two marks under the cup by Olla's elbow for the room and the meal, then took the heel of bread set out under a cloth on the table. Whether that had been left for morning or for her, she didn't check.

Outside, dew slicked the well rope. Vel filled the skin, cinched the cap, and turned north before the sky had decided on color. The fields smelled of wet dirt and bruised barley. The hum behind her sternum held at thirty-one, exact as ever. The journal rode flat against her back. Somewhere ahead, beyond the last report she trusted and whatever road the rumor had spoiled next, a Hoarded boy was either escaping or being shaped into a story about escape. Vel put the settlement behind her and kept walking before daylight could make staying look reasonable.


# Chapter 2

By the second afternoon outside the Vault, the smell of seven levels' worth of lunch was still following Kem Aldraste south.

It came over the rise in turns, sweet first and then rotten: split pears, cooling fat, wet sugar, the thick fly-buzz of food nobody had meant to leave in a meadow. Each time the breeze shifted, it told him exactly how far he had managed to get since feeding half the local animal life. Not far. Four hundred meters, perhaps. His right heel had broken open that morning. His left sole had split at the bend. The sun had got into the back of his neck and stayed there.

He kept the Aldraste signet turned inward so the crest stopped flashing in the light. Every few steps the ring bit his finger, as if the House wanted reminding that it had come with him. He let it bite. The road ahead ran through low grass and heat haze without improving at any visible point.

The road did not care who his father was. It did not care that he had been taught governance, precedent, and four dead languages. It cared about feet, water, shade, and whether a man understood the difference between wanting bread and accidentally provisioning a feast.

Two days earlier he had still believed leaving was the hard part.

---

In the Conservatory, the weekly objects had been a cracked bowl, browning flowers, an oil lamp, and a clock left unwound under excellent lighting. The great houses sat around them in formal silence while the objects failed in public. That was the Rite. One use from any person in the room could have repaired, lit, revived, or corrected the whole arrangement. Nobody moved.

His father had sat at the head of the Aldraste section with the full three hundred intact and his mind almost entirely gone. Lord Aldraste's body remained exact. The face still carried dignity. The posture still held. But the person inside had thinned year by year into pauses, vacant eyes, and the careful assistance of servants who pretended not to assist. The Hoarded called that Stillness and lowered their voices around the word. Kem's mother, precise as ever, had reached over midway through the Rite and corrected Lord Aldraste's collar without looking at him. A second flower petal had dropped from the arrangement. Kem had stood.

His mother said his name once.

That was all. No command. No plea. Just "Kem," spoken with the same control she brought to everything else, and enough feeling under it to make staying impossible.

He walked out. At the Conservatory doors he discovered, all at once and in the most absurd available way, that twenty-six years of never opening anything for himself had not prevented the Ledger from waiting. He reached for the handle. A Projection Shield answered. The doors went off their hinges hard enough to crater plaster. Three levels disappeared with the impact. Kem stared at the wreckage like a man who had sneezed and found a wall missing.

The Vault after that turned strange. Corridors he had crossed all his life without seeing them suddenly looked paid for down to the lamp oil. He took wrong turns because servants had always known where to go on his behalf. One of those turns carried him past a darker stretch of corridor the household never named: thicker walls, sealed doors, no carpet, silence with work in it rather than emptiness. He kept moving because curiosity was not more urgent than leaving.

At the gate hall two guards tried protocol. Kem said he was leaving. One of them began explaining notification requirements. He broke the gate latch with another Shield before she finished the sentence. Two more levels. Air hit him from outside, damp and cold and much too large. By the ceremonial pedestals he took the Aldraste signet because leaving it behind would have made the whole departure look considered. It was not considered. It was only late.

The road down from the Vault started teaching immediately. Dirt shifted. Roots caught at his shoes. By the time he reached open country his calves had begun to shake and his right heel had started to blister. He had brought robes for sitting, shoes for marble, and nothing else useful: no bag, no water skin, no map, no money he knew how to handle, and two hundred and ninety-five levels he did not trust.

Hunger arrived before competence did. Kem knew the theory of a Sustenance Charm. He knew it the way he knew migration histories and trade law, as clean information with no dirt on it. In a meadow beyond the pines, with his stomach pulling hard and the sun on the back of his neck, he asked the Ledger for bread.

The Ledger, having no experience of moderation through him, supplied House Aldraste.

Loaves hit the grass first. Then a roast. Then fruit, cheese, pastries, pickled vegetables, bowls of stew, and at the end a three-tiered cake that nearly slid off the stump where it appeared. He caught it badly and stood there with icing on his sleeves, looking down at one of the family's respectable guest lunches spread across weeds and dirt. The menu was unmistakable. The charm had reached into his memory for the only meals he knew and fed the meadow accordingly.

Seven levels.

He ate bread while birds and then a fox joined him. He tried to think of a practical way to carry any of it and failed. He left the feast to insects, animals, and the heat, and walked south with twelve levels gone in his first hours outside.

---

The arithmetic looked worse by nightfall.

He did not make another ability attempt. After the door, the gate, and the banquet, using a charm for anything smaller than a siege felt like asking a drunk giant to thread a needle. When dusk came he kept what remained of the bread under his robe, followed running water by sound, and found a stream narrow enough to step over and cold enough to hurt his teeth. He drank too fast, made himself cough, washed icing off the signet, and sat on the bank until the dark completed itself around him.

Sleeping outside turned out to be less like sleeping and more like lying on hard ground while the world refused to stay theoretical. Every sound demanded classification. Insects worried at his ears. Something heavy moved once in the brush and kept moving. Cold came up through the soil and settled in his hips. He dozed in short collapses and woke each time certain he had heard the Conservatory clock start ticking.

Morning did not improve him. His back had stiffened. The right heel had burst inside the boot. His left sole began to split before the sun fully cleared the horizon. He ate stale bread and walked because the alternative was turning north, and north led to the Vault, the Rite, his father's open eyes, and the life waiting for him if he returned to it.

By noon he had learned another road fact: injury made distance dishonest. Four hundred meters with good feet was a correction. Four hundred meters with wet fabric grinding against raw skin was an occupation. He kept stopping without meaning to stop. First to retie a lace that did not need retightening. Then to shift the signet because the metal had rubbed his finger raw. Then because black spots swam briefly at the edge of his sight when he stood too quickly.

Three times he considered spending.

The thoughts arrived dressed as practicality. A Mending Charm for the torn sole. A minor Restoration for the heel. Perhaps a careful pull for water. Each time he imagined the same result: a repaired boot costing a wall, a healed blister costing a horse, a cup of water arriving as a flooded ditch. He had enough levels left to do catastrophic things in comfort. He did not yet know how to do small ones.

So he sat when his body made him sit. He stood when the pain eased enough to permit it. He kept south because south was the direction available, and because motion, however stupid, was still different from the family art of remaining where one had been placed.

The wind shifted again. Rot and sugar found him.

Kem pressed the heel of his palm against his eyes until light broke into color. When he dropped it, the road remained. Low grass moved. Heat stood over the open ground. His foot throbbed in time with his pulse.

Two hundred and eighty-eight still hummed at the edge of him, ridiculous and enormous and useless in all the ways that mattered. Enough to buy doors off hinges, enough to feed forty people by mistake, enough, perhaps, to keep him alive if he ever learned how not to spend like a disaster. The number no longer felt like status. Out here it felt like something he had to drag south until it turned into use.

He made it to a fallen log by the roadside before the heel gave him terms again. Getting the boot off took longer than it should have. He hissed when the sock pulled loose. For a while he sat with his foot in his lap and watched the road as if watching hard enough might teach him how to use it.


# Chapter 3

She found him by the smell.

Two days north of the settlement where she'd healed the farmer, late-spring air went sweet and rotten at once. Vel stepped off the road into a meadow churned flat by one man's panic. A Sustenance Charm had gone off there with banquet logic: loaves split open by fox teeth, fruit liquefying in the grass, a metal basin of stew sagging as the conjured shape gave up. Enough food remained to feed a caravan, if the caravan liked flies.

The waste annoyed her less than the arithmetic behind it. The road could chew through a fool by way of weather, bad boots, streams that looked shallow until they weren't, and the small constant humiliations of having a body in open country. That part wasn't new. The new part sat farther south, beyond the towns and the ridge lines and the slow country where most people turned sensible and went home. The last pages of the route journal didn't care much about weather. They cared about reserve. They cared about what a person had left when the road stopped being road and started asking for something uglier.

Standing in the stink of spoiled fruit, Vel realized the idiot had brought enough.

She hated the relief before it had even finished arriving.

Four hundred meters farther on, she found him sitting on a fallen log with one boot off and his foot in his lap, staring at a blistered heel with the focused misery of a man trying to negotiate with his own skin. His road boots were soft Vault leather, good for polished floors and ceremonial corridors and not much else. One sole had split at the bend. As Vel watched, a pale Glow rose over the raw heel and cost him a level just to tell him what pain had already explained.

He dropped the light a moment later and swore under his breath without conviction.

Vel stayed in the tree line long enough to take him in. He was tall, maybe mid-twenties, with sunburn starting at the nose and ears where three days outdoors had already made an argument with his face. His cream-and-gray Vault clothes were snagged and dirt-marked now, but expensive even ruined. No calluses showed anywhere. Bright in the afternoon light, the Aldraste signet flashed on his right ring finger.

This was Kem Aldraste, heir to one of the richest dead houses in the world, sitting in the weeds with his skin coming off.

Vel stepped out of the trees.

He looked up fast. Surprise hit first, then caution, then a burst of naked relief he nearly got under control in time.

"Hello," he said. "Are you real, or have I finally overdone the heat?"

"Real."

He let out a breath. "Good. I was worried the hallucinations would start with someone less practical looking."

That was at least more useful than a greeting. Vel came closer. Up close, the damage looked worse. Blisters had burst and bled into each other. Dirt sat in the creases of his feet. He had walked on them anyway because nobody had told him not to and because the road never asked whether a person deserved the lesson.

"You spending on that?" she asked.

His eyes flicked to the heel in his lap. "I was trying not to lose the skin entirely."

"By looking at it."

"Yes." He winced. "No. All right. That sounded bad even before you said it back to me."

Vel crouched in front of him. "How many now?"

That brief inward stillness crossed his face while he checked the Ledger. "Two hundred and eighty-six."

Two more gone since whatever point the gate story had last reached her. One of them had vanished in front of her for the privilege of examining a blister. He still sounded more startled by the number than ashamed of it.

"You know that's terrible," he said quickly. "I do know that. I'm not pretending I don't."

"You know it's bad," Vel said. "Not the same thing."

He looked at her as if he wanted to argue and had already discovered he didn't have the footing for it.

"Are the Spent Towns close?" he asked instead.

"A week."

He stared.

"A week on a normal pair of feet," Vel said. "On yours, longer."

"Right." He looked down at the torn skin. "Yes. That seems fair."

The word fair, in that voice, almost made her leave him there.

"What else?" she asked.

"What else what?"

"What else have you spent on since this?" She jerked her chin back toward the rotting meadow. "I know about the food."

Color came up under the sunburn. "A little warmth last night."

"How little?"

"One level."

"For spring wind?"

"It was colder than I anticipated."

"What else?"

He rubbed a thumb against his knee. "I used a Glow this morning because I thought something was following me. It was a goat."

"Good use."

"I know you're being cruel. I just don't have a defense prepared." He paused. "And there was a stream."

"What about it?"

"I misjudged the bank. My clothes were wet. I considered walking damp an indignity of a kind I had not been trained for."

Vel waited.

He shut his eyes once. "I dried them with a Charm."

"Anything else."

"There are probably two or three smaller embarrassments that are not coming to mind because the larger ones are standing on them."

That was closer to honest than most people got while they were still above fifty.

Vel sat on the other end of the log because her knees had started making demands. The road lay empty in both directions. Heat pressed down through the trees. Behind them the feast kept rotting in the grass.

"You know what a level costs?" she asked.

He gave her a tired look. "If I say yes, you'll ask whether I know it in my body or just in theory."

"Do you?"

"In theory." He rubbed at his mouth, then corrected himself. "No. That's too easy. I know it's permanent. I know it doesn't come back. I know all the family language about discipline and sacred restraint and the moral terror of waste. But at two hundred and eighty-six it still feels like a number I could misplace and recover later. My body hasn't understood permanence yet. It still thinks this is all reversible."

Vel stayed on the log, jaw locking once against the ache in her knee. A minute ago she'd been close enough to walking back to the road. Honest made that harder than begging did.

"It'll learn," she said.

"I had gathered."

He shifted to pull the boot back on, and she caught his wrist before he could force torn leather over the raw heel. His skin was warm and softer than road skin had any right to be.

"No."

He glanced from her grip to his foot. "Do you mean no boot or no movement or no in general?"

"I mean stop doing that."

"Then I need a more detailed plan."

Vel let go. "You can walk six miles if I wrap those feet and if you stop acting like pain is a paperwork error."

"Six miles to what?"

"Trading post."

"And after that?"

"South."

He took that in. "You're going south?"

"Yes."

"To zero?"

She watched his face sharpen around the guess. He had the look of a man assembling a thought from scraps he didn't know he had.

"You know what it is?" he said.

"Enough."

"Enough to go there on purpose?"

"Yes."

He looked away first. His thumb found the signet as if it had moved there by itself.

"What are you doing out here?" Vel asked.

The formal answer arrived on his face before the true one did. She watched him feel it happen.

"My father is level three hundred," he said. "He has been level three hundred for sixty-one years. He sits in the Conservatory every week in a perfect body and there is less of him there each time. Everyone calls it composure. They say he has achieved peace. They say-" He cut himself off, pressed his lips together, started again smaller. "I sat beside him two days ago and realized we were all using pretty words for rot. That's the useful version. The longer one just gets angrier."

"Stillness," Vel said.

He nodded. "Yes. That."

"Everyone outside the Vault knows the word."

"I thought they might." He gave a short, humorless breath. "We pretend it isn't visible. That's a habit, not a fact."

Vel looked at him, at the ruined feet and the soft skin and the ring he could not stop turning. The Hoarded had preserved him badly. That much was already obvious. The less obvious thing was that he'd walked away anyway.

"And now?" she asked.

He answered without theatrics. "I want zero."

Vel's stomach pulled tight. By dark she could get him to the trading post, trade the coat if she had to, and keep him off those feet long enough for the bandages to matter. The stretch beyond the towns still wanted a spender with reserve to burn at the far end. Thirty-one wouldn't do it. Two hundred and eighty-six might.

She stood.

"I'm going to wrap your feet," she said. "Then we're walking to the trading post."

He blinked. "You have skipped over several negotiations I was expecting."

"We can talk while you bleed slower."

"That sentence would worry me more if I had alternatives."

"You do. Stay here and lose the rest by inches."

He opened his mouth, shut it, then held out his foot with bad grace. "Fine. But if you're about to cut anything, I'd like advance notice. I've had a day full of unwelcome discoveries."

Vel set her pack down, pulled out the small roll of clean cloth she kept for jobs that paid badly and bled anyway, and a needle wrapped in oilskin. She tipped water over his heel first. Mud ran over her skin. He hissed through his teeth and tried not to pull back.

"You don't get to spend on this," she said.

"I was not planning to."

She lifted a brow.

"I was planning to think about planning to," he said. "Is that also forbidden?"

"Yes."

The needle went in. Fluid welled and ran. Kem made a sound like a laugh being strangled. His grip locked on the log hard enough that the knuckles blanched.

"If you're trying to teach me anything," he said tightly, "you should know this is an unusual educational framework."

"I'm teaching you that pain isn't an emergency."

"It feels eager to disagree."

She drained the worst of the blisters, pressed the skin flat, and bound the heel. The other foot took longer. By the end his face had gone pale under the burn and sweat stood along his hairline.

"Can you stand?" she asked.

"Can and want are not aligned."

"Stand."

He did, badly. His knees shook once and corrected. He took a testing step and flinched hard enough to bare his teeth.

"Six miles," he said.

"If you stop talking about it."

"I talk when I'm miserable."

"Then today you'll talk a lot."

He looked at her, searching for the joke under the sentence and not finding one. "You really are like this."

"Put the boots on loose. We buy new ones there."

He bent to obey, slower this time.

"With what money," he said, "exactly?"

Vel looked at the signet.

He covered it at once. "No."

"Didn't even make an offer."

"You looked at it in a way I found extremely specific."

"Boots first. Sentiment after."

"This is not sentiment."

"Then it's stupid."

He laughed once in disbelief, then winced because even that seemed to pull somewhere lower in his body. "You have known me ten minutes."

"Long enough for a sample."

They started south.

Kem lasted less than a quarter mile before the road taught him the difference between wrapped feet and healed feet. He limped without trying to hide it. Vel cut his pace down until it stopped resembling normal travel and became a measured insult to distance. That was still faster than he'd have managed alone.

"Do people actually walk like this for weeks," he asked after a while, "or am I seeing a special degraded version?"

"People with working feet walk faster."

"That wasn't what I meant."

"It's the answer you need."

He breathed out through his nose. "You do that on purpose."

"Yes."

The road curved around a stand of scrub pine and dropped toward a creek bed lined with alder and stone. Kem stared at the water as if it were a legal trap.

"Can I ask a practical question without being mocked for upbringing?"

"Probably not."

"Encouraging." He pointed. "How do you tell whether that's safe?"

Vel crouched at the bank. "You smell it. Look for flow. Dead things. Upstream camp. Algae. Scum. Animal sign. Rot." She dipped a knuckle, lifted a bead of water, then tasted it. "This is fine."

He watched with concentrated offense. "You can tell all that from one look?"

"Mostly from twenty years of not wanting to shit myself to death."

"Useful. Horrifying, but useful."

He knelt with care and reached for the surface.

"Not there," Vel said. "Current runs cleaner a little in. Make a bowl."

He did. Water slopped over his cuff and he muttered something Hoarded and brittle under his breath.

"What was that?"

"A liturgical complaint from my grandmother. It seemed appropriate."

"Road doesn't care about your grandmother."

"The road has made that point repeatedly."

He drank, then sat back on his heels and looked at the creek, the bank, the trees leaning over it as if the world had committed a private offense by containing this much unarranged detail.

"How far have you come alone?" Vel asked.

"Three days."

"No. How far?"

He thought about it. "I don't know."

"That's worse."

"I didn't expect mileage to matter more than direction."

"Direction tells you where to die. Distance tells you whether it happens before dark."

Kem went quiet and stepped into the creek with his mouth set hard. The stones tipped under his weight because he placed each foot like a man asking permission. On the second one his balance went bad, arms windmilling, and pale light gathered under the signet before Vel said, "No," sharply enough that the Glow died before it fully formed.

Kem froze on the middle stone.

"I was going to catch myself," he said.

"You were going to spend because you leaned wrong."

"I was going to fall."

"Into knee-deep water."

"On cut feet."

"Then fall better."

He opened his mouth, closed it, and opened it again. "You're serious."

"Completely."

He got to the far bank the ugly way, soaked from one shin down and furious in a thin, bewildered fashion that would have been funny if it weren't attached to three hundred levels of catastrophe.

"If I die because I honored that instruction," he said, "I intend to haunt you in very specific language."

"You'll have to walk back from zero first."

That stopped him long enough that she got five quiet steps before he caught up.

"You have seen it," he said. "The place?"

"Yes."

"And you're still talking like that?"

"Like what?"

"Like it is a road marker and not the end of every known category."

Vel kept walking. The pack rubbed at one shoulder. Heat gathered between her shoulder blades. Ahead, the road cut through open grass and the trading post's roofline began to show above a stand of cottonwoods.

"What are you offering," Kem said, "beyond boots and cruelty?"

"A guide. Training. Fewer stupid spends. I get you to the south country alive."

"And what do you get?"

There was no point dressing that answer up. "Your reserve."

He took two more limping steps before the words caught up with him. "Meaning?"

"Meaning I'm at thirty-one and you're not. Meaning the road will keep trying to peel little pieces off you before the part that matters gets a turn."

He looked at her, sharp now despite the pain. "The part that matters."

"Yes."

"And you won't tell me what that is."

"Not yet."

He made a frustrated sound. "Then how am I meant to judge this?"

Vel kept her eyes on the roofline ahead. "Judge the part you have. I know the route. I know what kind of mistakes leave a person with a big number and nothing useful left of it. If you keep spending on cold toes and bad footing, your count won't arrive in one piece."

He stared at her. "So you don't just need levels. You need me manageable."

"I need you listening."

"That sounds worse."

"It's the same road."

"Then why spend the last hour teaching me how to drink from a creek?"

"Because if you die of infection six miles from soap the far end won't care why."

His mouth tightened. When he spoke again, the anger had gone out of it and left math behind.

"You want me intact."

"Useful."

"That is not a comforting correction."

Vel said nothing.

He walked in silence for a few paces. Pain made his limp worse when he stopped thinking about it. The trading post came clearer: weather-silvered boards, a watering trough, two tether posts, a wagon axle leaned against the wall waiting for repair. Someone had planted onions in a patch of dust behind it because road people would plant anything if it gave them one more reason not to move.

"If I go with you," he said, "the arrangement is that you decide when I spend."

"Yes."

"And when I stop."

"Yes."

"And where. And what counts as worth it."

"Yes."

He let out a tired laugh that had no pleasure in it. "You could at least pretend to be subtle."

"We're almost at the post. I'm not wasting breath pretending."

"So does being manipulated, apparently."

Vel stopped. The movement made him stop too because he was not coordinated enough yet to limp and think around corners.

"If you think that's what this is," she said, "go on alone. Spend to dry your cuffs. Spend to light your camp. Spend because the ground is hard and the owls sound judgmental. Keep enough for whatever speech you plan to make when you hit zero by yourself."

He flinched, not at the words but at the picture in them. That was good. Pictures held longer than principles.

"I didn't say no," he said.

"Then decide faster."

The post door opened. A woman in a stained apron came out carrying a sack of feed to the trough. She glanced at them once, took in Vel, took in Kem, took in the silk, and looked away in the efficient road manner that meant she had seen stranger trouble and had no intention of inviting new.

Kem lowered his voice. "I need one condition."

Vel waited.

"If you're leaving something out, say that you're leaving it out. Don't answer around it so neatly I only notice later."

"Fine," she said. "If I won't answer, I'll tell you I won't answer."

"That's not trust."

"No."

"It's not enough either."

"It's what exists."

He rubbed one eye, then dropped it. "I hate that you're the best option in sight."

"That's road for you."

"Is there anything you don't turn into a lesson?"

"Sleep, sometimes."

He almost smiled despite himself. Then the moment passed and he nodded once, short and irritated. "Fine. I accept the guide, the route, your authority over ability use, and the insulting educational framework."

"Good."

"I reserve the right to complain."

"You already proved that."

"I also don't trust you."

"That part's healthy."

He exhaled. "I was hoping, very briefly, that you might say something normal."

"We're buying boots."

Kem made a face and limped after her to the porch.

The woman at the trough had gone back inside by the time they reached the porch. Up close the trading post smelled of lamp oil, grain dust, leather, and old coffee. A narrow bell hung over the door and gave one halfhearted knock when Kem pushed it open.

Shelves lined the walls in cramped order: nails, soap, salt, thread, lamp chimneys, dried beans, cheap knives, boot grease, feed biscuits, bandage rolls. Everything a little more expensive than it should have been and a little less clean. The woman behind the counter was maybe fifty, broad across the shoulders, hair twisted into a knot so tight it pulled the skin at her temples.

Her gaze went to Vel first.

"Need boots," Vel said.

"For him," the woman said.

"No," Kem said. "For a passing dog with similar feet."

The woman's mouth twitched despite herself. "Then it's him."

Vel leaned on the counter. "Room for bandaging. Hard sole. Something that survives weather and bad judgment."

"That's a big category." The woman's eyes flicked down to Kem's feet, then back up. "How bad?"

"Bad enough that I don't want him trying them on more than once."

Kem looked between them. "I'm here. We can all proceed as if I have hearing."

"Can you pay?" the woman asked him, not unkindly.

He glanced at the shelves as if marks might appear there through force of etiquette. "That depends what form of payment the establishment accepts."

Vel looked at the signet again.

He covered it at once. "No."

The woman saw the motion. Her face flattened. "I don't take House pieces."

Kem stared. "You recognized it from that distance?"

"Everybody recognizes trouble." She wiped her hands on the apron. "I've got no use for Hoarded metal and less use for the questions that come after. You have coin, use coin. You have goods, use goods. Ring stays where it is or goes somewhere else."

For the first time since Vel had met him, shame moved across Kem's face clean enough to read.

"I don't have coin," he said.

"Then what do you have?"

He looked down at himself. The answer sat on him in cream-and-gray layers.

Vel said, "Coat."

"This coat cost more than the building."

"Then you'll get boots and a shirt out of it."

"This is extortion by weather."

"This is trade," the woman said. "Weather is separate."

Kem shut his eyes for a second. When he opened them, the formal mask had come back on in thin, strained patches.

"What would the coat purchase," he asked, "assuming the current market values are governed by daylight robbery?"

"Boots, one plain shirt, one pack if the seams hold, bandage roll, soap scrap, flint." The woman looked him over again. "Maybe not the shirt if the embroidery's mostly thread and not metal."

"It isn't embroidered."

"Then maybe."

He pinched the bridge of his nose. "I need the coat at night."

"You need feet more," Vel said.

"That is not mutually exclusive."

"Road says it is."

His jaw worked. He stripped the coat off with visible reluctance and laid it on the counter like a body being surrendered for burial. Under it he wore a fine linen shirt already damp at the spine, sleeves rolled badly, collar gone gray with sweat. He looked younger without the outer layer, less like a House son and more like a tall man making expensive mistakes.

The woman turned the coat, checked seams, lining, buttons, then gave one short nod.

"Good cloth," she said. "Stupid cut."

"That was my impression of most of the Vault, yes," Kem muttered.

"Shirt too," Vel said.

He looked at her in genuine alarm. "I am wearing the shirt."

"You have one in the trade."

"You are assuming both success and decency from this room."

"Decency's not for sale here," the woman said, disappearing into the back. "Boots might be."

When she returned she carried three pairs, a rolled shirt, and a pack that had been mended twice along one strap. She dropped them on the counter one by one.

"Sit," Vel said.

Kem sat on an upturned feed crate with the air of a man accepting public execution. Vel unwrapped the bandages while the woman handed over the first pair. Too narrow. The second rubbed the heel. The third had enough room for cloth and swelling. Kem set his teeth through the test and did not ask for Glow. That counted for something.

"Take those," Vel said.

"They're hideous."

"They're boots."

"I see the family resemblance."

The woman snorted. "Pack too. Not because the coat covers it. Because I'd rather not see you back here carrying blankets in your arms."

"That almost sounds kind."

"It's preventative." She shoved the rolled shirt at him. "Change in the storage room if modesty survived the road."

It had, apparently. He came back in the plain shirt a minute later looking offended by the fabric. The cut was coarse and hung badly on him. It also fit a person who meant to move in it, which seemed more relevant.

Vel took the discarded coat, folded it, and watched the woman weigh it with the rest of the trade. Kem stared at the counter where the ring sat alone now, stripped of everything else with a House smell on it.

"You'll want these too," the woman said, adding two pairs of thick socks after a moment. "Call it professional mercy."

"You do have decency," Kem said.

"I didn't say it was free."

She took the coat and old boots, passed over the boots, socks, shirt, pack, flint, soap scrap, a stub of candle, and the bandage roll, then looked at Kem's ruined feet once more.

"You walk in those tonight, you'll bleed through by moonrise," she said. "Slow him down or leave him."

"Planning to slow him," Vel said.

"Good."

Kem settled the new boots on the floor beside him and reached for the pack. The repaired strap rasped over his new shirt when he lifted it to test the weight.

"That's unpleasant," he said.

"You're carrying your own things now," Vel said.

"I had inferred the symbolism."

"It's not symbolism. It's load."

They refilled water at the trough outside. The sun had dropped low enough to throw long shadows from the tether posts across the yard. Kem moved with the caution of a man trying to keep his skin attached. The woman came to the doorway while Vel tightened the pack on his shoulders.

"If anybody asks," she said, "I sold to a road fixer and some fool from the north. I don't know houses. I don't know names."

Vel nodded once.

Kem looked at her. "Do they ask often?"

"Often enough." The woman's gaze touched the signet and moved off it. "Cover that if you know what's good for you."

He looked down at the ring as if he'd forgotten what was on him. For the first time since Vel found him, he twisted the crest inward against his skin.

Good. One less stupid question to answer at the next post.

They left the post at dusk and walked until the sky bled orange along the ridge line ahead. Vel got them off the road into a stand of scrub oak and low grass where last year's fire had left a ring of blackened stones ready for reuse. Kem set the pack down with a noise that was almost obscene in its relief.

"We're stopping," he said.

"We're stopped."

"Good." He sat on the ground too fast, flinched at what the jolt did to his feet, and let his head tip back toward the darkening sky. "I understand, abstractly and physically now, why people complain about walking."

Vel handed him the flint and steel.

He opened one eye. "What am I doing with this?"

"Making fire."

"No. I can tell from shape what it is. What I mean is, specifically, what delusion do you have in mind?"

"Kindling's there." She pointed to the scrub they'd gathered on the way in. "Tinder under the rock. Start learning."

He looked from the flint to the wood and back again. "You could do this in half the time."

"Yes."

"And instead we're staging a moral demonstration."

"Instead I'm not teaching you that your body is decoration."

He sat with that, annoyed enough to be quiet. Then he knelt by the fire ring and tried.

The first strike sent sparks into the dirt. The second jumped wide. The third clipped his knuckle and he swore, more feeling in it this time.

"Angle," Vel said.

"That is not a complete instruction."

"It's the one you earned."

He tried again. One strike sparked. The next missed. The third threw another spray of light and raised a thread of smoke. His whole face changed when the tinder finally caught, not triumph exactly, but startled satisfaction, a child-level surprise that effort could turn into result without numbers passing through it first.

"There," he said, breathless with annoyance at himself for being pleased. "That seems insultingly primitive."

"Still works."

"Yes. I see the argument."

They fed the flame carefully until it held. Vel set a small pot with creek water near the edge to warm, more for foot cleaning than drink. Kem leaned back, braced on his arms, shoulders trembling from the accumulated strain.

"You really mean it?" he said after a while. "No ability use unless you say so?"

"Yes."

"Even if something would be easier?"

"Especially then."

He nodded once, eyes on the fire. "All right."

That came too easily. Vel distrusted it on principle.

"Take the boots off," she said.

His head snapped toward her. "That sentence has become a threat."

"Take them off before they dry to the bandages."

He obeyed more slowly. Blood had spotted through at both heels, just as the woman predicted. It wasn't catastrophic. It wasn't good either. Vel cleaned and rewound the feet while the water steamed. He breathed through it and let the pain have the room it needed. That, more than the roadside obedience, made her look at him again.

"Why are you doing this?" he asked when she tied the last strip of cloth. "Not the grand answer. Not zero. This part. Me?"

Vel rinsed off in the pot and dumped the pink water into the dirt.

"Because thirty-one doesn't buy me enough mistakes," she said.

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one you need tonight."

"No," he said, then corrected himself with a tired shake of the head. "No, that's not fair. It is an answer. It's just not the one I was asking after."

Vel sat across the fire from him. The dark was coming on for real now, edges softening, tree trunks reducing to columns and breaks in the dark. Crickets started up in the grass.

"You said you'd tell me when you were leaving things out," he said.

"I am leaving things out."

He accepted that with a visible effort. "Thank you for at least not pretending otherwise."

For a while they ate in silence: hard bread from the post, dried apples, a heel of cheese Vel had been carrying since the farmer's settlement. Kem chewed like every bite required private argument. When he finished, he looked at the fire again with the same suspicious wonder he'd given it when it caught.

"Did you know," he said, "before you saw me? About me, specifically?"

"Only the story."

"House son walks out. Wastes levels. Heads south."

"Enough."

He worried the crust to crumbs. "You came north because of that?"

Vel said nothing.

"That's a yes by road standards, I think."

"It's a silence."

"You're very committed to classification."

"Keeps things tidy."

"Nothing about this feels tidy."

That was true enough that she let it sit.

The fire settled lower. Beyond it the dark held everything the world didn't need them to see yet. Kem rolled his shoulders once, then looked down at the ring turned inward against his skin.

"At the post," he said, "she looked at that and saw trouble. I spent twenty-six years being told people would see dignity."

"People in the Vault might."

"Yes. Well." He worried the ring with his thumb. "I suppose that counts as one more educational framework."

Vel watched the motion. "Why keep it?"

He didn't answer at once. That by itself told her it wasn't vanity, or not only vanity. Vanity answered faster.

"I took it because leaving it felt staged," he said at last. "If I walked out and arranged the symbolism of it nicely on a table first, then I'd still have been doing House work. I wanted to leave badly, I think. Untidily." He looked up. "And then, once I had it, taking it off started feeling like agreeing that they got to define what it meant."

That was more complicated than the road usually allowed people to be. Vel found herself tired enough to hear it anyway.

"It won't buy you much south," she said.

"No. I noticed."

"Might buy you trouble."

"That too."

He twisted it again until the crest faced inward once more. "Then I'll learn to carry trouble more practically."

Vel lay back on her rolled blanket with the route journal under her forearm. The leather had gone soft at the edges over the years. Kem noticed the motion.

"Is that the route?" he asked.

"Part of it."

"Yours."

"Mine now."

"That is not the same answer."

"No."

He watched the worn leather a moment. "The someone you're leaving out."

"Yes."

"What did she leave you besides directions?"

Vel looked past the fire. "The only notes I've got on where the road stops behaving clean. Where fear gets expensive and a bad spend keeps costing."

Kem was quiet for a beat. "And you trust that."

"I trust the pages."

"And her."

"Enough to still be carrying them."

"Why?"

"Because I owe her."

The word sat between them.

"For what?"

Vel kept her voice flat. "For leaving them with me."

He looked from the journal to her face. "So that's the real arrangement. I follow you south, and whatever she left in that book gets a say in what happens to me."

Vel did not correct him.

He studied her a moment longer, not angry exactly, but newly careful. "Right," he said. "That's worse."

He pulled his blanket up and turned onto one side with the awkward care of a man learning that every position had a cost. A little later he rolled back again because the first arrangement hurt too much. Then he rolled again because the second did. Vel listened to the rustle and the hissed breaths and said nothing. Pain taught better when it wasn't being watched.

The fire burned down. Night widened around them. Somewhere to the west an owl called once and waited, as if expecting a reply from a world with more manners.

Kem's voice came out of the dark after a long time.

"Are you asleep?"

"No."

"Good. I have one more question and then I'll shut up because I can feel your patience from here and it has edges."

Vel waited.

"If the count matters at the far end," he said, "what matters on the way besides feet?"

That was a better question than most.

"Judgment," she said. "Pace. Weather. Infection. Other people. Knowing which bad thing is worth paying to avoid and which one just wants to scare you into spending."

"And if I can't tell?"

"Then you ask."

"And if you're wrong?"

Vel turned her head and looked at him across the coals. "Then we pay for it."

He held her gaze a second, then nodded in the dark. "All right."

His breathing went longer and slower after that. Not sleep right away, but the road version of surrender. Vel waited until it tipped fully over, then drew the route journal into her lap and opened it by the last of the coals.

The earlier pages held mileage, wells, turns in the ridge, the names of settlements that no longer existed or had changed shape since the ink first dried. The later pages tightened. The handwriting there pressed harder, crowded closer, as if the writer had begun to understand that neatness was a luxury the south wouldn't reimburse. Weather notes thinned out. Supply notes disappeared. Distance stopped mattering. Reserve mattered. Timing mattered. The threshold pages had always looked wrong to Vel, less like travel instructions than an argument with a machine.

She traced the scored paper where the older hand had dug in too hard.

The threshold notes all drove at the same ugly point: whatever reached the far end had to arrive with reserve still in one piece and a head steady enough to choose where it went. She had enough for the road if nothing spectacular happened. She did not have enough to make the far end answer.

Two hundred and eighty-six.

The relief sat sour in her stomach.

Kem shifted in his sleep, one fist closing around the blanket edge, the other around nothing at all.

If he learned fast, if he stopped spending on fear every time fear changed clothes, if nobody from the Vault caught them first, he might still have enough when they reached the threshold.

Tomorrow meant slow miles, dry camp, and killing the Glow before it formed again if she had to.

Vel closed the journal and lay down without moving off it.

By dawn he would still be at two hundred and eighty-six. If he wasn't, the road had already started eating what she came north to keep.


# Chapter 4

Vel woke him before dawn by shoving the heel of her hand into his shoulder and saying, "Don't sit up fast."

Kem sat up fast anyway. Cold air hit the sweat dried into his shirt. For one confused second he thought they were under attack already. Then he followed her gaze to the dead fire.

At the edge of the ash bed, where they'd buried the coals under dirt before sleeping, a boot print pressed clean through the gray crust into damp black underneath. It belonged to neither of them.

Kem crawled closer on stiff elbows. The tread was narrow through the heel and dense at the ball, made for grip on wet ground, not the soft corridor leather he'd worn out of the Vault and not the secondhand road boots rubbing his heels raw now. He knew that pattern anyway. Hoarded enforcement kept their field boots on racks outside the retrieval offices with little waxed tags tied to the laces, each pair catalogued, maintained, and almost never dirtied.

"How long ago?" he asked.

"Night." Vel was already pulling the cook pot into her pack. "No dew settled in it."

"So they stood over us while we slept."

"Or one of them did." She kicked loose dirt over the print, not because hiding it mattered now but because she did not leave useful things lying around. "Move."

Kem got to his feet too quickly. Yesterday's climb had gone into his calves and stayed there overnight. The muscles answered his urgency with a bright, contemptuous cramp. He bent, breathed through it, and reached for the pack.

Vel caught his wrist before his hand closed on the strap. "Check the perimeter first."

"For what?"

"For what else they learned."

There were three more traces once she made him look properly. A branch bent toward the bedroll depression where he'd slept. Mud marked a stone where someone had stepped from the game trail into their camp margin and back out again. A second print, partial this time, sat near the place where he'd pissed after dark. All of it had clean edges and deliberate placement, the kind of neatness the Hoarded loved so much they mistook it for virtue.

"They didn't take anything," Kem said.

"They didn't come to steal."

She made him scatter the bedroll dents with a broken bough, kick soil over the piss patch, and carry the ashes downhill to dump them in running water. It felt absurdly domestic given the circumstances, like tidying a room while men with writs waited outside the door. Vel did not care how it felt. By the time a gray seam opened in the eastern sky, camp had become a patch of cold ground and flattened leaves, and they were climbing.

The ridge trail rose south through old highland forest, too narrow for carts, wide enough for deer and bad decisions. Vel had chosen it yesterday because the stone shelves under the mud kept a trail from printing deep. This morning it meant exposure. Rock spared them from leaving much sign, but it also kept them on one line with the people following.

Kem's new boots were honest boots, which was already an improvement over anything he'd ever owned. They were trader-bought road leather with thick soles and ugly stitching, overcharged because the man at the roadside stall had looked at Kem's clothes, the signet on his hand, and the careful indoor way he stood, then named a number that included contempt. The boots could survive ground like this. Kem was less certain about the feet inside them.

Vel moved twenty paces ahead, head tilted sometimes toward the backtrail, sometimes toward the trees downslope. She had spoken maybe twelve words in the last hour. Every few minutes she changed something small about their route, stepping from trail to rock, from rock to root flare, from root flare to the dry center of a fallen trunk, and Kem followed with the concentration of a man attempting a ritual in a language he did not understand.

When he slipped, she did not turn around. She only said, "If you have to catch yourself, catch yourself quiet."

"I'm trying."

"Try lower."

He did not know what that meant until the next slip, when he went down on one knee, felt the instinct to curse rise up, and swallowed it before it left his mouth. The silence afterward had a weight to it. The forest did not forgive noise just because it was human.

The trail kept climbing. Sweat started under his pack straps and found every rubbed patch of skin from yesterday. He did not check his Ledger. He knew the number still: 286. It lived at the edge of awareness like a second pulse, present without asking. Looking at it would not help his footing.

Vel stopped so abruptly he nearly walked into her.

She crouched beside a patch of mud where runoff crossed the trail in a shallow slick. Four prints sat in it clearly. Two were theirs. Two were not theirs. One of the new tracks had cut down hard through the heel, driving a V into the mud where the wearer had planted to turn. Another sat lightly, almost polite, but too narrow and too deliberate for any local trader hauling salt or cloth.

Vel touched one print with two fingers. "They're off the road now."

Kem crouched beside her. "You can tell that from this?"

"I can tell from this that somebody came up behind us, saw us leave the trail for rock twice, and adjusted." She pointed at a broken fern frond downslope. "I can tell from that that at least one of them is staying left to keep our line if we cut toward the ravine. And I can tell from the birds being wrong that they're close."

Kem listened. Wind moved through the high branches. Insects worked somewhere under the leaf rot. The ordinary chatter of birds had thinned to a few isolated calls that seemed less like conversation than warning after warning failing to spread.

"How close?"

"Close enough that the forest has already noticed before you did."

She stood. Kem did too, slower this time.

"Could still be local enforcement," he said, because the alternative was worse and because saying things aloud sometimes made them less true.

"Local enforcement doesn't spend on trackers for one man unless he's carrying tax silver."

"How do you know they spent?"

Vel looked back at the mud, then at the trees, and then at him. "Because we broke line on bare rock three times this morning and they're still holding our bearing. Somebody already burned a level on Perception, or something close enough that it doesn't matter what name you put on it."

The thought landed all at once. Somewhere behind them, in a room with lacquered tables and precise handwriting and men who believed the world could be solved by filing it properly, someone had authorized the first spend. Not for defense, not in emergency, and not because the Scour had opened or a village had gone under. They had authorized it for him.

"My mother," Kem said before he meant to.

Vel did not answer that. She only scanned uphill, down, and across the fold of the land ahead of them.

"We don't outrun a tracker in timber this thick," she said. "Not with you leaving half your education on every branch."

"I could use Concealment."

"You could waste ten levels making yourself dim around the edges." She pointed downslope. "Move."

The way down was not a path so much as a decision to stop respecting vertical arrangements. Vel cut through brush, across a slanted shelf of stone, and into a drainage gully slick with last week's rain and this morning's leaf slime. Kem lasted six steps before his right foot shot out from under him and dumped him hard on his back.
