He slid three meters, banged his elbow against buried rock, and opened the half-healed cut in his palm all over again grabbing for something to stop on.

Vel looked down at him. "Quiet."

Kem bit the inside of his cheek, tasted blood, and nodded.

She offered no hand. He got up with mud on his spine and pine needles inside his shirt. They crossed the gully, climbed the far side through young saplings that slapped wet leaves across his face, and came to a narrow basin where a deadfall lay rotting between two standing trees. Moss had claimed the upper curve of the trunk. The earth behind it dipped just enough to hide bodies without stealing the uphill view.

Vel took one look and chose it.

"Pack off," she said. "Stay low. If I say move, you move before the sentence finishes."

Kem slid the pack from his shoulders and crouched behind the log. It smelled of wet wood, fungus, and the sweet rot of things returning to dirt. Vel settled to his left, one knee down, one foot planted, gaze fixed uphill through a screen of fern and young ash.

"How many?" he asked.

"More than one."

"That's not a number."

"It's enough of one."

She closed one eye, sighting along gaps in the brush. Kem forced himself to do the same. At first he saw only forest: trunks rising, roots twisting, filtered morning light moving in narrow bands. Then the forest reorganized. He caught a shoulder where there should have been bark, gray cloth between saplings, the wrong geometry of straight lines in a place built entirely from curves.

Voices followed, low but not low enough.

"...not saying I object," one of them muttered. "I'm saying the paperwork says minimum visible injury and no facial damage if feasible, and I'd like somebody to define feasible before he starts throwing house-level tantrums."

Another voice answered, crisp and young. "The directive says intact and ambulatory. It doesn't say comfortable."

"That isn't better."

The third voice came from farther back and flatter than the others. "Trail holds two. One favors the left side now. Heavy one slipped in the drainage."

Kem felt heat climb his neck. Vel's mouth did not move, but one finger tapped once against the dead wood between them, telling him to stay still.

The flat voice spoke again. "They're ahead. Thirty meters at most."

Leaves shifted. Gray coats moved through the trees.

There were seven bodies in all. Six wore Hoarded field livery in the gray of minor-house enforcement, coat hems muddy now, insignia stitched small at collar and cuff. The seventh stayed ten paces back in plain field cloth the color of old bark, no crest visible, eyes on the ground more than the people around him. He was the tracker, lean enough to disappear until he looked straight at you.

The lead agent, a dark-haired woman around Kem's age, lifted one hand when she saw them, palm out in the formal sign of Hoarded authority. Kem had grown up with that gesture opening corridors, ending arguments, emptying rooms.

"Kem Aldraste," she said. "By directive of the Hoarded Council, under recovery authority granted to the enforcement branch, you are ordered to submit to escort and return to the Vault in substantially-"

"Move," Vel said.

Kem moved.

***

Vel went right and downhill, low enough that for a second Kem thought she had dropped rather than run. Then his eyes caught up. She was already across half the distance, one hand brushing trunks for balance, the other free.

The nearest agent barely got his stance set. Vel reached him before his weight did. Two fingers touched the soft place below his ear. Nothing dramatic happened. There was no flash and no blast. The man's knees unlocked as if some internal pin had been pulled, and he went down into the ferns with a confused grunt and no further usefulness to anybody.

One level had bought one agent.

The woman beside him reacted cleanly and went to shield first, which was good training. A translucent disc snapped into being between her and Vel, chest-high, textbook size, exactly the sort of thing Kem had watched in demonstration courts with polite admiration and no understanding. Vel did not bother testing it. She stepped outside the line, came in from the shield side the woman had left open because training exercises liked symmetry more than survival, and laid two fingers against the side of her neck.

The shield vanished before it hit the ground because it had never been on the ground at all. The woman followed it down.

Two levels had taken two agents.

The lead agent fired her Combat Pulse a fraction later than she should have, because for a fraction too long she had been watching her people disappear instead of acting. The pulse crossed empty air. Vel had read the movement in the woman's shoulders before the light gathered. Two short steps left, pivot through, heel of the hand to the sternum with almost insulting efficiency. The agent folded at the waist, then at the knees, then in a loose collapse all the way to the soil, eyes wide with the offended shock of a body no longer obeying orders.

Three levels had taken three agents, and Kem had not finished the breath he took when the formal hand went up.

The surviving line faltered. That mattered more than the bodies.

One gray-coated man broke first. He turned and shoved back through the brush so hard branches snapped after him. Kem did not blame him. Another agent half turned as if to follow, saw the tracker still in position, saw the lead woman on the ground, and stayed from pure bureaucratic momentum. If the order had not been cancelled, then perhaps reality had not changed enough to justify leaving.

It had changed plenty.

The tracker moved wide left, staying outside Vel's immediate reach, attention cutting between Kem, the trees, and the spaces a retreat might take. He was not here to win a fight. He was here to keep the line attached to its target until someone else won it for him.

The remaining gray-coated man looked at Kem and made the decision that training, rank, and paperwork all encouraged. He went for the politically valuable body.

Energy gathered around his hand.

Kem was still behind the deadfall with his pack half under one knee, blood on his reopened palm, and the absurd thought that perhaps he was about to be arrested by people splashed with mud in a forest basin that smelled like mushrooms. Then the man's Combat Pulse launched, and absurdity made room for terror.

The pulse came fast enough that he did not think a full sentence before his body answered it. Power rose through him the way vomiting rose, involuntary and total. It came up from the Ledger's hidden reservoir, slammed through his gut, flooded his chest, and broke out through both arms before intention could do more than shout after it.

He had meant to do what Vel had done. He had meant to keep it narrow. He had meant, stupidly, to be good at something by instinct.

What left him was a wall.

His own pulse swallowed the incoming one without effort Kem could feel. The gray-coated man disappeared inside the expanding force, then reappeared six meters back as a body no longer in command of its own direction. He hit a tree trunk hard enough to crack something that made a wet second sound after the wood struck. The woman beside him caught the lateral edge of the blast and cartwheeled into the brush.

The wave did not stop there. It hit the hillside at an angle above the basin, and the earth let go.

Soil sheared loose with a sound deeper than thunder and less honest. Roots tore. Stones broke free. A whole skin of slope started moving at once, sliding downhill in a broad, filthy sheet. One of the agents Vel had dropped rolled toward the new edge, and Vel lunged, caught the back of his coat, and hauled him behind the deadfall an instant before mud and shattered rock crossed the place where his head had been.

Kem stared because staring was easier than helping.

Trees tilted. One came over in a burst of leaves and splinters, smashed across the trail they'd climbed that morning, and split open down the middle. Dust punched into the air. Stone hit stone and kept hitting. The ground shook through the soles of Kem's trader-bought road boots until he felt the vibration in his teeth.

Then the slide spent itself against the fold of the basin, and the forest went quiet in the ugly, suspended way a room went quiet after somebody screamed.

Kem looked down at his hands.

They were shaking. Fear was part of that. Something worse was the rest.

Projection Shield in the Vault had felt like incompetence wearing light. The Sustenance Charm had felt like embarrassment made edible. This was neither. This felt like a sealed chamber in him had burst open and exposed moving machinery underneath. His arms buzzed. His chest was tight. Every finger ached with the appetite to do it again, but better this time, and maybe bigger.

His Ledger had changed. He felt the loss before he checked it, the way a tongue found a new gap between teeth.

Two hundred and seventy-one.

Fifteen levels for one Combat Pulse. He had reached for one and spent fifteen as easily as a panicked man emptied a bucket.

"Kem," Vel said.

He looked up.

She was on her feet again, mud to the knee from yanking the downed agent clear. The tracker had gotten behind a boulder near the far side of the basin. The woman Kem had thrown into the brush was pushing herself up on one arm with blood in her hair and the other arm hanging wrong from the shoulder down. The lead agent was still out. One man was running somewhere unseen. Two bodies lay where Vel had put them. The fight was not over. It had simply been interrupted by geology.

"If you're about to get clever," Vel said, "don't."

The injured woman dragged herself to one knee. Light gathered in her good hand, weak but real. The tracker leaned out from behind the boulder with both palms up, shaping something slower, narrower, and probably much more sensible than anything Kem was about to do.

Kem reached for Speed before he had even decided to disobey her.

The world thinned and lengthened. Leaves slowed in the air. The woman ahead of him turned her head through syrup. The tracker's hands were still coming together. Kem meant to cross the distance and stop them before either technique finished.

He crossed it.

He did not stop.

The tree appeared in front of him with the awful dignity of something that had always been exactly where it was and had no intention of apologizing. Kem hit it chest-first hard enough to drive all air out of him at once. Bark exploded against his face. Pain lit his left side white. A slab of bark broke loose above him and bounced off his skull.

Momentum refused to end just because impact had happened. It threw him sideways, spun him into the ground, and sent him sliding through loam and crushed fern until a boulder took over the work of stopping him. He came to rest with his cheek in wet dirt, mouth full of iron and rot, and a bell tolling somewhere behind his eyes.

Eight levels, he thought dimly. Speed had cost eight levels and delivered him directly into a tree.

For a few seconds he could not inhale enough to know whether the damage was serious. Then breath came back in sharp pieces and brought the rest of him with it. His left ribs clicked in a way ribs should not. His shoulder burned. Dirt stuck to his tongue where he had bitten it.

Above him, Vel shouted something that might have been his name or might have been an insult.

Kem rolled onto one elbow and pushed up.

The basin tilted once and settled. His Ledger hummed at 263 now. The number still did not feel low. It felt stripped of theory. Those twenty-three vanished levels were no longer abstract capacity. They were slope, impact, broken wood, pain.

The tracker was adjusting course toward him with irritating intelligence, using trees and rock, never offering the straight line Kem wanted. The injured woman had made it halfway upright and was gathering another weak pulse one-handed. The lead agent was still down. Vel was closing on the tracker from the other side, but the basin was too torn apart now for her to reach either of them before one of them fired.

Kem's mind grabbed for the next familiar tool: a shield, small this time, personal, human-sized, a thing meant to protect a body instead of redesigning a landscape.

He threw the shape out in front of him.

The shape did not listen.

Light burst from the ground at his feet and raced outward in a clear dome that kept widening after any reasonable person would have called it sufficient. It hit five meters, then ten, then fifteen. Brush flattened under the pressure wave. The tracker lost his footing and went down hard against a root mass. The injured woman was swept backward into fallen limbs with a cry that cut off when the branches caught her. Vel cursed and flung herself behind a boulder as the edge of the dome stripped bark from saplings and drove a dead branch across her forearm hard enough to score skin.

Air shoved out from Kem in all directions. Leaves tore free overhead. The dome shone for three impossible seconds, enclosing half the basin in clean borrowed geometry, and then collapsed because Kem had no idea how to feed it without feeding it everything.

The forest after it looked handled by a god with poor judgment.

Ten levels had produced a shield large enough for a village square.

Kem's breath came ragged. The cracked rib on his left side had stopped discussing the matter and moved on to direct complaint. Tingling ran through both hands again, fiercer now. It did not feel like strength. It felt like his nerves had memorized the route the spend took through him and wanted another pass.

His Ledger read 253.

The tracker was face-down in a tangle of fern and root, but he was moving. Kem saw one hand grope toward the cord at the man's belt. It was not a weapon. It was a whistle, iron or bone, something made to carry through trees. The injured woman, pinned under branches now, had enough use of her good arm left to raise it one more time. Weak light shook between her fingers.

Vel was still twenty paces out.

Kem did not try for a named technique. He shoved at the nearest thing inside him and gave it direction.

Force and light tore from his right hand, rough and unshaped. He aimed for the space between the woman and the tracker. He missed by three meters, which was still close enough to ruin everything there.

Earth blew upward. Stone, dirt, and roots punched into daylight. The blast ripped the whistle cord free before the tracker could get it to his mouth and flung both him and the woman in opposite directions. The woman landed in a folded heap beyond the branches and stopped moving. The tracker struck the ground shoulder-first, rolled twice, and ended flat with both hands spread in front of him as if surrender and self-preservation had become the same posture.

Silence came back in scraps.

Eight more levels were gone. Kem's Ledger read 245.

Forty-one levels were gone, and the fight had lasted less time than breakfast ever had in the Vault.

The basin looked as if weather had been weaponized. Half the hillside above it was now a raw brown scar bleeding loose soil. A crater smoked damply where his last blast had struck. Trees leaned or lay broken. Dust moved through the slanting light and turned it gold.

Vel stepped out from behind the boulder.

A narrow cut marked her forearm where the shield had driven the branch across it. Mud darkened one knee. She looked first at the agents, then at the slide, then at the crater, and only then at Kem.

Her face went still in the way faces did when anger had moved past heat into counting.

Kem was grinning.

***

He knew he should not have been.

His tongue was bleeding. Dirt had pasted itself into his hair. The reopened cut in his palm stung with every pulse. Something in his left side had cracked enough that breathing deep felt like being introduced to a knife. Forty-one levels had vanished in four minutes. Shame was already on its way, sensible and deserved.

The grin kept happening anyway.

His body had never done this before, not well, not badly, not at all. He had spent twenty-six years preserving a soft expensive arrangement of skin and muscle for sitting, standing, bathing, and walking from chamber to chamber on polished floors. In the last five minutes that preserved body had hit a tree hard enough to see white, bled into the dirt, thrown force through both hands, and stayed standing. Pain was everywhere. So was a filthy, rising joy that made pain feel less like warning than proof.

Vel moved before he did. She went straight to the tracker, kicked the whistle clear of his hand, checked his throat for a pulse, then crossed to the woman in the branches and shoved deadwood off her until her chest could rise properly. She worked without ceremony and without softness. The point was not mercy. The point was not leaving problems half solved.

"Pack on," she said. "Now."

Kem bent for it and nearly dropped again when the rib caught.

Vel looked over once, took in the way he had frozen around the pain, and changed plans without discussing the fact. She yanked open an enforcement field pouch, found a roll of binding cloth, and came back.

"Arms up."

"We're moving now, you said."

"We are. Arms up anyway."

He obeyed because the alternative was arguing while half folded. Vel wrapped the cloth hard around his ribs, twice, then a third time for spite or stability. He hissed when the compression locked the cracked place down.

"There," she said. "Now it'll hurt different."

"That's not a recommendation."

"I didn't sell it as one."

She shoved his pack into his hands and went back to the bodies. Kem got the straps over his shoulders one careful side at a time. The binding did help, which annoyed him because it validated her tone.

Vel stripped the field pouch of anything useful in seconds: more bandage cloth, a water flask, a packet of dried meat, two small waxed papers, and a leather document case from the lead agent's belt. She also took a second whistle from another coat and tossed both into the crater one after the other, where mud swallowed them with a sound too small for how much worse they could have made the day.

"If one of those had gone off before you blasted the ground open," she said, "we'd have had company before noon."

"More of them?"

"Not from the Vault. Nearest relay post, most likely. Minor house, whoever got saddled with retrieval duty in this stretch. Doesn't matter. We aren't on the ridge anymore."

That was new enough to cut through the afterglow. Kem looked past her at the ruined slope, the broken trail, the basin now marked so loudly a blind man could probably find it.

"You were going to keep us on the ridge?"

"It was the fastest line south." She opened the document case with her thumb against the clasp. "Now it's an advertisement."

The papers inside were thick and cream-colored despite the mud on the outside, sealed in waxed sleeves against weather. Hoarded script marched down the first page in the precise, bloodless hand bureaucracy favored when it wanted cruelty to look objective.

Vel scanned fast. Kem stepped closer despite himself.

The first line held his name. The second held his house. The third, smaller and somehow worse, held the condition of return.

SUBJECT TO BE RECOVERED AMBULATORY, RECOGNIZABLE, AND FIT FOR ADMINISTRATIVE PRESENTATION.

Kem laughed once before he could stop himself. It came out thin and disgusted.

"Fit for presentation," he said.

"You grew up with these people."

"I know. It still sounds insane when they write it down."

Vel turned the page.

"Thirty levels authorized for the team," she said. "Separate allotment for specialist tracking already approved and partially used. Contingency clause if contact goes bad."

"What kind of contingency?"

She angled the page so he could read the relevant paragraph himself.

IN EVENT OF ACTIVE RESISTANCE, RETRIEVAL LEAD MAY REQUEST SUPPLEMENTARY PERSONNEL AND FIELD ALLOTMENT FROM NEAREST COUNCIL-ALIGNED RELAY WITHOUT RETURN TO PRIMARY AUTHORIZING BODY.

The paragraph held no emotion and no alarm. It was just a line item for escalation.

"So if they don't report back," Kem said, "someone else comes south."

"Or if the runner gets to somebody who can read." Vel folded the pages once and slid them into her own pack. "Either way, this isn't one embarrassed family trying to fetch you before gossip spreads. It's institutional now."

Vel was already moving again, checking the basin's downhill edge, the broken trail, the quickest line to water.

"We drop into the creek bed east of here," she said. "We stay in it until the stone gives way or dark does. No ridge. No open ground if I can help it. If I tell you to step where I stepped, do it exactly. If I tell you to freeze, you stop breathing ambitious thoughts."

Kem followed her to the edge of the basin. "And if they catch us before that?"

She turned back. Her expression had changed, still flat but aimed at him now instead of the pursuers.

"Feet first," she said. "Hands. Whatever doesn't cost you levels." She checked the downhill treeline. "You don't reach unless I'm down or someone's about to put a hole in you and running won't fix it."

"I didn't know how big it was going to—"

"I know." She was already moving, scanning the slope below them. "You aimed for one and spent fifteen. That's not something you fix by trying harder next time."

He looked past her at the scar where the hillside had let go.

"I was trying to do what you did."

Vel pulled the strap tighter across her shoulder and started down. "I've spent forty years learning what one level does." She didn't look back. "You've been at it half a day and your hands are still shaking. Keep them off the Ledger."

It was true. His hands were shaking.

Kem could still feel the appetite in them, a live metallic itch under the skin. He could feel it in the way the sight of the ruined basin did not make him want to look away. Part of him was horrified. Part of him wanted one more chance at getting it right.

"I don't know how to turn that off yet," he said. That sentence came out more honestly than most things he had said since leaving the Vault.

Vel didn't stop walking. "Then you don't use it."

Kem followed her through the torn undergrowth toward the east side of the basin with the care of a man carrying his own structural weakness inside a wrapped shirt. Every step found a new objection in his body: left rib, left shoulder, cut palm, bruised knee, tongue, the fresh skin the pack straps had worn raw across his back. The trader boots were full of grit now, and the right heel had started rubbing again in the place yesterday had opened.

At the basin floor, Vel paused just long enough to kick loose dirt over the deepest edge of the crater, not to hide it, which was impossible, but to collapse the sharp outline into a messier wound. Then she led him into a stand of alder so dense their packs scraped bark on both sides. Fifty meters farther on they found the creek: narrow, cold, fast over stone.

Vel stepped into the current without hesitation. Water climbed over her boots and flashed silver around her calves.

"In," she said.

Kem obeyed. The creek was snowmelt-cold. Pain shot through his rubbed heels and up his shins. The shock of it made the whole rest of him feel briefly cleaner and worse at the same time.

They turned south inside the watercourse, walking from stone to stone where possible, through the current where not. The sound of it covered what noise their bodies still insisted on making. Above the creek banks the forest carried on in its damaged silence.

After a while Kem realized he was smiling again.

The grin did not come because the day had improved, because the Council wanted him back in presentable condition, or because forty-one levels gone in one morning was survivable math. It came from someplace lower and less articulate. His blood was up. His body hurt everywhere and felt awake in every hurt place. He had broken things. He had been broken against things. The world had hit him back and discovered he was there.

He should have hated that. A decent version of himself probably would have.

Instead he kept seeing the moment before the first pulse left him, the impossible rush after, the way the forest had changed shape around his mistakes. Shame moved beside the memory, heavy as wet wool. So did a mean, bright delight that refused to apologize.

Vel glanced back once, saw his face, and looked away again as if she had confirmed a fear.

The creek bent south. Mud took the place of stone for a stretch, sucking at his boots. Branches knitted overhead and cut the light down to green shade. Behind them, somewhere above the banks, lay the wrecked basin, the broken trail, the six agents, the tracker, the papers, the proof. Ahead of them lay more trees, less certainty, and whatever kind of education Vel intended to extract from him before the next team arrived.

Kem adjusted the pack higher against his shoulders. The binding around his ribs pulled tight. The Ledger rubbed at him from the inside with every step. It sat at 245.

Vel's three levels had dropped people clean. His forty-one levels had torn a hillside loose. The comparison was absurd, humiliating, and completely correct.

The grin came back anyway.

He kept walking south.


# Chapter 5

It had been two days since the outriders. Vel had put those two days to work. She took them off the main south track before sunset and kept them off it: through thigh-deep water that made Kem hiss through his teeth, over a scree shoulder where even goats would think twice, under deadfall so tangled he had to crawl on one elbow to keep from cracking the rib the hillside had given him. The first night they slept under an uprooted cedar with wet dirt against their backs and no heat at all. At dawn she sent him back half a mile to brush away the clean lordling tread he'd left in mud, then made him cross the stream barefoot because boots held prints in the leather. By midday the second day both of them smelled like cold water, leaf rot, and the iron note of his reopened palm.

Vel hadn't spent a point. She still had twenty-eight on the Ledger, enough if the Hoarded needed time to sort themselves out, enough if the next team still had to search instead of following a clean trail south. Kem hadn't spent either. That should have reassured her more than it did. He'd obeyed. He'd left the deep end alone. But every so often the grin from the ruined clearing came back over his face before shame drove it off again, and she could not tell which one would win if he got scared the wrong way.

The cost had come from other places. His left heel was rubbed raw through the secondhand boot he'd traded into after abandoning the corridor pair. His cracked rib had stiffened from two cold nights and was punishing every breath he didn't bargain around. He'd torn the split in his palm open three times on stone and thorn because his body still trusted walls more than ground. Vel's shoulders felt flayed where both packs had ridden the first day, and the grouse she'd snared that morning was the last real food between them and whatever the next three days offered. If they kept moving through another dark stretch without sleep or something hot in their stomachs, he'd start making stupid choices just to make the body stop hurting.

So when the stream bent under hornbeam and cut a narrow shelf of dry ground above its own bank, she stopped and made herself look hard before she let wanting become decision. The place gave her water, deadfall, and a strip of stones on both approaches where tracks would break up. Enough root and brush hid a mean low blaze if she kept it short. The bank below narrowed into a throat of rock. Anyone splashing up it would make noise long before they reached camp. The ridge above was too steep for horses and too exposed for men who'd been trained to think mud was an administrative inconvenience. It was barely safe enough.

It wasn't safe. It was safe enough to risk because Kem's stride had shortened to the careful, held-together walk of a man one bad step from locking up entirely.

"Here," she said. "Camp."

Kem let his pack slide down one shoulder at a time. Halfway through, the rib caught him. He froze with his teeth bared, waited out the white spike of it, then lowered the load so carefully it looked ceremonial.

"Sit," she said. Then, before he could take that as permission, "No. Don't. If you lock up now, you'll stay locked."

"I'm not sure I have a vote."

"You don't. First wipe our approach. Then shelter. Then heat. Then food. You're doing all of it."

She handed him a willow switch and pointed down the shelf. "Start at the bend and work back. Use the stones when you can. If you kick loose dirt, smooth it."

He looked at the bank, then at her. "This feels obsessive."

"It keeps us from getting found."

"It's a stream shelf. No sane man would want it."

"No sane man sent outriders after us either. Go."

He went. Bad temper made him fast for twelve steps. Pain took over after that. He moved down to the bend with the switch dragging in the gravel, stopped twice to brace his side with his forearm, then kept going because she'd ordered it and because the order was easier to take than pity. By the time he came back, sweat had silvered the line above his lip and the reopened cut in his palm had striped the willow red in two places.

"Happy?" he asked.

"No."

"Comforting."

"Drop the branch. You're setting shelter."

He let out a breath through his nose, glanced at her as if checking whether this particular insult was negotiable, then crouched. The motion cost him enough that annoyance gave way to the tired compliance she'd started trusting more than apology. Standing again was worse. That was good. The body taught faster than speech and lied less.

She handed him the rolled canvas, cord, and two forked stakes she'd cut that morning.

"Between those roots," she said. "Back edge low. Opening away from the stream."

He looked at the canvas, then at the ground she meant him to claim. "This is a home."

"It's an argument with weather. Start."

He did, and he did it badly.

The first line sagged because he'd tied the knot as if elegance mattered. The second pulled free because he drove the stake into soft bank instead of gravel. When he bent to reset it, the rib made him stop halfway and breathe with his eyes shut. Vel watched without moving. If she stepped in now, he'd learn the wrong thing again. She'd already taught him enough wrong lessons just by being the person who knew how to fix what he broke.

He got the ridge line up on the third try. The back edge still listed.

"Too high," she said.

"If I lower it more, I won't fit."

"Then you'll sleep curled. Lower it."

The rope went still. Three days ago he would've argued. Now the protest came out as one hard tug on the knot, then he pulled the whole thing lower. That counted as improvement.

By the time the lean-to was more or less standing, sweat had pasted his shirt to the groove of his spine despite the cooling air. A tremor ran through his arms when he worked the last knot tight. He hid it by reaching for another length of cord he did not need.

"Enough," Vel said.

"It doesn't look stable."

"It looks like it'll still be there when you wake up. That's stable enough."

He stepped back. One corner drooped. The opening was narrow enough that he'd have to crawl in sideways if the rib was still bad by night. It would probably keep dew off the blankets. It would also offend every instinct he'd inherited from generations of people who thought a servant should absorb all visible mistakes before breakfast.

He saw her reading that on him and made an unhappy sound.

"I know," he said. "I know what face I'm making."

"Do you."

"It's the one you're trying to beat out of me."

"I'm not beating anything out of you. I'm keeping us alive."

"By making me tie ropes until I understand class relations?"

Vel bent, picked up the fallen stake, and slapped it lightly against his shoulder. "By making you learn what your hands are for."

He took that and looked away toward the stream. Below them the water ran shallow over stones, clear enough that the last light kept catching silver off its skin. His arms flexed once at his sides. It wasn't just pain. She saw recognition in it, the itch.

"There," Vel said quietly. "That's the part I care about."

He kept looking at the stream. "What part."

"The one where you're deciding whether a level would be easier."

His thumb found the split and pressed at the edge of it. "It would be."

"Yeah."

"And this is punishment."

"This is calibration."

He laughed once without humor. "Those are not far apart."

She did not answer. Silence had become one of the few tools that made him fill the space honestly.

It worked.

"I keep thinking about the hillside," he said. "Not the scouts. The pulse. The moment before it went wrong. It was mine and it felt..." He stopped, angry already at the direction of his own sentence. "It felt clean. That's disgusting. I know it is."

Vel said nothing.

"I saw what I did after." His gaze moved to the split across his skin as if the memory were written there. "The woman with the arm. The tracker on the ground. You yelling at me. I know all of that. I was sick when I looked back at it and I was still..." He swallowed. "Still wanting it again."

That landed harder because he had not shaped it to land. He just let it sit between them, ugly and alive.

"That's why you're watching me," he said.

"Yes."

He nodded once. There was no argument in it, only the flatness of being correctly read.

"All right," he said. "Keep watching."

Vel took the canvas bag off her shoulder and set it by the flat rock. It smelled of waxed cloth, damp tinder, and old smoke. Kem's attention moved there at once.

He tried for a smile and lost it halfway through. "Heat next."

"Heat next."

"I know how to do this in a room."

"You know how to point at a grate while someone else carries the bad part."

"That is unkindly accurate."

"Use it."

The wrap held flint, striker, char cloth, and the tinder she'd kept dry under the cooking pot. He picked them up like evidence against his upbringing. She built the first nest where he could see, using dried grass, shredded birch bark from a deadfall, and twigs no thicker than a matchstick. Every practical instinct in her wanted to take the kit back and finish it before he bled on everything. She stayed where she was.

"Char cloth on the edge," she said. "Strike down. When it catches, fold it in and breathe small. You're coaxing a spark, not blowing a trumpet."

"That was almost encouraging."

"It wasn't."

The first strike threw nothing because the angle was wrong. On the second, steel skipped and shaved the blister on his thumb instead of the flint. Blood welled bright against skin made for paper, porcelain, signet wax, not this. He sucked air through his teeth. She made herself watch the mistake land. If she took the striker now, he'd remember rescue instead of angle.

"Shit."

For half a beat his whole body leaned toward the easier answer. A Flame Charm would have cost him almost nothing at two-forty-five. It was less than the skin he'd already lost. The option moved through him like remembered music. So did her stare. He reset the cloth with two careful pinches and tried again.

A spark flashed and died.

"Closer," she said.

He missed again. Then again. He swore at the striker, at the flint, at the whole stupid subject of combustion. On the next pass the cloth took. The corner glowed ember-red. His breath caught. For a beat he looked almost offended that the flint had finally agreed with him, and that nearly pulled a laugh out of her.

"Now the nest," she said. "Careful."

By the time he got onto his haunches the rib had him breathing through his nose in thin controlled pulls. He tucked the char into the grass and blew too hard. The glow flared and vanished.

"It was there."

"It was."

"I had it."

"You did."

His shoulders dropped. For a second she thought he'd ask whether half a level was a smaller embarrassment than learning. Instead he built another nest. It was looser this time and better for it. He did not look at her while he worked. His next breath came smaller. Smoke thickened through the nest, then a thread of flame found the bark and climbed. Relief moved through her before she could stop it. Fire meant food, less shaking, a better chance he'd sleep instead of lying awake with the rib. She disliked how personal that relief felt.

Kem made a soft astonished sound, almost private. It wasn't triumph. It sounded more like disbelief that the world would answer him slowly and still answer at all.

He fed it kindling too fast, nearly choking it, corrected, and got a usable bed of coals going with her voice at his shoulder and nowhere else.

"Water," Vel said. "Then the bird."

His head came up. "No."

"Yes."

"I have just now achieved one successful tiny domestic miracle."

"You'll waste it if you stop."

His stare landed on the canvas bag where the grouse waited wrapped in dock leaves. "I don't think that's how miracles work."

"Good thing this isn't one."

She sent him to fill the pot first. That bought him time to steady and gave the coals time to settle. While he knelt at the stream, dipping and lifting with the awkwardness of a man raised among basins that arrived full, Vel checked the bank again. There were no bent fern stems above the shelf, no fresh dung, and no stone rolled recently enough to show a clean underside. The sky over the ridge was paling toward evening. If searchers were close, they'd choose easier ground than this. If they were smart, they'd wait for dawn and look for smoke.

Which meant the risk wasn't gone. It was only delayed.

When he came back with the pot balanced carefully in front of him, he set it too near the coals and hissed as steam dampened his burn. She nudged it with her boot into the right position. His glance at her was brief, automatic, already asking whether that counted as help he should not have taken.

"It counts as me not wanting dirt in the water," she said.

"Comforting distinction."

The grouse hung from his grip gray-feathered and cold, its neck at the wrong angle from where she'd wrung it that morning. He held it farther from himself than was practical, as if modesty might survive another thirty seconds. Watching him hesitate over it took longer than killing it had.

"What's first?" he asked.

"Pluck it."

A long time passed.

Feathers came away in clumps or single stubborn barbs. He pulled too lightly, then too hard, stripping skin along the breast until wet pink showed through. Down pasted itself to the blood on the reopened cut. Every few minutes he straightened halfway, braced against the grinding pain, and bent back down. Every pause pulled at the part of her that wanted food now and less mess later. She let that part go hungry.

She let him do it badly. There was no other way to teach honest work except to let it stay ugly long enough to become real.

"Now open it."

"I don't know where."

She touched the blade-tip to the line below the breastbone. "Short cut. Keep shallow. If you burst the guts, you're eating the mistake too."

He looked at the bird, at the knife, at her. "That feels pedagogically loaded."

"It is."

He punctured the intestines anyway.

The stink hit and stripped the joke out of him. His shoulders locked. One swallow worked through his throat. Vel smelled it too, rank and immediate, and had to drag her own dinner-hunger back under control. Then the knife went back in. Guided by the line she'd shown him and his own refusal to quit now that quitting would look like exactly what he feared, he opened the cavity, hooked his fingers inside, and drew the wet rope of the bird's insides onto the rock beside him.

"Everyone does this?" he asked, not looking up.

"Everyone who eats. Or somebody does it for them."

That landed. He went still over the bird, not from the rib this time. When he started again, he rinsed the cavity slower than before.

"Somebody did it for me for twenty-six years," he said. "Every bird, every fish, every plate lifted away while it was still warm enough that I never had to think about what happened before."

"Yeah."

He rinsed the cavity in stream water, jaw tight. A green hazel switch became the spit. The branch sagged when he set it over the coals. He adjusted it wrong. Fat began to run at once. Residual feathers crisped. Within minutes one side was blackened and the top stayed offensively raw.

"Turn it," Vel said.

He did. The other side darkened faster than the first. Reaching farther across the heat pinched the rib hard enough to make him stop and brace against his thigh. On the next try he lifted too fast, the spit rolled, and the bird dropped straight into the embers.

"God. Damn it."

He snatched for it and planted the heel of his hand in a coal. This time the swear came out loud enough to shake a bird from the brush.

Vel knocked the grouse back onto the stick with another branch before he could make the burn worse. A fresh blister was already swelling beside the older ones, clear fluid beneath soot and blood. Enough. Fresh burns stopped teaching and started making people stupid.

"It's ruined," he said.

"It's dinner."

"But look at it."

The bird listed over the glow, black on two sides, ash in the skin folds, one pale strip still insultingly uncooked.

"I'm looking."

"Nobody would serve this."

"I'm not asking anybody."

She put the spit in the right position, showed him how high to prop it and how slow to turn. She'd given him as much failure as hunger could afford. Under that much supervision the third side finally cooked through. It was done enough, anyway. She cut it down onto the flat rock between them and salted the worst of the shame with the last pinch from her packet.

By the time they ate, full dark had settled through the hornbeam branches. The stream ran behind them. The little fire in front of them had dropped to a red bed with three thin tongues of light. No voices moved in the trees.

The bird tasted of ash, smoke, and punishment. Vel cut away the worst of the blackened skin with her knife and ate what was left. Across from her, Kem chewed without complaint on meat he'd made badly and honestly. They drank hot stream water that tasted of iron and leaves and called it enough.

For a while the only sounds were chewing, the water, and the small settling noises of wood giving itself up to heat.

"In the Vault," Kem said at last, "meals arrived as if plates grew them. Bread if someone had decided bread matched the rest of the arrangement. Wine already breathing in the glass. Garnish placed by a person I never saw and therefore did not have to imagine."

"You've imagined one now."

He looked down at the carcass between them. "Apparently by dropping it into ash and then burning myself to rescue it."

"Still counts."

A small breath escaped through his nose. The line of his shoulders loosened by the smallest degree and stayed there.

Vel sat with her knees up and the knife across them. Firelight caught on the new burns striping his skin, on the half-dried blood, on the thin grim concentration he hadn't managed to relax out of himself even while eating. He'd spent the whole day hurting in ways he could have bought his way out of and, for once, had not.

"Can I ask you something?" he said.

"You can ask."

"Why today."

She looked at him.

"Not why you started," he said quickly. "I know you've already decided that's not mine. I mean why this. The shelter, the bird, the whole lesson. You could've kept walking and insulted me tomorrow."

"I considered it."

"I gathered that."

She stripped a curl of bark from the stick. "Because tomorrow doesn't help if your body moves before your head does."

He let out a breath that turned into something like a laugh and failed. "So we're back there."

"We never left."

He nodded. The honesty of that seemed to ease him more than a softer answer could have.

"I thought if I didn't spend for two days it'd stop," he said. "The wanting."

"Did it?"

"No." He pulled at a shred of meat with his teeth, swallowed, and stared at the bones in his lap. "It changed shape. It's less the blast now. More the thought that I could fix everything wrong about my body in half a second if you'd let me. The rib. The burns. The walking. I keep thinking about how small that cost would be compared to what I already lost."

"That's how it starts feeling cheap."

"Isn't it cheap for me?"

Vel looked at him until he met her eyes.

"No," she said. "It only feels cheap because you've got enough left to fool yourself with."

He absorbed that without flinching. Maybe he was too tired to flinch. Maybe he knew it was right.

"My father would've called that excellent moral instruction," he said. "Delivered with inadequate bedside manner."

"I wasn't hired for manners."

"No. You were hired for contempt, apparently."

"That part's free."

A breath of laughter escaped him this time and stayed. The sound was small, brief, and real. Then the quiet came back.

He turned one of the bones over and over. "I was ashamed after the fight."

"I know."

"But it wasn't only shame." He rubbed his thumb along the snapped end of the bone until it clicked. "I don't want you thinking I've solved anything because I bled on a bird. I was sick about what happened and I was still..." He looked out into the dark beyond the shelf. "Still half in love with it."

Vel did not look away. If she softened now, he'd think she was sparing him. If she pressed wrong, he'd close.

"That's the honest part," she said.

"It isn't a good part."

"No. Still honest."

The bone clicked again against his thumb. "Does that ever stop."

She thought about the first time a level answered under her skin. About the way power offered itself as clean arithmetic while consequence came later, muddier, harder to count.

"No," she said. "You just get better at hearing what comes after it."

He nodded slowly. "That seems worse than what I was hoping for."

"It is worse than what you were hoping for."

Another strip of grouse disappeared. He ate because the body wanted food even while the mind kept circling uglier things. Vel respected that more than speech.

They sat until the meat was mostly gone and the water in the pot had cooled enough to stop steaming. Night tightened around the bank. Somewhere across the stream something small pushed through brush and kept moving. It wasn't a boot. It was too light.

Kem scrubbed the grease off on the grass and spoke so quietly she almost missed the start of it.

"I keep meaning to ask about your first time."

She let the question sit. He did not hurry to fill it.

"Not the route," he said. "I know you won't — that's fine. The spending. When you started." He picked at a strip of bark beside him. "You don't talk about it the way people do when they hate something. But you don't sound like — I can't work out which direction you come at it from."

He'd asked before and she'd shut him down. He knew better now than to ask like he was owed it.

Vel dragged a strip of bark through the dirt with her thumbnail. The little fire had dropped low enough that the light coming off it turned every ridge of his knuckles ocher and made the cuts look deeper than they were.

"Avermouth," she said. Then, because the word didn't mean anything alone: "I was seventeen. My mother sold cloth at the canal market."

She hadn't planned to say that much. But he was sitting there with his fresh burns and his honestly cooked bird, and the asking had been careful enough.

"A girl went off the towpath rail into the water. Eight, maybe nine. Rotten wood — everybody knew it was rotten, everybody complained, nobody fixed it. She went through and the current took her under the bridge."

"In spring?"

"Snowmelt still coming down. She came up once and that was it."

Vel watched the bark split under her thumb.

"Forty people standing there. Hoarded coats. Spent traders. You could see them doing the math. Three levels, maybe four, for a pull. Wet clothes. One man with a House seal on his coat close enough the splash hit his boots." She snapped the bark. "Nobody moved."

"Were you scared?"

She looked at him. That wasn't the question she'd been ready for.

"I don't remember scared," she said. "I was seventeen and full and angry. I went in." She let the stick drop. "Missed her on the first grab. Water was faster than I thought. Spun us both under the bridge. I spent one level on a grip — didn't know the name for it, just knew I needed a hand to stick. Got one hand on the piling and one in the back of her dress and held on till two men came in far enough to drag us out."

"You were seventeen," he said, quieter.

"She cried the whole time they wrapped her up. Good sign."

"And you."

"Two ninety-nine. Dripping canal water over my mother's stock. Nobody thanked me. One woman asked if I'd ruined the cloth."

Kem's jaw worked once in the low light.

"What did your mother say?"

Vel hadn't thought about that in years. "She said, 'Get out of the street before you fall over.' Cleaned my hands when we got home. Asked if the girl was breathing when I let go. I said yes. She said good."

He made a sound under his breath. "Just good?"

"That was the whole thing."

The fire shifted. A tongue of flame climbed and failed.

"Two ninety-nine after the canal," Vel said. "Then two ninety-eight. Then the next one. Forty-one years of that."

Kem sat with the number.

"The Hoarded would call it waste," he said at last.

"They would."

"My father would've said it cleaner than that. Back when he could still finish the sentence all the way to the end."

"Tell me about him."

Ash striped his wrists and knuckles. He looked down between his knees.

"He was loud," Kem said. "Not — it wasn't noise. He was just there. In every room. He argued about everything. Trade routes, seating, whether Breve was an idiot or just dedicated to the appearance of it. He'd correct your arithmetic at dinner and then make you defend the answer." He picked at the soot on his knuckle. "That was the part that was alive."

"When did it change?"

"It wasn't one day." He looked at the fire. "Pieces went missing. Small ones first. He stopped finishing jokes. He'd ask something and forget he'd asked before anyone could answer. Someone would say his name and there'd be this — " He waved vaguely beside his head. "He'd come back from it. Give you one sentence that sounded fine if you didn't know he'd been gone."

"The Hoarded liked that."

"They needed it to be something. Uncle Aldris called it balance. The physician — I can't remember if it was deepening discipline or refined preservation. One of those. He said it while my father was holding a cup he'd forgotten to drink from." Kem gave a short ugly sound that wasn't quite a laugh. "Ten minutes with the cup. And there was a word for it."

"And your mother?"

He started to answer and stopped. His throat jumped once, as if the words had gone down wrong.

"She never used the clean names," he said. "She just — adjusted. Moved a plate before he had to search for it. Repeated things. If she straightened his sleeve after he spilled it looked like household order, not — " He broke off. "If anyone had said what was happening out loud, they'd have had to say what all of it was. So nobody did."

Vel listened to the stream and his breathing.

"I kept thinking it would stop where it was," he said. "Not get better. Just stop. Let him stay at the level of damage I'd already — " He rubbed the bridge of his nose hard. "That's a disgusting way to put it."

"It's an honest one."

"Yeah." His arm fell back to his knee. "Last Seventhday before I left. He sat through half the Rite staring at the centerpiece. The cup tilted and my mother straightened it without looking at me. Uncle Aldris smiled."

Vel shifted the stick in the coals. "How many years of that?"

"Fifteen. Give or take."

"And you stayed."

"Yeah." The word came out flat. "I couldn't do one more. I didn't have a theory about it. I just couldn't sit there and smile with the rest of them."

"He let you go?"

Kem froze. "My father wasn't — he didn't see it. My mother said my name once. Just once. I didn't turn around."

For a while neither of them spoke. Kem rubbed ash off one knuckle with his thumb and only smeared it wider.

"Can he still talk?" Vel asked.

"Some days. Fewer now. The words come out right but the meaning's somewhere else." He stared into the coals. "His Ledger's still full."

The words sat there.

Vel nudged a stick deeper into the coals and waited until the tip of it glowed.

"Eat," she said.

"I am."

"More."

So he did. The grouse was cold now, the fat turned white along the charred skin. When the last of it was gone, he stacked the bones in a neat little line and wiped the grease on the grass.

"That was the worst meal I've ever had," he said.

"Yeah."

"I made it."

"You did."

The line of bones held his attention for a while. Smoke had dried into his hair. Ash streaked one cheek. Under the dirt and soreness and fatigue, something in him had gone quieter.

"Thank you," he said. "For making me do it. All of it."

"Tomorrow you'll do it again."

He looked over. "All of it?"

"If we need it. And you're taking the heavy pack."

He took a careful breath and glanced toward his side before looking back at her. "How heavy."

"Thirty-five."

Silence held for a beat.

"You've been carrying thirty-five pounds through this country with twenty-eight levels left."

"Yes."

His eyes moved to her bedroll, her pack, the scarred hands in her lap, as if he were trying to assemble the story she'd told and the body sitting across from him into the same person.

"All right," he said. "I'll take it."

Vel banked the coals and laid the heavier pack beside his blanket so he couldn't miss it in the morning. His went close to the fire. Hers went between him and the trail.

The highlands stayed quiet.

From her blanket she could hear him settle by stages, each small adjustment negotiated around the rib. The stream kept running in the dark. Twenty-eight hummed low and steady at the back of her mind. In the morning he'd build the fire himself, shoulder the wrong pack, and hurt through it without buying the easy answer.

For tonight, that was enough.


# Chapter 6

Five days after Kem Aldraste walked out of the Vault, and the morning after the outrider recovery detail failed to bring him back, Sable Orm stood in the Council chamber.

The chamber had seventeen chairs, and Sable knew the occupant of each by the sound it made sitting down. Councilor Breve's knees cracked. Councilor Hain breathed through his mouth when he thought no one was listening. Lady Aldraste settled into her chair without a sound. Around this table, that counted as rank.

Sable had spent twenty-three years in this room, nearly all of them behind the recording desk and none of them under summons. To her, the chamber was a ledger with furniture: every column aligned, every entry supposed to end where it began.

"The Aldraste situation," Councilor Breve said.

Sable waited. Across two decades of Council service, she had learned that the first sentence of an address was rarely the one that mattered.

"Young Lord Aldraste has been outside the walls for approximately five days. He has spent an estimated twelve levels in that time. The outrider recovery detail authorized under standing protocol made contact yesterday. It did not recover him."

Around the table, pages stopped moving. Twelve levels. In the Vault, the number was obscene. Sable's nearest colleague had spent four across an entire career and had been spoken to about it afterward as if survival itself required moderation.

"The House signet was taken from the ceremonial stand," Lady Aldraste said. Her voice carried the calm of a woman who had decided exactly how much of herself the room was going to get. "My son did not plan this. That is the charitable interpretation, and I will be offering no other."

Sable recognized the structure at once. Lady Aldraste was entering a breach into Council business, not a son into family trouble.

"The question before the Council," Breve said, "is the next method of retrieval."

The first team had gone out under ordinary authority. Whatever came next would carry the Council's name more openly than anyone at the table wanted.

"There has been discussion regarding the deployment of specialized resources."

Specialized resources. Sable had encountered references to them in Council records three times in twenty-three years. Each reference had been redacted before filing. The hand changed each time. The secrecy had a procedure.

Her mind went at once to the sealed wing on the eastern corridor. Twenty-three years of passing those doors had taught her that some silences were manufactured more carefully than others.

"The Red Reserve," Lady Aldraste said.

Breve looked directly at Sable. "For the benefit of those present, the Red Reserve is a Council-maintained unit of Hoarded personnel authorized to train in the use of abilities. For security purposes."

They were authorized to train. They were permitted to spend. People had been drilling behind the eastern doors all along.

"The Reserve is not a viable option for this operation," Hain said. He said it too quickly.

Lady Aldraste answered in the cadence of someone reading from a prepared document. "Deploying trained spenders for a retrieval would constitute a public acknowledgment that the philosophy of preservation cannot address all institutional concerns. The existence of the Reserve is predicated on its non-existence. Using it visibly destroys the premise."

"Agreed," Breve said. "The Reserve is not deployable for this purpose."

"Auditor Orm," he said.

Sable's stomach performed a maneuver she did not have an administrative term for.

"The Council would like to discuss your availability for a field assignment."

"I am available for any assignment the Council requires." The correct response came out before the incorrect one finished forming.

"The retrieval of Kem Aldraste requires a lead operative with institutional credibility, administrative competence, and discretion. You have been recommended by multiple Council members." Breve did not look at Lady Aldraste when he said it.

"Councilor Breve. I have not left the capital in forty-seven years." Sable delivered the line as relevant data, not protest. "I have never operated field equipment. I have never navigated terrain. My longest recorded distance on foot in a single day was approximately two kilometers during the record-room relocation of Year Eighteen."

"Which you managed with distinction," Breve said.

"The logistics of document transport across three buildings are not comparable to pursuit."

"You are precisely what we need." Lady Aldraste met Sable's eyes as if confirming a selection already entered into the file. "Someone who represents what we are. Not what we have hidden."

Nobody contradicted her. Hain cleared his throat and stopped there. One edge of Lady Aldraste's thumbnail was torn. In twenty-three years, Sable had never seen that.

"I will need a team," Sable said.

"Of course. We have prepared a list of suitable operatives."

"Field-trained?"

Breve looked down at his papers. Hain adjusted a cuff. No one said yes.

"I see," Sable said.

Breve slid a folder across the table. The cover page read *Council-Authorized Retrieval: Aldraste, Kem / Lead Operative Acceptance Packet*. Beneath it sat route warrants, equipment inventories, secrecy acknowledgments, district courtesy notices, and a sheaf of blank Form 7-Cs clipped in groups of five.

"You will sign before departure," Breve said.

Sable opened to the liability page. "Any unauthorized expenditure, unlogged route deviation, equipment loss, barter commitment, or local procurement undertaken in the field will be assigned to the team lead for review."

"That is standard language," Hain said.

"For what standard."

"Field work."

Sable looked at him. "By your own account, the Council is not deploying field personnel."

No one answered. Lady Aldraste adjusted one page in front of her, a motion so small it could only have been irritation.

Sable turned the next page. "No cash allotment."

"Unnecessary," Breve said. "This is not a trade excursion."

"Food, ferries, local guidance, emergency transport, replacement supplies, animal hire, and passage fees all exist outside trade excursions."

"The districts understand Council priority."

Sable had drafted enough interdistrict correspondence to know that districts understood delay, procedure, and the profitable edges of both.

"And the expenditure forms?" she asked.

"Total expenditure is not to exceed the pre-authorized limit of two levels per operative per day," Breve said. "Ability use must be pre-authorized by the team lead and documented."

"Documented when."

"At the time of use."

She let the page rest against the folder. "In the field."

"Yes."

There were moments when the Council's devotion to doctrine rose high enough that it stopped resembling belief and became architecture instead. A person could stand inside it and still not understand how it had been built.

"I would like a clarification entered into the file," Sable said. "If immediate danger requires expenditure before documentation can be produced, is the field team expected to preserve paperwork or bodily integrity."

Hain answered first. "The protocol contains an emergency provision."

"Reducing the written explanation from two hundred words to one hundred," Sable said, still reading. "Yes. I have located the mercy."

For the first time that morning, Breve looked almost embarrassed. It improved him slightly.

"Sign the packet, Auditor Orm," Lady Aldraste said. "Retrieve my son. Keep the Council deniable. You may categorize the rest as you please."

Sable signed because refusal would only have delayed the same signature by the length of a more crowded argument.

The Council's list of suitable operatives contained eight names. By midday it had become five citizens in Sable's office and three written refusals: one joint condition, one inventory conflict, one exemption request citing seven separate protocols. All five remaining operatives were level 300. That narrowed nothing.

Tarrell arrived with three notebooks and a personal seal and announced, before sitting down, that he had been told this was a documentation assignment. Kess had volunteered and looked pleased with herself in the strained, overbright way of someone who had mistaken apprehension for zeal. Lenn came with a field manual memorized and a pack cinched high enough to flay him by noon. Marris kept checking her Ledger as though the 300 might turn out to be a clerical error. Chael, a records porter, brought a compass and the only shoulders in the room that looked as though they had lifted anything heavier than paper.

"I was told this was a documentation assignment," Tarrell said again.

"It is a field retrieval," Sable said.

"Then my classification headers are wrong."

The crate from Council improved nobody. It held six ration packs, six bedrolls tagged with serial numbers, six Communication charms in lead-lined cases, a medical kit, a hand-drawn map, and a four-page protocol that repeated the two-level daily expenditure cap three times and omitted practical money entirely. The medical instructions referenced an Appendix D that did not exist. No weapons had been issued.

Sable read the Form 7-C requirements aloud once so the room could absorb the insult in full. Two hundred words before any authorized ability use. One hundred in direct and immediate physical threat, still prior to deployment.

"Prior to," Lenn repeated.

"Yes," Sable said.

One trip down to the service corridor outside Archive Three sorted most of what the packet had mislabeled. Tarrell hit a wall with his notebooks before the second landing. Kess bled through a heel wrap. Lenn knew the names of load-bearing knots and not the feel of them. Marris kept her balance and lost her breath. Chael carried his own pack, two spare bedrolls, and everybody else's dignity without complaint.

Sable reassigned the weight accordingly. Chael took the extra rations and the medical kit. Tarrell was permitted one notebook. Marris got the field log because it was the only object she could manage without fumbling. Kess lost the parasol. Lenn retied every pack in the room, including Sable's, because competence made of theory was still competence of a kind.

When Sable asked who could start a cooking fire without lamp oil, only Chael answered.

"Records furnace," he said. "I used to help in winter."

"That is now a qualification."

By dusk she had a team that could carry itself badly, a stack of pre-labeled emergency forms, and less faith in the Council's definitions than she had that morning. She wrote the narrowest instructions she could devise across the top page of each packet: if you are bleeding, spend first and write later; if you are not bleeding, find me before you do anything permanent; if I am unconscious, Chael authorizes.

They left at dawn.

The gate was where Kem Aldraste had walked out five days earlier. The ceremonial stand where House signets were displayed still held an empty square of flattened velvet in the Aldraste position.

"Senior Auditor." The gate warden held the departure ledger open. "How many in the party?"

"Six. Council-authorized retrieval operation."

"Estimated return date?"

"Three to four weeks."

He wrote it down with immaculate handwriting. Nothing in his manner suggested that six level-300 Hoarded citizens walking out to pursue a defector across open country was unusual enough to warrant a different pen.

From its case, Sable took the Wayfinder charm, a small metal disc cool against her skin, the tracking glyph cut into one face. The cost was two levels.

Two levels. Her mother had died at 296, ashamed of the four she had spent across an entire life. Sable had spent none.

Kem's file supplied the face: formal, composed, twenty-six years of stillness and then the gate.

She activated the glyph.

Her knees went first.

Stone struck through her skirt as she caught the archway. Direction flooded in, south-southeast, distance rendered as strain rather than miles, the clean pull of a living line running through her body toward someone moving away. The knowledge had nothing to do with sight or hearing. It entered lower and stranger, as if some buried instrument inside her had been struck hard enough to wake.

300 became 298.

The threshold stone hit the back of her legs when she sat.

"Senior Auditor?" Lenn asked.

"A moment."

Breathing came too fast. The Wayfinder did not care. It kept pulling at the base of her skull. Against the pace her present team had any honest chance of holding, the target felt four or five days ahead. Against a single road-hardened body, less. The charm did not bother separating distance from incompetence.

298. Her mind kept returning to the number and dropping against it, the way a foot drops when it expects one more stair and finds only air.

She made herself stand.

"The target is south-southeast," Sable said. Her voice held. "Still moving. We proceed south."

They walked through the gate. Warm air struck her face carrying dust, animal smell, and sunlight already stored in stone. The sky was too large. Nothing overhead contained it. The road under her boots gave slightly where packed earth had been worn by wheels and weather, and each irregularity announced itself all the way up her legs. Behind her rose the walls that had contained her entire life. She did not look back.

By the first hour, the road had begun revising the Council's assumptions. Kess had bled through her wrap again. Tarrell surrendered his notebook to Chael after discovering he could not carry paper and a pack at the same time. Marris could not keep the field log while walking. Lenn abandoned the pace he had planned in his head. Chael, carrying half the room, was the only one who never complained.

The Council map showed a crossing two kilometers on: a district-maintained cut with a plank bridge and, in the margin, the notation *cooperation expected*. Sable had seen the phrase on correspondence for years. On paper it meant somebody else would do something inconvenient later and invoice the correct office.

On the road it meant a queue of grain carts, three sweating laborers knee-deep in a runoff channel, and half a retaining wall lying open in the water.

The bridge had not collapsed. It had been removed. Two long planks rested on the far bank while the laborers reset the stone lip that held the crossing in place. One of them was using a low Shaping ability in short, practiced bursts, smoothing fractured edges so the new stones would seat cleanly. Each burst cost something. The worker did not treat that fact like a sermon. He treated it like lifting.

A woman standing on a wagon tongue held a ledger board and shouted names as if this were a market queue rather than a blocked road. When she saw six clean-coated Hoarded citizens approaching with Council packs and untouched faces, her mouth flattened into something that was not surprise.

"Crossing's one cart at a time once the plank goes back," she said. "If you're on foot, you wait behind the grain."

Sable presented the district courtesy notice. "Council-authorized retrieval operation. Passage is required."

The woman read the first line, skipped the stamp, and handed it back. "Fine. Required. The wall's still broken."

"The notice states cooperation is expected."

"It is. We're cooperating with the people whose grain goes rotten if this road stays shut."

"This operation has priority standing," Sable said.

"With who?"

Sable almost said *the Council* and realized half a second before speaking that the answer would change nothing here unless it arrived attached to labor, marks, or food.

"If the district clerk needs to log the warrant, where is the office?" she asked.

"East spur. Two miles." The woman nodded toward a side road. "If you want it written up and billed later, go there. If you want across now, you wait or you help."

Tarrell stared at the channel. "We were issued no local payment instruments."

"That sounds terrible," the woman said, not sounding terrible at all.

Behind them, a mule stamped and shook flies from its ears. Ahead, the Shaper pressed a wrist to the reset stone and spent again. The line moved by inches. The Wayfinder kept tugging south.

Sable calculated. The east spur would cost time they did not have. Waiting behind three grain carts would also cost time they did not have. Activating a Communication charm to report delay would cost a level, require a justification, and reach an office that had omitted cash from the kit and called a dismantled crossing manageable.

Her preparation had accounted for signatures, schedules, and the possibility of immediate bodily harm. It had not accounted for the road's refusal to care about any of those.

"Chael," she said. "Lenn. Can you move stone?"

Chael looked at the wall. "Yes."

Lenn looked at it as though the field manual might still produce a hidden appendix if he stared hard enough. "I believe so."

"Good. We will help."

The woman on the wagon tongue studied Sable for one beat, then jerked her chin toward the channel. "Get in, then. If the next cart sits till noon I want it to be because the work's bad, not because you lot stood there thinking about it."

The water in the cut was only calf deep, but it ran fast where the retaining stones had come loose. Chael climbed down first and planted himself wide. Lenn followed with the rigid bravery of a man who would rather have received a diagram. Sable handed Marris the field log, stripped off her gloves, and went in after them because ranking six people from dry ground while they carried stone for her seemed likely to end the wrong part of her life.

The first rock nearly took her balance with it. Its weight was not the abstract kind recorded on manifests. It dragged, scraped, shifted, and taught her more in one wet second than the Council's packet had managed in four pages.

Kess and Tarrell stayed on the bank until the woman with the ledger board pointed at the loose end of a plank and said, "If you've got arms, use them." Kess did. Tarrell did after a visible conversation with himself. Marris took names and crossings because it was the only task no one had to explain twice.

For forty minutes the retrieval team became unpaid district labor. The Shaper seated stone. Chael and Lenn hauled. Sable learned which muscles objected to current and which objected to being asked anything at all. Kess slipped once and barked her shin hard enough to sit down in the mud. Tarrell dropped his end of the plank when a wagon wheel jolted sideways, then apologized to the plank.

The woman on the wagon tongue ran the line with ruthless fairness. One grain cart, then another, then the team on foot, then the third cart. No one cut because of a seal. No one bowed because of 300. A child riding sacks of meal watched Sable stagger up the far bank with her hem soaked to the knee and asked his father, not quietly, whether priests were supposed to do roadwork.

Sable would have corrected the category if she had not been busy getting air back.

When they finally crossed, the woman held out the courtesy notice again. A strip of mud had dried across the Council stamp.

"Take it to the clerk if you care about repayment," she said. "Or don't. We were fixing the road either way."

Sable took the paper. "What is the nearest settlement south?"

"Depends what you call a settlement." The woman pointed with the edge of her board. "You keep on this road another three hours and you'll hit Low Bridge and whatever passes for an inn there. If you're looking for a young man in clothes too good for his judgment, he came through yesterday with a woman who looked like she could sleep in a ditch and wake up winning the argument."

The Wayfinder pulled south-southeast. The line held.

"Did he ask for supplies?"

"He paid for bread and walked out before it cooled."

Kess said, very quietly, "He paid."

The woman heard her anyway. "That surprises you."

"We were issued paperwork," Tarrell said.

"Then I hope it eats."

No one answered that. There was too much of it to answer.

They walked on because the target was still moving and the day had already spent more of itself than Sable had authorized. By the time the light began thinning, Kess's heel had opened again, Lenn's shoulders were raw where the straps rubbed, and Tarrell had started coughing after swallowing runoff. Low Bridge remained ahead. Sable stopped the team short of it because getting there by dark would have required a pace Kess could not keep and Lenn should not.

The camp they found was a hard patch beyond the road where wagon ruts widened and an old fire ring suggested other failures of planning. Sable ordered boots off. Lenn cleaned what he could under instruction. Chael coaxed a fire out of bark scrap and stubbornness on the third attempt. Marris portioned rations. South kept humming inside Sable's bones.

Only then did the blank Form 7-Cs reassert themselves.

Sable spread one on her knee while the others ate and stared at the printed headings.

DATE OF EXPENDITURE.

LOCATION OF EXPENDITURE.

AUTHORIZING OFFICER.

NATURE OF THREAT.

JUSTIFICATION OF PERMANENT LEVEL LOSS.

MINIMUM 200 WORDS.

She wrote the easy parts first: the date, the gate departure, her name, the expenditure amount of two levels. She stopped at *Nature of Threat*.

What had the threat been, precisely. They had risked losing the line before the pursuit began. They had risked sending six untrained citizens into open country with no more guidance than a hand-drawn route and doctrine. Without the Wayfinder they would have left the gate already slower and stupider than the person they were trying to catch.

She wrote: *Target mobility combined with absence of trained tracking capacity within assigned party.*

It was true. It was also nothing like the truth of the charm entering her body and staying there.

Tarrell leaned toward the page before she could stop him. "If you require additional language, the packet's doctrinal appendix suggests several approved phrasing structures."

Sable looked up.

"You memorized the appendix."

"Only the first page," he said. "The second appeared repetitive."

That was the nearest thing to humor the road had produced since morning. It passed around the fire in exhausted forms. Kess made a sound that might have been a laugh if her heel hurt less. Even Marris lifted her head.

"Recite it," Sable said.

Tarrell blinked. "Recite."

"Yes."

He did, because Hoarded training had turned obedience into a reflex long before any of them had begun questioning the uses of it.

"When evaluating justified expenditure, the reviewing authority shall consider proportionality, institutional necessity, availability of lower-cost alternatives, and maintenance of doctrinal confidence among observing parties."

"There," Sable said, and wrote down the words that could survive audit.

She completed the report because she had signed for the irregularities and because the Council would notice missing paper before it noticed missing people. At the bottom, in the box marked *LESSONS FOR FUTURE DEPLOYMENT*, she wrote: *Initial operating assumptions require revision.*

Then she set the form aside and opened the map again.

Kem had left the Vault five days earlier. A woman at the crossing had seen him in Low Bridge yesterday. Those were the first numbers all day that meant anything. The target was one day ahead at the last confirmed stop and still moving south with a companion who knew how to keep him moving. The rest of the gap was not country. The rest of the gap was Sable's team: Kess's torn heel, Lenn's shoulders, Tarrell's breath, the lost time at the bridge, the whole polished stupidity of sending six preserved people into the road and calling it pursuit.

South of Low Bridge the line opened into farms, wagons, and side tracks. Witness memory would thin there. If she put the team into Low Bridge by midday, they were still one day behind a person. If she missed it, they were back to chasing what people said they had seen.

She folded the half-dry report, leaned across the fire, and woke Chael first because he would stand up the fastest.

"We leave before light," she said.

Chael pushed himself onto one elbow. "How bad?"

"Kem Aldraste passed Low Bridge yesterday. Low Bridge by midday or we lose the line."


# Chapter 7

The next morning the wrong pack sat beside Kem's blanket.

Vel had switched them while he slept. He knew it the moment he hauled it upright. Flour, pan, both water skins, something dense and wrapped in canvas at the bottom. The load rode meaner than the one he'd gone to sleep beside. Yesterday's bruises woke as soon as the straps found them.

He built the fire himself. The tinder caught, died, caught again. By the time Vel came back from the trees, there was a low ugly flame chewing through damp twigs and Kem was standing beside it smoke-eyed and pleased in a way he would've denied if asked. She looked at the fire, then at the pack on his shoulders.

"Walk," she said.

He did. By noon the straps had cut hot lines into his collarbones and the base of his spine felt nailed together. The Ledger sat behind all of it, patient and available. A minor Strength Charm would've made the next climb easier. A balance correction would've kept the load from dragging him sideways on the descent. He thought about both. He kept walking anyway, because she'd said he'd do it again and this was plainly what she'd meant.
