That first day he spent nothing. He got the evening fire going with wet bark and temper. He carried the heavier load until his shoulders stopped feeling like separate parts of him. When they made camp, Vel lifted the pack off him, pressed a thumb once into the strap dents, and said, "Better."

That night his arms shook when he tried to work the buckles loose. He slept mostly on his back because either shoulder lit up when he rolled. At dawn Vel switched the packs again without comment. The day after that she made him take the heavier one only on climbs. Then she stopped explaining the pattern at all. He quit asking what each variation was meant to prove and started assuming the proof was whichever part of him hurt.

He held to that longer than he would've guessed and less long than he should have. On the third night the flint turned slick in steady drizzle and he burned nearly a level on a Flame Charm because he was cold and tired and had started to hate friction. Vel looked at the clean blue coal, then at the wet flint beside it.

"Warm now?" she asked.

"Yes."

She nodded once and went back to chewing dried meat. That was worse than anger. By the time he lit the cave two days later, he was already explaining the spend to himself before he made it: one level, barely one, dark like that wasn't useful to anybody. The morning before the settlement, he shaved a climb with Strength after waking sore in places he hadn't known to name. None of it was necessary. Each time he found a reason first and the spend second.

By Vel's reckoning, the new Hoarded pursuit had been on the road for six days. On Kem's own count, he'd been gone from the Vault eleven. The road improved before the town appeared. For six days Kem had been walking on dirt tracks, game trails, whatever path Vel chose that kept them below ridgelines and out of sight. Now the ground under his boots was packed and graded, not anything the Vault would recognize as a proper road, but something a Shaper had laid down and the town had kept passable. The surface was compressed and even, rainwater channels cut along both sides. The ditch edges were sharp. Somebody had cleared them recently. The road had been paid for again and again.

Vel didn't comment on it. She adjusted her pack and kept walking, her pace the same efficient tempo she held whether the ground was flat or climbing, and Kem had stopped trying to match it and settled into his own stride, which was longer but less economical. The blisters he'd raised the first two days had hardened into calluses. That counted as progress.

The settlement came into view as they crested a long rise: a cluster of buildings in a shallow valley, sixty or seventy structures, with fields spreading out on three sides and a line of low hills to the south. Early summer light sat heavy on the rooftops, and the heat had a different quality here than in the forested highlands, drier and closer, pressing against exposed skin and staying there. It was the largest thing Kem had seen since the Vault, and it looked nothing like the Vault. The buildings were stone and timber, shaped but not uniform. Some walls curved where a Shaping ability had been applied freehand, others squared off block by block. Repairs showed as visible seams between ability-work and manual labor, and nobody had bothered to make the seams invisible. The Vault's architecture existed to deny that maintenance was necessary. This place wore its maintenance like working clothes.

What struck him was the sound.

The Vault was quiet.

This settlement hummed.

A low, warm vibration ran under everything: Fertility Charms, he realized, active in the fields to the east. Not one but dozens, the combined output of farmers spending levels daily to make soil yield more than it would on its own. The sound was like standing near a hive, a thrumming he felt in his back teeth before he heard it with his ears. Layered over that came the crack and grind of Shaping work, someone reshaping stone on the settlement's far side, a distant rhythm of ability striking material. And closer, another pulse he couldn't identify at first. Rhythmic, gentle, warm. It rose and fell like breathing.

"What's that?" he asked.

Vel had stopped to let him look. She followed his gaze toward a low building on the settlement's southern edge, its door propped open with a stone. "Healing ward. They've got a healer who takes patients. That pulse is a Restoration Charm running."

"Running. As in, continuously?"

"She keeps it going for an hour each morning. Costs her a level every few weeks, depending on how many people she's working on."

A level every few weeks. If the healer was at, say, 180, she'd been feeding that ward for years, maybe decades. Morning after morning, a little of her own body turned into other people's knees and lungs and knitted skin. In the Vault, polite families would have lowered their voices over her name: *she had such potential, but she spent it all.* In the Conservatory Rite, they would have seated her near the door, if they'd admitted her at all.

From where Kem stood, listening to the warm pulse of her ability carrying over the morning air, she sounded like the most useful person in the valley.

They walked in. The settlement's main road was wider than the approach, lined with buildings that served as both homes and businesses: a tanner's workshop with hides stretched on a frame outside, a grain store with its doors open and the smell of dry seed carrying out, a place that sold tools and rope and had been expanded three times in different decades, each addition visible in the stonework. People were out, working, calling to each other across the road, carrying loads without haste. A man with a crate on his shoulder nodded at Vel as he passed. She nodded back. Two women sat on a bench outside a doorway, shelling peas into a bowl between them, talking in voices too low for Kem to catch. One of them looked up at him, registered the way he kept stopping to take things in, and went back to her work.

Nobody nodded at Kem. They looked, though. Briefly. Long enough to sort him: outsider, soft, sheltered. The last one was accurate enough that it landed.

A woman was repairing a section of wall on a building to their left. She had her fingertips flat against the stone and the material was moving under them, bulging outward to fill a crack, settling back to flush. Her face was concentrated, her free arm braced against the wall for balance, and when she finished she stepped back and shook out her wrist like the joint ached. A man on a ladder above her called down a criticism Kem couldn't catch, and she laughed and told him to do it himself if he was going to complain about her pointing.

She moved to the next section and started again. Each repair cost a sliver of level, not much at once but enough to add up. Every time she leaned into the stone, the wall smoothed and a fresh stiffness climbed her wrist. She kept going.

Kem looked down at his own scraped knuckles. His Ledger pulsed at the edge of his awareness: 225. Twenty levels gone in six days, most of them to impatience. More than this woman was spending to keep a wall standing and her neighbors dry.

"We need supplies," Vel said. She'd been watching him watch the settlement, but she didn't say anything about it. "Food for the next stretch south, and I need route conditions. If the pursuit's on the trade road, we stop using the trade road."

She led them to a tavern, broad and low, with a clay-tile roof and a yard where someone had set up benches under a canvas shade. A few chickens scratched in the dirt near the entrance. Inside was dim and cool, smelling of grain beer, cooked onions, and the sour bite of old ale worked into the wood over decades. Half a dozen patrons sat at tables, men and women in working clothes, eating midday meals or drinking slowly. They looked up when Vel and Kem entered. The looks settled on Kem in the same measuring way they had outside, held for a beat, and moved on. One man went back to his food. A woman at the bar turned fully to study him, then turned away.

The tavern keeper came from a back room, a crate of clay bottles braced against her hip. She was perhaps fifty, heavy-set, with her hair pulled back under a cloth and forearms thick from years spent lifting, pouring, and hauling stock. A leather brace wrapped her left knee, dark with old oil where the straps crossed. When she shifted her weight, she did it around that leg. She set the crate on the bar with a practiced thud and looked at them.

"Passing through?" she said to Vel.

"Couple of days. We need dried food for six days' travel south. Whatever you've got."

"I can put that together." The keeper's eyes moved to Kem and took him in once. "Him too?"

"Him too."

She started pulling items from below the bar: wrapped packages of dried meat, hard bread, a pouch of grain that smelled nutty. She moved with quick practiced economy, no abilities, each package found without looking, each bottle set down without a clink. Kem stood back while Vel negotiated quantities and price, and he let his eyes move around the room. The tavern was plain but swept clean. Decorative grooves had been shaped into the stone walls near the ceiling, a small touch of craft that had cost a fraction of a level and was worth it to whoever lived here. The tables were scarred from use. A cat slept on a chair near the window, its tail hanging off the edge.

The keeper set the food packages on the bar and named a price. Vel counted marks from a leather pouch, Kem's marks, technically, converted from the portable valuables he'd taken from the Vault. Coins and small trade goods that amounted to more money than most people in this room earned in a season. Vel paid without comment, and the keeper sorted the coins into a box beneath the counter.

Then her eyes dropped to the ring against the bar.

His House signet was still on him. He'd taken it from the ceremonial stand at the Vault gate eleven days ago, not because he'd decided to bring it but because leaving it would have meant he'd thought the whole thing through. He'd been wearing it since. It was his father's crest and his grandfather's. In the Vault, he'd worn things like that the way he'd worn sleeves.

The keeper's face changed.

It wasn't anger. Her face simply shut a door. The professional courtesy she'd been showing Vel drained out of her expression and what replaced it was flat and careful. She looked at the signet, then at Kem's face, then at the signet again, and her mouth became a line. The crease between her eyebrows deepened. Whatever memory the crest had pulled up, it had already reached its verdict, and Kem's road dust and awkward posture in her tavern did nothing to disturb it.

She finished stacking the food packages. She pushed them across the bar toward Vel, not Kem. "Anything else?" she said, and the temperature of her voice had dropped to a place where *anything else* meant *please leave*.

"No," Vel said. "That's fine."

Kem opened his mouth. He didn't know what he was going to say. An explanation, an apology, some version of *I'm not what you think,* which was absurd because she was looking at the physical proof of exactly what he was. He closed his mouth. The keeper picked up her empty crate and went back through the doorway without looking at him again.

He stood there with his wrist on the bar and the ring still visible in the silence the keeper had left in her wake. The man at the nearest table glanced over, saw it, and looked away, finished with him already.

Kem looked down at the signet. On him, it had always looked ordinary. The keeper had looked at it the way people looked at rot in a grain sack. He turned his wrist inward.

Vel was shouldering her pack. She'd seen the whole thing. She didn't turn toward him or offer commentary. She gave him room to stand in it.

"I didn't know," he said, once they were outside.

Vel squinted against the midday sun. "You didn't know what?"

He started to answer and stopped. The keeper's face came back to him, the neat way it had closed. The ring had done all the talking.

"Any of it," he said. "What any of this looked like from out here."

"Yeah." She adjusted a strap on her pack. "Come on. I need to find someone about the southern routes."

---

They found Dren at the market yard.

Vel had stopped at the settlement's well and asked a man drawing water about a trader who knew the country south of the Spent Towns. The man jerked his chin west, then let his gaze drop to the ring. "Dren. He's loading up over there. Supposed to head east tomorrow. Unless east can wait."

"We need him heading south."

"Then offer enough to make him stupid." The man braced the bucket between his boots and looped the rope over the well post. "Bellwasher came in from the north road at dawn saying a Hoarded party spent silver in Ashmarker for a description of a tall pale man and an older woman traveling together. Somebody mentioned a house-ring." His eyes flicked to the signet again. "That's been the morning's entertainment."

Vel's expression stayed flat. "How far north is Ashmarker?"

"Day and a half by wagon. Less if they're riding light."

Kem tucked the ring against the strap of his pack without meaning to. It pressed cold into his skin.

"Thanks," Vel said.

The market yard was a packed-dirt clearing on the settlement's western edge where carts and pack animals and stacked goods created a working chaos Kem found difficult to parse. People argued over quantities, loaded crates, inspected produce. A boy chased a loose goat between two carts and crashed into a woman carrying a bolt of cloth. She swore at him without surprise and kept walking. A man was using a minor Shaping ability to repair a cracked cart wheel, the wood swelling and sealing under his touch, a job that would've taken a carpenter half a day and here took thirty seconds at the cost of a fraction of a level. The spending was constant and casual, part of the working rhythm, and nobody around treated it as anything other than getting things done.

Dren was loading his cart near the yard's far fence. He was shorter than Kem had assumed, stocky and road-darkened, with a pale rope-burn scar along the outside of his left forearm. He loaded with old-route precision, each item placed where it balanced the load, each tie cinched with a knot Kem couldn't imagine coming loose. His boots were battered, resoled at least once, the leather cracking at the ankles. He wore a belt crowded with gear: a knife, a water flask, a coil of cord, a pouch Kem couldn't identify.

Vel walked up to him directly. "You Dren? I'm told you know the routes south."

He looked up from a strap he was tightening. His eyes went over Vel first, quick and practical: the knife, the road dust, the fact that she came straight to his cart instead of looking for someone else to ask. Then he looked at Kem longer, at the signet, at the half-step of hesitation before he settled where he was standing.

"I know them," Dren said. He went back to his strap. "Southern routes are shit. Towns thin out after two days, then it's hill country, then the Margins." He didn't look up. "Nobody goes there who doesn't have to." His gaze touched the signet. "And unless this yard's produced another pale bastard in the last hour, you're the pair the north road's talking about."

"We're going on purpose," Vel said.

"Figured." He tested the strap with a sharp tug. "You and the lord."

"He's not —"

"He's Hoarded. I can tell by the way he's standing. The ring helps. He's standing in my market yard like he's waiting for someone to bring him a chair." Dren looked at Kem. "No offense."

"I'm not waiting for a chair," Kem said.

"Sure. What are you paying?"

Vel named the sum. Kem watched Dren's face as the number landed — a flicker around the eyes, then a glance down at the cart, as if the marks were already being cut into smaller uses.

"That's a damn double eastern run," Dren said. "Before I lose half of it dumping the cart."

"Yes," Vel said.

He straightened up. Where Vel counted levels, Dren counted marks, sale prices, and days away from home.

"South to where?" Dren asked.

"Past the settlements' southern edge. Through the hill country to the rougher terrain beyond. We need someone who knows the water sources and the safe camps."

"I know the water. Terrain's fine." He spat to one side. "It's the people in it. Bandits. Folks drifting north from the Margins who'll take your pack and maybe feel bad about it." He squinted at Kem. "He know how to handle himself?"

"He's learning," Vel said.

"How fast?"

"Fast enough."

Dren didn't look convinced. He rubbed his jaw with the back of his wrist, leaving a streak of road dust on his cheek. "How long?"

"Five, six days to the edge. Then you're done and you head back."

"I was heading east tomorrow. Callowfield. Reliable buyers, reliable contract." He picked at a rope knot near his hip. "I need that money."

"I'm asking you to take better money for a week's work."

"Yeah, and I know why it's better." He leaned against his cart and crossed his arms. "South routes pay well because south routes are shit." His forearms were thick from years of loading cargo, and the old scar caught the light. "I've got a healer up in Roth I'm trying to get to, and every run's part of that." He looked at the wheel rut by his boot. "My girl. Mira. Her legs are —" He rubbed the back of his wrist across his jaw and looked at his cart. "Point is, your number gets me closer than Callowfield. But Callowfield doesn't go south."

"It's six days," Vel said. "We're not looking for trouble."

"You're paying me double an eastern run to go south fast. I've been hauling cargo twenty years." He looked at Kem. "He's wearing a Hoarded signet in Spent Towns. That's not six days I want to sign up for."

A silence opened. The market yard moved around them. Carts loaded, people argued, the boy chased his goat back the other way. Dren stood with one arm braced on the cart wheel, saying nothing. The market kept clattering around them. He let the silence sit until it started to feel expensive.

"There are people looking for him," Vel said. "They're behind us. If we move smart, they stay behind us."

Dren absorbed this. His expression didn't change. "Hoarded retrieval?"

"Close enough."

"Great." He scratched the back of his neck. The gesture pulled his collar aside and showed more road-darkened skin, shirt salt-stiff at the seam. "So if they catch up, I'm the bastard standing next to him."

"We've handled them before," Vel said.

"How many levels did 'handling them' cost?"

Vel didn't answer immediately, and that was its own kind of answer. Dren caught it.

"Right." He uncrossed his arms. "Half up front. Other half before I turn back." He looked at the cart. "Cart stays here — I take it south and every wheel rut tells them where we went. I need today to dump what I can, stash the rest, and hear whether Bellwasher was drunk or useful."

"We leave tomorrow morning," Vel said.

"Tomorrow morning means I'm selling at a loss. Anything I can't move by tonight sits in someone's shed and I'll be lucky to get two-thirds of it back. That cost comes out of your side."

Vel looked at Kem. Kem looked at the pouch on her belt. His Vault money, converted into the currency of a world where people worked for what they had.

"Agreed," Kem said.

"Agreed," Dren said. He stepped away from the cart and took a fuller measure of Kem, like he was deciding whether the load was worth the road. "You ever carried a pack for more than a day before this?"

"Ten days now."

"How's it feel?"

"My shoulders hurt."

Dren's mouth twitched once, the closest he'd come to approval. "Tomorrow morning. Be ready." He turned back to his cart, then stopped. "And put that ring somewhere it can't catch the light. I don't need the southern settlements deciding I'm running escort for a Hoarded inspection."

He started untying the ropes on his cargo, then glanced back once. "Camp outside the settlement. I'll find you after dark. If Bellwasher was telling the truth, tomorrow changes."

Vel turned and headed for the edge of the market yard. Kem followed, adjusting his pack where the straps bit into the muscle at the base of his neck. The ache ran from there into the shoulder joint. Vel had told him it would eventually become background noise. It hadn't yet.

They found a spot outside the settlement to camp for the night, in a grove of trees where the ground was soft with leaf litter and the air smelled of warm earth and dry grass. Vel built a fire the way she always did, flint and dry tinder, no abilities, each motion practiced. The settlement sat below them in the fading light, its shapes softening as evening came on. The Fertility Charm hum had quieted as the farmers stopped for the day, but the healing ward's pulse still ran, faint and steady, carrying over the distance.

From the rise between the trees, Kem could still see the market yard. Dren worked it until the light thinned, unloading his cart at a pace that made the haggling uglier. A woman in a blue apron talked him down on a stack of cured hides and he let her, jaw tight, because taking the insult cost less than waiting for a fairer buyer. Two boys rolled a barrel toward a shed while Dren argued with a man over storage space and finally counted out marks as if peeling skin. Kem watched until distance turned them into shapes. Every hurried sale was another price attached to escorting him.

Kem sat on a rock near the fire and worked the signet around with his thumb. The metal was warm from his skin and from the long day's sun. He thought about taking it off, putting it in his pocket. Then he pictured the keeper pushing the food toward Vel and left it where it was.

He left it on.

Full dark had not settled yet when footsteps came through the leaf litter. Dren emerged between the trees with a road pack on his shoulders and no cart behind him. He set the pack down by the fire and wiped his forearm across his mouth.

"Bellwasher was useful," he said. "So was a cooper's apprentice who came in late. Your people were in Ashmarker asking smart questions and paying stupid money. They know you're south. They don't know which road we take. I'd like to keep it that way."

Vel looked up. "Cart's dealt with?"

"Sold half. Stashed half. Lost money on both. You'll hear about it if I live long enough." He pointed south. "We leave before the sky's properly light. Ditch line east of the barley, then west once the farms stop watching. No fire after we move. No abilities unless somebody's bleeding or about to stop. And cover the ring."

His eyes settled on Kem for the last part. "I mean it. People here don't love the Hoarded enough to keep their mouths shut for free."

"I heard you," Kem said.

Dren nodded once. "Good." He jerked his chin downslope, toward a darker patch of trees. "I'm bedding down there. Wake me if somebody's dying. Not for anything smaller."

He picked up the pack and went.

Vel watched him disappear between the trees, then ground the fire lower with the end of a branch. "Sleep while you can. We move the minute he wakes."

Kem pulled a strip of cloth from the bottom of his pack, one of the clean scraps Vel kept for binding cuts, and wrapped it over the signet until the crest disappeared under dingy linen. The knot came out ugly. It held.

Below them, the settlement darkened by degrees. A door thudded shut. Someone laughed once and stopped. Farther off, a dog started up and another answered. Kem tried to picture the route Dren had just bought for them: ditch line east of the barley, west once the farms stopped watching, no wheel ruts, no fire.

A day and a half north, people had paid to learn his face. Before dawn, they would be off the road and into the fields before that money could catch up.


# Chapter 8

Kem reached for Strength on the rise before the depot and stopped with his thumb already on the ring.

"Don't," Vel said.

He dropped his arm. "I wasn't casting."

"You were deciding whether walking counted as an emergency."

Dren kept moving up the slope without looking back. "If the hill beats you, wait till you meet south."

Kem muttered something profane he still wore like a new coat and shifted the pack instead. The strap had rubbed one shoulder raw over the last three days, and the road had finally bullied the old careful posture out of him. He climbed it the ordinary way, boots slipping in the dust.

Three days south of the Spent Towns, that was what tomorrow had turned into. No levels for heat. No levels for bad footing. No levels because a body objected to being a body. South of the last managed farms, the trade road had narrowed to dirt and the fields had gone from rich to merely serviceable. Early summer sat thick in the air. Cut grain, drying mud, old Shaping left in stone too long. Vel counted the road south in levels. Twenty-eight sat behind everything else.

Dren walked point and read the track by habit: fresh ruts, local bootprints, a wheel listing left under too much weight, last night's soft spots where a wagon had cut deeper after dark. Twenty years on the southern routes showed in how little of his attention looked busy. At the crest he nudged the brim of the sun-faded hat he only wore when the road got honest enough to hurt and said, "There's the depot. Grain storage, one inn if they still call it that, water by the road. We fill up and keep moving."

"How big?" Vel asked.

"Thirty, forty inside the fence. Farms all around it. Folks there count strangers the same way they count sacks."

The depot lay in a shallow basin of barley and low stone walls. A square silo lifted above the roofs on the north side, gray and practical, the mortar darker where recent patchwork had failed to match the old. A trough sat by the road with two mules tied beside it. Three people were moving sacks from a wagon into a shed. Another two worked at the field edge with hooked knives, cutting an irrigation clog out of the ditch.

"We stop long enough for water and nothing else," Dren said. "No trading, no questions, and nobody touches the silo wall unless they want a lecture about municipal property."

"You make it sound personal," Kem said.

"I have had the lecture."

"Was it informative?"

"It was long."

Vel studied the silo, the ditch, the tree line west of the road. The depot people moved with the tight economy of a place that could not afford waste and knew it. One of the workers by the ditch looked up at them and kept looking. Strangers near grain made every settlement uneasy. Strangers with packs and city cloth made them worse.

What she expected from pursuit was precision. The Hoarded liked the cheapest clean answer. She expected a Perception pulse or a tracker, or a small team that knew how to spend one level exactly where it mattered and then stop spending. They liked neat recovery, neat paperwork, neat cause and effect.

"There's creek water south of here," Dren said. "Three kilometers, maybe a little more. Better spot to stop if we make good time."

"We make good time," Vel said.

"Fine by me."

Kem looked past the settlement to the darker strip of trees beyond it. "If someone were tracking us, would they wait at the creek or take the road where the cover is worse?"

"If they knew what they were doing," Vel said, "they'd take the road before the creek and leave the water for later."

"And if they don't know what they're doing?"

"Then they make noise first."

The hedge along the west field answered her.

Something caught in it. Cloth tore. A branch snapped. A man's voice said, with deep bureaucratic injury, "This was not in the briefing."

Another voice answered from the trees, female, precise, and pitched to carry without ever sounding raised.

"Tarrell, the notebook is closed during contact."

Dren went to the knife at his belt. "Well," he said softly. "They do make noise first."

The woman in the trees kept speaking as if she were correcting minutes at a meeting.

"Lenn, the garment remains a cloak. Kess, if your heels are opening again, you say so now. Chael, hold formation. Marris, do not spend until you have a line."

Kem stared at the hedge. "Those are instructions for an ambush."

"Off the road," Vel said.

Dren moved at once. Kem swung after him, caught his pack on a fencepost, and made the wire sing down the ditch. The warning had come half a second too late, and the road gave them nowhere to disappear before the gray cloaks broke from the trees.

Five figures hit the open ground in matching gray and sorted themselves fast. Tarrell tripped into the lead with his notebook slamming one hip and his seal cord already around his leg. Lenn came after him with his cloak hacked for freer movement and the look of a man reciting directions inside his own skull. Kess limped in with the parasol still lashed to her pack and panic already trying to brighten itself into courage. Chael carried the only body in the group that looked built for weight instead of records. Marris came last, hood down, silent, reading ditch and silo and road before she bothered to look at the people she was meant to catch.

The sixth figure stayed in the shade under the trees: dark hair in a formal Hoarded arrangement, notebook open, no gray cloak, no hurry.

Their Ledgers pressed at the field hard enough to make the background hum feel weighted.

There were five maxed bodies in front of her, five people at 300. That was enough stored force to ruin a small settlement by accident.

Vel counted before any of them spent. She put herself at 28, Kem at about 225 and still unable to measure himself cleanly in motion, and Dren at 138, useful on a road and useless in a stand-up fight. Civilians stood at the trough and the silo. The barley lay open, with no cover worth the name. There was one competent watcher in the trees and one competent agent in the road.

If Kem threw a perimeter now, he would throw it too wide. She could already see it: a shining dome the size of a meeting hall, anchored in flat ground, with five level-300 idiots outside it trying techniques they did not understand until one of them got lucky or all of them got catastrophic.

She needed movement. She needed the Hoarded to stay stupid for one more minute.

"Kem. Behind me. Don't cast unless I tell you."

"I can fight."

"Not yet. You'll pen us in."

"I can narrow it this time."

"You can hope you can narrow it."

Dren had already left the road and gone low in the ditch grass. "If this turns into running," he said, "the tree line south is the only line that matters."

"I know."

Tarrell set up for a Binding with meticulous care, exactly as if he had memorized the sequence from a manual and never once used it in a body that sweated and shook.

From the trees, the notebook woman said, "Tarrell, you anchor to the target, not the nearest mass."

"I know that," Tarrell said.

At 300, a proper Binding could have pinned all three of them to the road and left Vel spending precious levels just to breathe.

Tarrell missed everyone and hit a boulder by the ditch.

His palm sealed to it with a wet slap. He jerked sideways, stumbled, and ended up half hanging from the stone with both boots skidding in the dirt. The notebook flew from his belt and landed open in the barley.

"That is not the target," the woman in the trees said.

"I am aware," Tarrell said, and then, with sudden panic, "I'm still stuck to it."

Kess, bright with the kind of courage that had not yet survived consequences, stepped into a Perception Sweep before anyone told her to. The parasol knocked against her spine as she opened the sense wide.

Vel knew the posture. At 300 it should have mapped targets, exits, lines of retreat, usable cover, expenditure traces.

Instead it gave Kess everything.

She heard every insect in the barley, every crack in the silo mortar, the pollen moving in the ditch air, the two mules by the trough, the seventeen breathing people in the depot proper, worms under the wet bank, the ditch workers' knives in the water, the salt drying in Lenn's collar, and the blood already oozing under her own heel wraps where the road had rubbed them raw again.

Kess made a wrecked little sound and sat down hard in the dirt.

"There are seventeen," she said. "The south wall is split. The ditch is full of beetles. I can hear the worms. I can hear all of it."

"Narrow the field," the woman in the trees said.

"I don't know how."

Two of them were useless already.

Vel started angling south. If she could get Kem and Dren off the road before the others figured out which mistake hurt most, the perimeter never had to happen.

Lenn chose violence because he had reached the part of the manual where action felt clearer than thought.

He planted his feet exactly wrong, muttered, "Sightline, release," and threw a Combat Pulse at Kem.

He missed by forty meters and hit the silo.

The upper third sheared off with a sound too large for the morning. Stone blocks split loose, grain burst through the seam, and the whole top section came apart into falling masonry and yellow weather. Barley husks sprayed the road. Dust rolled over the trough. One of the mules screamed and tore free.

The ditch crew dropped flat. Somebody in the depot shouted for everyone to get away from the wall. The shout arrived after the wall was already coming down.

Dren had gone low in the grass six meters from the road. He did not have to be heroic to be in range. A chunk of mortared stone, no bigger than a cookpot and moving much faster, struck his left forearm and opened it from just below the elbow nearly to the wrist.

The impact made a flat wet sound.

Dren said, "Shit," in the same tone he used for spoiled cargo, and then he looked at his arm.

The cut gaped immediately. Pink meat showed under the blood. Stone grit and barley chaff packed the opening. He slapped his right sleeve over it, but the blood soaked through at once and ran black-red over his wrist.

Vel marked the wound and left it there. She could not stop for him yet.

Chael had been the quiet one until that point. Seeing Lenn turn a silo into shrapnel seemed to decide him. He dropped his weight, rolled his shoulder once as if testing the joint, and reached for Strength.

The woman in the trees said, sharper now, "Chael, controlled application only."

Chael swung at open air.

The blow shoved a ring of compressed force across the field. Barley flattened in a widening stripe. Dust went sideways. The trough water lifted and slapped back down. Two trees at the south edge snapped through the middle and dropped together.

The pressure hit Vel in the sternum from fifteen meters away. It was only the edge of it, only a preview, and it still shoved air out of her lungs hard enough to sting her teeth.

If Chael learned control before they reached cover, the fight ended here.

Marris had still not spent. She watched Kess in the dirt, Tarrell stuck to the boulder, Lenn staring at the ruined silo as though destruction had happened to him personally, and Chael blinking at the trees he had not meant to break. Then she looked once toward the treeline.

The woman with the notebook did not raise her voice. "Observe the failures. Spend on what remains."

That was when Vel understood the sixth figure was not there to help. She was there to learn.

"Kem," Vel said. "If I say now, you cast and walk."

"I am ready."

"Be ready faster."

Marris lined up a Force Push.

Vel turned on instinct. The shove caught her across the left ribs instead of square in the chest.

Two ribs cracked with small internal pops. Then something hot and wrong slid under them as cartilage tore loose near the sternum. Breathing stopped being a simple act and became a bad negotiation her body had no patience for.

She hit the ground on her right shoulder, dragged in one shallow breath full of dust and grain husk, and got up again because the alternative was staying there for the next one.

Her left arm folded over the fracture site before she told it to.

She had cracked at least two ribs, maybe three. The cartilage felt loose under the skin. A small Restoration would fix it for two levels. Two levels would take her from twenty-eight to twenty-six before she spent another thing in a fight that had barely started to learn from itself.

She was not spending two on pain.

Vel threw a one-level Combat strike into Marris's hip and knocked her stance sideways before the second Push formed. The expenditure bit cleanly and dropped Vel to twenty-seven.

Marris staggered, caught herself, and looked surprised only for the length of a blink. Even surprised, her casting stayed steadier than anyone else's.

Chael wound up again.

Vel burned an Evasion charm and moved two feet right. The borrowed twist through her ribs nearly dropped her, but the blast hit where she had been standing and sent dust hard enough across her neck to sting. That sidestep cost another level and left her at twenty-six.

Kem's Ledger shifted behind her. He had been waiting for the order since the gray cloaks broke cover, and now Dren was bleeding into the grass, the field was full of falling stone, and the only useful agent had found her range.

The open-ground plan was dead.

"Kem. Perimeter. Now."

He cast too big.

The barrier snapped up around them in a blue-white dome nearly thirty meters wide. It enclosed road, ditch, two fenceposts, half a burst of grain dust, and more useless air than Vel wanted to think about. It crackled where barley husks struck it and slid.

Kem swore under his breath and then louder. "That's not the size I wanted."

"I know."

The next Push hit the shell and ran around it in a white ripple that made the whole dome hum. Marris had aimed at center mass this time. Next time she would aim lower, or at the footing, or at the seam where Kem's concentration wavered.

Vel spent one more level on a Combat strike and cratered the dirt in front of Chael's boots. Stone and hard clods burst into his face. He swung blind, overcorrected, and tore a furrow through the barley long enough to look surveyed. Twenty-five remained.

Tarrell had finally torn himself free of the boulder. Skin came with the stone. He stared at the raw red mess in disbelief, then tried another Binding anyway, working around the injury because he was too frightened to stop obeying his own training.

The notebook woman said, "Tarrell, do not repeat a failed action unchanged."

"I am adjusting," Tarrell said, and immediately anchored the Binding to a fencepost.

The post sealed upright, humming faintly with borrowed force. Tarrell swore at it with the formal helplessness of a man who had not learned profanity young enough to make it useful.

Kess was on her knees now, covering her ears, still talking because the Sweep had not narrowed yet.

"There are nineteen now. No, eighteen. Someone ran from the inn. The mule's in the barley. There's blood by the ditch. I can hear his blood. I can hear the grain pouring out of the upper chamber. I can hear-"

"Stop listening," Dren snapped from the grass, voice flat with pain.

For one second Kess actually did. Her eyes found him, wide and human and terrified.

Lenn recovered from the sight of the silo just in time to become dangerous to everyone again. He had the field-manual look of a man who believed that if the technique failed, the answer was more technique.

"I had the angle right," he said to nobody in particular. "The release point was bad."

"Lenn," the notebook woman said, "do not test revisions on the grain stores."

"Then on what?"

He answered himself by hurling a second Combat Pulse at the perimeter.

The strike hit high. The shell held, but Kem gasped and bent one inch at the waist as the force ran through the dome.

"I'm holding it," Kem said, too fast. "I am holding it. I know I am saying that while holding it. I hear it. I am still holding it."

"Good," Vel said. "Keep talking if it keeps you upright."

Marris moved left. Better angle. Better footing. She had watched the dome ripple once and learned from it.

Vel spent another level and struck her knee before the next Push fully formed. Marris dropped, one leg folding under her, but the half-built force still bled off into the barley and flattened another wide section of field. Twenty-four remained.

Chael wiped mud and blood from one eye with the back of his wrist and looked at the perimeter with slow concentration. Unlike the others, he was not flustered by failing. He was simply recalculating.

That made him the second problem.

Tarrell tore free of the fencepost-binding by abandoning it mid-cast. The backlash flayed the same raw patch worse. He made a sound like a man swallowing his own scream and then finally, blessedly, stopped spending.

Kess lurched to her feet with the Perception still partly open. The parasol slipped loose from her pack, fell into the dirt, and rolled until it struck the perimeter in a sad little tap.

"Your wall is thirty-one across," she said to Kem, horrified by what she knew. "No, thirty and a half. South side thicker now. North side weak where the ditch bends. There are three people under the wagon because they think wood matters more than stone. The woman in the shed bit through her lip."

Kem looked at Vel without moving anything but his eyes. "I hate that she knows that."

"So do I."

The notebook woman under the trees had stopped giving broad corrections. She watched Kess's collapse, Tarrell's failures, Chael's wide-force problem, and Marris's aim. Her pen moved constantly. Once, Vel saw her look from the flattened barley to the dome and count the distance between them with her eyes.

Silo dust settled over everybody. Grain kept pouring from the broken upper chamber in a steady pale stream. One of the depot men had crawled from under the wagon and was now dragging the other by the collar, both of them low to the ground.

This was what a fight looked like when nobody aimed at civilians and civilians still bled for it.

Vel drove another one-level strike into Chael's shoulder. The expenditure pulled at her already abused side as if reaching through the break. Chael's arm gave with an ugly hitch and dropped half useless. Twenty-three remained.

He went to one knee, good arm clamped over the bad shoulder, mouth open on shocked breath.

Marris, still down on one leg, looked at Chael's shoulder, then at Vel's left arm clamped over her ribs. She had seen enough to start sorting targets by cost.

That was the true change in the field. The others were still disasters. Marris was becoming an opponent.

Vel leaned toward Dren without taking her eyes off the road. "Can you move?"

"If I'm vertical, I can move."

"When the south side opens, you run for the trees. No heroics."

"Didn't feel heroic."

Kem swallowed and reset his feet. Sweat had soaked his collar dark. "If I drop the north edge and reinforce south, I can walk it. I think. I mean I can try it. I know those are different statements."

"Do it on my mark."

Lenn drew breath for a third pulse. Kess screamed because the sound of it arrived before the cast. Tarrell, pale now and bleeding down his wrist, stooped for his notebook with his other arm as if salvage of the record might justify the rest of him. Chael tried to stand and failed. Marris set up again from one knee.

Vel spent a final one-level strike and clipped Marris across the forearm before the Push landed. The cast broke apart. The force went wild and blew a notch out of the road twenty feet short of the dome. Twenty-two remained.

She had spent six levels in less than three minutes.

Her ribs ground every time she dragged air in. Dren's blood had soaked the ditch grass black. Kem's overbuilt dome hummed like a live wire around them. Beyond it stood five people at 300, four of them incompetent and one of them learning fast enough to matter.

"Now," Vel said.

Kem let the north side dissolve and shoved the saved force south. The dome lurched, thinned behind them, and thickened toward the trees in a brighter arc.

"Walk," Vel said.

Dren came up out of the grass and nearly fell before finding his legs. He clamped his left arm to his body and started south at a hard, ugly half-run. Vel followed with her torso locked rigid against the broken ribs. Kem backed after them, arms out, face white with concentration.

The agents finally did what the notebook woman had wanted all along. They stopped spending blindly and watched them move.

Marris saw first where the south wall had thickened. She pointed steadily. The notebook woman wrote it down.

Tarrell, clutching the rescued notebook against his chest while blood ran off the raw injury, stared at the path they were taking through the grain and said, to nobody useful, "They favor the injured side."

"Yes," the notebook woman said. "Continue observing."

Kess pressed at her own temples and said, "The barrier's thinner at the ditch seam. The left one has ribs. The trader is losing blood faster when he runs. They are heading to water."

Vel heard that through the shell and hated her for being right.

Lenn looked as if he wanted to prove he was not the silo man forever. Marris said, quiet and practical, "Do not spend high. You'll hit the shell and drain nothing."

The notebook woman's pen paused. "Good. Again."

That, more than the cracked ribs, more than the blood on Dren's arm, was what put cold into Vel's stomach.

They were getting less incompetent.

***

The first line of trees closed over them in a rash of shade and insects.

Kem kept the perimeter moving another thirty paces before Vel said, "Drop it."

He let the dome go all at once and nearly folded with it. The hum vanished from the air, leaving only creekward birdsong, Dren's breathing, and the wet patter of blood off his sleeve.

Vel braced herself against an oak and checked the damage with two careful presses. The fifth and sixth ribs on the left had cracked. Cartilage peeled loose near the sternum. The place was already starting to swell. A small Restoration would fix it for two levels and leave her at twenty.

Twenty belonged to the Margins, not to this road.

She left the bones where they were.

"Kem."

He bent double, braced on his thighs, sweat dropping off his jaw. "One-eighty-three," he said. "I know that means forty-two for the perimeter. I know that before you say it."

"I wasn't going to say it."

"You were absolutely going to say it later."

Maybe. The barrier had held. Later was not guaranteed.

Dren sat down hard under a birch and made the wound bleed fresh when he checked it. Blood ran over his wrist and into the leaf litter. Vel stripped a clean length of cloth from what remained of her spare shirt and crouched as far as she could without driving the broken ribs white-hot through her side.

"Hold it closed," she said. "Hard."

Dren took the cloth in his teeth long enough to loop it one-handed around his forearm. He cinched it. His face changed color but not expression.

"There's water ahead?" Vel asked.

"Creek crossing. Three kilometers. Maybe a little less if we cut the cart path."

"We cut it."

Kem had turned back toward the field. Through the trees the depot looked broken into separate pieces: half a silo lying where it had landed, gray cloaks spread around the barley, settlement people keeping a distance that was close enough to witness and far enough to live.

The dark-haired woman had stepped clear of the treeline now without a gray cloak. A dark coat cut for office work hung off her instead of anything made for the road. Her hair, arranged in the formal Hoarded way, had started coming loose at the nape. She kept the notebook open at her side.

She had not fought. She had watched.

Vel followed Kem's gaze. "Who is she?"

Kem did not answer at once. His mouth had gone thin.

"I know her by sight," he said. "Sable Orm. Senior Auditor."

"Auditor of what?" Dren asked.

Kem gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "Whatever the Council wants described as procedure instead of violence."

Through the trees, Sable bent to pick Tarrell's fallen notebook out of the barley. She handed it back without looking at his face, then made him show her the raw injury while she wrote something beside it. When Kess pointed shakily at the south edge of the field, Sable turned and measured the distance to the trees again.

Vel watched Marris limp three steps to Sable's side and sketch shell height, line of retreat, the point where the dome had thickened. Sable listened. Chael, one arm hanging wrong, leaned in to hear. Lenn kept looking at the broken silo as if he might will it unbroken by regret alone. Tarrell clutched the notebook and bled on the page. Kess still had no cloak and no business being out here.

"She stayed clean so she could count it," Dren said.

Kem nodded once. "Yes."

"And now she's got measurements."

"Yes."

Vel looked back at Sable. "She's not a field agent."

"No," Kem said. "That's why she stayed out of it. She's an office creature with authority and no business throwing power blind. She was there to see what five people at three hundred could do, what they ruined, and what almost worked."

"Which is worse," Dren said.

"Yes," Kem said again, quieter now.

Sable closed the notebook, spoke to Marris, and pointed not at the road but farther south, where the trees thinned toward the creek lands. Marris looked once in that direction and nodded.

The threat had a name now, a notebook, and a direction south. It was a named woman giving the five failures a better use for next time.

Vel pushed off the oak. The movement sent a blade of pain under her left breast and straight through her back.

"We keep moving," she said.

Dren got to his feet with the bad arm clamped in and said, "Yeah."

Vel looked at him. "You can still break west at the creek and take your fee north. We'll manage south without you if we have to."

Kem opened his mouth, startled by the offer, but Dren was already shaking his head.

"No."

"You're hurt."

"I'm not dead."

"That wasn't the question."

Dren tightened the cloth knot with his teeth again, then spat a thread loose into the leaves. When he spoke, he did it without looking at either of them.

"Those people just opened my arm on a grain road and wrote down how long it took me to reach cover. I trade this stretch. I trade north of it. Roth sits off one of my runs. Mira waits there when the healer keeps her overnight." He finally looked up. "I don't peel off now and walk them straight across every stop I use. We break the trail first."

Vel nodded once. "Fine."

Kem looked back through the trees one last time. "Marris is the dangerous one," he said. "The one with the proper cloak."

"I know."

"If Sable is letting her learn from the others-"

"I know that too."

Dren started south before the talk could turn into anything less useful than walking.

The cart path he found was barely a path at all, just twin compressed lines through dry grass where small wagons cut toward the creek in better seasons. He took it at a hard pace anyway, shoulders tucked against the pain. Vel followed with one arm locked against her ribs, every step a negotiation. Kem came last, turning too often to listen behind them.

No one came through the trees after them.

That did not calm anybody.

The creek announced itself first by temperature. The air dropped a degree, then another. Insects thickened. The grass along the bank went from dry-stemmed yellow to green enough to hide mud under it.

They reached the water where it widened shallow over flat stone. The crossing was only knee deep on Dren, thigh deep on Kem, fast enough to pull at balance and not fast enough to wash anything away cleanly.

Dren stepped in first and hissed when cold water hit the torn arm.

The creek went red around him at once.

Vel saw it the same second Kem did: the bright spreading trail, too clear against the stones, a marker any half-competent Perception sweep could follow even if Kess had finally stopped hearing worms.

"Concealment," Vel said.

"How much?"

"Two."

Kem did not argue. He bent over the current, ring turned down, and pulled the charm wrong on the first try and right on the second. The water dulled. The red thinned into a general murk of creek silt and moving shadow. The scent lifted and went flat.

Kem swayed when the spend landed.

"One-eighty-one," he said automatically, not because anybody asked.

"Walk," Vel said.

They crossed with Dren leading and Vel close enough behind to shove him if the footing went. Halfway through, her ribs reminded her that twisting was now an expensive luxury she no longer possessed. She almost lost her own balance on a slick stone, recovered, and tasted blood where she had bitten the inside of her cheek.

On the far bank, Dren planted one boot on wet clay and the earth sheared under him. He went down on the wounded side with a strangled sound he did not quite let become a cry.

The fall yanked the cut open wider.

Blood ran free through the cloth. Dren's knees buckled. He caught himself with the good arm and nearly slid back into the water.

"Strength," Vel said.

"I can pull him up without it," Kem said, already reaching.

"You can try. Then you can watch him rip the arm the rest of the way open. Spend."

Kem spent.

The change hit him from feet to shoulders: thighs hardening, breath catching, the jolt of borrowed force moving through a body that still did not trust it. He dropped down the bank, got Dren's good arm over his shoulders, and hauled him the last stretch with brute enhanced leverage while the mud came away under both of them.

"Three," Kem gasped when they were up. "That was three."

"I know."

"I'm at one-seventy-eight."

The number sat in the air for a second, smaller now and permanent.

Dren leaned hard against a sapling until the worst of the shaking passed. "Next time," he said through his teeth, "I vote for the ambush where the grain stays standing."

"Noted," Vel said.

They moved again because stillness was as good as a trail marker. The land south of the creek rose into a stand of birch and scrub oak, then dipped toward a long shallow fold in the ground where the old cart path vanished under leaf litter. Dren found a small clearing there with the reflex of a man who had slept in bad country often enough to judge safety by radius and inconvenience.

"Here," he said.

It was not much of a camp, only a flat patch under birch branches with enough canopy to break sight lines from the path and enough dry ground to lay a wounded man down without soaking him in rot. It was enough.

***

Vel dropped her pack and immediately wished she had not, because bending for it again sent heat through the broken ribs so bright it made the clearing jump.

Kem saw her face tighten. "You should heal that."

"No."

"You can barely stand."

"I can stand enough."

"That's not the same thing."

"No," she said. "It isn't."

Dren lowered himself to the ground with the careful anger of a man offended by his own body. Once he was down he let the control slip for half a second, head tipped back against the birch, jaw gone white.

Vel knelt beside him as far as her ribs would permit and cut away the sleeve with her small knife.

The wound looked worse clean than it had blood-packed. Silo stone had not sliced; it had torn. The edges were ragged where grit and mortar had gone in. Grain husks stuck to the clotting blood. The deepest part near the elbow showed pale tendon under the red.

Kem swallowed hard and did not look away.

"Water," Vel said.

He handed over the skin. She poured creek water through the cut until the mud loosened and the blood ran thinner. The tendons at Dren's wrist twitched once against his own knee and then locked there.

"Spirits."

Kem rummaged through the half-broken pack and found the squat bottle wrapped in cloth. Vel took it, braced an elbow against her own ribs, and looked at Dren once before she tipped it.

"Bite something if you need to."

"Just do it."

She washed the wound with spirits.

Dren's whole body jumped. He sucked air through his nose so hard it sounded like a blade being drawn.

"That's ugly," Kem said quietly.

"Yes," Vel said.

"Will he keep the arm?"

"If it doesn't rot, yes."

"Helpful," Dren muttered.

"You asked him to look."

"Didn't ask for commentary."

Vel rinsed again, picked out a grain hull with the knife tip, then another. Every forward lean drove the cracked ribs against each other. She kept going until the edge of her vision started to pulse.

Kem crouched opposite her, arms held uselessly away from his body. Under the last of the borrowed Strength, his forearms still looked too tight with stored effort.

"Tell me what to do," he said. "I can hold things. I can pass things. I can do more than that if you tell me exactly what more than that is."

Vel sat back on her heels too fast and had to brace her forearm to her side.

The pain was clean enough to think through, which was almost worse.

"Thread kit," she said.

Kem found it where she told him: cloth wrap, curved needle, waxed thread, the small pliers she had bent years ago and kept because tools that worked did not stop working just because time passed.

She took the needle and tried to settle herself close enough to sew.

The first movement told her no.

Bringing the wound edges together meant leaning over Dren's arm and holding steady over the cut. Her ribs would not let the torso fold. The minute she tried, the left side seized so hard the needle jumped in her grip.

She stopped before the point touched him.

Dren watched her face, then the arm at her ribs. He did not waste breath pretending not to understand.

"Can't do it?" he asked.

"Not cleanly."

"Good to know we've got standards."

"Shut up."

He shut up.

Kem looked from one of them to the other. "I can do it."

Vel laughed once because the alternative was cursing, and the laugh hurt enough to feel like punishment.

"No," she said.

"I can."

"You've never stitched anything."

"That is true," Kem said. "It is also true that you cannot bend."

He was right and young enough to say it plainly.

Vel closed her eyes for one breath and opened them again. The clearing smelled of spirits, wet cloth, birch bark, and the rank copper stink of fresh blood. Somewhere beyond the trees a night insect started up early, fooled by shade into thinking it had more authority than the sun.

Dren shifted his weight and the wound welled again.

"Fine," Vel said.

Kem blinked. "Fine?"

"Fine. You're doing it."

"Oh."

"Don't sound surprised."

"I am surprised."

"Stop being surprised and listen."

He moved in at once, knees in the leaf litter, needle held with far too much care. He was steady for about two seconds and then started to shake.

"That's not encouraging," Dren said.

"You are bleeding on me," Kem said. "I think I am allowed a moment."

"You had a moment."

"I had many moments. None of them involved this."

Vel took the waxed thread back long enough to knot it and pass the needle through the first loop. Even that small motion asked too much from her side. She gave up before the knot was perfect and handed it back.

"You're closing skin, not tying off a grain sack," she said. "Straight through. Both sides, same depth. Pull it snug, not tight. If you strangle the flesh, it tears worse later."

Kem nodded too many times.

"If I do it wrong-"

"Then you do the next one less wrong."

He looked at Dren's arm as if waiting for it to turn into something a library had prepared him for. It stayed an arm split open by silo stone.

Behind him, the last light through the birches had started to thin. Vel thought of the field north of them: Sable's notebook, Marris's better aim, Kess hearing the creek in the wrong direction, Tarrell bleeding on his records, Lenn learning not to throw at silos because he had already done it once.

No one mentioned the western break again.

Dren held his torn forearm out without ceremony. "Do it," he said.

Kem set the point of the needle to skin.


# Chapter 9

The needle went in wrong the first time. Kem felt the resistance of Dren's skin, the rubbery give of it, and his wrist jerked sideways. Dren hissed through his teeth.

"Sorry. I'm sorry."

"Just do it," Dren said. "Don't talk about it."

Kem set the needle back over the split in Dren's left forearm. Silo stone had opened it from mid-forearm nearly to the elbow. Vel had washed the grit out with creek water and spirits, but the flesh still gaped dark and wet around the edges.

A few feet away, Vel sat against a trunk with one arm locked over her cracked ribs. She had tried the first stitch herself, gotten the needle in, and stopped when the pain took her breath away. Now she watched Kem do the part she could talk him through and not do herself.

He knew what the job was supposed to be. He needed to bring the split edges together without tearing them worse and close the wound well enough that Dren kept the arm.

The second pass was better. It caught both edges of the wound, and the waxed thread dragged through in a wet pull that made his stomach jump. The line it made looked nothing like a clean seam. It looked like a child sewing up a split doll.

"Christ," Dren said.

"I know."

"Keep going."

Kem kept going. The third stitch went crooked. On the fourth he bit too deep on one side and barely caught the other, bunching the skin into a ridge that would scar ugly. Dren's forearm was tanned and ropey from years of hauling cargo. Every time the needle punched through, the muscle jumped under the skin.

The fifth stitch finally sat right, even and snug, close enough to clean that Kem stared at it for a beat before moving on. Then the sixth hit something fibrous under the skin, Dren grunted, and the small flare of competence vanished. By the seventh, his grip was cramping. Blood and wax slicked it.

Packed dirt pressed hard through Kem's knees. Late light slanted through the trees and turned the blood on Dren's arm almost black. Vel had not changed position. She was still against the trunk with her arms folded carefully over her ribs, breathing shallow and measured. She watched without helping, which was its own kind of instruction.

By the eighth stitch, rhythm started to replace panic. He pushed through, pulled, checked the gap, tied off. He still shook, but the shake belonged to the job now.

"You're pulling them uneven," Vel said.

"I know."

"The last two are going to scar."

"I know."

"Finish."

The ninth stitch pulled the seam mostly closed. It was ugly work. The thread crossed in dark Xs over swelling skin. Some of the twelve stitches were serviceable. Some were a mess. All of them held.

Dren flexed his fingers and then his wrist. The skin tugged. Nothing split.

"That's bad," he said.

"It's ugly."

He looked at the arm again. "Looks like you stitched it in a moving wagon." Another flex. A careful test of grip around the pack strap. "But it'll get me down the road. Thanks."

No one at the Vault had ever thanked him for bad work. There, thanks had marked rank and attendance, the right body in the right room. Dren had looked at the ugly seam and meant it.

The needle went back into its cloth wrap after he cleaned it with the last of the spirits.

The strip Dren had tied over the stitches was already pinking through.

"Too loose," Vel said.

She pushed off the trunk to inspect it, got one knee under her, and stopped there with her teeth set hard. The ribs caught her halfway up. Kem moved before she could tell him not to. He took her good elbow and felt how carefully she was holding the left side of herself together.

"Fold another cloth," she said when she had enough breath for it. "Pad under the seam first, then wrap. If his fingers go cold, you've done it wrong."

Dren watched Kem tear a strip from the cleanest rag they had left and fold it double. "Comforting."

"It's better than bleeding on your boots," Vel said.

Kem slid the pad over the stitches and wrapped again, trying not to look too long at the swollen, uneven line his thread had made. The first turn sat too low. Vel corrected him with a brief motion in the air. The second was better. By the third wrap he could feel the difference between pressure that steadied and pressure that crushed. Dren kept his jaw locked and his arm still, except for the one time the cloth bit wrong and he said, "If you kill the hand after all that, I'm going to be offended."

"Then keep it alive," Vel said. "Do the sling next. Wrist higher than elbow."

Kem tore another strip, longer this time, and looped it under Dren's wrist and around the back of his neck. The first knot slid at once.

"That's decorative," Dren said.

Kem redid it. Vel watched the angle, then nodded once.

"Better. Tomorrow he'll hate you instead of me."

"That feels like a low bar."

"It is."

By the time the arm sat high against Dren's chest, Kem had learned three new ways cloth could fail. Dren had learned to keep his complaints short because each one jarred the stitches. Vel let Kem lower her back against the trunk again, one arm tight across her ribs until the worst passed.

"It'll scar," Vel said.

"Good. Then I'll remember not to let him do the other arm." Dren tested the wrap lightly. Pink seeped through, but slower now. "We staying here?"

"Tonight," Vel said. "We move at first light."

There was no point pretending they could do anything else. Light was going. Dren could walk, but not fast. Vel could stand, but every deep breath sharpened her face. Kem still had the burned-out ache of the Strength Charm in his thighs, as if somebody had packed hot sand into the muscles and left it there.

Vel had him strip the useful things from the torn packs and set them in a pile near the fire lay: bread, dried meat, the cloth wrap, flint, the little cooking tin, the small journal she kept tucked inside her coat instead of stowed where sensible people kept things. The pile looked smaller than it should have after three adults' worth of travel. They had two clean cloths, one heel of bread, a swallow of spirits, and thread enough for maybe one more repair if tomorrow went badly. Every item that made it onto the dirt felt like something rescued from sinking.

Dren tested the pack strap again with the bad arm, let it drop, and adjusted the load with his right arm. The calculation showed in small movements: grip, pull, reach, stop. The arm would stay functional, but slower. It would carry less weight. He had fewer clean days before the joint stiffened. Kem watched him take stock with the blunt efficiency of a man who lived inside margins.

"Arm's fine," Dren said to the trees.

"Then stop advertising it," Vel said.

Blood had dried under Kem's nails. Wax clung near the little puncture the needle had left at the base of his left thumb. He looked used now, marked in ways the Vault never had room for. He stared at himself as if the damage belonged to someone else.

Then he checked his Ledger.

178.

He had been at 183 when they broke from the silo. Two levels had gone on Concealment at the creek. Three had gone on Strength while he dragged Dren up the bank. The subtraction sat clean. It still made his stomach go hollow.

He had walked out of the Vault with 300. Now he had 178. A hundred and twenty-two levels were gone in less than a month. At 245, after the first fight, loss had felt like exhilaration, proof that his body could do something besides sit in ceremonial rooms and decay politely. At 178, the thrill had thinned out. Thirty no longer sounded like a number that belonged to other people. Combat Pulse was gone. Three Utility charms were gone. Sustenance was still there, but it sat in his awareness like a coin he kept touching in his pocket to make sure it had not vanished too.

Dren had been watching him.

"How many?" he asked.

"What?"

"Levels."

"178."

Dren nodded once. He did not whistle or pity him. He heard the number the way a trader heard freight weight or winter grain and put it in the correct column.

"That'll get you somewhere," he said.

He picked once at the sling knot with his teeth, dissatisfied, then let it be. "It's also the kind of number that makes people stupid."

Kem looked up. "How."

"Because it still feels big." Dren settled more carefully against his pack. "Men at thirty know they're at thirty. Men at a hundred and seventy-eight start buying themselves comfort and calling it judgment."

"I spent five levels getting us out."

"Yeah. That's why I'm not insulting you." Dren glanced at the wrapped arm. "I'm telling you the next bad spend won't look heroic. It'll look easy."

Vel's eyes were still closed. "He's right."

Kem wanted to dislike the lesson on principle. Instead he looked down at the Flame Charm still sitting in his roster and understood exactly what Dren meant.

Vel was not asleep. Nobody breathing that carefully was asleep. Dren found a position that kept pressure off the stitches and went still.

Kem scrubbed at the blood with grass, dirt, and water until only faint brown stayed in the creases. The puncture kept stinging. He ignored it.

Evening slid through the clearing. They had not eaten. Tiredness settled into his jaw, forearms, and shoulders, the kind that came from concentration and the long drop after fear. No ability would fix that. It had to burn out on its own.

The Flame Charm sat in his roster like an invitation he no longer trusted. It would cost one level for dry flame, one level to avoid bent knees and scraped knuckles, one level that might matter tomorrow if Dren slipped again or if Vel finally admitted the ribs were worse than she said.

So he went for wood instead. He pulled dry twigs from under a fallen spruce, shaved bark thin with Vel's knife, and dug out a fistful of old grass from beneath a rock where the day's damp had not reached. He stacked fine kindling first, then small sticks, then heavier pieces once the flame could bear them. It was the same order Vel had taught him in the highlands, and the same order he had ignored twice already in his head while imagining a faster way to do it.

It took four tries.

On the second, the spark died before it caught. On the third, he fed the new flame too soon and buried it in its own smoke. The fourth held because he forced himself to wait through the ugly little stage where nothing looked impressive and everything mattered.

"It needs air more than faith," Vel said without opening her eyes.

Dren made a sound that might have been a laugh and might have been pain. "Useful distinction."

By full dark, the flames had settled low and steady. Vel opened her eyes when the fire caught. She looked at it, then at Kem. Something eased for a second around her mouth before she pushed herself closer to the heat and stalled halfway there when the ribs bit.

Kem crossed the space, crouched on her good side, and got an arm under her elbow.

"Not the left," she said through her teeth.

"I gathered that."

"Good."

He lowered her the last two feet. Her coat brushed cold against his wrist with sweat trapped under the wool. She sat by the fire without thanking him or waving him off.

---

They ate dried meat and the stale heel of bread from Dren's pack. Nobody wasted energy pretending it was better than it was. Dren tried to saw the bread apart with his knife braced against his boot and failed twice before Kem took it from him and tore the pieces himself. Dren let him, then let him retie the top of the bandage when the first knot slipped while he reached for the meat.

The second wrapping went faster. Kem knew now where the cloth needed to sit and how much weight the sling could take before Dren's mouth flattened.

"See," Dren said when Kem finished. "Give you ten more wounds and you'll almost be employable."

"Let's not set targets."

"Fine. Five wounds."

Dren chewed one-handed and fell asleep almost as soon as he lay down, turned on his good side with the pack under his head. He could sleep anywhere. Kem watched how he tucked the pack just right under his neck and rolled onto the good side without waking himself fully.

Across the fire, Vel held a tin cup near her mouth without drinking. Every few breaths she stopped and waited out the bite in her side before she took the next one.

"You could spend on the ribs," he said.

Vel looked at him over the rim of the cup.

"Could," she said.

"You're not going to."

"No."

He waited. She did not add anything.

"Why not."

"Because they'll still be ribs tomorrow."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the answer."

Dren, already folding down into sleep, said, "Healing's expensive when the job is pain instead of dying."

Vel glanced at him. "Look at that. He learns."

Dren shut his eyes. "I've paid other people's healers before."

Kem looked back at Vel. "You spent two on Hael."

"Hael had blood in his chest and an hour left if I was generous." She shifted, stopped when the ribs caught, and let the breath out slow. "I've got cracks on the left side and a bad temper. The cracks will mend on their own."

"And if they don't?"

"Then I get meaner for a week." She took another shallow breath. "If I spend now, I still wake up hurt, only with fewer choices."

Kem looked at Dren, already out cold on his good side, then back at Vel with the left side of her coat pulled tight over the ribs. In the Vault a cracked rib would have brought servants, tincture, a healer, a tray. Vel sat still and counted what tomorrow might cost.

"Where does it go?" Kem asked.

She looked up.

"Zero," he said. "When it finally runs out, where do people go."

For a while the fire did all the talking. Vel watched the flames sink around a blackening stick. Then she checked Dren's sleep and looked back at Kem.

"It's not death," she said.

"You've said that before."

"Because that's the first thing people ask."

"Then say the second thing."

A small breath went out of her nose. It was not laughter. It sounded more like irritation at being cornered by a fair question.

"When you hit zero in the right place, you cross," she said. "You go out of here, out of the Ledger."

Kem held still.

"There's a breach in the south where it can happen. It doesn't happen anywhere else. If you spend down to nothing in a field, you stay in the field. If it happens there, with the threshold set right, you go through."

"Through to what."

"A place without the Ledger. There's no number there, no abilities either. It's just your body and whatever you've learned to do with it."

He looked past her at Dren's sleeping shape on the other side of the fire. Dren lived by keeping clear of zero. Dren had a daughter. Dren had route math. Zero was not a destination to him. It was a cliff edge you backed away from all your life.

"How do you know that?" Kem asked.

Vel rubbed at an old burn with her thumb once. "I saw someone cross."

"Who?"

"Someone I was on the road with for years."

The answer hit harder for being that small. Kem waited. This time she kept going.

"It was night," she said. "Cold enough the rocks kept the day's heat underneath and froze your face above it. We'd been arguing about where to bed down. I wanted the north lip. They wanted the cut because the wind broke there. I was still holding their cup because they'd shoved it at me and told me to drink instead of talking. I was close enough to grab their sleeve if I'd known what I was looking at." Her eyes stayed on the fire. "When their number hit nothing, my own Ledger yanked inside me like a hook through the middle. Every insect in the cut went quiet at once. Dust lifted off the stone toward them. Then there was empty air where a body had been."

The fire snapped. Kem realized he had stopped breathing and let the air out slowly.

"There was no body," Vel said. "There was no blood either. The pack was gone too. They didn't fall. They didn't burn. They were here and then they weren't." She flexed once, as if the old burn still hurt. "I stood there long enough for the metal to burn me before I remembered to drop it."

"And that's only there," he said.

She didn't answer.

"And only if it's done right."

"Done wrong, you die in a field and nobody knows the difference." She shifted her weight off the bad ribs. "So yeah."

"What does 'done right' mean?"

"It means zero isn't enough by itself." Vel met his eyes then, flat and direct.

The answer shut one door and opened three others. Kem could feel the questions crowding up behind his teeth, each worse than the last.

"Why tell me now?"

"Because before tonight you'd have heard 'there's a door' and spent half your Ledger trying to slam into it." She glanced toward Dren and then back. "Tonight you spent five getting us out, made a fire yourself when you could've spent for it, tied his sling three times until it held, and then stared at 178 like it was a wound. That's different."

She was right, which irritated him more than if she had been cruel. Three weeks ago he would have heard about a threshold and treated it like a challenge built for him. Some part of him still wanted to. Another part kept seeing the five-level subtraction line up clean inside his head and feeling panic under his ribs.

"So this whole time," he said, "you weren't chasing a story. You saw someone go."

"Yeah."

"And you've been walking after that ever since."

Vel looked into the fire for so long he thought she would leave him with silence again. When she spoke, the voice came out flatter than before.

"I went back at dawn," she said. "I thought maybe I'd missed something because I was scared or stupid, that there'd be a body in a crack, blood under the dust, a boot, anything." Her mouth flattened. "There was the fire ring, two bedroll marks, and the cup where I'd dropped it. That was all, so yes, I kept walking."

Kem pictured the campsite at dawn: the fire ring gone cold, the two bedroll marks still pressed into the dust, one cup beside them and no sign of the person who should have been there.

"What was the place like?" he asked.

"It's a cut in the rock wide enough for three people to stand abreast. The stone's black. There are old scorch marks all through it. The wind doesn't move like the rest of the world there. You feel it before you see it. Your Ledger goes tight."

He tried to picture it: hard stone underfoot, wind pulling the wrong direction, his own number dropping toward zero while the last of what he had narrowed to one choice.

"People live there," he said.

"That's what I was told, and I watched enough to believe it."

"There's no Ledger there," Kem said. "There aren't any abilities either."

Vel pulled her coat tighter.

"And you think the ones who cross stay there."

"I think the ones who cross don't come back north." Vel shifted one shoulder in the smallest possible shrug. "That is not the same thing. It's just what I've got."

"What if it's only another kind of dying?"

"Then it's an organized version." She looked back at the flames. "Bodies usually leave evidence."

Despite himself, he let out a short, startled laugh. Vel's mouth moved at one corner, brief and unwilling.

Heat hit one side of his face. Night cold sat on the other. The thought of losing every ability he had left should have been pure terror. Some of it was. The rest sat lower and stranger. His body still remembered the drag of thread through skin. The fire in front of him existed because he had coaxed it up from bark and patience instead of burning a level on command. Dren's arm was closed because Kem had done clumsy work and kept doing it until it held. He could not tell whether what tugged at him was dread or relief.

"I'm not sure if that sounds better or worse," he said.

"It depends on the day," Vel said.

"What about today?"

She pressed once at her ribs, then let it fall. "Today I'd settle for sleep."

They let the conversation stop there. Dren slept. Insects resumed their work in the trees. Far to the north, the agents they had escaped were probably limping through a story that made them sound less foolish.
