The black cut Vel had described kept replacing the clearing every time Kem looked into the fire.

Kem looked again at the blood in the cracks of his knuckles, the needle prick near his left thumb, the callus beginning where there had only been softness a month ago.

"I'm going to sleep," he said.

"I'll take first watch."

"You should sleep. Your ribs are bad enough."

"I'll take first watch."

That ended it. Kem lay down with his pack under his head the way Dren had shown him. The ground was cold and uneven. A root worked into his shoulder blade until he found the angle that hurt least and accepted it. Stars showed above the branches. He did not know their names. In the Vault, nobody had ever needed the sky for anything.

Sleep came in pieces. Once he woke to a low cough from the fire and heard Vel swallow the rest of it, quiet enough that Dren did not stir. Later there was only the hiss of wood settling into ash.

Then Vel's voice came across the fire, so low he nearly dreamed it. "Check his fingers."

Kem pushed himself up on an elbow. Cold had settled harder through the clearing. Dren was snoring through his mouth, the sling still riding high. Kem touched the exposed knuckles of the bad arm. They were warm. He pressed higher at the wrist the way Vel had shown him and watched Dren's face tighten without waking.

"Still there?" Vel asked.

"Yeah."

"Then ease the knot. Not enough to drop it."

He did, slower than before, feeling where the cloth wanted to bite and where it would hold. He eased the sling higher and tucked the blanket edge under Dren's elbow so the arm stopped rolling outward. This time he did not have to ask if it was too tight.

"Go back to sleep," Vel said.

Kem fed two sticks into the coals first. Then he lay down again.

In the last piece before dawn, his father sat in the Conservatory with the usual blank eyes and a Ledger at zero. This time the face was not empty. Kem woke before the dream could make sense of that, to gray light, white ash, and Vel still sitting upright where he had left her, watching the trees.


# Chapter 10

The morning after Vel told Kem about the threshold, she handed him Maren's journal because her ribs would not let her climb the shale cut with one arm locked over her left side and the other guarding the book.

He caught it against his chest, startled enough to make Dren snort. The cover was dark with years of rain and handling, the corners repaired twice, once badly and once by somebody patient enough to make the bad repair hold. Kem looked down at it as if she had passed him something alive.

"Don't bend the spine," Vel said. She was already turning sideways to take the slope, boots testing the loose stone before she trusted it. "And if you lose it, I'll spend the last twenty-two breaking your neck."

Dren shifted his pack higher with his good arm. "There. That's affection."

Kem opened to the page she had marked at dawn. Maren's handwriting ran slanted and impatient along the top margin. Vel's newer notes sat underneath in smaller blockier script, water marks and trader names and dates squeezed around the older writing without crossing it out.

"These circles?"

"Clean wells."

"The double slash."

"Washed-out road."

He nodded and followed her down the cut, careful with the journal and, for once, satisfied with only the questions that kept them moving. He did not read past the marked spread. Four days later, while the camp ash still held a little heat, Vel scratched a new line in the margin.

"We're cutting west."

Dren looked up from the strip of dried rabbit he was working between his teeth. "West is away from the river."

"Only for half a day."

"Why?"

"I need current route talk."

Kem watched the pencil move across the margin. Usually he would have worried at the answer until he got the whole logic or annoyed her into silence. This time he only asked, "From who?"

Vel capped the pencil. "Someone who used to hear the south before it went thin."

That was not much of an answer. Kem let it sit. That was enough to make Vel dislike the walk down.

Four days after the grain-silo fight, the settlement looked the same.

From the ridge she could still read the old layout: stone-and-timber houses around the communal well, gardens on the south edge where the light held longest, the storehouse by the eastern road. Twelve years had changed details, not the bones; the storehouse had grown a second story, and a fence cut across what used to be pasture, but the place itself had not moved an inch. Nothing here had been allowed to sag, split, or go to seed.

"How far?" Dren asked.

"Half an hour."

He shifted his pack and winced when the movement tugged his arm. "She knows we're coming?"

"No."

The switchback path was all gravel and runoff scars. Vel's feet remembered the bad places before the rest of her did: the second turn where the stone liked to roll, the patch of bare rock lower down that could throw a careless ankle, the place near the bottom where the hillside still smelled faintly of old goat shit whenever the sun got on it. Heat settled early in the valley. Below them the gardens gave off bean leaf, damp soil, and the bruised green smell of herb stems snapped by quick hands.

At the northern edge she saw the cart.

It sat outside the storehouse, clean-sided and too fine for local work. The hardware had the machined neatness that only the Hoarded thought looked trustworthy. A settlement trader would have oiled the boards dark, patched the joinery twice, and tied something useful under the seat. This one looked as if a policy had approved every screw.

Vel stopped short. Kem stopped with her. Dren had already narrowed his eyes at the cart.

"Hoarded," he said.

"Yeah."

"Collector or messenger?"

"Representative, I'd guess."

They cut wide through the south gardens, keeping bean frames and a row of early peas between themselves and the well. The settlement was too small for stealth to last. First sight still mattered.

Thessa stood by the well with a woman in Hoarded trade linen, the kind cut narrow through the sleeves so fabric would not bunch under account cuffs. A leather case was tucked under the woman's arm. She was saying, in the dry patient tone of someone who had filed whole villages into columns, that the bean shortfall could be logged as seasonal variance pending corrective compliance.

Then the words thinned.

Her mouth kept moving, but the sound ran out halfway through the sentence, and her gaze slid past Thessa's shoulder to fix on the air beside the well post. For three beats she stood there without blinking or shifting, lips parted, waiting on a thought that would not come back.

It was Stillness, three weeks south of the Vault, with quota language still in the woman's throat.

The lapse lasted only seconds. The representative blinked, found her place, and resumed the sentence as if weather had crossed between one word and the next. She made a note with the stylus from her case. Even the stylus had stayed poised through the blank gap.

Thessa's face gave nothing away. She had seen it before; Vel could tell from how little surprise there was in her.

Cold moved through Vel, not fear but recognition. The Hoarded liked to speak of discipline as if it were a moral triumph. Here it was instead: a woman in good linen while the system ate pieces out of her between one quota and the next.

Kem had gone rigid beside Vel. His breathing changed.

"That's what my father does," he said quietly. "When he goes away in the middle of a sentence."

"I know."

"He looks exactly like that."

The representative closed her case, said she would return next month for updated figures, and headed back toward the cart with the careful steps of somebody carrying liquid close to the lip.

Thessa watched her go. Then she looked toward the bean frames and saw Vel.

Recognition hit first, and then the settlement version of Thessa slid over it: open face, easy posture, a voice made to calm neighbors and send traders away feeling seen. She said something quick to a woman passing with a bucket and came toward them.

The warmth held until she was close enough for Vel to see her eyes.

"Vel," she said. Then, after a beat that took in all three of them, "Fine. Inside. Put the packs down before one of you falls over."

"Thessa."

"Don't start in the lane." She had already turned toward the stone house at the northwest corner. It was the same house, the same narrow front step, the same patched shutter Vel had once promised to mend and never had. "Bring the wounded one. I've got water hot, or close enough. Bread too, if nobody insults it."

"You're very welcoming."

"I've had twelve years to practice." She kept walking. "Come on."

Vel followed. Kem came after her in silence. Dren's boots scraped gravel with the even drag that meant he was listening hard and saying nothing because the saying could wait.

---

Thessa's house smelled of dried thyme, old stone, and baking crust. Under the front window sat a table with four chairs, and shelves of labeled jars lined one wall above a cooking alcove blackened by long use. A broom hung by the door with the bristles up so they would dry straight. Nothing in the room was decorative unless you counted order.

Thessa moved through it without asking what anyone wanted. She set the kettle back on the fire, pulled cups from the shelf, and cut bread in thick unembarrassed slices. The knife went down beside the board, not thrown but firm. By the time she turned around, all three of them were waiting on her without meaning to.

Kem sat when she pointed. Dren took the wall by the door. Vel stayed standing until Thessa came at her with a hard look, steam lifting off the kettle.

"Sit down."

"I'm fine."

"You're limping on the left and carrying your ribs like they owe you money. Sit down."

Vel sat.

Thessa poured tea, set the cups out, shoved the bread to the middle of the table, and then saw the dark stain creeping through Dren's sleeve.

"No," she said. "Absolutely not. Come here."

Dren glanced at Vel, then pushed off the wall with a grimace and came to the table. "You're sweeter than you sound."

"And you're leaking on my floor." Thessa reached for his wrist. "Sit."

He sat because she made it sound less like a request than a structural fact.

She unwound the outer bandage far enough to inspect the stitches. Kem tried not to stare, failed, and stared anyway. Thessa pressed near the wound, just shy of cruel and well past gentle.

"Who did this?" she asked.

"I did," Kem said.

Thessa looked at him over Dren's arm. "You."

"Yes."

"On purpose."

"That seemed to be the idea."

Some unwilling amusement tugged at the corner of her mouth and vanished before it became anything generous. "It's ugly."

Dren said, "Arm's still on."

"That is the strongest argument for it." She peeled the bandage back a little more, inspected the angry red around the stitches, and reached for a squat jar from the shelf. "He'll keep it if you all stop pretending a road heals faster than a bed."

Vel took her tea. The cup was warm enough to sting. For a few breaths the room held only the crackle from the hearth, the sound of Thessa rewrapping Dren's arm, and a child shouting somewhere outside about a runaway chicken.

Then Thessa tied the cloth off, released Dren, and finally sat across from Vel.

"You vanish for twelve years," she said, "and this is what walks through my door."

Dren lifted the freshly wrapped arm a fraction. "Door's still standing. That's a start."

Thessa ignored him and kept her eyes on Vel. "You're heading south."

"I need routes."

"Of course you do." Thessa took a sip of tea, set the cup down, and rose again for the map shelf before she had fully swallowed. "The old bridge is gone, or gone enough. Flood finished the center span three years back. Traders use a ford east of it if the water is low enough to be trusted, which it usually isn't. If the river's up, you'll find the bridge first and curse me from there."

"What about farther in?"

"Eat first." She nudged the bread toward Vel. "Mara baked that at dawn and she'll haunt me if I let it go stale on company."

"I'm not hungry."

"Then hold a piece and make me feel useful."

Vel took the bread because arguing with hospitality was a waste of ribs.

Thessa spread a cloth map over the table. Vel pulled Maren's journal from her pack by habit, opened it to the river pages, and set it half-turned toward Kem so he could line the older notes against Thessa's newer marks.

Thessa's eyes flicked down to the motion. Something small tightened around her mouth.

"You let him lean over that now," she said.

Vel frowned at the map. "He can read."

"That wasn't what I meant."

Kem kept his gaze on the page. That at least was tact, or near enough.

Thessa pinned the western edge of the cloth map. "After the river it gets thin. There are field camps, more Hoarded contracts, and fewer settlements willing to say they saw anyone. There is a dry creek bed south of the bridge that still carries storm water in spring. If you hit it, follow it only until noon. After that it twists you east and wastes half a day."

Vel tapped a penciled mark in Maren's margin. "The ridge path still open."

"Sometimes." Thessa reached over and marked the cloth with a charcoal stub. "Depends whether the wash ate the switchback this year."

Kem kept his place on the journal page as he followed the exchange. He did not ask for permission each time he moved the book. Vel noticed that too. So did Thessa.

"And south of that?" Dren asked.

"South of that I hear things and choose not to go find out if they are true."

"Why not?"

Thessa looked up at Vel.

"Because I live here now," she said. "Because I mend roofs and weed gardens and haul sacks and mean to keep enough of my number to stand up when winter gets ugly. Because I got tired of opening the door to people I loved and counting how much of them was still left. Pick whichever answer annoys you least."

Vel's jaw tightened. "I'm not asking you to come."

"No." Thessa's mouth twitched, not close to a smile. "You never ask plain. You show up with road dust on your cuffs and your plans already packed and pretend that isn't a form of asking."

Kem looked between them. Dren tore bread one-handed and watched the front window as if it might become useful.

Thessa's gaze shifted back to Kem and stayed there longer this time. She took in the pallor still clinging to him under the sunburn, the fresh calluses, the road wear starting to roughen him, the way he sat like a man who had spent twenty-six years indoors and was only recently learning that chairs were not obligations.

"You're from the Vault."

"I was," Kem said. "I'm not, exactly. It's complicated."

"It usually is." She studied him another second. "What level?"

Vel answered first. "Not relevant."

"It's relevant to me."

Kem glanced at Vel once, then back to Thessa. "One-seventy-eight. That was this morning."

Thessa went still. Her own cup stopped halfway to her mouth. Vel could see her making the comparison whether she meant to or not. The gap between them was a hundred levels, more than a person like Thessa could spend in a lifetime of bad decisions and decent reasons.

"Oh," she said.

Then, flatter: "You went looking again."

"Thessa."

She set the cup down with care that looked one slip away from throwing it. "Don't put my name in your mouth like that fixes anything."

"I came for route information."

"And a map. And a meal you didn't plan to stay for. And whatever else you dragged in with you." She tapped the table grain once, twice. "Fine. You want route information. South from here turns worse after the river. There are more Hoarded camps and more local men willing to sell your footsteps for winter salt. Does that help."

Vel reached for the journal again to mark the dry creek. Thessa's eyes followed the motion.

This time she really looked at the book.

She stared at the bent spine, the gut-thread repair at the corner, the dark smear where rain had once dried into the leather. Recognition crossed her face so fast it was almost ugly.

"You're carrying her pages."

Vel left the journal open between them.

"Maren's journal." Thessa touched the air above it and did not touch it. "Hallson's trader did that repair. Couldn't knot a cord to save his life, so she made him do it twice while you laughed at him." Her voice pulled tight. "You still have it."

Vel said nothing.

"You still have it," Thessa said again, softer and therefore worse. "You walked out of here twelve years ago. You heard from Barrens eight years ago that she crossed. Barrens. And you're still dragging her handwriting up and down the south road like the paper's going to answer back if you wear it thin enough."

"Thessa."

"No. Let me get this wrong if I need to." Her eyes stayed on the journal. "I found out from Barrens. Not from you. You were a ten-minute walk away and you sent the news through three other mouths because that was easier than knocking on my door and standing there while I heard it."

Vel took a breath too deep and pain hooked under the left ribs hard enough to make her grip tighten on the table edge. The room smelled of thyme and hot bread. Maren's notes lay open under Thessa's stare, cramped handwriting running up against fresh pencil marks and bread crumbs.

"I know whose journal it is," Vel said.

Thessa looked up at last. "Do you."

It was not really a question. It was an accusation with the grammar half stripped off it.

Kem's attention moved between them. "Vel said Maren found the threshold."

Thessa turned to him so fast the chair legs scraped stone. "Five years ago she sat where you are. Same table. Same stupid good bread. She didn't start with Maren. She started with numbers."

Kem went still.

Thessa shoved on before anyone else could. "She asked if any Hoarded had run south. I said probably, people run from everything. She said no, not names, what numbers. What their Ledgers looked like. Who still had enough on them to keep spending after the river. Whether anybody above two-fifty had vanished angry enough to burn through the road. That is what she wanted from me."

Vel's voice went flat. "Enough."

"No." Thessa's warmth dropped clean out of her. "No, not enough. He gets to know you didn't just stumble into him out there. You were asking the arithmetic before you had his face."

Kem's grip tightened on the cup. "Before me."

Vel did not look at him. "Thessa is angry."

"I am," Thessa said. "I'm angry that you took my routes and her pages and the news I should have had from your mouth. I'm angry that you went south, came back lower every time, and expected the people who loved you to clap because somebody else's child kept breathing. I'm angry that five years ago you sat in my kitchen with that book open and asked me which Hoarded runaways still had enough left in them to matter."

"Stop talking around it," Kem said.

The words were quiet. That made the room tighter, not looser.

Vel looked at him then.

He had the same expression he wore when he was fitting one confusing fact against another and refusing to let either one slide out of place just because the picture would be kinder without it.

"Did you know," he asked, "that you were looking for someone like me."

The question was stripped down enough to hurt.

Vel wanted the smaller one. She wanted bridge, ford, dry creek, anything with ground under it.

"I knew I couldn't do the south alone."

Kem did not let her have the smaller answer. "That's not the same thing."

Thessa laughed once, raw and short. "No. It isn't."

Vel tightened on the table edge until the wood bit into her skin. She could feel the old map marks, the fresh charcoal from Thessa, the bent warmth of the cup she had barely touched.

"At first," she said, and hated how much air the two words needed, "yes."

No one moved.

Kem's eyes stayed on her face. "Because of the number."

"Yes."

He swallowed once. The movement in his throat looked painful. "And now."

Vel could feel every bad answer lined up behind her teeth. I don't know. It changed. You changed it. None of them would land clean, and anything clean here would be a lie anyway.

"Now you're here," she said.

He stared at her a second longer, as if waiting for the part she had not managed to force into the open. When it did not come, he set the cup down so carefully the ceramic made almost no sound.

"Fine," he said. "Then don't ask me for another level blind."

The words landed harder than if he had shouted. Dren looked away from the window at that, finally, and then back again because the front of the house had started to matter more than the inside.

Thessa wiped under one eye, angry at the wetness there. "Later, when the road gets quiet, ask her what she'd have done if you'd walked out of that place at seventy-eight instead of one-seventy-eight. Ask that and watch her mouth."

Vel turned on her. "Don't."

"No. You don't get to choose the tidy version now."

Dren's head came up toward the front window. "We've got company."

No one moved for a beat.

"The Hoarded woman," he said. "Coming here now."

Thessa was on her feet before the sentence finished. She crossed to the window, looked once, and came back with the last of the softness gone out of her face.

"She does quota review once a month," she said. "Settlement reports. Traveler notes."

Vel stood. "Traveler notes."

Thessa looked at the bread, the cups, the map, the journal, and then at the front door as if she could already read the report the representative would file.

"Three years ago they offered grain priority," she said. "Timber allotments. A healer twice a season. Medicine when the children got lung-fever. All I had to do was keep track of who passed through and where they were headed."

"You took it."

"Yes." There was no softness in it. "I took it. Winter stopped feeling like a knife at the door for a while. Roofs got fixed. People ate. I took it."

No one argued with her.

Through the window, the representative was already crossing the open ground toward the house. The document case was under her arm. In the other she carried a short relay scroll, the kind that could wake a station thirty miles away if the stylus touched it right.

Vel asked, "Does she know it's us?"

"She saw you by the well. Whether she understood it fast enough, I don't know." Thessa scrubbed at her face. "Her lapses are worse now. Last month she lost nearly a minute in my kitchen while I was lying to her about barley totals. She stood there with her eyes gone and then asked me for the count again. I've been making bargains with a woman who's disappearing a piece at a time."

"And the relay is already live. If she finds you in my front room, the bargain is dead. If I drag you out to her, the bargain is still dead and I get to help them do the writing. Grain goes. Timber goes. Maybe the healer too. So don't stand there acting like handing you over is the smart clean choice for me. It isn't cleaner. It's just meaner."

Kem rose. "Why help us then?"

Thessa looked at him with something close to pity and nothing close to comfort. "Because she saw you. Because the relay's warm. Because I'm done feeding road names to a woman who forgets her own mouth mid-sentence. Because if I have to lose the deal anyway, I'd rather lose it lying."

Dren was already reaching for his pack. "Back way?"

Thessa nodded at once. "Kitchen door. Out the lane, then west between my place and Garran's shed. Orchard after that. The irrigation ditch runs south about a mile before it empties into a dry creek. Stay under the bank if they scan."

She caught Vel's eye then, and the look had none of the public settlement warmth left in it.

"I can buy you a little time," she said. "Not much. If she asks, I had northbound grain men in for water and sent them on. If she asks twice, the lie gets worse. So move while I'm still good at it."

Vel folded the journal shut and slid it into her pack. Thessa watched the motion with a face that had gone flat from holding too much at once.

"Thessa," Vel said.

"Don't thank me."

That should have been the end of it. Instead Thessa's mouth tightened and she added, not looking away from Kem, "Ask her the seventy-eight question when you're three days south and too tired to pretend you forgot it."

Kem said nothing.

Vel had no answer that would not cost more time than they had. She went.

---

They left through the kitchen, past drying herbs and the chipped washbasin, into the narrow lane behind the houses. Sun hammered the clay. Before Vel hit the back gate, she heard the front latch lift and Thessa's voice swing bright and practiced through the house.

"Back already? I was just writing the barley count. No, nobody interesting. Three grain men from the north road, thirsty as cattle and twice as stupid."

The lie was warm, easy, and entirely irreversible.

Vel spent one level on a tight Perception sweep.

The world snapped sharper and hit her behind the eyes. The representative had reached the front room. The relay scroll was already lit. Thessa was standing between the woman and the table, smiling with all her teeth and talking about northbound wagons that did not exist. Two settlement enforcers were already coming in fast from the east road, drawn by procedure, nerves, or the relay. Fifteen minutes, maybe less, before the search narrowed to directions and guesses.

Twenty-one.

They cut through the orchard. The trees had been charmed hard years ago and then left alone, so the branches sprawled low and ugly, fruit still green, irrigation channels sunk deep enough to hide a crouched body. Dren dropped into the ditch first. Kem followed, stooping to keep below the lip. Vel came last, boots sliding on clay split by old water lines.

"How long?" Dren asked.

"Quarter hour before they're pointed south. Maybe less if she stops forgetting the room she's in."

Kem kept moving. He did not look at Vel when he spoke. "I'll cover us."

"I know."

"Overlay first. Shield if they get close. Blur if they track." He said it to the ditch wall, to the dry roots sticking through it, to anything but her. "Eighteen if I keep it tight."

Dren said, "Then keep it tight."

Kem lifted both hands. The concealment settled over them in a fitted shell, light bending off the ditch lip, sound thinning, their movement smearing into heat shimmer and dust.

Seven levels.

It was not the wild excess he had spent at the start of the road. It was measured work now, held close, calibrated to bodies and ditch walls and the distance they actually needed.

Vel dropped a second concealment over their tracks, one level only, enough to fog the prints in the clay and blur the broken edge where they had climbed in.

Twenty.

They moved south in a bent half-run. Dren favored the left arm harder now, keeping it pinned close to his ribs. Every bad step showed in his jaw. The ditch curved away from the orchard toward a dry creek.

Behind them, ability use prickled through the Perception residue still fading in Vel's skull. A scan passed over the orchard. Over the lane. Over the ditch. It went north first.

Thessa had bought them that.

Vel did not turn to look back. She could still hear the lie in her head, warm and easy.

Forty minutes south, Kem's concealment wavered.

"It's thinning," he said.

"How long?" Dren asked.

"Not long."

He shifted without waiting for Vel this time. A shield snapped around them so close the air pressed at Vel's skin. Five levels. Then the tracking blur, six more, washed over their movement and turned their outlines watery.

That made eighteen.

When he lowered his hands, his fingers ticked once against his palms. He closed them and kept them closed.

They came out of the ditch into a dry creek bed and kept going. Open ground spread ahead in scrub and brittle grass, with low hills lifting toward the river. No pursuit sat visibly behind them. That did not mean much. Visibility had never been the same thing as safety.

Kem glanced inward at his Ledger. "One-sixty."

Vel nodded. The number landed between them and stayed there.

Dren had the cloth map one-handed, folded down to the charcoal marks Thessa had made at the table. When the trail split around a wash of broken stone, he took the left branch after one quick look.

Kem went with him at once.

Usually he would have checked with Vel, or asked for the journal, or looked back long enough to make it clear the road was still being chosen together. This time he stayed beside Dren and matched himself to the trader's pace.

Thessa's kitchen kept riding in Vel's head: bread torn open on the plate, Maren's journal spread wide, Barrens in Thessa's mouth, Kem saying because of the number. Thessa had lied for them anyway. Thessa had asked the meaner question and sent it south in their packs with the bread smell and the map charcoal and the image of that Hoarded woman standing blank in the middle of a sentence.

Ahead, Dren said, "Water by nightfall if the map's right."

Kem answered him without turning. "Good."

When the trail narrowed to single file, Dren kept the lead, Kem kept the middle, and Vel took the back without anyone naming the change out loud. The gap between Kem and Vel was only a few paces. It held anyway.

The hills lifted toward the river. Heat shook above the ground. Vel adjusted her pack over the sore line of her ribs and followed them south.


# Chapter 11

Three days after they left Thessa's settlement, they found the bridge. Kem's shoulders still remembered every borrowed bit of Strength he'd used to drag the pack up bad hills, and Thessa's question had only gotten meaner with distance. What remained of the bridge, gray timbers slumped into the current and iron bolts orange with rust, looked like a bridge the flood had finished after years of neglect. The southern end had been torn open and then left to rot. Northern pilings stood like teeth in a broken jaw, with water rushing between them in brown surges that carried sticks and clods of soil.

At the bank, Kem watched the river. It was wider than he'd expected, twenty meters at least, loud with the kind of sound water made when it was working rather than resting. The surface moved fast and uneven, cresting over submerged rocks, carrying silt from somewhere upstream where it had rained recently. During the three days south from Thessa's place, the terrain had turned barer and less maintained. Fences had thinned. Cultivated ground had given way to rougher scrub. The road itself had narrowed from packed earth to a track that was mostly suggestion. This was the boundary between the Spent lands and whatever lay beyond them.

"How deep?" he asked.

Vel was crouching at the bank, testing the current with a stripped branch. She pulled it out and studied the wet line. "It'll be waist-deep at the edges. Chest-high in the middle, maybe higher. Rain upstream pushed it up."

"So we spend."

"Not first."

"Bridge Conjuration would take three levels. I could span it in twenty seconds."

"Bank shifts under it and you're out three, still in the river."

"I've got Water Walking at two."

"Keep it." She pulled the branch out of the water. The current had already stripped the bark below the waterline, leaving white wood that looked gnawed. "You walk it with your legs unless the river changes the job. If you can plant your feet, you walk. If the bottom goes, if the pack starts dragging you sideways, if you can't make that rock under your own body, then you spend and get yourself there. Not before."

Dren was sitting on a flat rock a few meters back, working his boots off and lashing them to the top of his pack in a configuration Kem had watched him perform half a dozen times, leather soles turned outward, the knot cinched with a trader's twist that left a tail he could pull to release. Dren had used the same knot on cargo bales, saddlebags, and once on a loose tent stake that would not hold in soft ground.

"Current's stronger than it looks," Dren said, without looking up. "Don't aim at the bank. Aim at that flat rock out there. Midstream, closer to the south side. Let the current carry you to it, then cut the last channel. If the shelf disappears under you, spend for the rock. Don't get proud."

"Right." Kem was already assessing options. Bridge Conjuration, three levels. Flight Charm, two, but he'd never used one and the calibration problems from his first weeks still haunted him. Or a Strength boost and just bull through.

"You heard me," Vel said.

Kem looked at her. She was standing now, the branch discarded, her expression the flat practical one she used when she'd already made a decision and considered the discussion administrative.

"The river's dangerous."

"The river's crossable."

More questions were available. How much drag counted as dragging. Whether chest-high on Dren meant chest-high on him. Whether Water Walking on bad current counted as getting himself out or as panicking early. The part of him that might have asked them was still thin and new. The older part wanted a rule he could obey cleanly. Vel knew the road. Dren knew the water. He told himself that should be enough.

"Say it back," Vel said.

Kem looked from her to the rock. "If I can keep my feet, I walk. If the bottom goes or the pack starts pulling me sideways, I spend for the rock."

"Good."

Dren went first. He hoisted his pack and walked to the bank without ceremony. River crossings were part of trade routes the way stairs were part of buildings. Water came to his thighs on the first few steps. Then he took the current at an angle toward the flat rock, moving with the unhurried steadiness of a man reading terrain, each foot planted, tested, weighted. The current pushed against him, but his face did not change. His attention stayed on the water, tracking where pressure eased, where footing held, where cost stayed low.

At the deep middle run, the water reached his chest. He lifted his pack above his head. One boot touched the rock. Three breaths there, a small angle change, then the last eight meters of side channel to the south bank.

Twenty minutes from bank to bank. On the far side, he pulled himself out, shook water from his pack, and sat down to wring out his shirt.

Vel went next. Boots came off. Her knot differed from Dren's and did the same job. She entered the water as if stepping into a room she'd visited before. Being shorter put the current at her ribs sooner than it had on Dren, but a lower stance and decades of practice solved most of that. One hand hit the rock. From there she crossed the side channel without pausing and climbed out on the south bank.

Ten minutes. She climbed out on the south bank, wrung her shirt once, then looked back long enough to make clear the earlier rule had not expired.

Both of them were across. The river ran between Kem and the rest of his group, loud and brown and indifferent to his situation.

He loosened his pack straps, tightened them again, then picked up the stripped branch Vel had left behind and waded one step into the edge current. Gravel held. The second probe found the lip where the bottom pitched away, and the branch shivered in his hand hard enough to carry force all the way up his wrist. From the far bank, Vel pointed first at the outcrop, then at his pack.

"If that starts carrying you instead of the other way around, that's the line," she called. "Cut it loose or spend. Don't stand there deciding whether it counts."

"Keep your hips low," Dren added. "Stand up straight and it'll knock you sideways."

Boots came off.

---

He entered where Dren had told him to, twenty meters upstream of the outcrop. From shore, the line had looked simple: let the current carry him diagonally to the rock, brace there, then cut the last eight meters of side channel to the south bank. Water hit with a cold that felt stored rather than made. His ankles locked. Another step found slick green stone and forced a quick correction.

Waist-deep water pressed at his thighs with flat, steady force. The pack shifted on his shoulders. Both hands locked on the straps. Beneath his feet, gravel gave way to smoother stone in all the wrong places.

A third of the way across, the bottom dropped.

Six inches, maybe eight. Enough. The river rose from waist to chest and changed the problem completely. Force that had been shoving at his legs started driving into his ribs. He staggered, caught himself, took one blind step, then another.

*If I can keep my feet, I walk. If the bottom goes or the pack starts pulling me sideways, I spend for the rock.* Vel had been plain. Kem told himself the bottom had not gone, not fully. It had only shifted. The pack was heavy, not dragging. One more careful step and the rule would stay theoretical.

"Angle more," Dren called from the far bank. "You're going too straight."

Kem angled. A hidden stone rolled under his foot. His knee went sideways and the river made a hard, practical case for lying down. Somehow he stayed upright through a movement that used both arms and no dignity. By then the pack was soaked and his clothes were dragging at him, heavy enough to make those Strength boosts on the road feel less like waste and more like ordinary sense.

Water Walking sat in him at two levels, easy and available and humiliating. He took another ordinary step instead.

Halfway out, his feet found the submerged shelf that fed the rock. For one breath the line seemed recoverable. Water lapped at his shoulders. The current shifted around him in indifferent patterns that had nothing to do with him and would keep going after he was gone.

Vel was watching from the south bank with the same exact attention she gave any situation she was still evaluating. Nearby, Dren sat squeezing water from his shirt.

One more step ended the shelf. His foot found nothing and the current took him.

There was no crash of white water. The river simply moved him sideways until his body stopped being a thing that walked and became a thing being carried. His toes searched for bottom and found none. The pack dragged him backward. Water went down his throat in a hard, accidental gulp, and coughing only pulled more in.

The river pulled him under.

Sound changed first. Then the whole river closed over him, brown and loud and close. Cold became enormous. Kicking got his head back up once. A gasp. Another shove under, casual as a hand pressing a floating stick back beneath the surface just to watch it rise.

His shin cracked against rock. Pain came sharp and particular, the exact wrongness of bone meeting stone through thin skin. He used it. One push off the strike point got his head clear long enough to cough until his ribs hurt.

Ten meters gone, just like that.

"Get your feet downstream!" Vel shouted. Her voice broke against the water and reached him in pieces, but the order was clear enough. "Feet first — don't fight it, ride it to the shallows!"

His feet went downstream. Rocks found him anyway. One ripped his shirt and laid open a hot stripe of skin from shoulder blade to ribs. Another caught the pack and spun him under again, just long enough for cold to fill his ears and certainty to fill everything else. The river could kill him. It was not even making an effort.

When his head came up again, the south side looked close enough to touch. The river had dumped him onto a gravel tongue downstream of the outcrop, a waist-deep shelf where he could stand. Standing did not help much. His legs were shaking from cold and effort. Blood from the cracked shin spread in the water around him in pale pink ribbons.

Vel and Dren were upstream and across from him, on the actual south bank. Between the gravel tongue and that bank ran the side channel Dren had crossed in the last push, dark water folding hard along the shore. The outcrop that anchored the route was now behind him and upstream.

Water Walking came to the front of his teeth. He swallowed it. Spending now would mean admitting the crossing had gone wrong exactly where they'd said it could, and some stupid part of him still wanted to finish it the correct way.

Upstream through the shallows looked possible until he started doing it. A step at waist depth turned chest-deep on the next stride. Gravel gave way to clay that sucked at his feet. One half-submerged branch offered a handhold for a single pull.

The branch snapped.

Down to the neck again. Current shoved him sideways while his hands clawed at mud and roots that came apart in numb fingers. For a moment his feet found purchase. Then the clay bottom gave way and he dropped again.

On the bank across the side channel, Vel took a step into the shallows.

Up again, coughing. Vel's weight had shifted forward. She stood at the lip of the water with the contained focus she used right before acting, close enough to the edge of her own assessment that the sight of her did more than the water in his lungs had.

She had been wrong about the river. They both knew it now.

Some submerged root caught under one heel and let him shove himself upstream. Vel stepped back out.

The next stretch was worse than the first dunking because it went on. False bottoms. Sudden cuts. One feeder channel carrying water even colder than the main river and smelling of raw clay. Neck-deep there, with his hands slipping on green-slick stones and his feet touching only often enough to keep panic from turning final.

Each breath kept arriving a moment before the river took it away. The margin got thinner every time.

By the time he fought his way back to the route, the flat outcrop rose a meter above the waterline exactly where Dren had said it would. Bloody hands slipped twice before finding enough purchase to climb onto it. From up there the map finally held still. Main channel behind him. Side channel in front. South bank eight meters away.

The outcrop had not solved the real problem.

Eight meters of hard water separated the rock from the bank where Vel and Dren waited. Dren had his arms folded. Vel stood at the edge of the shallows with the look of someone trying not to make a second bad decision.

Kem slid off the rock. The side channel took him hard and he fought it badly, with frozen hands, lead legs, and the last reserves of a body that had never been asked for this kind of work. The current spun him once. Then a foot hit gravel. Solid. Shallow enough. He lunged. The bottom held. Another lunge and the water dropped from waist to thigh to knee.

Two hours after he'd entered the river, he crawled onto the far bank on his hands and knees. Face-down in the dirt, he breathed mud and didn't move.

---

Dren said, quietly, "Better than I expected, honestly."

That did not help.

Kem rolled onto his back. The sky was pale and ordinary, with a few thin clouds sliding east. His body had become a list of complaints: the shin split open on rock and still leaking into the dirt, the long scrape on his back where torn shirt stuck to raw skin, hands turning from numbness into a bright painful tingling, thighs and lower back aching from two hours of fighting current. All of it earned. None of it permanent.

When he sat up, Vel was standing three feet away, wringing out the hem of her shirt. Her legs were wet to the knee. So she had gone in far enough to be ready.

"You were going to come in," he said.

"You were struggling."

"You told me not to spend."

"I did."

"That could've killed me."

"Yeah."

Fury came on slowly and cold, matching the rest of him. Brown water closing over his head. The bottom dropping away. That flat, absolute second of understanding that the river could take him and his Ledger would never change.

"Why?" he said.

Vel sat across from him on a rock. Dren moved farther up the bank and started laying out his pack to dry, giving them the distance of a man who had opinions and no interest in billing for them.

"Because I thought you could make it," Vel said. "Wrong call."

"So I nearly drowned because you made a bad call."

"You nearly drowned because I made a bad call and because when it crossed the line I gave you, you kept treating the first half like law."

Kem waited.

"I said walk it while you had bottom," she said. "I said if the bottom went or the pack started dragging, you spent for the rock. The middle came up sooner than I thought. That's mine. Once it did, you still kept trying to finish it without spending."

"Because you told me not to spend."

"I know what I told you. I also told you where the line was."

He'd expected an apology. Some attempt to ease it. Vel was telling him he should've overruled her.

"Rain upstream raised the middle. I should've read that. I should've put a line on you or sent the packs over separate. That's mine. But once you lost the shelf, you had enough to decide for yourself. You had the rock. You had Water Walking. You had the same river I did. You still kept looking at me like I could do the deciding from the bank."

"I was trying to do what you said."

"You were trying to make it my mistake all the way through."

She picked up a stone from the bank and turned it in her hands without looking at him.

"Back in the Vault they told you keeping the number whole made you good," she said. "I tell you not to spend on stupid things. Those are different instructions. You keep cramming them together so you don't have to decide anything."

His father at the Rite came back to him at once. Perfect stillness. Perfect obedience. Same shape, Kem thought. Different furniture.

"That's not the same thing."

"No. It isn't." Vel's voice stayed flat. "That's why you're sitting here half skinned."

The river kept moving behind them, unchanged by the point it had already made. Arguing was possible. Winning felt less so.

"So what do I do?" His voice came out raw from the water he'd swallowed.

"Next crossing, you check depth yourself," she said. "Bottom. Current. What changes where. If it stops being a walking problem and starts being a drowning problem, spend. If I call it wrong, say so before you're under. If you call it wrong, live with the bill. But make the call yourself."

The stone clicked back into the dirt.

"Next river, I make my own call," he said.

"Good," Vel said.

Three days since Thessa's kitchen, and the question still sat where it had landed. Was Vel teaching him to judge for himself, or building the kind of man she needed? Both possibilities fit too neatly to let the matter rest.

"One more thing," Vel said. "Since the Spent Towns."

"What about them?"

"You've bled eighteen levels on convenience," Vel said. "Six before Thessa. Two heating washwater. Two on light because dusk made you nervous. Two in Strength taking that wash in a jump instead of walking the bank."

His mouth opened. Closed.

"Since her settlement, another twelve. Two on cooking fires when the flint would've done. Six in Strength getting your pack over hills your own shoulders could've handled. Four on light when Dren had a torch or we had oil."

He had not been counting.

"Check your Ledger," she said.

The number was there, persistent and precise, the Ledger's one reliable quality. He had not looked at it closely since Thessa's settlement, but the earlier figures were still there in memory now, tied to places more than dates.

One hundred and forty-eight.

Two hundred and twenty-five when they'd entered the Spent Towns. One hundred and eighty-three after the silo fight. One hundred and sixty after the settlement escape. One hundred and forty-eight now. Seventy-seven levels gone since the Towns. Eighteen of them to being warm, lit, clean, and spared the weight of his own pack while he had been congratulating himself for restraint at the river.

"Today the river should've cost you," Vel said. "Hot water, bright camps, hills you can walk under your own weight, those shouldn't. That's the split. Start making it."

Vel stood and began unpacking wet things. Conversation over.

Dren came over with a strip of cloth he'd torn from his pack. "Your shin," he said. "Before it fills with dirt."

Kem took the cloth. "Thanks."

"Wasn't what I signed on for," Dren said. "The motivational drowning."

"She said she was wrong about the river."

"She was." Dren sat on his heels and looked at the water. That particular expression was becoming legible to Kem now, the one that showed up whenever Dren's daughter and his Ledger entered the same calculation. "She's usually not, though. Wrong, I mean. That's the problem with traveling with somebody who's been doing this forty years. When she's right, you learn. When she's wrong, it almost kills you."

Dren stood, brushed dirt from his knees, and went back to his things.

Kem wrapped his shin and sat on the bank looking south. Terrain on this side of the river was different, drier, with thinner vegetation and soil so pale it looked leeched. The road, such as it was, continued as two ruts in dust-colored dirt. Whatever lay ahead did not look like the Spent Towns.

He didn't check again. The number was there whether he looked at it or not.

They kept moving until dusk.

First night south of the river, they made camp on a chalk rise above a dry wash. Kem would have walked past it if Vel had not stopped and said, "Pick it."

He looked at her. "Now?"

"Now."

The wash offered shelter and easier water. The rise offered wind, sightlines, and ground that looked mean but honest. Kem started toward the wash because it was closer and his shin was throbbing in time with his pulse. Then he saw the drift line caught in the reeds: sticks, grass, one dead fish gone to skin and spine, all combed into the same narrow stripe well above ankle height.

"The rise," he said.

"Why?"

He pointed with his chin. "That wash filled recently. If rain hits upstream again, I'm not sleeping in it. And if somebody comes up the road, I'd rather see them before they're on top of us."

Dren glanced at the reeds. "Good enough."

Vel said, "Then set camp."

That turned out to be a different job from choosing the ground. Kem dropped the packs where the rise looked flatest until Dren said, "Runoff," and nudged one bundle six feet over with his boot toward a patch of dirt the last storm had not cut open. Kem fetched scrub for the fire, judged half of it wrong, fetched more. The striker skipped in his numb fingers twice before sparks finally took in the bark.

For a second the old answer came up bright and easy. Flame Charm. Two levels. Hot water in a blink, dry shirt sooner, no need to crouch here shivering over a spit of fire like a fool. He could feel the ability in him, available, almost insulted not to be used.

Vel set the cook pot beside him. "Boil water. Clean the shin."

He looked up, half expecting instruction on whether this counted. She was already spreading blankets to dry and not looking back.

So he made the smaller calls himself. More scrub. Then thicker branches. Pot close enough to heat fast, not close enough to soot the whole thing black. When the water steamed, he carried it off with a folded cloth and sat on a rock above the camp. Mud had dried around the cut in a rusty crust. He poured, and the pain was clean and immediate, nothing abstract about it. River grit washed down his ankle in pink threads.

Dren came over long enough to hand him the rest of the torn cloth. "Again," he said. "You missed some."

Kem cleaned it again.

By full dark the fire held on its own. They ate cold bread, onion, and the last of Thessa's hard cheese because nobody had the patience left to tend a pot after that day. The river was out of sight now, but he could still hear it when the wind shifted, a long low rush that made the back of his neck tighten before he could tell himself where he was.

No one returned to the argument. Vel asked once whether the packs would stay dry if the wind turned. Kem looked at the ground, looked at the wash, and moved them another yard uphill before answering. She said nothing. He kept waiting for correction anyway. It didn't come.

---

Sable built the fire herself.

She used striker and steel from the field equipment kit she'd taken six weeks earlier, back when the kit had been an object of institutional comedy, designed by administrators who had never lit a fire and packed by clerks who could not tell kindling from deadfall. Since then she had learned the difference as she learned everything, systematically, with attention to process, noting which materials caught and which smoked and died.

Sparks caught in the shredded bark. Cupped hands fed the flame dried grass, then twigs, then larger pieces in the order five weeks of trial and revision had established. The fire grew. The care was the same care she had once given quarterly audit reports, and this time she let the comparison stand.

Three weeks of peeling away from the team whenever terrain allowed had made camp into procedure. Tonight the remaining agents were one ridge north with the spare animals and their own fire, close enough for retrieval if something went wrong and far enough that she could think. Two of the five had been unable to continue. Spending had unsettled them in ways decades of Hoarded propriety had not prepared them for. The other three could still function, but growing competence made them harder to predict. They asked fewer procedural questions now. One had started gutting rabbits without looking ill afterward. Another had begun correcting the maps from memory. Unpredictable spenders at level 280 were a liability. Distance suited the work better than she had expected.

Her camp was organized. Boots aligned by the fire. Provisions inventoried, another week at this pace before resupply. An annotated map lay open beside her, margins filled in her own precise hand, and even the language in those notes had changed. The landmark the official map labeled "Terrain Feature 7" was a ridge. In her notes it was a ridge too. That shift felt larger than it should have.

Down the slope, somewhere beyond the scrub, a mule bell knocked once. One of the agents answered it with a curse too faint to make out. She had put enough ground between herself and them to keep the call private, not enough to pretend they had already left her.

Then she activated the Communication ability.

Two levels left her in the familiar precise subtraction, a third sensation between pain and loss that Hoarded vocabulary had never bothered to name. Her Ledger adjusted to 270. Six weeks earlier it had read 300. Thirty levels had gone to pursuit, tracking, travel, and the accumulated expense of being alive outside a building for the first time in her adult life.

The ability connected. Lady Aldraste's voice arrived clean and cold, carrying the authority of a woman who had chaired institutional meetings for three decades and learned to compress command into the pitch of her syllables.

"Report."

"The subjects are moving south," Sable said. "Past the boundary settlements. Direction is consistent with a Scour approach."

"Rate of travel."

"Reduced. Their guide is injured, and that limits their pace." She paused. The next sentence required a choice between the institutional frame and the accurate one. "The Aldraste subject's incidental ability use has declined. He is spending less on routine tasks."

"Meaning?"

"He is being trained. The Ashwick woman is teaching him to function without abilities." Even saying it, Sable could hear the omitted portion. The training was working. The reckless spender she'd watched fight with the exhilaration of a child was becoming more deliberate, and that transformation no longer fit neatly under decline.

"His current level?"

"Approximately 148. He was at 160 after the settlement escape."

"That is a considerable expenditure." Administrative frost. Numbers that meant money, waste, institutional failure. Underneath it ran a different arithmetic, one Sable knew well enough to hear by now. A mother counting how much of her son was left.

"The majority was combat and defensive spending," Sable said. "Recent losses are incidental."

"Your instructions remain unchanged. Retrieve the subject with minimal force and minimal additional expenditure. The Council's patience with this operation is not without limit."

"Understood."

"And Auditor Orm."

"Yes."

"Your current level."

Firelight banked steadily in the structure she had built with her own hands. Six weeks earlier this would have been impossible. So would eight hours on rough terrain, sleep outdoors, navigation by stars, weather read from cloud patterns, or a single operational decision not routed through committee and countersigned by two superiors.

"Two hundred and eighty-five," she said.

At 270, the lie came out in her official voice, the voice that filed reports and presented audit findings, and in that register all official statements sounded true. The institutional reason for it was simple. A thirty-level expenditure on a retrieval would trigger a spending review, and a review would expose the gap between what the Council had authorized and what she had done with that authorization. Underneath that sat the real reason, less presentable and more important. Those hidden fifteen levels represented choices she had made for herself, spent on becoming someone the Council had not approved. They were hers in a way almost nothing in forty-seven years of institutional service had ever been.

"Noted," Lady Aldraste said. "Continue southward. Report at standard intervals."

The connection held for a moment longer than protocol required. Sable waited. In that silence, Lady Aldraste sounded like a woman deciding whether to step outside the institutional frame that had contained her for decades, long enough to watch her husband go still, her son walk out, and her house become a political problem instead of a family.

"Is he well?" Lady Aldraste asked. The words were quiet. They did not sound like a Council directive.

"He is functional," Sable said. "He is adapting to field conditions."

"That is not what I asked."

Sable thought of Kem in the fight, burning levels with the efficiency of a dropped torch, grinning afterward with a happiness nobody in the Vault had displayed in her presence, ever. She thought of his habit of asking questions, interrupting, circling back, genuine every time. She thought of the trader's grudging thanks afterward — a few flat words, nothing ceremonious, plain and real. Kem had looked at that as if someone had handed him a thing he had not known he wanted.

"He seems well," Sable said. "That is not in my report."

The connection ended.

Sable sat by the fire and watched the flames work through the fuel she had arranged. New calluses had formed over skin that had never gripped anything rougher than a pen or the spine of an audit ledger. They were still pink and tender. Her hair was pulled back in a knot copied from a Spent Town woman three weeks earlier, nothing like the formal arrangement she had maintained for thirty years. What she looked like now, she did not know. There had been no mirror since the capital.

For weeks, pressure had been loading into the same hidden place in her thinking, weight and then more weight, waiting for the crack.

Habit reached first for the institutional frame, a report-shaped sentence about expenditure correlating with field capacity. It failed against the fire, the calluses, the knot in her hair, the level she had kept for herself.

*Does spending make you more?*

She made no entry in the field notes. The observation log she had been maintaining out of bureaucratic reflex stayed blank. Instead she sat with the fire she had built, the calluses on her hands, and the number 270 that was hers, and let the question remain where it was, uncategorized, unprocessed, without protocol for what to do next.

The fire cracked. A log shifted and sent a constellation of sparks upward into the dark. She watched them rise and vanish and did not reach for the tracking ability that would have followed them. She let them go.


# Chapter 12

Vel didn't sleep the first night south of the river. She sat with her back to a tree and her knees drawn up because anything flatter made the ribs catch, while Kem took second watch and kept it past the point where he should have woken her. When she opened her eyes in the dark, he was still by the coals, looking past the tree line to where her pack sat against a log with Maren's journal buckled inside it.

The river itself kept moving a few yards away, flat and black in the dark, as if it hadn't taken two hours out of three people who couldn't spare them. The crossing had rope-burned the skin across Kem's grip. He kept opening and closing his fists when he thought nobody was looking, feeling out the rawness. Dren's boots had dried stiff with silt and his left sleeve kept brushing his side because the arm under it still wasn't right. Vel could feel every bend and twist in her cracked ribs when she shifted against the trunk. None of it was dramatic. It was just the body collecting interest.

He didn't ask what she'd left out at the crossing. He started asking smaller questions and keeping the answers: how much time the river had cost, how many days to the next real water, what she was spending and what she wasn't. Vel gave him the clean numbers and left the rest where it was. That made the silence sharper.

At dawn Dren rolled his blanket with one arm and his teeth, swore once when the bad arm pulled, and stood. Kem doused the ash, checked the knots on the packs, and said nothing. Vel said nothing back. They walked into gray morning under trees already thinning at the edges.

By the second day south the forest had given way to scrub and then to open ground, and the soil was the color of chalk. It was exhausted chalk, the specific pale of ground that had been asked for everything it could give and then asked again. Vel had seen depleted farmland before, in settlements where Fertility Charms had been overworked for a generation. This was different in scale. The depletion went to the horizon, south and east and west, the fields marked by wooden stakes driven into ground too tired to hold them upright. Half the stakes leaned. Nobody had straightened them. Nobody had the levels to care about stakes.

Two days after the river, the land had changed in stages, first the forest thinning, then the soil lightening, then the work showing on people's bodies. Now there was no pretending they were anywhere but the Margins.

The heat didn't help. Early summer in the south was dry and heavy, the kind that pulled moisture from skin and pack straps and road alike. Dren's canteen was half-empty by midday and he hadn't mentioned it. Kem had stopped reaching for Sustenance Charms weeks ago, one of the economies Vel had hammered into him, but she could see the old reflex in the way his attention kept snagging on the belt pouch at his hip and pulling away. He drank water and kept walking. That much she gave him.

The first settlement appeared around noon around a central well, not so much built as gathered there. Chalk-mud had dried in white fans around the stones. Three people waited with buckets and didn't waste speech on one another. The buildings behind them were low and tired, Shaped once and left to settle however they settled.

A woman sat outside the nearest building on a stool, shelling dried beans into a wooden bowl. She looked up when they approached. Her eyes went to Kem first, to the House signet gone dull with road grit, then to the boots that were battered now but still identifiably Vault-made. The beans kept clicking into the bowl while she measured him.

"Water?" Vel asked.

"Well's there." The woman nodded toward the cistern. "Thirty-two people drawing from it, so take what you need and move on."

"We're passing through," Vel said.

"That's what they all say."

Vel filled their canteens. Seven people were visible in the settlement. Nobody crossed the square without carrying something worth the steps. A man stopped at the well and hauled up one bucket, then stood leaning on the rope a moment before he tried for the second. A girl dragged a sack of meal by its tied mouth instead of hoisting it. Vel recognized the pace because she'd worn it herself for years, before Kem, before she'd had someone else's levels to budget alongside her own.

The notice board was by the well. It was proper construction, Hoarded administrative hardwood with iron fittings, the kind of thing a district overseer would have had installed. The notices on it were layered deep, older ones buried under newer ones, the whole thing a geological record of bureaucratic attention paid to a place that had never received any other kind.

The top notice was current. Hoarded administrative stock, cream-colored paper:

NOTICE OF PRODUCTIVITY RECLASSIFICATION: MARGINS DISTRICT 7, SECTOR 14-B

Pursuant to Directive 1172 (amended), all residents of the above-designated sector are hereby classified under the revised Productivity Tier system:

TIER ONE (Levels 40+): Full daily output expected. 0.3 levels-per-annum standard rate. Interest on existing ability-debt accrues per schedule (Appendix C-7, Table 3, subsection ii).

TIER TWO (Levels 15–39): Reduced output. 0.15 levels-per-annum. Supplementary manual labor allocation may be assigned. Interest rate adjustment per Formula 9-B (revised).

TIER THREE (Levels 5–14): Maintenance category. Residents classified as Renewable Expenditure Units and may be reassigned to Development Opportunity programs at District Overseer discretion.

TIER FOUR (Levels 1–4): Non-productive. Account closure processing per Standard Protocol 6.

APPEALS: Reclassification appeals may be submitted to the Bureau of Expenditure Oversight, Northern Administrative Center, during standard business hours (first and third Wedgeday of each month, 10:00–14:00). Filing fee: 2 levels.

Vel read it twice. Once for the language, once for what it would do to a life.

"Renewable Expenditure Units," Kem said. He was reading over her shoulder. His voice had that careful, too-even quality it took on when something from the world outside the Vault didn't fit any framework he'd brought from home. "That means people."

"Renewable because they have children," Vel said. "Children are born at three hundred."

Kem gripped the edge of the board. "And the filing fee. Two levels. If you're Tier Four, you've got one to four levels left."

"Half of everything you have," Vel said.

He stared at the notice: Appendix C, Table 3, subsection ii. The wood gave a dry little squeal under his grip.

Dren hadn't stopped at the board. He was standing thirty feet down the road, watching south. His bad arm hung slightly forward, the way he carried it now. Kem's stitches had held, but the flesh underneath had healed tight and wrong, and Dren protected the arm like a thing he couldn't afford to break a second time.

"We moving?" he said.

Vel pulled the notice from the board. It tore along a nail hole. Underneath was an older one, its ink fading to brown:

DEVELOPMENT OPPORTUNITY PROGRAM: COHORT 47
Duration: 5-year rotating assignment (renewable)
Daily ability expenditure requirement: Per Tier classification
Compensation: Room, board, and 2% annual reduction in outstanding ability-debt balance

There was no reason given. Vel folded both notices and put them in her pack anyway.

***

They passed two more settlements by mid-afternoon. The boards were the same, the equations were the same, and the people moved like every trip across a yard had to justify itself. The notices varied in age but not in content: Productivity Tiers, Expenditure classifications, debt schedules referencing appendices nobody here had ever seen. In one settlement, someone had written across the bottom of a notice in charcoal: *formula doesn't balance; asked district office; they said resubmit next quarter*. The handwriting was shaky. Tier Three, Vel guessed. Or Four.

In the third settlement, they found the labor operation.

It was visible from a quarter mile: a compound surrounded by a wall of Shaped stone, about eight feet high, the Shaping layered thick enough that the stone had that fused, seamless look of serious ability investment. Inside, the tops of low buildings were visible, and workers moved between them. They came and went through a single gate that a woman in her fifties controlled.

The enforcer sat on a chair by the gate with a ledger book in her lap and a clay cup on a crate beside her. When workers passed through, she checked names. When they came back in, she checked again.

"Debt work," Dren said. He'd seen it before. "Hoarded contract. Workers spend their daily quota, she marks it, end of the season the district office processes the reduction. In theory."

"In theory," Vel said.

"In theory."

A child sat against the compound's outer wall, in its shade. Maybe eight years old, small for the age, wrists no thicker than broom twigs. His face was flushed, not from the sun but from fever, the glassy heat of infection. An older girl sat beside him, twelve or thirteen, one palm on his forehead and the other braced in the dust. She did not look away when they came up.

Kem saw the child at the same time Vel did. His stride changed, a slight hitch, a redirection, his body deciding before his mind had caught up.

"Don't," Vel said, quietly enough that she wasn't sure whether she'd said it to him or to herself.

***

Kem knelt beside the child. The girl pulled her brother closer.

"He's got a fever," Kem said.

"I know he's got a fever." The girl looked at the way Kem had gone straight to his knees in the dust, as if a man with his voice and boots wasn't supposed to do that. "He's had it three days."

"Three days is too long. Has anyone healed him?"

"Has anyone what? Healed him?" She said it the way you'd say *has anyone flown him to the moon*. "With what?"

Kem looked at Vel. She knew what he was asking. She also knew what the answer cost, not to the boy but to the cascade, the mechanism, the plan she'd spent eight years building. She didn't say yes. She didn't say no.

He touched the child's forehead and closed his eyes. The Curative ability came as a clean pulse of warmth that Vel could feel from six feet away, like a door opening into a heated room. Kem's calibration had improved. This was targeted and precise, the fever and infection only. Vel felt eight go. A month ago he would have burned twenty or forty on the same job. The boy's face lost its feverish tightness and relaxed into something that looked like ordinary sleep.

The girl stared at Kem. She touched her brother's forehead, then his cheek, shaking hard enough that her nails clicked against his skin. "How many was that?"

"Eight," Kem said. He stood up, and Vel caught the usual change, slight and wrong at the same time, as the Ledger settled at 140.

"Thank you," the girl said. It didn't sound simple. Gratitude was in it, but so was the fury of having needed a stranger's charity in the first place.

The loss hit her a beat later. She'd built the basin around Kem reaching the Scour with about 90 levels, enough for a sustained descent to stabilize the threshold alongside her own. At 140, he was already ten shy of where she'd wanted him by this point. She could adjust. She'd adjusted before.

Then Kem walked toward the compound gate.

"What is this?" he asked the enforcer.

The enforcer looked up from her ledger. "Labor compound. Contract work under District authority."

"And the wall?"

"Compound perimeter. Workers stay on-site during assigned shift. Per regulation."

"How long's a shift?"

"Sunrise to sunset. Per regulation."

"And if someone needs to leave? If their kid's sick?"

The enforcer's face didn't change. She was practiced at this. "Shift completion is required before exit authorization. Per regulation."

Kem looked at the wall. Vel saw the muscles jump in his forearm. The reflex came before every ability use now.

"Kem," she said.

He didn't look at her. He put both hands flat against the Shaped stone and pushed.

The Disruption came as a sustained wave, not a single blast but modulated pressure, weeks of Vel's teaching turning into something close to control. The Shaping cracked along its seams first. The fused layers split with a sound like ice breaking on a river. Then fifteen feet of wall, the gate, and the mechanism holding it together slumped into rubble and pale dust. The enforcer fell off her chair. Workers on the other side stood blinking in the sudden daylight where the wall had been.

The shove ripped twenty off him in one go. Vel felt the remaining 120 pull tight across the next three days. The slack she'd built into that route was gone.

"You can leave," Kem told the workers. Some were already moving. Others stood where they were, staring at the gap as if it might rebuild itself. One woman picked up a tool she'd been using and set it down again, carefully, in the exact spot where it had been. Then she walked through the opening without looking back.

The enforcer got up from the dust, ledger book clutched to her chest. "You've destroyed Hoarded-contracted infrastructure. There'll be — the district office will —"

"Send the invoice to House Aldraste," Kem said.

Vel wanted to laugh. The sound died behind her teeth.

The backup arrived within the hour. Three enforcers from a larger compound to the south. Two men and a woman, all somewhere in the fifty-to-seventy range by their movement, all built like people who'd spent careers on enforcement rather than labor. They assessed the rubble, the dispersing workers, and Kem sitting on a chunk of fallen wall drinking water, and drew the correct conclusions.

The first one opened with a Combat Pulse aimed at Kem's chest. Kem threw up a shield that caught it. The detonation punched the air and blew dust in a circle around him. Four levels, maybe five. The second enforcer was already flanking left, and Kem caught it with a Perception flash that cost him two more, his head snapping toward the movement before the man had committed. The third enforcer hung back, gauging.

Then all three pressed at once. Kem's defensive barrier was rough but solid, a sustained field that held while the enforcers hammered it with everything they had. The air around the barrier rippled with spent energy, nine levels going in just to keep it standing.

He dropped the barrier and hit the first enforcer with a targeted Force Strike before the man could reset. The hit cost three and put the enforcer down hard. The second caught a Strike to the shoulder. Three more, and the man's arm went slack. The third enforcer looked at her two colleagues on the ground and at Kem, who was breathing hard and bleeding from a gash on his forearm where a glancing pulse had opened the skin, and she turned and ran.

By then the fight had burned thirty-five off him. He was at 85.

Kem sat down in the rubble. He tore a strip from the hem of his shirt and wrapped his forearm, the blood soaking through immediately, a dark spreading stain. Two months ago he would have healed the cut with an ability and not thought about the cost. Now he tied the cloth tight with his teeth and tested the tendons with a careful flex. It was field triage, the kind Vel had taught him in the first week, kneeling over a scraped knee he'd wanted to burn three levels on.

A worker came back through the gap in the wall. An old man, sixty or seventy, stooped and slow. He walked to the enforcer's chair, which was lying on its side in the dust, and he righted it. Then he sat down in it. He didn't do anything else. He just sat there and looked out at the road where the enforcers had gone.

Nobody stopped him. Dust kept sliding off the broken wall. The old man sat in the enforcer's chair and watched the road.

Vel stood at the edge of the rubble and felt the bottom drop out. The Scour needed Kem at 90. He was at 85. Five levels gone past the edge, and Kem was wrapping his arm with torn cloth and wearing the expression of someone who wasn't finished.

She needed to say it plain. She did not need to explain the mechanism. She only needed to tell him to stop.

She opened her mouth.

She closed it again. Workers kept coming through the gap. The girl stayed by her brother, still checking his cooling forehead. Behind her, a man waited with a split roof brace across both shoulders. Another woman stood with her thumb plugged into a bucket seam to slow the leak. If Vel said it now, all of them would hear it.

Vel bit the inside of her cheek until she tasted blood.

Word ran faster than dust settled. By the time Kem had the bandage knotted, people were standing at the edge of the rubble in a loose line, not crowding, not begging, just waiting with the patient faces of people used to hearing no. Vel looked at them and heard her own voice before she'd decided to use it.

"One at a time," she said. "Anything that keeps water moving or a roof up goes first."

A woman in a split straw hat stepped forward with a hoe handle tucked under one arm. "East cut's gone," she said. "If the water reaches it like this, it spills into the gravel bed and dies there. Channel feeds four settlements when it works."

Vel knew the cut. She'd seen the line of it from the road, the old contractor ditch carrying runoff east toward a chain of wells. If the break held, the water they'd just freed could actually travel. If it didn't, the whole thing turned into a wet joke.

Kem looked at her. This time he waited.

"Fifteen if you build ugly," Vel said. "More if the foundation's rotten all the way through."

"Ugly's fine," the woman said at once.

"Show me," Kem said.

The east cut had caved where old Shaping met a later patch job. Water was already nosing down the reopened ditch, brown and impatient. Kem climbed into the break, boots sliding in powder-dry clay, and put both hands against the collapsed face.

"Don't chase pretty lines," Vel said. "Pin it. Give it somewhere to bear."

"Right."

He spent. The Shaping dragged through him in pulses that made the tendons in his neck stand out. Earth compacted under his push with a deep grinding sound. Old stone shifted. One section slumped anyway. Kem swore, reset his feet, and shoved harder. The cloth on his forearm darkened where the wound had opened again. Water hit the rebuilt wall in a hard brown sheet, wavered, then bent where he'd forced it to go.

The woman went into the ditch without taking off her shoes. Water climbed over her ankles and around her calves. She slapped the new wall once, testing it like a live thing. Then she covered her mouth.

The repair ate fifteen. When the wall held, he was at 70.

Vel could have stopped it there. Instead she said, "Next."

A girl led her mother forward by the wrist. The older woman's eyes were milk-clouded, the pupils almost gone under the white. "She hasn't seen my face clean in four years," the girl said. "She knows me by the scarf and by my voice."

Kem looked at Vel again. "Ocular?"

"Fine work," Vel said. "Easy to make worse."

The older woman gave a dry little laugh. "Worse is I miss the piss pot half the time and sew my own thumb into the hem. If he ruins it, I'll hate him accurately."

Dren snorted behind them. Even Kem smiled, brief and startled.

"I've never done it," Kem said.

"Then don't pretend you know it," Vel said. "Take the film. Leave the rest."

He knelt in front of the woman and set a light touch at her temples. This spend came thin and bright, not the broad warmth of fever-healing but something narrow as wire being threaded through wet tissue. Kem flinched with her when she sucked air through her teeth. The white cloud loosened across one pupil, then the other, milky strands burning away to gray.

When he let go he had a line of blood under one nostril. Vel did the subtraction before the red reached his lip: six more, leaving him at 64.

The woman blinked hard and looked straight at the girl. "Blue," she said, voice gone rough. "Your head rag's blue. I forgot it was blue."
