The girl laughed once like she hadn't meant to and caught her mother by both shoulders.

"It's blurry," Kem said. "I can't get deeper than that today."

"Blurry's still a face," the woman said. "That's plenty."

After that the line kept moving. Dren handled it without being asked. "You, later. You, now. If it isn't falling or rotting today, it waits." He said it flat and nobody argued with him. When a man with a broken finger came forward splinted in sticks, Dren held it steady while Kem spent three levels knitting the joint back into place. When the leaning wall had to be braced, Dren shouldered half a beam with his good arm and hissed through his teeth when the bad one knocked his ribs. A child's infected tooth took two levels, and she cried more from the warm wrongness of the ability than from pain. A man who'd spent four years limping on a hip healed crooked took four more, and Dren gave Kem a shoulder to brace against when the man nearly fell in surprise trying his full weight. By full dark another fourteen levels were gone. Kem was at 50.

Vel didn't spend on a sweep. She climbed the rise above the channel and took the terrain the slow way with her eyes and the habits that had kept her alive this long: road bend, washout, three low roofs west, dead cedar stand, south track splitting around a salt-white ridge.

Dren had been quiet all day. He'd watched Kem heal the child and said nothing. He'd watched the wall come down and said nothing. When Kem was rebuilding the irrigation channel, Dren had walked into the ditch and started carrying rubble out of the way with his good arm, the bad one tucked against his body. He didn't ask. He just worked.

Now he sat on a stone at the edge of the repaired channel, watching the water run. His face had the look of a man who'd started holding his daughter next to the people around him and couldn't stop. He was at 130. The people around him were at 4, at 8, at 15. A month ago 130 would have felt like wealth. Here it looked like distance, the space between his daughter and the people in this settlement, measured by whether anyone with levels was willing to spend them.

***

They found Fallow an hour before dark, at the edge of the furthest settlement.

Fallow's structure was smaller than the others and better maintained. The roof patch sat square. The swept floor visible through the open door had been brushed right up to the threshold. A wooden chair with one leg shorter than the others had a folded cloth wedged under it, and the cloth had been refolded so many times the middle had worn thin. Outside, a garden held three rows of ash-beans in the chalky soil. Each plant was staked with a twig and tied with strips of rag, the rows dead straight. Half the plants were failing. All the ties were fresh.

Fallow was sitting in the chair when they arrived. Vel didn't need a Perception ability to read the four in them. Fallow turned their head by degrees. Their posture didn't shift. Nothing on their body moved without a reason.

Fallow was about sixty, though the body read older. Field sun had cut deep lines into the skin. The knuckles were all tendon and callus, locked into the curve of tools held so long the shape had stuck. Fallow wore a shirt mended in seven visible places, each repair in a different-colored thread: brown, white, blue, red, green, yellow, brown again.

"Sit," Fallow said. There was one chair and they didn't gesture at it. "Ground or wall. Chair's mine."

They sat. Dren leaned in the doorframe.

"Dren said you know the routes south," Vel said.

"I know the routes everywhere around here. I've walked most of them at one point or another." Fallow's eyes moved from Vel to Kem and stayed there long enough to count the boots, the pack straps, the bleeding sleeve. "You're the one who broke Danner's wall."

"Danner?"

"The enforcer. She's been running that compound six years. Longest operation in this district." Fallow said it without judgment. The wall was a fact. Its absence was another. "She'll file a reconstruction request with the district office. Takes three weeks to process. Four more for a Shaper to come down. Your people get maybe seven weeks before it's back up."

Kem had spent twenty levels and bought seven weeks. Vel let the number sit where it landed.

"What were you before this?" Kem asked. He was leaning forward, the way he did when he was genuinely trying to understand something and not performing interest.

"They sent me down here while the Ledger still needed three digits to write me down," Fallow said. "They called it a Development Opportunity. Thick paper. Red seal. Thirty years of development. Very developed. Now I'm four."

Kem sat with that. His arms hung loose between his knees.

"My mother grows the same thing," Dren said. He was looking at the garden. "In her yard back home. Those're ash-beans, right? Alkaline soil?"

Fallow glanced at him. For a second some older alertness came back into their face. "They don't produce much."

"Hers either. She says they're stubborn enough to be worth the effort."

"Yeah. They're worth it." Fallow turned back to Vel. "You want the south routes."

Vel had the route already, inked in Maren's script. What she needed was everything that had happened there since. "I need the south route," she said. "And I need to know whether zero still takes people there."

"Three days south. The land drops into a basin, big, all rock and wind, nothing growing. Old mining territory. Hoarded ran Shaping crews down there sixty, seventy years back. Stripped it and left." Fallow rubbed a thumb along one knuckle. "And yes. It still takes them."

Kem looked from Fallow to Vel. "Takes them how?"

Fallow was quiet for a moment. Not for effect. Just spending the breath carefully. "Not often. Long gaps, then it happens fast. I watched two go."

"Gone," Kem said.

"If I knew where, I'd charge for the map. Sera was first. I was hauling stone on the rim and saw her pick her way down. She stopped once, bracing on her thigh, breathing like every step cost her. I looked away to shift the load. When I looked back the rock was empty."

"And the second?"

"Hoster was the other. Eight years ago, maybe. Windy enough to crack your lips. He reached the floor, stood there swaying, and a white slit showed up in the air ahead of him. Thin as a knife cut. Then it was only stone again."

It had been eight years. Vel kept her face still and felt the old flash come back behind her eyes.

"Nobody talks about it," Fallow said. "What's the point? People at zero disappear. It's one more thing that happens to people nobody protects. Hoarded don't come down here to investigate. Margins people don't have the levels to care about mysteries." They looked out at the garden. "Sera and Hoster are just names now."

Vel reached into her pack and pulled out Maren's route journal. The leather had gone soft from years of handling, the corners dark with sweat and dirt. She opened to the southern pages.

Maren had mapped this stretch in detail: water sources, drop-offs, settlement names that might already be gone. On one page she had drawn the basin: an oval, hash marks for the descent, a small x on the floor. Beside it, in careful script: *here*.

"Route journal?" Fallow asked.

"Yeah."

"That handwriting's not yours."

"No."

Fallow watched Vel handle the pages. They didn't push. They turned to Kem instead. "You're the one at what, fifty now? After today?"

"Fifty," Kem said.

"And you're still heading south with your sleeve bleeding through."

"Yeah."

Fallow's mouth moved a little. Not quite a smile. "Most people around here don't walk themselves toward nothing. Nothing comes and gets them."

Vel closed the journal. The leather settled against the folded notices from the board when she put it back in her pack.

The basin was three days south. She could feel the distance in her body, in the dry pull under her ribs, in the narrowing room between what she needed and what was left.

***

They left Fallow's settlement in the failing light. Vel wanted hours. Three days to the Scour, and every hour mattered now that the route she'd built in her head no longer fit the body beside her. They walked in the formation they'd developed over weeks: Vel reading the terrain ahead, Kem carrying the heavier pack because his body could take it, Dren at the rear watching behind.

The road was empty. A footbridge over a dry creek bed was about to give, so Vel reinforced it with a Structural ability. The bridge took one of hers. The wood stiffened under their weight. She was at nineteen.

They walked in silence for a while. The pale soil cooled under their feet as the light faded. Somewhere to the west, a settlement's cooking fires put a thin haze against the sky.

Dren caught up and walked beside Vel. This was unusual. He kept rear guard and didn't seek company unless he had logistics to report.

"My agreement with you was to the edge of the Margins," he said. "We're past that."

"Yeah."

"Payment was for guide work. Routes, getting you through the Spent Towns and pointed south. That's done."

"Yeah, it's done."

He was quiet for another fifty paces. Vel could hear his breathing, steady, a walker's breath, with a hitch on the left side where his bad arm moved against his ribs.

"I'm not leaving yet," he said.

Vel looked at him. His face was set in the dying light, committed the way a man's face gets after he's turned the same choice over too many times already.

"I'm not asking for more payment," he said. "I'm just — I'm staying on."

"Okay."

"Don't ask me why."

"I won't."

They walked. Behind them Kem was a dark shape against the road. Fifty levels sat in Vel like a cracked tooth.

A minute later he came up beside her. The cloth on his forearm had dried stiff. "Fifty's bad for whatever you've got in your head, isn't it."

"Yeah."

"You don't have to make it look polite."

"I wasn't."

He walked three more paces before speaking again. "If something ahead needs spending, I'm not asking permission first."

"All right."

He glanced at her, not satisfied. "No. I mean it. You tell me what the road costs. Distance. Water. What I burn if I use something. You don't do the deciding in your head and hand me the clean version after."

That landed close enough to bone that Vel felt it in her teeth. "Fine," she said. "I give you the number. You spend or you don't."

"Good."

"Don't mistake that for trust."

"I don't."

He dropped back again, as if that settled what could be settled.

Half a mile later the south track split around the salt-white ridge Vel had marked from above. The broader branch dipped right. The left kept to higher ground and matched Maren's notes only if an old marker stone, now broken off at the base, had once stood where the wash cut through.

Wrong branch here meant a bad dawn: lost water, an ankle in loose shale, hours gone finding the climb back. Vel crouched, touched the chalk, and spent one on a tight Perception sweep. The ability slid ahead in a narrow strip and came back with the answer she needed: hollow roadbed to the right, firmer line to the left if they cut early. That left her at eighteen.

"How much?" Kem said from behind her.

"One. Wrong branch costs half a day."

He nodded once. That was all.

The notices in her pack rasped against the journal buckle every time she moved. Once the corner of the Productivity Tier notice caught in the leather and tugged at her shoulder until she jerked the strap straight.

She had not told him to stop. She had done worse. She had started giving him the numbers.

Three days south the basin waited. Maren had marked it. Fallow had named two others, Sera and Hoster. When the flash came back now, it brought white stone, hard wind, and the dark wet line creeping through Kem's sleeve.

She walked south, and every mile pressed harder.


# Chapter 13

The road failed under a farmer before noon.

For two days Kem had felt the hollowness in it through his boots. The top layer looked ordinary enough from a distance, packed dirt, wheel ruts, a skin of pale dust, but underneath it the old Shaping had gone soft and ratchety. Every hundred yards the ground gave a little different under his weight, as if the whole route had been assembled out of promises and forgotten the minute the workers were sent on. The Margins were full of that kind of construction. The Hoarded paid for a thing once, named it permanent, then let weather and need chew through it until permanence looked exactly like neglect.

Twenty yards ahead, a farmer in a sweat-dark shirt stepped onto a place that had already gone gray with strain. The surface gave a long dirt sigh and dropped. One side of the lane folded into the drainage channel underneath, and the man sprawled on a slab that kept seesawing under him. His herb pack slid toward the opening. A mule behind him jerked its head up and brayed once, ears flat.

Kem stopped with one arm already half lifted.

He was at forty-three. Seven levels gone in two days, not in one heroic burst, just in the way a road ate you if you let it. Light in a culvert when the roof over it had come down and somebody inside started screaming. A clean bind around a stranger's wrist after a blade slip in a field kitchen. Warmth against basin wind when a night dropped too hard for an old couple sleeping under a cart. Two levels to ease the worst of the pull in Dren's bad arm when the scar seized and his grip stopped working right. Water. Fire. One cracked axle braced long enough for a family to get their grain off it. None of it had been catastrophic. None of it had been part of the route budget either.

Vel hadn't said a word about any of it.

That silence tracked him harder than her old corrections ever had. She had spent the whole journey telling him where he was wasting himself and what kind of fooling around would get him dead by the basin. Now every time he spent on somebody in front of them, she watched once, measured it, and went back to walking. The absence of protest had begun to feel like its own arithmetic.

Dren stopped dead beside him, left arm held a little away from his body the way he carried it when the old wound had started burning. The road kept tearing outward. Dirt sheeted down into the channel in little slides. The farmer reached for the pack because it was worth something, and the grab shifted his weight and took another foot of lane with it.

"Don't move," Vel said.

She was already looking at the breaks in the surface, not the man, counting load and angle the way she counted everything. Kem knew the look. If they hauled him wrong, the rest of the shelf would go with him. If they waited too long, it would go anyway.

The charm came to him before he'd finished choosing it. Stabilization. Expensive for what it did. Stupidly expensive, given where he was.

He threw it.

Twelve levels left him in a burst that hit his sternum and flashed white behind his eyes. The world narrowed to pressure and the taste of old metal. The collapse locked in place mid-failure: broken road frozen at wrong angles over the exposed channel, dirt and old Shaping held together by force and nothing else. The mule screamed and yanked backward on its lead until the farmer let go of it.

"Go," Vel said, and Dren went.

He dropped his own pack, crossed the tilt in eight careful steps, hauled the farmer upright by the belt and arm, then went back for the herb pack while the man made the sound of someone trying not to be sick in public. Kem stayed where he was because if he stood too fast the burst in his chest would turn into a full stagger. The Ledger did its subtraction in the background with obscene calm.

Thirty-one.

The number did not feel abstract. At forty-three he had still been able to sense depth behind him, enough room in the list that a bad decision could be a bad decision and not an amputation. At thirty-one the list felt close enough to count by touch against a wall. A Barrier he had been assuming was there had gone. So had the speed ability he had used twice in the Margins without thinking much about it. Other things were still present, but nearer now, as if all of them had taken a step toward the front of him.

Vel crossed last, weight spread, ready to catch her balance if the road tried something while the charm held. She caught the mule's lead before it bolted, wrapped it once around her wrist, and shoved it at the farmer hard enough to mean take your own damned animal. The man stared at Kem as if he had dropped from the sky by mistake.

"How long?" he asked.

Vel looked at the frozen break, then at the channel, then at the old seams in the far edge. "Not long enough to trust it. Get off the lane and tell the next idiot to do the same."

The farmer nodded too many times. "I can pay a little. Herbs. Dried root. My wife uses them for fever."

"Keep them," Dren said.

Kem tried standing. The first attempt put a black fringe at the edge of his sight. The second worked. His skin had gone cold. The body always knew first.

The farmer was still watching him. There was gratitude in it, yes, but there was something else too, the stunned inventory people made when they saw a spend that large at close range. How much had that cost. What kind of person could afford to do it. What kind of person did it anyway.

Kem looked past him to Vel.

"Thirty-one," he said.

She met his eyes once, sharp and unreadable. "Then you know."

The farmer glanced between them, sensing the thing he had stepped into but not understanding it. Dren had already bent for his pack. Nobody said anything else on the road. They walked south along the field edge until the lane gave way to a scatter of broken stone and dead thistle, then cut toward an abandoned farmstead crouched behind a stand of salt-scrub.

Half a mile on, the place proved real enough to lean against. Four walls, a sagging roof, one room still mostly dry, and a dead well in the courtyard with its crank arm gone. A labor notice had been pasted to the outer wall so long ago the paper had gone leathery with sun. RENEWABLE EXPENDITURE UNIT, the top line read in fading Hoarded block script. Below that, smaller print had blistered away except for PROJECTED ANNUAL OUTPUT and COMPLIANCE REVIEW WINDOW.

Dren found a clay jug in the corner that still held water under a layer of grit. He poured some into a cup, sniffed it, tasted it, waited, then drank the rest in three slow swallows. Across from Kem, Vel sat with Maren's route journal open on her knees, thumb pinning one page flat against the breeze sneaking through the broken shutters. The handwriting on the open spread wasn't hers.

Kem set his pack down and did not sit right away.

Thirty-one stayed with him as a hum in bone and teeth. It was not pain exactly. Pain was easier because it announced itself. This was the body's running count of what it could no longer afford to pretend.

"I want to know what you meant."

Vel did not look up. "What thirty-one feels like."

"Not that."

She closed the journal and kept it pinned under her thumb anyway.

Kem sat because if he stayed standing his anger was going to break loose before he had words to put on it. The wall behind him still held heat from the day. He could feel individual abilities now when he checked for them, not in names but in absences and weights: healing still there, light still there, a few combat uses, not much room left for stupidity. He said, "You told me zero was a threshold. Fine. You didn't tell me how it opens."

"No."

"Tell me now."

For a second it looked like she'd give him one of the smaller adjacent answers she used when she wanted a conversation to end without ending. He could see her deciding which part to trim away. Then she looked at his face, found whatever was there, and said, "It takes a cascade. Spending near the site. One person can hit zero and get through, but the opening barely holds. Two people spending together, it holds longer. More descent means more force."

The room seemed to get hotter on the word together. Kem felt the number he had just become sit between them like a fourth body.

"How much more."

"Two hundred. Two-fifty. Three hundred." Vel's voice stayed level. "The bigger the drop, the stronger the crossing."

There it was. Not the shape of a hope. The shape of a requirement.

Kem's first stupid thought was not betrayal. It was arithmetic. He had left the Vault at three hundred. Two hundred levels of descent. More, now. Enough to make a door for someone else. The thought hit so cleanly he hated himself for how long it took the feeling to catch up.

"And you knew that when you found me."

"I knew a solo crossing wasn't enough."

His pulse kicked once, hard enough that he felt it in his throat. She was narrowing the question again, cleanly enough that on any other day he might have let it pass just to avoid the work of fighting through it.

"That's not the question."

Dren's pouring slowed to almost nothing. Water ticked against the clay cup in slow, separate sounds.

Vel looked at the far wall. "No."

The single syllable left a cold space inside Kem, not because he hadn't expected it, but because some part of him had apparently still been waiting for the denial anyway. He had wanted her to lie. The want embarrassed him so quickly it almost covered the hurt.

"You needed someone high enough to burn down beside you."

"Yes."

Kem went still on the floorboards.

Something Thessa had said in the settlement came back, not the whole speech, just the hard center of it: years ago, Vel asking after defectors still high enough to matter. Kem had pushed it aside because there had always been the next emergency, the next cost, the next mile. On the road everything was urgent enough to postpone any question that threatened the structure holding you upright. Now there was nothing between him and this one except a hot room and a woman who had just confirmed the ugliest part of it.

"How long were you looking?"

"Several years."

His stomach turned over once. Several years. Not a bad choice made in desperation when she met him. Not opportunity. Search.

"How many people."

"Two serious leads. One had already spent too far down. The other wasn't willing."

"So I was the first one who was high enough and foolish enough."

"You were the first one who wanted zero."

It did not help. If anything it made it filthier. Wanting something did not make a trap less of a trap. It just meant he had walked into it under his own power.

The first weeks of the journey shifted in his head, not cleanly, just enough to make him sick. Vel watching him in the forest, correcting his fire, teaching him how not to waste himself. The campfire talk. The restraint lessons. Her face at the labor camp while he burned through stranger after stranger because he couldn't stop. Every memory now had another set of numbers under it. Had she been angry because he was being reckless, or because each reckless act was eating the door she needed him to become. Had there ever been a way to separate one from the other. The answer might have been yes in some scenes and no in others, and that was somehow worse than simple fraud.

He looked at the closed journal under her thumb.

"When were you going to tell me."

Vel was silent long enough that the answer started forming without her. At the site. Once turning back was impossible. Once he had spent too much for refusal to mean anything except dying somewhere less useful.

When she spoke, she only proved the thought right. "Later."

Kem laughed once. The sound scraped on the way out. "That's not a time."

"At the basin. When it had to be dealt with."

The room tilted for a second, not with dizziness this time. With recognition. Every withheld answer, every redirect, every practical correction given exactly when he needed it and not a breath sooner. He had mistaken that control for experience. He had called it competence because he liked having someone in front of him who seemed to know where to put her feet.

"After I'd burned half my life getting there."

Vel did not defend the sequence. She didn't have to. The silence did it for her.

Dren set the cup down. "I'm going outside."

Neither of them looked at him. His boots crossed the courtyard and stopped just beyond the doorway, close enough to hear if something broke, far enough to deny it later.

Kem braced himself against the floorboards because every part of him wanted to do something stupid. The wood was hot and gritty. Thirty-one hummed in him like a trapped insect.

"That's the — you know what the word is." His voice had gone unsteady without him noticing. "Component. That's what I was."

Vel's jaw tightened once. She did not say no.

"I was keeping you alive."

"For what."

She didn't move.

He stood because staying seated had begun to feel like accepting the room's terms. Through the doorway the dead fields flashed white in the heat. The old labor notice on the wall had curled at the edges, but the ink still held where the euphemisms mattered. COMPLIANCE REVIEW WINDOW. RECOVERY YIELD. The same patient administrative obscenity as the camp, bureaucratic enough to hide the body inside it.

His voice came out too polite. He heard his father in it and hated that too.

Kem pointed at the notice. "That's what they do. Here. They look at a body and ask what it can still yield. You found me and started there."

Vel opened her mouth, stopped, then said, "At the start, yes."

The answer landed so cleanly it emptied him out for a second. He had wanted her to narrow the question again, or lie, or do anything that would leave him room to keep fighting the same part of it. Instead she gave him the ugliest piece whole.

"And after."

"I kept you alive."

"For the basin."

"For the road too." She swallowed. "For both. I don't have a cleaner answer than that."

He could feel his mouth trying to shape a laugh and refusing. Cleaner. As if the problem was wording.

"Every time I spent," he said. "The camp. The couple under the cart. The farmer today."

Vel's eyes shut once, briefly. "I counted what was left. I also wanted you alive at the end of the day. Both were true every time."

That hit harder than the first admission. Not because it was worse. Because it was mixed. He wanted a neat fraud. He got the road as it had actually been, every kindness and calculation braided together so tightly he could not pull one free without bringing the rest with it. Vel taking his wrist at the river to show him balance. Vel awake through his fever. Vel watching his remaining levels the same way she watched rotten bridgework.

"And the lessons," he said. "The restraint. All that talk about waste."

"Some of it was budget." Her voice had gone flatter, as if she was forcing each word over something sharp. "Some of it was me watching you throw years away in front of me and not being able to stand it. I can't split those now."

His shoulders tightened so hard he felt the pull down his spine. He had not known until then that he had been saving a childish little piece of the journey from this. Not the practical alliance. Not the first days. The middle of it. The part where he had stopped feeling like cargo and started feeling like company.

He stepped toward her before he had decided to. Not far. One pace. Enough to make the air between them feel chosen.

"Did you ever look at me and forget it."

Vel's eyes went to his face and stayed there. This time the silence was different. Not evasive. Not tactical. Just costly.

Kem understood the answer before she said it, and the understanding came with an immediate second thought that was almost as bad: there had probably been moments when she had forgotten, and those moments did not save either of them.

"Sometimes," she said.

The word should not have hurt more than yes. It did. Sometimes meant she had needed an effort to remember or an effort to forget, and in either case the arithmetic had always still been in the room.

He wanted to leave then. The impulse came whole and immediate: north, away, let her solve her own crossing, let the basin take her plan and choke on it. Thirty-one levels said otherwise. North meant broken road, no journal, no map, Sable somewhere behind them, the Vault at the far end of every retreat whether he ever reached it or not. South meant Vel at eighteen, Dren with one bad arm, and the only route any of them had. Leaving was a plan for people who knew where they were going.

He looked down at the signet instead.

The signet still rode there, silver-dark, the closed-circle crest worn smooth by weeks of sweat and dust. House Aldraste. Preservation by standing still while everyone else paid. Even once he stripped it free, the thing knew how to keep claiming him.

He pulled it off.

Pale flesh showed underneath, soft where the metal had sealed itself against weather and grit. The ring sat against his skin with ugly familiarity. For a second he pictured throwing it over the wall into the scrub and hearing nothing when it landed.

Vel watched him do it and said nothing.

That silence answered in its own way. She did not tell him to keep it. She did not tell him to throw it. She did not pretend the object wasn't part of the room with them.

He curled around it and put it in his pocket.

Dren came back in before either of them spoke again. He took one look at Kem's face, another at the pale strip where the ring had been, and decided correctly not to ask. "South road's passable if we cut the fields for a stretch. Another collapse ahead. Dry channel under it."

"We move now," Vel said.

No one argued with the order. That was its own fresh insult.

Kem shouldered his pack first. The rough seam of a new callus caught on the strap, enough to sting, and then the weight settled where it needed to. Ten feet out into the lane he stopped just long enough for the others to fall in, then kept going.

Dren took the middle without comment. Vel stayed behind him. No one argued the formation either.

They left the farmstead and cut south through fields where grain had gone thin and brittle on the stalk. Once there had been irrigation here. Kem could see the dead channels in the land, straight cuts that had collapsed inward where the lining broke. Survey stakes leaned at odd angles out of the ground, each one branded with a Hoarded lot number and a year old enough to have become mockery. The sun pushed at the back of his neck. Flies worried the corners of his eyes where sweat collected. Thirty-one stayed present in him the whole time, not alarming now, just impossible to forget.

Vel gave directions without looking at him. "Fence line. Then left at the salt stand."

Kem followed them because he wanted south more than he wanted dignity, which was humiliating in a new way.

An hour later they heard shouting ahead and came over a low rise to find six people hauling grain sacks out of a leaning storage shed. The ground under the east wall had opened into a split no wider than a ditch but deep enough that darkness held at the bottom even in full sun. Every time a sack thumped across the packed earth, dust sifted from the rafters. A crudely painted board above the wide door read HARVEST HOLDING ANNEX 6B. Under that, in stenciled Hoarded print too neat for the building around it, was AUTHORIZED SHRINKAGE TOLERANCE: 14%.

The place looked as if someone had told the joke years ago and left the punchline standing in weather ever since.

One of the women saw them first. "You three walking or useful?"

Dren muttered, "Useful, apparently," and turned toward the shed before Kem had answered.

Vel had already scanned the crack, the sag in the roofline, the stacks inside visible through the doorway. She pointed once at the west side. "No one goes under the east beam again. Drag from the back stack only. If that wall starts talking, drop and run."

The woman nodded as if orders from strangers were an ordinary sort of weather out here. Maybe they were. Kem stood one second longer than everyone else, long enough to feel how instantly his body wanted to obey Vel when the subject was not trust but collapse.

The want made him angry enough to move.

Inside the shed it smelled of dust, old grain, mouse piss, and the deep mineral cold that came off ground already deciding to fail. A boy no older than fourteen was trying to drag a sack twice his width by brute pull. Kem took the other end without speaking. Together they got it to the door where Dren shouldered it away one-armed, swearing softly at what that did to his scar.

"How many left?" Vel asked.

"Nine," the woman outside said.

Vel glanced once at the east beam. "Make it six."

"Six gets us three days."

"Then have three days and your bones. Move."

The next minutes ran on body logic. Lift, drag, pass it back, go again. Kem's anger did not leave. It simply had to share space with work. The floor under his boots had a give in it he hated. Every time he crossed near the split wall, the bracing charm rose to the front of his mind on pure reflex. He could do it. He could spend and lock the corner long enough for them to clear the whole shed. He could feel the mechanism already half assembled behind his eyes.

He did not.

Thirty-one. Six levels in the wrong place and chapter by chapter the door at the basin got smaller. That calculation had been Vel's weapon against him. Now it was his too, and hating that did not make it less true.

"Left side," Vel said from the doorway.

He adjusted without answering.

The boy slipped on spilled grain. The sack went sideways. Kem caught it against himself and the rough weave burned across the seam of the new callus at the base of his right thumb. He felt skin split. A hot line opened there under the dust. The pain was stupidly useful. It kept him in the body he actually had instead of the one that could solve everything by tearing more years off itself.

At six sacks the east wall made a low internal pop. Everybody heard it.

"Out," Vel said.

The boy lunged for another sack anyway. Kem grabbed the back of his shirt and hauled him hard enough that the cloth tore at the collar. They hit the yard together just as a ribbon of dirt spilled down the inside of the wall and the beam dropped an inch.

Nobody argued after that.

They stood in the sun, breathing dust. The woman who had first shouted at them braced on her thighs and stared into the doorway with the face of someone doing ruin math in real time. Three days of grain. Six sacks gone. No more safe way to get the rest without a spend too big for any of them to volunteer and still keep living the week.

"Shit," said the boy.

"Yep," said Dren.

The woman straightened and wiped at her mouth with the back of her wrist. "Water's yours if you want it. Out of the cistern, not sweet. Better than the road."

"We'll take it," Dren said.

Vel looked at the crack again, then at the foundation stones on the west corner. "Don't sleep in the house nearest this. The ground's going to keep going tonight."

"You from inspection?" the woman asked.

The question hit Kem like a slap for reasons he could not have fully explained. Maybe it was the word. Maybe it was the fact that Vel, even dusted white and down to eighteen, still looked more like authority than help.

"No," Vel said.

"Good." The woman jerked her chin at the stencil over the door. "Last inspection wrote up the shrinkage tolerance and went home."

No one in the yard laughed. The line did not need help.

They drank from the cistern under a lean-to roof of patched tin. The water was warm and mineral-thick and had a dead beetle floating in the second bucket. Kem drank anyway. The cut kept leaking where the sack had skinned him. He wrapped it once in a strip torn from an old feed cloth. Vel noticed the bandage and looked away before the gesture could become an offer.

That hurt more than if she had tried to help.

The woman told Dren the next intact shelter south was an abandoned weigh station two miles on, stone base, roof mostly sound, pump dead but no one came through enough to contest it. "If you hear ground move in the dark, don't wait to see which direction," she said. "Just run."

"Comforting," Dren said.

"It's free."

They left before the shed came down fully. Kem could hear the family behind them arguing over whether to risk another trip inside. He did not turn around. He knew what it would do to him if he saw them choose the grain anyway.

The fields gave way to waste ground by degrees. Salt brush replaced crop rows. Broken channel walls surfaced and vanished again. Once they passed a toppled marker stone listing permitted maintenance intervals in language so bloodless it might have been funny anywhere less ruined. Vel kept the route. Dren kept the middle. Kem kept his distance and his own head full.

Anger was easier while they were moving. Motion gave it something to lean on. The minute they stopped to climb a fence or edge along a dry wash, other things crowded in behind it. Vel's face in the farmhouse when he said component. The accuracy of her silence. The memory of the first week on the road, when he had thought her impatience with him was the impatience of someone keeping a fool alive. It had been that. It had also been inventory management. The two truths kept refusing to separate.

By late afternoon the weigh station appeared where the road widened around an old stone platform and a rusted scale big enough for wagons. The building attached to it had once been whitewashed. Now the lime had gone patchy and the wood shutters hung crooked on leather-dry hinges. A board over the platform read ROUTE COMPLIANCE POINT 4. Another sign underneath, newer by only a few years, instructed haulers to declare undepleted labor assets before entering basin territory.

Dren stared at that one for a second. "That's a sentence."

"Unfortunately," Kem said.

It was the first thing he had said to either of them in an hour that was not route or warning. The sound of his own voice came out strange, dry from disuse.

Inside, the main room held a counter, a broken bench, and three shelves full of ledgers so warped by damp that their pages had fused. A scale ledger still lay open under a stone paperweight. The last readable line listed grain tonnage, mule count, and a separate column marked HUMAN SUPPORT HOURS with a neat check beside VERIFIED. Somebody had left halfway through writing the next date and never come back.

The place smelled of mouse nests and old paper. It would do.

Dren went through the room with a trader's eye and took the least rotten corner for his bedroll. Vel checked doors, window slits, roofline, then set her pack against the wall nearest the entrance. Kem chose the far side by instinct, as far from her as the room allowed without making a statement so obvious even Dren would have to comment on it.

No one commented.

Dren found a strip of iron to brace the main door and said, "If either of you start another honesty session tonight, do it outside."

Vel's mouth moved as if she might answer. Kem got there first. "I hadn't scheduled one."

"Good," Dren said. "Because I'd like six hours where the only collapse risk is geological."

He built a miserly little fire in the old cook pit out back, using fence splinters and a piece of broken crate. They ate cold grain mash from his stores with dried onion shredded into it and water that tasted faintly of metal from the station's catch barrel. It was not a meal anyone would remember on purpose. That made it useful. Ordinary business still needed doing even when the day had just broken in half.

The cut stung every time he flexed. Dren noticed and tossed him a clean scrap of cloth. "Wrap it right. Dust'll make it ugly."

"I know how to wrap it."

"Then do it."

Kem did. Vel sat two paces away on the station platform, knees up, route journal open in the last light. She read with the concentration she gave everything practical, one thumb following a line down the margin. Now and then the page shifted and he caught the second script in the book, the one that wasn't hers. He looked away before the curiosity could turn into a question. He was too full already.

When darkness finally flattened the fields, Dren took first watch without asking, mostly because his wound made him slowest to settle and fastest to wake anyway. Kem lay on his bedroll with the signet heavy in his pocket and the Ledger light enough now that sleep should have come easier. It didn't.

The station timbers clicked as they cooled. Somewhere out in the fields ground moved with a slow granular hush, not enough to call danger, enough to remind everyone what the land liked to do after dark. Dren coughed once, spat into the yard, and settled again. Vel turned a page in the journal.

Kem closed his eyes. Opened them. Closed them again.

Every time he drifted, some memory of the road came up altered. Vel teaching him how to build a fire low so it would not advertise them at night. Vel standing over him after the fever, not touching him, just staying awake because the body might turn bad too fast if nobody watched. Vel on the labor road, face white with pain in the cracked ribs, still snapping instructions while he flung levels around like a man trying to buy absolution at retail price. It would have been easier if the memories had gone false cleanly. Instead they kept arriving as proof of two incompatible things that had both happened.

He rolled onto one elbow. Dren had his hat over his eyes now, which did not mean he was asleep. Vel was still outside on the platform, journal closed, both arms braced behind her. She was looking south into the dark as if it could be counted.

Kem sat up.

Dren moved the hat a fraction. "Your watch?"

"Need to piss."

"Take a knife."

Kem picked one up from beside the cook pit because refusing would start a conversation. He walked past the scale platform, past the last of the station stones, and kept going even after he had reached a patch of scrub that would have served the stated purpose just fine.

The night air had cooled by a few degrees, enough to make sweat dry tacky on his shirt. Ahead, the road was only a lighter strip through darker ground. He could still turn north from here. The thought came without drama. Just a direction laid over another direction.

He kept walking.

At twenty paces the station fire was a pinprick. At fifty it disappeared behind the rise. The signet was in his fist without him remembering taking it from his pocket. Metal warmed fast there. He imagined following the road back through the farmstead, back past the broken lane, back through the Spent Towns, back into every version of himself he had already left. The image would not stay coherent. Too much had happened in between. There was no clean reverse route. There was only weathering.

The road dipped, and he stopped at the lip of another failed section. Not a full collapse, just a long split where the shoulder had peeled away into darkness. In daylight it would have been a nuisance. At night it was a warning against melodrama. One wrong foot and all the furious clarity in his head would reduce to a snapped leg in a ditch.

He stood there, breathing through his nose, and understood with exhausting precision that leaving now would not be refusal. It would be stupidity. He did not have the map. He did not know the water points. Sable was still somewhere behind them with whatever remained of the pursuit. The north did not mean innocence anymore. It meant choosing different people to use him, or dying on principle in a drainage channel while the world went on being itself.

Thirty-one hummed under his sternum. Not enough to feel invincible. Enough to feel every bad option.

He looked down at the signet in his grip.

In the dark it had no family resemblance at all. Just weight. A circle of metal cut for a finger that no longer matched the body carrying it. He thought of throwing it into the split and losing it to the same ground that had taken so much else. He even drew his arm back a little. But the gesture stalled before release, not because he had forgiven anything about where it came from, and not because he still wanted to belong to it. He kept it because it was evidence. Because the road south kept insisting that his origin mattered even when he hated the fact. Because one day, if he made it to zero or didn't, he wanted something solid to hate without having to reconstruct it from memory.

He put it back in his pocket and turned around.

The station reappeared gradually, a darker block against the dark, then a small amber line where the coals still lived. Dren was awake when Kem came back. He did not lift his head from the rolled blanket under it.

"Any trouble?" Dren asked.

"Road's split fifty paces north."

"Everything's split."

Kem stood there another second. Dren opened one eye.

"If you're going," Dren said, "go in daylight. At least then you'd know which mistake killed you."

There was no accusation in it. No comfort either. Just the sort of practical statement Dren made when emotion threatened to turn into inefficiency.

"I'm not going," Kem said.

Dren's eye closed again. "Then sleep."

Kem looked toward the platform. Vel had not moved while he was gone, or if she had, she was back in the same posture now, elbows on knees, shoulders folded in. Maybe she had watched him leave. Maybe she hadn't. Maybe she had decided that stopping him would be another kind of manipulation she had no right to attempt. He could not tell from this distance, and tonight he distrusted every guess he made about her.

He took second watch anyway, mostly because sleep still felt impossible. Dren went inside without argument. Vel stayed where she was until the moonless dark had fully settled, then came to stand by the platform steps.

"There's a wash east of here," she said. "If the ground talks loud, don't run that way."

Logistics. Nothing else.

"Fine."

She nodded once and started back inside.

The fact that she was still giving him useful instructions made anger surge up so quickly he almost called her back just to start it again. Instead he let her go and sat on the edge of the stone platform with the knife across his knees.

Night watch emptied the head out in strange intervals. For a while all he could think about was the specific pressure of the scale platform under him, the roughness of stone through his trousers, the ache in the cut, the reduced but constant hum of the Ledger. Then thoughts came back in loose clusters. His father in the Conservatory, preserved into absence. The labor notice on the farm wall. The family at the grain shed counting days of food against risk of burial. Vel at eighteen, still moving south with cracked ribs and no room left for error. Himself at thirty-one, furious enough to leave and too altered to do it cleanly.

At some point a dry cough came from inside the station. Vel's. Then another, choked off hard in the middle as if she had trapped it against her own ribs. He could picture the pain of that too well after watching her with the cracked side. Instinct rose before judgment, the old ready motion of getting up, checking, asking if she needed anything.

He stayed where he was.

The staying did not feel like victory. It felt like setting one hurt down on top of another and calling the stack discipline.

When dawn finally started paling the eastern edge of the fields, it came colorless at first, then yellow and merciless. Dren emerged rubbing his face and muttering about his arm. Vel came out last, journal packed, expression blank with the kind of tiredness she wore as function. Nobody mentioned the night walk. Nobody mentioned the cough. Nobody mentioned anything from the farmhouse because there was no version of the conversation that would not either reopen it or fake its closure.

They packed in near silence.

Kem slid the signet into the side pocket of his bag instead of his coat. It belonged off his body for now. He checked the wrap, tightened it, and shouldered the pack. The rough edge of the cloth under the strap pressed into the split skin and reminded him of the grain sacks, the boy's torn collar, the six sacks salvaged instead of nine, the kind of choices that had become normal so gradually he could no longer remember what normal was before them.

They left the weigh station as the sun got its full heat on the land. Dren walked between them again, bad arm close to his ribs. Vel kept the route and Kem kept the silence. The road narrowed, then gave up and became two tracks through broken ground. Somewhere ahead the basin waited. Somewhere behind them the farmer's road had probably finished collapsing. The day already smelled of dust and hot stone.

Kem kept walking.


# Chapter 14

"Path forks," Vel said. "South-southeast. Water first."

The lower road dropped into a settlement built around a crack that had not finished deciding what it wanted. Kem saw the seam first: a split running north-south through the middle, three houses on the east lip leaning toward it, the west side clustered around a well and a retaining wall of quarried blocks that looked older than the trust placed in them. One roof had already gone in. Somebody had stacked the salvage beside it in neat unhappy rows.

Dren squinted at the well. "Good."

That was all. Not good ground. Not good rest. Water. His bad forearm was wrapped tight against his chest and he kept looking at the bucket as if it might crawl off before he reached it.

Vel headed straight for the well. Dren veered with her. Kem followed because anywhere else would have read as performance.

At the curb, a woman with a shaved scalp and flour on her forearms kept winding the bucket rope as if newcomers were no more surprising than weather. Dren reached for the handle.

"You pull, you fill it for the next person too," she said.

"Fine."

"And if your people are planning to quarrel, do it west of the crack. East side's touchy today."

Dren looked at Vel. Vel looked at the bucket. Kem, against his own judgment, said, "We're not planning to quarrel."

"Then you're ahead of most people." Her attention caught on his voice, the careful old structure still clinging to it.

Dren snorted once. It cost him.

He started hauling one-armed, the bad forearm braced under the rope. The pulley shrieked. Water came up slow and black and cold enough that Kem's mouth hurt before it touched him. Vel drank first, quick and efficient. Dren drank next. Kem washed grit from his lips, then kept scrubbing at his wrists after the useful part was done.

That was how they traveled now. Vel gave directions. Dren handled the world if it needed handling. Kem answered when silence would have drawn more attention than speech. Everything Vel had taught him still lived in his body after trust went bad, which was almost funny if he let himself think about it that way. Thirty-one hummed at the back of his skull, the number she had carried when they met, his now. He could not decide whether that made the whole thing intimate or obscene.

The flour-armed woman jerked her chin toward the west rise. "If you're staying till dark, camp up there. East lip's moving."

"South road?" Vel asked.

"Depends what you mean by road." The woman let the rope run and caught it again. "Track south out of here still exists. Ground under it's got opinions. Quarry cut on the west side's ugly but holds. Mostly."

"Mostly," Dren repeated.

"You want certainty, try the Vault."

Kem let that one land and kept his face out of it.

A quarryman came up carrying a brace of salvaged planks across one shoulder. Stone grit lived in the seams of his neck. "If you're camping, west side only. East side's for people who already know which houses are lying."

"Three bedrolls," Dren said. "One fire."

The quarryman looked from Dren to Vel to Kem and paused in the open space between the last two. "One fire's ambitious."

No one answered.

He shifted the planks. "Your problem. Keep it uphill from the grain and don't put your packs where kids can find them."

That should have been the end of it. It almost was. Then somewhere east a voice started calling for help, not loud and not wild, just steady with the rhythm of someone who had been saying the same words for long enough to wear the panic down to function.

Both Vel and Kem turned toward it at once.

The two-story house at the eastern lip had gone past leaning and into decision. Its upper floor slanted toward the crack. A man in a stone-colored shirt kept circling it without getting close enough to matter. A child was crying somewhere inside in the flat repetitive way children cried when the part of them that expected adults to fix things had started to fail.

Vel set down her pack. Kem did the same. Dren took three steps after them, looked at his arm, swore, and changed direction toward the crowd instead.

"I'll keep them back," he said.

The stone-shirted man was saying, to anyone and no one, "East wall shifted. My sister's in there. Two kids. Floor's gone soft."

No discussion. Vel went to the west side and laid her palm on the stone. The stillness of her body changed in a way Kem knew too well, attention narrowing until she seemed built around it. He could not feel the spend leave her. What he saw was the intake of breath afterward, small and annoyed, like a tax she had expected and still resented.

Dren called from behind the crowd, "How much?"

"One," Vel said without looking up.

There it was. Seventeen left, then. Kem hated that part of himself was relieved to hear her say it out loud.

"Northwest corner's taking weight," she said. The old operational register had survived the fracture intact. "Southeast is gone. Beam runs north to south and it's cracked midway. Reinforce that. Clear the east door. Woman inside, maybe two children."

The familiarity of it hit before the resentment could. "How much beam?"

"Two meters. Both sides."

The brother started for the door. Dren caught him by the back of the shirt with his good hand and hauled him short.

"No," Dren said.

"That's my sister."

"And that's a house trying to eat anybody sentimental enough to go first. Wait."

The man strained once against him, then sagged. Dren let go only enough to keep him from falling.

Stone grated under Kem's boots as he crossed to the east wall. The doorway was choked with a dropped lintel and loose blocks. Through a slit between them he could make out dark interior, suspended powder, a shape low to the floor.

"We're coming in," he said.

At two hundred, Reinforcement had been easy enough to mistake for entitlement. At thirty-one it fought him. He pressed both hands to the wall, found the cracked timber by feel and vibration, and spent.

Two levels. The timber did not heal. It clenched. The house answered with a lower groan, still failing, but slower. Twenty-nine.

"Holding," he said.

"Door," Vel said.

Lifting the fallen stone cost two more. The blocks came up badly, scraping and bumping and catching on one another like they resented being moved. A burst of lime and grit came through the opening and coated his tongue. Twenty-seven.

Behind him somebody in the crowd said, not quietly enough, "You hear that? They don't even have to look at each other."

Another voice answered, "Maybe they did too much of that already."

Kem would have laughed if he had been less busy.

He ducked inside. One room, half divided by a storage partition that had already collapsed on one side. Breath rasped somewhere beyond it. The air was thick with old plaster and fresh stone powder, enough to chew.

"Light," Vel called from outside.

She had moved to the northwest corner and was bracing the wall with both hands, human weight added to whatever calculation she had made. Her jaw was locked. A tremor ran through her forearms that had not been there at the well.

He spent a level for light. Weak white glow pooled across the room. A year ago he could have lit a hall. The smallness of this hurt more than the cost. Twenty-six.

The woman was against the far wall with a child on each side. Pale tracks ran through the grime on her face. "Can you walk?"

"Floor dropped," she said. "I can't get them over it."

A gap split the boards between her and the door. One joist hung broken and twisted into the dark below.

Down on his knees, Kem put one hand on the intact board and one on the broken joist. Another groan rolled through the house. Powder sifted from above onto his shoulders and hair.

"Ceiling's moving," Vel said. "Minutes."

One more level. The joist locked hard enough to take weight. The hanging boards settled. Twenty-five.

"Come now," he said. "Take the small one first. Keep hold of the other."

The mother obeyed without thanking him. Good. There was no room in the building for gratitude. She lifted the younger child to her hip, caught the older girl's hand, and crossed with the careful, flat-footed pace of someone walking a river stone in spring runoff. The boards held. Kem kept his palm against them until all three were through. By the time he stumbled back into daylight, a section of the second-floor wall had smashed into the place they had just left.

Sun hit him full in the face. Across the yard Vel let go of the corner and stepped back breathing hard, grit in her hair, eyes on the house instead of on him. Six feet of air stood between them. A crowd rushed through it.

The brother reached his sister first. Somebody shoved a cup of water at her. Somebody else started hauling loose stone away from the base of the wall because the next problem had already arrived.

Then the younger child planted both hands on her mother's face and started checking. Cheeks. Mouth. Ears. Hair. Then back to cheeks again, pressing skin as if she could keep the woman in place by touch alone. The mother shut her eyes and let her do it.

Kem had just enough breath left to think sit down before the quarryman with the planks stepped into his path.

"Good," the man said. "Now come look at the west wall."

Kem stared at him.

"We just dragged three people out of your house."

"That was her house," the quarryman said, jerking his chin toward the sister. "The wall's mine, and if it goes the well follows. I said thank you in my head."

"He did not," the flour-armed woman called.

The quarryman ignored her. His gaze moved from Vel to Kem, took in the distance and the way neither crossed it. "You traveling together?"

Silence did the answering.

"Right," the quarryman said. "Whatever you've got going on, keep having it for another hour. The wall doesn't care."

That should not have been funny. It was. Kem felt the laugh hit his ribs and fail there.

The west retaining wall ran above the well and below six houses built on optimism and reused stone. Grain sacks, crockery, two bedsteads, and half a cooper's shop had already been dragged back from the lip. Chickens worked calmly through the debris as if collapse were just another season.

Vel crouched at the lowest course and went still. When she rose, some of the color had gone out of her face.

"One more," she said. "Sixteen now."

There. Exact. Public. No guesswork.

The rescued brother looked lost enough to resent arithmetic. "Can you hold it?"

"Till dawn if you stop making it work so hard." Vel pointed along the wall. "Strip the top course by the third house. Move the grain farther west. Nobody on the upper path after sunset. If anything starts shedding, wake me before you wake your priest."

"We don't have a priest," the flour-armed woman said.

"Good," Vel said. "One less delay."

That should have ended their part in it. It didn't. Orders started moving and the settlement made room for them inside the work before any of them had agreed to stay. Kem got put on stone. Dren got rope. Vel got every question with weight in it. Refusing would have taken more energy than lifting.

Kem lasted three loads before his shoulders started to shake. The cut reopened under the fourth block. Dren worked the bad arm like he hated it into obedience. Across the yard, the younger child kept climbing onto the cot to check her mother's face every time the woman coughed, as if features might come loose between breaths.

Twenty-five rang thin behind Kem's eyes. Sweat dried cold under the grit on his back. Stone had found its way into one boot. Every bruise from the last hour started speaking at once.

For one breath every hurt place in him pulled the same direction: preservation. Sit still. Stay whole. Never crawl into another house that wanted down. Never learn the exact price of split skin, a lung, a level. The Conservatory rose in him in scraps: cool stone, untouched air, filtered quiet, his father's face emptied out into calm.

That calm had looked false to him there. Standing on a wall that could still take the well and the grain and half a night's sleep with it, it looked almost merciful.

Then the smaller child flattened both palms to her mother's cheeks again, counting what was still there by touch. Kem bent and picked up the next block.

By dusk the top course was down where Vel wanted it, the grain was stacked farther west, and the upper path had been cleared. The quarryman walked the line, nodded once, and declared that if anyone died overnight it would at least happen on ground that had been looked at properly.

"That's almost poetry from him," the flour-armed woman said.

"Shut up, Sena."

So she had a name. It did not make her any softer.

Sena came over with a strip of clean cloth and held it out to Kem. "Your cut."

"I have bandages."

"Bad ones, from the look of it."

He glanced toward Vel before he could stop himself. She had been talking to the brother in low clipped sentences, probably about where not to sleep. Her head turned a fraction, old reflex catching the movement. For half a second the corrective look came over her face, the one that wanted to clean the cut, wrap it properly, and tell him where he had been stupid. Then she let it die.

Sena saw that too. Of course she did. "All right," she said. "You're not married. You're just exhausting."

Kem took the cloth.

"Thank you."

"If you were married I wouldn't have thanked you either," she said, and went to shout at a boy who had climbed where Vel had forbidden climbing.

By the time the light finished going, the settlement had sorted the three of them into a category of its own: useful, inconvenient, and worth watching.

---

By full dark they were on the west rise above the crack because there was nowhere sensible else to sleep. The fire was small because the wood was poor and because nobody in the settlement believed in burning more than a pot required. Dren laid his bedroll between theirs without comment. Nobody argued with the geometry.

Sena brought up a pot of barley and greens thickened with something that might once have met meat. "Eat this before the wall changes its mind."

Dren took the bowl first. "You talk kindly."

"I save my manners for funerals."

She handed bowls to Vel and Kem without bothering to ask whether they wanted them. A moment later she came back with a heel of bread and looked from one bedroll to the next.

"If you're all leaving at dawn," she said, "take the west cut. East path's done until we pull two sheds down. If you're not leaving at dawn, tell me before first light so I don't waste water on travelers."

"We'll tell you," Vel said.

Sena's eyes shifted to Kem, waiting for a contradiction. None came.

"Fine." She started away, then stopped. "And if either of you plans to run north in the dark, do it quietly. The dogs bark at everything and we're tired."

Dren choked on his stew. Vel kept her face flat. Kem looked down into the bowl until the heat in his ears passed for campfire.

That, apparently, was the settlement's official opinion on their private business.

Dren sat against his pack after eating and cleaned his knife one-handed. When he flexed afterward, two fingers lagged before closing.

He worked them open and shut until they answered a little better.

"Still useful," he said. "Mostly."

Below the rise, the settlement moved in smaller noises now. Pot lids. A baby wailing once and being settled. Somebody hammering after dark because panic did not honor sleep. From farther east came the occasional small clatter of loosened stone.

A child drifted in from the lower fires and started investigating Kem's pack with the fearless entitlement of the half-feral. Eight, maybe. Bare feet tough enough for rubble. Thin wrists. She turned over his spare belt, his cloth roll, his waterskin, and finally found the signet where it had slipped loose near the bandages.

"What's this?"

Silver-dark metal flashed warm in her grip. In a settlement like this it looked less noble than ridiculous, a little polished piece of some other species' habits.

"House signet," Kem said.

"What's that mean?"

He opened his mouth on the old explanation and hated it before it formed. "It means," he said, starting again, "that I was raised somewhere doors opened before I touched them."

The girl looked at the ring, then at him, then at the settlement spread below them in patched roofs and unstable stone. "That's lazy."

"Yeah," he said. "It was."

She slid the ring onto her thumb, watched it sink to the knuckle, and held it up to the fire. "Silver, though."

From below, Sena's voice carried without the rest of her. "Only if you melt the house off it."

"Can I melt the house off it?" the girl asked.

"No," Kem said.

"Then why are you carrying it?"

That was the question. Not what it meant, not where it came from, not whether he had been stupid enough to believe in it. Why he still had it after all the chances to throw it into water, scrub, roadside pits, a collapsing settlement's bottomless crack.

He did not have a good answer, which was how he knew any true answer would sound bad.

"I left wearing it," he said at last. "Then I kept leaving."

The girl considered that with more seriousness than the sentence had earned. "That's not much of a reason."

"No."

"Can I keep it anyway?"

"Also no."

She made a face, dropped the ring into his lap, and reached instead for the buckle on his pack.

"No," Kem said again.

"You don't keep anything fun."

"There is nothing fun in that pack."

"Then why keep it?"

Dren laughed openly at that. Even Vel's mouth shifted, not into a smile exactly, but away from total refusal.

The girl caught the movement. "See? She thinks I'm right."

Vel said, "Eat something and go bother people who aren't armed."

"He's armed."

"Badly," Dren said.

The girl looked at Kem's knife, which he had set beside the bedroll, then at the bandaged cut and road-raw face above it. "Yeah," she said, and wandered off toward the larger fire when some more interesting argument broke out there about who had stolen whose spoon.

Warmth lingered in the silver-dark metal when he picked the ring up. Grit sat in the worn grooves of the crest. The thing had been made for polished rooms, careful storage, servants who knew where to place the tray. Here it looked rich and stupid and small enough to lose.

He put it away. Not deep in the pack, not back on his body. Between.

Sena came up one last time with a waterskin and a kind of administrative exhaustion that reminded Kem, unwillingly, of the Vault. Not in manners. In certainty.

"We've got room in the shed if the three of you decide you're sick of proving something with bedroll distance," she said.

"We're fine," Vel said.

Sena looked at the exact spacing of the three bedrolls. "Sure."

She handed Vel the skin. "If the east side starts shedding, I want warning before the dogs make it everyone's problem."

"You'll get it."

"And if you wake the wall with whatever this is" - she jerked her chin between Vel and Kem - "I'm charging you for the panic."

When she was gone Dren said, "I've been on roads with worse innkeepers."

"No, you haven't," Vel said.

"Fair."

The silence after that did not feel peaceful, just occupied.

Across the fire, Vel had Maren's route journal open to tomorrow's stretch. She had added to it since the wall: a small correction beside the next distance mark, a note where the east path had failed, a darker angle drawn toward the quarry cut. The journal kept absorbing disaster like it had room for infinite bad roads.

Dren squinted over the rim of his bowl. "What're you at now?"

Vel did not look up from the page. "Sixteen."

That answered more than the number. She had heard him earlier, or guessed the question, or simply decided the settlement did not get to be the only witness to her arithmetic.

Kem watched her follow tomorrow's route down the page. She paused once halfway through the distance column, where certainty seemed to thin out, then kept going.

North existed, not grandly and not as some pure escape, just as a road back out of all this. The quarryman had offered solid ground and maybe work in the morning. Twenty-five levels spent sparingly could buy Kem years if years were all he wanted. He could turn around, disappear into some Spent town, learn to mend roofs or unload carts, and keep himself alive a long time.

But the joist under his wrist came back first, then Dren hauling grain one-handed because he had already attached a future to a girl waiting north, then Vel saying one and then one more and then sixteen because he had stopped letting numbers stay implied, then the child on the cot below checking her mother's face every time the woman coughed.

He got up and crossed to the fire.

"What changes after the quarry cut?"

Vel looked at him for the first time since the wall. She understood the question and also that it was not the one he had almost asked.

"Bad footing till noon," she said. "Better after the ridge. Less cover. More distance between water."

"How much more?"

"Enough that we fill everything here."

"And if the ridge is gone too?"

"Then we cut west and lose half a day."

He nodded once. "What are the other surprises?"

Her face shut for a second on the word. Then reopened, not to the truth he might have wanted, but to the smaller thing he had actually asked. "Loose shale in the cut. Old blasting pits. One washed bridge footing that isn't a bridge anymore. That's what I know."

It was not apology. It was not confession. It was usable.

He pointed at the journal. "How far before that stops being route and starts being guesswork?"

After a beat Vel touched the page. "Two, maybe three days south. Depending on the ground."

He looked at the mark, then at her. "Then you tell me when it does."

Her face shut for a second. "All right."

"And the sixteens and seventeens."

"What about them?"

"I don't want to guess anymore." He heard how flat he sounded and kept going anyway. "If it changes and it matters to me, you say it."

Dren stopped pretending not to listen.

Vel kept the place in the journal. Firelight tightened the tendons at her wrist. "All right," she said.

It was so plain it almost missed him.

"And the road," he said. "If we're moving tomorrow, I want the road before we're on it."

"All right."

Nothing repaired in the saying. The damage was still there, whole and waiting. But the next day's terms had shape now. Not trust. Terms.

He reached toward the journal without having planned to. Vel looked at him. For a moment he thought she would refuse, not out of secrecy but out of habit so old it would take more than one night to break. Instead she rose, came around the fire, and set the book down on the ground between their bedrolls.

"Don't lose it," she said.

"I wasn't planning to."

"People plan badly."

It was the longest thing she had said to him since the house. It was also, in its bent and meager form, almost ordinary.

She went back to her side of the fire and lay down with her boots still on.

Dren watched the journal where it lay between them. "That's cozy," he said.

"Go to sleep," Vel said.

"Trying."

Below them, a dog barked once and was answered by another. Somebody coughed behind one of the west-side houses. The wall held. The crack kept its own counsel in the dark.

Kem sat with the journal a long minute before he opened it. The mileage held clean for two, maybe three days. After that the margins thickened with contingencies, quarry cuts, washed crossings, routes that had once been lines and were now negotiations.

He closed the book and kept it beside his pack.

The pack stayed buckled because unbuckling it would have meant claiming something either way. Staying. Leaving. Morning. He was not ready to call any of those done.

But he left the journal where he could reach it, and when the east side gave one small settling clatter in the dark, all three of them woke at once.


# Chapter 15

The Margins smelled like exposure: hot stone, alkali, and old water dragged too far underground. Six weeks earlier Sable would have filed the place under dirt and moved on. Now the heat lay on her scalp, along her collar, and in the raw place where the Vault's formal neckline had given way, three weeks earlier, to an unlaced opening she no longer noticed.

She kept track of the two hundred and thirty-eight the way she used to keep track of her schedule: automatically, while she was already doing something else. The schedule had never shifted. The number did, and every drop kept carrying the same contradiction. She had less of what she had been and more of whatever she was becoming.

The settlement appeared where the former supply road bent around a dry wash. Eight structures stood around a collapsed well. Half the roofs had been patched with sheet metal that did not match, and the Hoarded road ended in front of the place as if whoever approved the extension had concluded that eight structures were all the settlement deserved.

Sable would have kept walking if the man outside the largest house had not been sorting grain one kernel at a time, pausing in the wrong places.

The motion caught at old memory. She had seen its cousin in Council chambers, in the administrative wing, in Lord Aldraste reaching for a glass and arriving there late. The man moved three kernels, stopped, moved two more, and then held the third above the table as if the rest of the instruction had not yet reached him.

Sable crossed to the low stone wall opposite him and sat.

"You were Hoarded," she said.

His eyes found her a beat after the sound of her voice. Once they did, the person behind them was still sharp.

"Was," he said. "Fifteen years ago."

"What level did you leave at?"

"Three hundred." He put down the grain. "I know what you are looking at."

His name was Rennic. He had spent forty years in the House of Accounts. He had never written policy, but he had prepared the papers that let other people pretend policy had assembled itself around their decisions. His Ledger had held at three hundred for fifty-two years. It held there until the day he walked out.

"I left because of this," Rennic said, lifting his left hand. The tremor was fine enough that a Council physician would have called it negligible and a working person would have called it trouble. "One morning I reached for a pen and nothing happened. Half a minute, maybe. Then the arm remembered itself. My supervisor said I was experiencing a focus-discipline lapse."

The last three words came slower than the rest, as if they had to cross thicker ground to reach his mouth.

"I believed him for two months," Rennic said. "Then it happened in a briefing. The room waited, the pen sat there, and my arm simply declined the assignment." His mouth tightened. "Listen to me. I still sound like I am filing it."

Something drew tight in Sable's chest. She knew that maneuver. Make the terror procedural and perhaps it would stay sortable.

"What did they do?"

"At first they praised my restraint. Then they reassigned me to retrospective account review, because that required fewer live signatures. The memo called it a distinguished-service accommodation."

There was a template for it. Subject displays intermittent executive delay; recommend accommodation commensurate with service record. Sable had approved three versions in the last decade and corrected the punctuation in one.

"How long had it been happening before you left?" she asked.

"Long enough that I had started buying time by speaking slower on purpose." He set down one kernel, then another, then had to decide again where the third belonged. "People said it would clear outside. Spend a little. Wake the dead parts up. I spent plenty. I am at a hundred and twelve. Fifteen years of using what I have got, and it still catches." He pressed both palms to the table hard enough to steady them. "Whatever preservation does, it gets finished before anybody admits it started."

The houses around them bore the same logic. The failed well had been reinforced with the cheapest masonry anyone thought they could defend in a ledger review. The oldest roof brace had split and been sistered with raw timber instead of replaced. Somebody had patched a cracked jar with pitch because the jar mattered more than neatness. In the Vault damaged things were stored. Here damaged things were needed again tomorrow.

Rennic tipped his chin toward the road behind her. "You are not local."

"No."

"You are also not on your first week outside anymore."

Sable looked at the thickened callus at the base of her thumb. There had been no paperwork for calluses. The Council preferred transformation when it could still be categorized as incidental wear.

"No," she said again.

Rennic returned to the grain. "Three travelers came through last night. Healer. Young Hoarded man trying not to look like one. Trader with a bad arm and the face of somebody doing sums while the sums keep worsening."

Sable stilled. "They stayed here."

"Until dawn." His mouth bent in a tired approximation of amusement. "The boy carried water because he did not know what else to do while the adults were deciding things. The trader did not sleep much. The healer looked at every wall like she expected it to confess something."

"Which way did they go?"

"South to the ridge farmhouse. Last roof before the land gives up all pretense of cover." He nodded toward the distance. "They left at first light. You reached us after noon, so they had half a day on you before the heat flattened everyone."

That answered the first question. It made the second unavoidable.

"How far from the farmhouse to the Scour?"

Rennic's fingers stopped above the grain. "That depends on whether you want to arrive or survive. The straight southern line reaches the basin in two hard days for one body carrying light and not minding that half the world can see it. Three bodies with one bad arm would be stupid to try it. The eastern cuts are slower. Old irrigation channels, relay posts, half-dead pump houses. They buy cover if the cover still stands."

Sable sorted it the way she sorted everything now, half by record and half by the newer instinct the road had taught her. Alone she could make the basin in two hard days. Vel would not take open ground with Dren's arm and Kem's still-conspicuous body unless she had run out of alternatives.

Rennic looked at her more directly. "You are here for the Aldraste boy."

"I was."

His attention flickered and returned. "You have spent. I can see that much. You move like somebody who has had to use her own weight."

Six weeks ago she had expected doors to open when she reached them and food to arrive because it was time. Now she checked the angle of the ground before she sat. Now she judged water by sound in the skin. Now her knees had opinions she could not overrule.

"Yes," she said.

Rennic lifted the cracked cup beside him and hesitated over the distance. Sable took it from him before the pause had time to harden into embarrassment. The water tasted of stone and old metal.
