"They were not hiding particularly well," Rennic said while she drank. "The healer asked three route questions and pretended she was only making conversation. The trader asked about water. The boy asked nothing and listened too hard every time somebody mentioned the road south."

"Did they say where they planned to sleep?"

"At the ridge farmhouse if the roof was still there. The boy asked whether the eastern wall had really fallen in, and I told him yes, but not decisively enough to be useless." Rennic watched her over the rim of his cup. "If you mean to catch them before they choose a route, you will. If you mean to drag him home, you will have a harder day than you think."

Councilor Erenn's pen stopping halfway through a signature. Lord Aldraste's delayed smile at the last reception. The quiet administrative wing where senior officials were transferred when their work became inconsistent. She had filed those memos herself. She had typed reduced capacity due to distinguished service and never once read it as diagnosis. She had read it as kindness, because kindness was easier to administer when it had a heading.

"Thank you for the water," she said.

"That is not an answer."

No answer came. Her pack went over one shoulder, field-made now, assembled from Margins canvas and cord after she abandoned the Vault equipment none of them knew how to live with. Four days earlier she had cut her walking staff from a dead tree and skinned the bark with a knife bought for three marks and two levels spent on a Warming Charm for a family whose fire had died in the night. The authorization field was empty. She had spent it anyway.

Rennic had gone back to sorting by the time she left. The governing rhythm of the place was patience with damaged machinery inside living flesh.

Fresh tracks held through the wash and up the ridge. Halfway to the farmhouse the road broke into two credible lies: the direct southern line widening into a white scar across open ground, the eastern route dropping into old irrigation cuts where walls and dead reed beds still offered partial concealment. Sable stopped long enough to look at both. Rennic had been right. Alone she could take the straight line. Vel would not, unless necessity overruled judgment.

The farmhouse stood on the ridge between those choices, three walls and a bowed roof holding out against the usual collapse. Sable kept walking.

###

From fifty meters out she counted what the structure could still do. Enough shade for people who understood the odds and stayed anyway. Enough wall to fool the eye into calling it shelter.

Inside, the first thing she registered was separation. Two packs sat against the north wall, not touching. An open field journal lay on the floor between them with a cracked spoon across one page, as if nobody wanted to admit who had left it there. A third pack rested nearer the fire. Lentils thickened over low coals. A man she did not know sat on an upturned crate near the gap where the eastern wall had been, his left arm half unwrapped for fresh bandaging and his good shoulder angled toward his pack as if he had already decided what left with him if the roof came down.

He looked at her and did not reach for a weapon. "She is here," he called toward the back room.

Vel Ashwick came through first carrying a strip of boiled cloth. Sable had studied the file for weeks: healer, unauthorized user, forty years in the field, wanted in three jurisdictions. The paper had listed violations, approximate levels, likely associates, and once, with dry irritation, noted repeated difficulty maintaining custody due to subject competence. None of it had caught the way Vel stood as if every stillness in her had already been costed.

Kem Aldraste followed her. The Council portrait tried to rise in Sable's memory and she pushed it down. The man in front of her was leaner than the painting, shirt collar gone soft from repeated washing, one trouser knee whitened by road dust, and carrying himself with the wary readiness of someone who had learned, late, that rooms did not arrange themselves around him.

"Auditor Orm," Kem said. The title came out first from habit. The rest of him arrived a moment later. "I'm not going back."

"I know."

Plaster let go somewhere overhead and dropped to the floor in a soft clatter. Nobody bothered looking up.

Sable found the old entry point by instinct. "The current situation has altered since my last -"

Vel's face changed very little. It changed enough. "If you say current situation in this room, say it from outside."

Dren made a brief sound through his nose that had to stand in for laughter because full laughter would have shaken the arm.

Sable let the sentence die. She stood in a broken farmhouse with three people who had every reason to assume she was about to ruin the day. The language that had carried her through twenty-seven years of meetings felt suddenly like equipment made for some other climate.

"I'm not here to bring him back," she said.

Vel stayed in the doorway between rooms. "Why are you here, then?"

Several responses presented themselves and none survived inspection. Sable could have explained that the retrieval order no longer matched the facts on the ground, that her understanding of institutional duty had altered beyond usefulness, that she had already lied to the Council in a way she could not rescind. Every version sounded like a report trying to apologize for itself.

"I can give you an explanation," she said. "It may come out in the wrong order."

"That sounds likely," Dren said.

Sable looked down at the dirt floor. "May I sit?"

Vel glanced once at the ground, as if checking whether permission was really the missing condition. "All right."

The floor met Sable's knees the clumsy way it always did. Six weeks earlier she would have treated that as humiliation. Now it was logistics. Kem crouched across from her with the automatic movement of somebody who no longer expected furniture. Dren resumed winding the fresh cloth around his arm until Vel crossed to take over the knot without comment. The journal stayed open between the north-wall packs. Nobody moved it.

Kem said, "How many have you spent?"

"Sixty-two."

He absorbed that in visible calculation. "How does it feel?"

The answer that rose first belonged to the office. "The first expenditure produced more proprioceptive feedback than I had been prepared for. After that the recalibration period was -"

Dren shut his eyes briefly. Vel did not move.

Sable heard herself and stopped. "It feels like being in my body," she said. "Ridiculous thing to discover at forty-seven, but that is the nearest version."

"It isn't ridiculous," Kem said.

Silence made the room plainer. Vel and Kem were close enough to share a fire and far enough to keep the space partitioned anyway. Dren occupied the practical middle between them, which was either temporary or disastrous.

Kem looked at her steadily. "You knew my father."

"I worked with Lord Aldraste for twenty years."

"Have you seen what is happening to him?"

"Kem," Vel said.

He did not look at her. "I'm asking."

There was a phrase the Council used. Reduced continuity of attention. Progressive executive delay. Preservation-associated procedural slowdown. Sable could have recited any of them.

"There is a phrase the Council uses," she said.

Kem's face hardened. "I don't want the phrase."

"No," Sable said. "Neither do I." She held his gaze. "Yes. I have seen it. The Stillness."

Kem let out a slow breath. "In the Vault they call it serenity when they are being pious. At table they just wait. He loses the middle of a sentence, and everyone sits there looking respectful until the rest of it comes back."

"At least four Council members show the same damage," Sable said. "Councilor Erenn stops halfway through his own name. Councilor Dais asks the same question twice in one meeting because the first one has gone missing by the time the answer reaches her. Your father too. You have the closer look at that than I do."

She thought of Rennic and went on before the room could shift away from it.

"I met a man in the settlement below the ridge. Rennic. House of Accounts. He left the Vault fifteen years ago at three hundred because one day his arm stopped taking instructions. He is at a hundred and twelve now and it still catches. Sorting grain takes him most of an hour because every few motions the signal disappears."

That brought Vel's attention fully onto her. "Outside too."

"Yes."

Kem turned to Vel. "You knew it didn't clear."

Vel held his gaze. "I knew people who left still had trouble. I didn't know it followed them this far down."

"And you didn't mention that."

"I thought we had enough to carry."

Dren looked at the dirt between his boots.

"And the Hoarded know," Kem said to Sable. He did not put it in the form of a question.

Sable thought of reassignment memos and quiet corridors and the administrative wing where older officials were sent when their work became inconsistent but their names were still too large to retire cleanly.

"Yes," she said.

"So the whole philosophy is rotting people from the head down," Dren said, "and everybody in the nice clothes is calling it policy."

Sable could have corrected the phrasing. She did not.

Kem asked, "If you took me back now, what would they do?"

This part had always been easy to name, so the answer came at once. "Protective observation protocols. Restricted movement. Monitored contact. A secured room with access determined by House priority and Council concern."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning you would not leave when you wanted. Meaning you would see your father when permission aligned. Meaning every unsupervised conversation would be treated as a risk."

Kem kept looking at her. "Would I be allowed to speak to him alone?"

Sable saw the truth before she assembled the words. Lord Aldraste at the head of the table. Kem on the other side of a monitored room. A clerk recording duration. Another clerk deciding whether a son's distress counted as destabilizing.

"No," she said.

Nobody moved. Vel lowered her gaze for a moment and lifted it again.

"I watched Rennic sort grain for the better part of an hour," Sable said. "If I take you back, that is what I am delivering. Cleaner walls. Better linen. The same ending."

Kem nodded once. The nod cost him something.

Vel broke the silence first. "What happened to your team?"

Sable could have answered with simple numbers. Five agents. Six weeks. Dismissed three weeks ago. False report filed. Instead she felt herself reaching for the old architecture again because confession still wanted headings.

"By the end of the second month field competence had become its own operational problem," she said.

Dren looked at the roof. "There it is."

"I am aware." Sable steadied herself. "Three agents took involuntary expenditure badly enough that ordinary road problems became emergencies. One fused himself to a boulder with a Binding ability because he had never been told that direct skin contact and panic were a poor combination. We chipped him free over two hours. He spent nine levels on the extraction and cried most of the time, largely because nine was more than he had spent in the rest of his life combined."

Dren winced in practical sympathy. Vel did not smile, but the corner of her mouth moved as if the world had offered her a small bitter confirmation.

"Another agent used reinforcement on a grain cart because the wheel had splintered and the woman pulling it was crying," Sable said. "That was not the problem. The problem was that he looked at the repaired wheel afterward as if the result had justified the cost. He had been taught his whole life that action was waste. Then the action worked."

Kem said, "So you sent them back because they were learning."

"Yes." Sable looked at the callus at the base of her thumb. "The remaining two adapted fastest. They were getting useful. Too useful. Another few weeks and I would have brought home five people who had learned that doing things solved problems better than preserving themselves through them."

Vel said, "And that was intolerable."

"Institutionally, yes."

"To you?"

The question was plain enough that Sable could not reframe it.

"I do not know when it became intolerable to me," she said. "I know I filed the report three weeks ago."

Kem lifted his head. "What report?"

"Mission complete. Target deceased by natural depletion in hostile territory." The words sounded exactly like what they were: a form turned sideways until it concealed a person. "It has been sitting in the archive since the morning I sent my people back. I have been following you alone ever since."

"Natural depletion," Dren said.

"Yes."

"That's a clean phrase for lying."

"It is the official phrase for several kinds of lying," Sable said.

No one laughed. The line had not been intended as humor.

Vel said, "Why tell us now?"

"Because there are things you need to know before you choose your road from here."

Vel's gaze stayed flat. "Start with the one that gets us killed first."

Sable set the staff down beside her knee. "The Council has now authorized the Red Reserve."

Vel's expression tightened around the eyes. Dren swore once, quietly.

"Four trained combat spenders are relevant to you," Sable went on. "Two were already attached to the permanent Scour posting. Two more were authorized when retrieval was judged a failure. If the roster I saw was current, the new pair will be in the two-fifty to two-seventy range. They are not like the team I brought."

Kem looked at Vel. "How bad?"

"The ones she brought panicked and overspent," Vel said. "These won't." She glanced at Dren's arm, then away. "Trained two-fifties know how to make you burn faster than you can afford."

"How fast do they reach the basin?" Dren asked.

Sable drew a line in the dirt and then a second line branching east. "From this farmhouse the direct southern road reaches the Scour in two hard days if one traveler goes light and accepts being visible most of the way. The eastern cuts are longer. Three people with one damaged arm using pump houses and irrigation walls for cover can turn the approach into four days."

Before she finished, Kem leaned back and reached for the open journal between the packs. The movement was quick enough to show habit and recent enough to show it had not been one a week ago. He thumbed to the back pages and held the book low between himself and Vel.

"There's a pump marked east of the line," he said. "And a relay shell here. If the roof's gone, the wall might still be worth something."

Vel looked down at the page instead of taking the journal away. "If that mark is still right."

"If it isn't," Kem said, "then we lose time and get no cover."

"Yes."

The book stayed in his grasp. Vel bent far enough to see the page, then used one knuckle to indicate the dirt line Sable had drawn. The exchange was brief, practical, and new enough that Sable could feel it changing without understanding its history. The journal moved between them more easily than speech did.

Dren watched both of them. "Four days is a lot."

"Yes," Vel said.

"The direct road isn't impossible," Kem said.

"No," Vel said. "It's open ground watched by better spenders."

"I can still spend us through open ground."

Vel's head turned toward him. "You can spend us to the basin and arrive poorer than the fight."

Kem looked back at the journal. His thumb stayed wedged between two pages as if he had not decided whether holding the place mattered tactically or otherwise.

"How much worse are the cuts?" Dren asked Sable.

"If the old irrigation walls still stand, they are slower and less visible. If they do not, then you lose time without gaining cover." Sable looked at Dren's bandaged arm. "The direct road asks the wounded to keep pace in the open."

Dren nodded once, too quickly. "Fine."

Kem heard the answer for what it was. "Fine because it is safer or fine because nobody asked what four more days costs you?"

Dren's eyes flicked to him. "Both."

Vel did not intervene. Whatever repair or rupture was coming between them was not solved by hearing the same fact in three voices.

"That is the first thing," Sable said.

Vel's voice stayed level. "You said two."

"The second is older." Sable looked at the line she had drawn toward the south and erased part of it with her thumb. "The Red Reserve has kept a permanent classified garrison at the Scour for years."

This time nobody spoke at all.

"How many?" Vel asked.

"One commander. Two stationed operatives. The new pair may join them before you do." Sable kept going because stopping would have made the sentence feel less obscene than it was. "The commander has held the post for five years at minimum. There is a dedicated budget line, a dedicated reporting cycle, and a standing renewal. I saw the line item in a restricted ledger six months ago. It was filed under erosion management."

Dren let out a dry sound. "They put soldiers on a hole in the ground and called it erosion."

"Yes."

"Five years," Vel said.

"At minimum. Retrieval access was not intelligence clearance, so I do not have the whole structure. I have enough. A standing post means supply caches. Terrain notes. Rotation policy. It means they were not reacting to rumor. They were guarding something."

Kem kept looking at the dirt between them. "They sit my father at the head of the table and call his disappearing discipline, and all that time they had soldiers on the threshold because they knew." He swallowed. "They knew what was down there."

Nobody corrected him.

Vel stood and crossed to the gap where the eastern wall had been. She looked out over the open southern line, then east toward the irrigation cuts invisible from here.

"If the commander has held that post five years," she said, "she knows the basin better than I do now."

"Yes," Sable said.

"And if the new pair catch the direct road first, they watch the obvious line and force everyone with sense into the cuts."

"Yes."

That gave Vel something solid enough to use. She turned back into the room. "Then we don't take the road they want."

Kem looked up from the journal. "You mean the cuts."

"Yes."

He held her gaze a moment longer, then looked down at the page again. "All right."

It was not reconciliation. It was a changed ask and a changed answer, small enough to miss if nobody had been watching for leverage all her life. Vel did not thank him for agreeing. Kem did not ask for more explanation than the room could bear.

Sable said, "There is more."

Vel's attention came back to her. "Say it."

"If zero is a door, then the rooms we built for men like your father were not protection. They were custody. The observation protocols. The reassignments. The speeches about restraint. The garrison disguised as erosion management." She heard the office in her own sentences and did not fight it this time. "I signed too much of that machinery not to know what it was for."

Kem looked at her very steadily.

"I am going south," she said.

"With us?" he asked.

"No. Alone."

Vel did not move from where she stood. "Why."

The question came flat, not rhetorical. That made it harder to evade.

"Because if the threshold is real, then the Hoarded order was not preserving people from waste. It was preserving a system by keeping people from an exit it could not control." Sable felt the sentence arrive in pieces and let it. "That is not a question I can walk back to the Vault without answering."

No one tried to make what she had said kinder than it was. Dren looked away first, toward the gap in the wall and whatever road he was already measuring against Mira. Kem's mouth tightened, then loosened. Vel's face gave almost nothing, but the nothing had changed.

"You're not joining us anyway," Vel said.

"No."

"Good."

There was no warmth in the answer, but there was a kind of acceptance in it. Not alliance. Not forgiveness. Only the practical acknowledgment that one more body in the eastern cuts would complicate the route and not necessarily improve the odds.

"I can take the direct line alone," Sable said. "Two hard days if the wells still hold. You should assume the road is watched and use the cuts. Four days, perhaps a little less if the pump houses have not collapsed."

Dren's jaw worked once. "Four days."

"I know."

Vel looked at Kem instead of at Sable. "Keep the book open tonight. I want every standing pump and relay wall marked before dark."

Kem's answer came after the briefest pause. "All right."

That was the first cooperative instruction she had offered him that was not an order disguised as impatience. It still did not make the room easy. It made the room usable.

"Do not wait for me," Sable said.

Kem's thank-you came late enough to cost him something. "All right. Thank you."

Vel said, "If you see the direct road moving, leave a mark where the eastern wall used to be at the next standing house. We'll check."

"I will," Sable said.

She stepped out through the missing wall into the late heat. Behind her the farmhouse kept its cracked beam, its lentils over coals, and the ugly little beginning of an agreement neither of them would have named.

South lay flat and white beyond the ridge. East dropped into the old irrigation cuts where cover cost time and wounded men cost more.

Sable set the staff against the ground and started down the southern line.

If the threshold turned out to be wind and damaged rock and nothing more, she would have walked two hard days to disprove a sentence that had already broken her loyalty. If it was real, then Rennic over his grain, Lord Aldraste at the table, the quiet wing, the reassignments, and the permanent garrison hidden in a neutral budget line all slid under one ugly heading. The Hoarded had not built procedure to save people from waste. They had built it to keep people from whatever came after the count ran out.

The road gave nothing back except glare and heat.

Sable kept walking.


# Chapter 16

Two days after Sable walked out of the farmhouse, the Scour still was not under them.

Yesterday, belly-down in a dry canal with reeds turned to wire around their faces, Vel had watched two riders take the direct road south. Not Margins enforcers. Their spacing was too neat, their horses too quiet, and neither man looked around with the loose greed of somebody hoping to extort travelers. One had stopped at an old relay frame and flashed a polished signal plate toward the basin. Ten minutes later another flash answered from farther down. After that she stopped thinking in terms of warnings and started thinking in terms of coverage.

The detour had cost them anyway. Kem spent seven levels in the stupid little bites that passed for caution when you still had levels left: one for light in a collapsed culvert, two bracing a washout while Dren crossed with his left arm pinned to his side, one forcing water through a pump lever rusted half shut, three more in scraps of strength and reinforcement when Margins infrastructure gave out under their feet. Vel spent two of her own, one on concealment when that first dust line swung off the road toward their ditch, one keeping the waystation stairs from coming down under Dren's weight after dark. By dusk Kem was at eighteen. She was at fourteen. Dren was still at one hundred and twenty, which only meant he'd managed not to do anything desperate yet.

He was packing before sunrise.

Vel heard him through the thin wall of the waystation, a bedroll tied wrong, retied, tied wrong again. A buckle knocked stone. A strap dragged over wood. A muffled curse when leather caught on a splintered doorframe. She knew what she was listening to. She had been expecting it since the farmhouse, since Sable's warning, since the ditch yesterday when Dren watched those riders and went quiet in a way that had nothing to do with road discipline.

Outside, dawn was thin and colorless. Summer heat already pressed down before the light had properly arrived. The station had been a Hoarded supply post once, square-built and joyless, with a loading platform facing a road that no longer carried anything worth logging. The stamped sign on the doorframe still read PRODUCTIVITY TRANSFER STATION 7-SOUTH, though half the paint had gone to powder. Someone had carved *fuck off* under it in neat letters. Farther south, on the stump of a relay mast that should have fallen years ago, something flashed once. Then again.

Dren came out a minute later. He carried his pack low on his right shoulder because his left arm would not take the weight for long. Twice he tried to drag the strap higher with the bad side and twice had to let it settle back.

"Morning," he said.

"Yeah."

He set the pack down by the broken foundation wall and looked toward the relay mast instead of at her. Another flash. Whoever was down there had cleaned old Hoarded signal junk back into use. That alone made the morning feel smaller.

"I'm heading back," Dren said.

Vel nodded. She had known, but the words still altered the air. "When?"

"Now. While they're still watching south." He jerked his chin toward the mast. "By full light yesterday they were pushing east off the road. I'm not walking into that with a third pack on my back."

"You don't owe us an explanation."

"I know." He sat on the wall. The stone was already warm. "I'd rather say it than keep hearing myself think it all the way north."

Vel leaned against the doorway and waited. Kem was stirring inside, floorboards creaking under a body that had gotten leaner over six weeks of bad road. Kem at eighteen. Vel at fourteen. Two numbers that would have been a punchline in the Spent Towns, right up to the part where everybody died.

"My daughter's sick," Dren said. "You know that. What you don't know is what it means when the healer in Roth burns forty of her own levels on a child's legs and then hands me the price in marks like marks are bone and I can just buy some more. The money you put up last time covered half. Maybe half. I still left home short." He worried a callus at the base of his thumb. "I've got one hundred and twenty left and a girl who's eight and doesn't care about thresholds or the Scour or any of your clever road math. Some mornings her legs won't take her when she swings out of bed. She hits the floor and looks at me like I was meant to keep that from happening."

"I know."

"You know the count." He looked at her then. "I've been picking her up off the kitchen floor since spring."

Vel did not argue. He was right. She knew Dren's life as arithmetic, exactly and usefully, and had never once been in the room when his girl hit the floor.

Wind crossed the old fields carrying dust, pump grease, and the sour after-smell of Fertility work done too often and too long. Three hundred meters south the direct road ran between plots that had once been farmland, rows still visible as ghost lines in exhausted earth.

"I watched the kid spend ninety-eight levels on strangers," Dren said. "People he'd never met. I watched you count every one and say nothing because if you said stop then you had to say why." His gaze stayed on hers. "Yesterday we lay in a ditch because two men on decent horses know this ground better than we do. Maybe that's the garrison. Maybe it's the pair Sable said might be coming. Doesn't matter. It means the road you sold me got smaller while mine didn't."

"That's fair."

"It's not enough." He scratched at his jaw. Sun had bleached his beard in patches over the last weeks. "If I keep going south, I'm not backing a job anymore. I'm backing a woman I never met and a hole in the ground. Mira doesn't get to lose for that."

Vel looked at the station sign instead of Dren's face.

"I've still got a healer in Roth waiting on the second half of a bill," he said. "That's the road I should be on. Mira's my zero. Not this."

Grief tightened under her ribs, not the old break there, but the other thing behind it.

"You've been good company," she said. The sentence felt thin as paper.

"I've been paid company." His mouth twitched once. "Some of it was paid. The last bit wasn't." He shifted on the wall, worked the left side once, let it fail and hang. "I don't think you're wrong about the Scour. I think you're short on the number of things that can go wrong before you get there."

"Probably."

"Yeah." He picked at a loose thread on his pack strap. "And the kid still doesn't know why he's walking south. I can see that from here. I'd rather not be around when he works it out."

Kem came out blinking into the raw light. His face was thinner than at the start, the softness of Vault life stripped off by walking and heat. Since the fracture he had gotten quieter. Not Hoarded-quiet. More like somebody who had finally realized words cost breath and breath was work.

"Dren."

"Yeah." Dren looked at him. "Heading home."

Kem took it in. He took in the pack, the arm, the way Dren had timed the leaving to the flashes down south. "Now?"

"Now's when north makes sense." Dren nodded toward the relay mast. "I wait an hour and whoever's working that road starts wondering what came out of this wreck."

Kem glanced south, then back at the bad arm. "Your grip."

Dren flexed it once. "Still there."

"I could look at it. I mean properly. Not..." Kem made a small useless gesture and dropped it again. "Not the way I did before."

"Don't," Dren said it gently. "You've got eighteen and a bad plan. Spend them on the bad plan."

"My arm isn't your problem," Dren said. "Mira is. Mine too. That's why I'm going north with what I've got left."

Kem looked as if he might argue anyway. Then he didn't. "All right."

"There's a first." Dren stood, hitched the pack higher with his good shoulder, and winced when the movement pulled across the scar. "For what it's worth, you spend different now."

Kem's mouth tightened. "I would hope so."

"In the Spent Towns you threw levels around like a drunk lord at a festival. Now you stop and count before you do something stupid." Dren looked between him and Vel. "Keep that. Both of you."

Kem put out his hand. Dren looked at it, then took it. One firm shake. No performance in it.

"Tell Mira..." Kem started, then stopped.

"Yeah," Dren said.

He turned to Vel last. Another flash answered from farther south, quick and flat in the growing light.

"Don't let them pin you to the road," he said. "That's all."

Then he went north through the broken yard, cutting away from the direct line before a watcher could put three bodies together with one ruined station. His boots made a dry scraping sound over old loading gravel. Vel watched him until the ground folded and took him. He never looked back.

After that the waystation felt wrong. Two packs where there had been three. The empty place Dren left behind had weight. So did the south.

Vel did not let them stay long enough to feel either one properly.

"Pack," she said.

Kem did. No argument. Bedroll. Canteens. Journal. The little salvage that made up their remaining life. By the time the sun was fully over the flats they were moving through the old service lane east of the road, using broken pump walls and dead orchard lines for cover.

Two sets of footsteps made the morning sound wrong.

Vel kept expecting Dren's heavier tread on her right and getting scrub, wind, cracked masonry. The service lane had once fed farm units and transfer sheds. Hoarded planners had laid it with the same bad confidence they laid everything: straight enough to look efficient, shallow enough to fail as soon as maintenance stopped. Plates of old road rose at angles out of the dirt. Rebar showed through in rusted seams. She mapped the breaks as she walked, step left here, skirt the sink there, old habit running ahead of thought.

They passed a farmstead where someone was still trying. An old woman pushed a narrow plow through ground that broke in thin plates under the blade and gave back a dry ceramic sound. She never looked up. By her well a cracked tin placard hung half loose above the ripped brackets of an appeal box somebody had stolen for scrap.

Kem slowed beside it. "They put the well under review."

"They put anything under review if they plan to count it."

He looked like he might say more. Light hit her eye from the south instead. One quick hard flare. Then a second.

"Down," Vel said.

Kem moved before asking why. Good. Better than good. He dropped with her off the lane and behind what had been the far wall of a storage shed, both of them sliding the last foot on gravel and old mortar dust. Vel pulled him farther until the broken wall took them below sightline.

"Relay?" he whispered.

"Yes."

Another flash. Then, after a stretch long enough to be intentional, a third from farther east.

Kem turned his head toward her. "What does that mean."

"It means somebody saw movement and doesn't want to waste a horse guessing."

She risked a glance over the wall. Dust lifted off the direct road in two separate lines now. One kept south. One broke east.

"Stay down," she said.

The rider came off the road at an angle that told her he had done this before. No hurry. No theatrics. Just a measured sweep across the side lanes and field cuts, letting the horse pick the best ground while he looked where a tired person would try to hide. Vel flattened farther into the hollow. Beside her Kem had gone still enough to pass for somebody who had learned fear properly.

The horse passed thirty yards out. Close enough for the leather creak to carry. Close enough for Vel to see the rider's coat was travel-dulled red at the collar and cuffs, the color hidden from distance and obvious once you knew to look. Reserve, then, or somebody equipped by them. The man did not search like a Margins collector. He searched like a person who expected prey to be intelligent and therefore disappointing.

He circled once around the old farmstead. The woman with the plow kept pushing her blade through the field as if armed riders quartering past her beans were weather and not worth the neck turn. The rider paused by her well, looked at the ripped brackets under the placard, drank from his own canteen instead, and moved on.

Vel waited until the hoofbeats had gone far enough to become road sound again. Then she waited longer. Her back was damp against the broken stone. Flies found the sweat at her hairline. Beside her Kem's breathing stayed shallow and controlled. Good. He was learning that panic made noise.

"We're not moving yet," she said.

"How long."

"Until the second rider clears or until they decide the first one saw nothing worth the trouble."

Kem stared up at the cracked underside of the wall cap. Sun bled through one split seam in a blade of white.

"So this is the road now," he said.

"This is the road now."

He let that sit. The heat in the hollow rose fast. The smell was hot stone, mouse droppings, old plaster, and the faint iron stink of rust cooking in the sun.

"How long were you going to wait," Kem asked, "before you told me that from the farmhouse we weren't taking the basin road at all."

"I told you we were looping east."

"You told me we were taking cover." He turned his head toward her. His face was close in the dimness, dust already pasted to the sweat at his temple. "This isn't cover, Vel. This is a different journey."

"It became a different journey when Sable said commander, two stationed, maybe four. That changes the line."

"It changes the line. Fine. It also changes what I need to know."

Vel kept her eyes on the gap in the wall. "I've been telling you what matters."

"No." His whisper sharpened, then checked itself. "You've been telling me the piece that keeps me walking. That's different."

She could have answered that. She could have said that keeping him walking had in fact been what mattered. The heat, the rider, the narrow strip of shade they were sharing made the small answer look cheap even before she put words on it.

Outside, a second set of hoofbeats crossed the service lane and kept going east. This rider did not come as near. Vel counted the steps by sound, felt them in the wall more than heard them by the end, and kept counting until there were none.

Kem waited through the counting. Then he said, quieter than before, "No more steps. Not until you tell me why."

"I told you the mechanism. The cascade, the sites, what zero does-"

"Not the mechanism. You." He dragged in a breath and let it out slow. "Why you, specifically. Why you've been walking toward zero for eight years. Why you carry that journal like it's the only object in the world. Why Thessa looked at you like you'd killed someone. Why Dren can look at us and see a shape you still won't say out loud."

The Ledger hummed at the edge of Vel's awareness. Fourteen. Enough to do something foolish if the riders came back. Not enough to waste on lying.

She looked through the crack in the wall at the old woman still working her field. The plow blade lifted. Dropped. Lifted again.

"I could answer the route part first," Vel said.

"I know you could."

"And it'd be true."

"I know that too."

Kem wiped sweat from his upper lip with his wrist. Dust streaked there and stayed. "Tell me the other one."

Vel let the silence run until it stopped being a tactic. The riders had moved on. The road had not. South was still being watched by people who knew what they were doing. Dren was already gone because of it. There was no cleaner hour coming later.

"All right," she said.

She shrugged her pack off, worked the inner flap loose, and took out Maren's journal. The leather was warm from her back. When she set it in her lap the heat of it stayed against her skin.

"Her name was Maren."

Kem said nothing.

"We spent together eleven years. Deep routes, Margins work, settlements nobody with sense or money volunteered for. Healing. Repairs. Pulling people out of structures that should've been condemned before I was born." Vel kept her voice low out of habit now, though the riders were gone. "She was better at the people part. She'd take a fever with the inside of her wrist, tell the patient not to lie to her, and half the room would decide to trust her before I had my tools out. I always had to earn it."

A fly buzzed around Kem's ear. He ignored it.

"We split the jobs. She took the uglier healing because she was better at it. I did structural work, field repairs, the boring things that keep roofs up and pumps running. She kept the route journal." Vel opened it to the middle without really seeing the page. "Her maps were bad. Useful, but bad. She'd mark a well half a mile wrong and note which mule bit, which house still had clean bandages, who lied about their cough."

Kem's mouth moved once at that. Not a smile exactly. The beginning of one that knew better.

"You loved her," he said.

"Yes."

That answer was easier than the next one. Vel kept going before she could stop herself into smaller pieces. The old plaster taste came back at the root of her tongue so fast she had to swallow before the next sentence would hold.

"She hit zero first. Eight years ago. It wasn't planned." Vel looked down at the open page. Maren's script slanted left when she was tired. This page did. "We were south of here in a settlement built over old mine galleries. Row houses, two rooms each, all patched floor joists and borrowed walls. We'd already spent too much that week. Fever, bridge work, one idiot who thought he could move a kiln alone. Maren was at three. I was at sixty-two."

Kem stayed still. The rhythm of his breathing shifted. Less anger in it now. More attention.

"The floor went first," Vel said. "Not the dramatic kind. Just a crack under your boots, one plate, then the next, like somebody breaking crockery inside the walls. Dust came up hot and chalk-bitter. The front room dropped knee-deep and kept going. There was a kid under the lintel and another one still inside. Maren spent one stabilizing the back wall so the father could drag himself clear. Spent another on the boy when the beam came down on his shoulder. I was braced under the crossbrace already full of splinters. She went in for the girl."

Vel could still see it if she let herself, so she did not have to invent anything cleaner. Dust so thick it turned spit to paste. The sound timber made one breath before it stopped being timber. Maren's hair tied back in the red strip she used when she needed both arms free. The page edge dug a crease into Vel's thumb.

"I told her not to." Vel shut the journal halfway, then opened it again because shutting it felt worse. "That wasn't principle. I just did the count faster. One level left, roof already moving, no clean way out. She went anyway."

Outside the wall the plow scraped. Scraped again.

"She got the girl halfway to the door. The ceiling came after. Maren spent the last of it getting the weight off the child's chest." Vel had to wet her mouth before the rest would come. The dust was back in it anyway, old plaster and blood. "The threshold opened right there in the room. Not metaphorical. Not a feeling. A place. Cold air hit my face hard enough to raise the sweat on my arms. It smelled like wet stone. The light was clean and white and wrong for that room. I could see stone steps, a terrace wall, a woman turning with a basket because of the noise." Her grip tightened on the page edge until the paper bent. "Maren looked back at me. Then it closed and she was gone."

Kem drew breath to say something, stopped, and tried again. "You saw through."

"Yes."

"And you knew. Right then."

"I knew it wasn't death." Vel looked at him. "I didn't know what it was. I knew it wasn't death."

Heat crawled under her shirt. There was mortar grit in the seam of her trousers digging into the back of her knee. None of it touched the older thing.

"That was eight years ago," Kem said.

"Yes."

"You've been trying to repeat it since."

"Not the collapse." Vel looked away toward the crack in the wall. The old woman had finally stopped plowing. She stood with both shoulders set against the handles, staring nowhere Vel could see. "But yes. I've been trying to find a way to make the crossing happen on purpose ever since."

He shifted one shoulder against the stone. "The journal. The routes. Every settlement where somebody vanished."

"All of it."

"Vel."

She turned back.

"Say the rest."

There it was. No room left to split the answer finer.

"I was at sixty-two when Maren crossed. I spent thirty-one levels over the next eight years working out what mattered and what didn't. Other sites. Other stories. Old Margins talk that the Hoarded treated like peasant superstition right up until they started stationing soldiers on it. Two years after Maren crossed, an old woman in the deep south told me the only useful thing anybody ever had. She'd seen three vanishings. She said the threshold held wider when the drop was bigger and when the spending around it piled up fast enough to push somebody through."

Kem nodded once. "Cascade."

"Yes."

He looked at the journal in her lap. "Someone with levels."

Vel's thumb moved along the journal spine. "The cascade needed someone who could—" She stopped. Kem was watching her, not angry, just waiting for whatever came next. "I went looking for somebody to use. Somebody with enough levels that the drop would hold the threshold open."

He didn't move but something behind his face shifted.

"I heard about a Hoarded lord's son who'd walked out with two hundred and eighty-six and a habit of burning whatever was in front of him." She kept her eyes on him. "I went north. I found you. I saw a road south."

Kem's eyes stayed on hers, which was harder than if he'd looked away.

"At the start," he said.

"At the start."

He ground a chip of mortar to powder against his knee. "You keep saying that like it helps."

"It's true."

"It's not the question." He wiped the dust on his knee. "I know you used me first. I learned that on the road collapse. I learned it again when Thessa told me you were asking after defectors years before I existed to you. What I don't know is how long you kept doing it after you stopped being able to pretend I was just a number."

Vel let that hit full weight. Nothing outside the wall moved except the half-loose placard on the well post ticking gently in the wind.

"A while," she said.

Kem laughed once without humor. Very quiet. "Useful."

"You wanted the real answer."

"I did."

"So listen to it the first time." Vel settled the journal flatter on her thighs. "You stopped being a number fast. Faster than I had any use for. You were soft and stupid and proud in all the wrong places, and then you kept doing things I couldn't fit back into the plan. Back with those scouts you spent yourself stupid and I wanted to shake you. You stitched Dren's arm shaking hard enough to miss the needle and I didn't know what to do with that. You burned ninety-eight in the Margins and every level of it made the Scour worse for me and still I couldn't tell you to stop. Not there. Not with people standing in front of you already half used up by the system I was asking you to survive."

Kem looked down. "So every decent thing I did was you watching the route get longer."

"Sometimes, yes." The word scraped going out. "Sometimes I watched you save somebody and thought about what it cost me. That is true. It stayed true after I hated it."

The admission sat between them. No clever shape to it. No excuse big enough to go over the top.

Outside, a horse called somewhere far enough away to sound ordinary.

"I've been asking for weeks," Kem said. "After Thessa. After the mechanism. Since the farmhouse, really. Every time you gave me one more useful piece and stopped right where it started to matter."

"Asking isn't the same as stopping."

He looked up sharply.

Vel met it. "You kept walking. I could keep the answer broken up. That's not noble. That's just what I did."

He breathed out through his nose. "At least that sounds like you."

"I didn't have a better version."

"No." He touched the journal cover once, not taking it, just resting a knuckle on the leather like he had to confirm it was an object and not a shrine. "Tell me the part with the Margins. Dren already did. I want yours."

Vel watched him pull away.

"I'm doing the same thing," she said.

Kem said nothing.

"I spent my first level at seventeen because a girl was drowning and the Hoarded standing on the bank wouldn't—" She looked at Kem. His face had gone still. Her thumb pressed hard into the journal spine. "That's the version that makes me sound like I've always been the person who spends for strangers. Maren was. She'd walk into a place and spend because someone was hurt in front of her." Vel looked at the crack in the wall. "I went looking for someone to spend. That's different."

Wind moved through the broken wall. Dust settled on the journal page.

"I walked into the Margins and saw what the Ledger really looks like when it's a bill instead of a philosophy. Fallow at four. Labor compounds. Kids already counting what parts of themselves were worth debt. And I still had this private thing underneath it. This stupid huge personal thing."

Kem's voice was barely above breath. "You kept asking me not to waste levels because you needed them for her."

"Yes."

"You let me spend in the Margins because you couldn't say no in front of them."

"Yes."

"And you were angry when I did."

"Yes."

He scrubbed his face and left it there a second. "That's filthy."

"Yes."

The old wordless rhythm of the field carried on outside. Blade in dirt. Lift. Drop.

Vel turned a page in the journal and found one of Maren's cramped later notes crowded into the margin of a water table sketch. The letters were nearly on top of each other, written in the dark or half numb or both.

"She'd walk into a place and spend because someone was standing there hurt," Vel said. "I went looking for someone to spend." She shut the journal this time. "I don't know how to make those the same thing. I kept trying anyway."

Kem stared at the cover in her lap. "Dren thinks that's what the system does. Uses people. Counts what's left after."

"Dren's right."

"Are you sorry."

Vel could have said yes and meant part of it. She could have said no and meant part of that too. The problem with truth was that it kept coming in ugly pieces.

"I'm not sorry I found you," she said. "I'm not sorry I kept you alive long enough to get here. I'm not going to lie and say I wish I'd left you in the snow with your signet and your two hundred and eighty-six." The next part took longer. "I am sorry for every careful word I used to make it sound like that wasn't what I was doing."

Kem took that in with no expression at first. Then something in his face changed, not softening, not forgiveness. Just the registration of a thing finally said without padding.

"What if she's not there?" he asked.

Vel had rehearsed better answers than the true one. She let them go.

"Then I'll be at zero in a world without the Ledger," she said. "And that's still better than this one."

He watched her a long moment. Enough time for a rider to come back. Enough time for the heat in the hollow to settle into the bones.

"All right," he said at last.

"All right what."

"All right I'll keep walking." He shifted, braced himself on the wall, but did not stand yet. "Not because it's fine. It isn't. You used me. Same road, same number, you'd do it again."

Vel thought of Maren in the collapsing room. Of Dren going north on one good arm. Of Kem in the Margins, bloody from field medicine and his levels vanishing into strangers.

"Yes," she said.

"Good." He nodded once, almost to himself. "Then no more pieces. From here to the Scour I want the whole route. Distances, bad ground, what you think the Reserve has waiting, where you think the commander will want us. All of it before we spend another level or walk into another ditch."

Vel looked at him, then at the journal.

"All right," she said.

"I'm not forgiving you."

"I know."

"Good."

A fresh flare hit the relay mast to the south. Kem's head turned toward it before Vel's did. That too had changed.

"We should move when it stops," he said.

"Yes."

"You're sure they're gone."

"No."

He stood anyway.

This time, when they left cover, he didn't walk ahead out of anger or lag behind out of thought. He stayed beside her and made her talk.

They cut west first, because east was what the riders had just checked. A dry drainage run let them move bent over for nearly half an hour before it opened onto broken service ground again. Vel kept the journal out between them as they walked, holding the bottom edge so the wind would not take the pages.

"From the farmhouse," Kem said, keeping his voice low out of habit now, "if we'd gone straight."

"Two hard days on foot to the basin rim. Maybe a little less if the road held and nobody cared who saw us."

"And instead."

"Instead we burned the same two days staying off the obvious approach. Yesterday we made maybe six miles south in total. The rest was east, hiding, and waiting for riders to get bored somewhere else." Vel tapped the page once. "This station." Then again. "Dead orchard line. Then the canal where we hid. The direct road is here. Basin here."

Kem leaned closer. "So we're east of the approach."

"Yes. About half a day. That helps if the commander expects desperate people to come straight at her. It hurts if she's been on the ground five years and already closed the side lanes."

"Which one do you think."

"I think five years is long enough to distrust easy answers."

He made a face. "Useful."

"You asked for all of it."

"I did." He looked back at the page. "Water."

"One communal well ahead. Maybe. If it hasn't been fouled or watched. After that a dead settlement west of the basin. Maren marked a cistern there. Could still hold. Could hold a body. Route notes were optimistic."

"I noticed."

Vel glanced at him. "You can read her script now."

"I can read your writing pretending not to be hers." His mouth did a small unpleasant thing. "That came with time."

They took the communal well at a crawl. Not the direct path. Vel circled wide through a stand of dead trellis posts and watched the lip from fifty yards out before letting him go near it. Fresh hoof marks ringed the stones. The bucket rope had been cut and tossed aside. Red paint had been dragged across the well house wall in a fresh band, and a site-command seal had been stamped into it while it was still wet.

Kem took one look and went very still.

"Recent," he said.

"Yes."

He touched the cut rope with his boot. "They're pushing perimeter out from the basin."

"Yes."

"Because of us?"

"Because of the threshold. Because of whoever came before. Because they don't guard a hole in the ground out here for sport." Vel looked along the service line, checking the empty openings between walls. "Maybe because of us too."

Kem crouched, rewound the frayed end of the rope once around his fist without seeming to notice he was doing it. "Take me through the basin."

Vel did.

They moved from cover to cover while she talked. Not fast. Nothing about the day allowed fast. The land had opened and failed and been opened again by mining until it carried old intentions badly. Broken field walls. Transfer sheds without roofs. A stretch of trackbed leading nowhere. One glass-fronted records case sunk crooked in a drift, its ruled columns and empty hooks preserved under a rusted pane. Hoarded care always outlasted Hoarded usefulness.

"The basin's wide," Vel said. "Too wide to rush. Wind-scoured. Layered rock. Very little cover once you break rim. The old gallery line comes in from the west under the upper shelves. Maren used it once for weather, not for soldiers. If it still goes through, it gives us a descent without being silhouetted the whole time."

"If it still goes through."

"Yes."

"And if it doesn't."

"Then we pick a shelf and pray the commander likes doctrine more than improvisation."

Kem snorted once. "You really do think highly of her."

"I think five years on one piece of ground turns a person into part of it."

He took that in, then nodded toward the journal. "What about the permanent station."

"Maren's notes were old even when I started using them. I know there was a camp. I don't know where it sits now." Vel turned a page while walking and immediately regretted it when the wind tried to fold the paper back on itself. She caught it before it tore. "Sable's warning gave me bodies, not layout. Commander. Two stationed. Maybe four if the new pair got there first. That means observation on the rim, water under control, and at least one line of retreat they trust more than the others."

Kem was quiet for a stretch. Then: "You didn't tell me the farmhouse was still two days out."

Vel kept her eyes on the ground ahead. "I didn't think it mattered once we left it."

"It mattered to me."

"I know."

"Did you know then."

"Probably."

He let that ugliness breathe a few steps. "I hate how often your honesty gets worse when it arrives late."

"That's fair."

They found shade at noon under the shell of a loading shed with one wall still standing and the floor sunk into an old mine void. Vel crouched on a beam that complained under her weight and laid the journal open across her knees. Kem stood until the heat beat the principle out of him and then sat opposite her with his back to a steel upright warm enough to sting through cloth.

The last pages were dense with notes. Maren's smaller slanted script crowded the margins around later additions in Vel's flatter writing. Distances marked, revised, crossed out. Small warnings. One crude map of the basin west face. Another of the gallery line as Maren had known it before the Hoarded started paying soldiers to care.

Kem pointed. "This mark."

"Collapsed storehouse. Good sightline north. Bad sightline south because the rim breaks there."

"Useful to us or to them."

"Depends who gets there first."

"This one."

"Dry wash. No cover except in the bottom. If we go that way, we're seen crossing the lip."

"Then why write it."

"Maren wrote everything. Some of it to use. Some of it so we'd know why not."

Kem traced the old gallery line without touching the page. "If the commander knows desperate people won't want the open rim, she'll watch the gallery."

"Yes."

"And if she knows that we know that."

"Then she might leave it thin and expect us to outsmart ourselves."

Kem looked up. "That is a hateful woman to meet on fourteen and eighteen."

"Yes."

He stared at the map a moment longer. Then, quieter: "Was Maren practical."

"Annoyingly."

"That's not an answer."

"She was practical the way some people are honest. As a habit. She'd put food by before weather changed. She'd count rope before a bridge. She knew exactly how much tincture was left in the box without opening the box." Vel looked down at the cramped margin note by the cistern mark. "She'd also give away the last clean bandage if somebody bled in front of her and then act surprised when I swore."

Kem rested his forearms on his knees. "That sounds expensive."

"It was."

"And you loved her anyway."

"Yes."

"That's at least consistent."

Vel almost smiled. Almost was all it got.

They waited through the worst of the afternoon under the shed because the road belonged to people with more patience than they had shade. When the sun started to tilt and the heat shifted from hammering down to pressing sideways, they moved again.

The dead settlement west of the basin showed itself first as upright lines against the glare. Then as houses without roofs. Then as a street grid the Hoarded had once bothered to straighten. Shaping-built walls were still standing in places where every timber member had gone. Windows opened onto rooms full of light and no life. At the entrance only the iron uprights of the old notice board remained, the plank long gone for heat.

Somebody had gouged into one post: *They left. We're still here.*

Kem stopped long enough to read it twice.

"The tone is consistent," he said.

"With the Hoarded or with the dead."

"Both."

They used the settlement carefully. Too much geometry. Too many sightlines cut by straight streets and missing roofs. Vel kept them in the back runs where kitchens had once opened onto alleys, stepping around fallen cookstoves and the white powder of old plaster. One courtyard held the cistern Maren had marked. It was half full, silted, and alive with insects. Good enough. Kem lowered a cup on a length of scavenged cord while Vel watched the alley mouth. He handed her the first fill without comment. Their knuckles brushed on the metal and moved on.

That might have been the whole exchange if not for the sound that came next.

Not hoofbeats. Not close. A metal clack from the north street and then a voice, too far to make out words but near enough to collapse the air in her chest.

Vel caught Kem by the sleeve and pulled him backward into the cistern yard. He came without asking. They flattened behind the low retaining wall while sound moved through the empty houses: boot on stone, then another, then a scrape like somebody shifting a rifle sling or a tool against masonry.

Kem's lips were at her ear. "Reserve?"

"Probably."

"One?"

"At least one."

The steps stopped. Vel counted three breaths. Four. A dry laugh came from the street, cut off fast. Then the steps moved on again, crossing the next intersection instead of turning into their yard.

Kem did not look afraid in any theatrical way. He just looked very awake.

"That settles the water question," he murmured.

"Yes."

"And the perimeter."

"Yes."

They stayed down until the steps had gone fully and the next sound from the street was only wind pushing dust through a broken doorway.

When they rose, Kem did not wait for her to say move. He put the cup away, shouldered his pack, and followed her deeper into the settlement where walls still made shadows long enough to hide in.

By the time the sun dropped red against the far haze, they had found a room with half a roof and one doorframe that gave onto an alley instead of a street. Vel chose it because there was only one obvious angle of approach and because the wall opposite the opening still held the faded ghost of a Hoarded safety stencil: a little falling figure, one black line for a ledge, and beneath it a row of empty signature boxes. Nobody had bothered to strip the place for firewood in years.

Kem set his pack down and stared at it.

"They really did have paperwork for the roof killing you."

"They had paperwork for everything."

He sat on the floor with his back to the wall and shut his eyes for a second. When he opened them, they went to the journal in her lap again.

"You were going to tell me at the Scour," he said. Not accusing this time. Just assembling the timetable.

"Yes."

"Because if I walked out then, the route was over."

"Yes."

He breathed out. "That's vile."

"Yes."

"And efficient."

"Yes."

He huffed a short laugh that had no amusement in it. "You are having an exceptionally honest day."

Vel leaned her head back against the wall. The stone held the day's heat and gave a little of it back. "Don't count on a second one."

Silence settled, but not the bad silence from before. This one had edges and shape. A thing two people could sit inside without pretending it was anything else.

After a while Kem said, "Tell me one thing that has nothing to do with thresholds."

Vel turned her head toward him. "What."

"About her. Maren. Something useless."

Vel looked at the journal. Outside the doorframe the alley had gone the color of bruised copper.

"She cheated at cards," she said.

Kem blinked. "That's not what I expected."

"She was terrible at hiding it. Thought the point of cheating was letting the other person know you respected them enough to make the game interesting."

"That's not cheating. That's a cry for help."

"It's why nobody played with her twice unless they liked her."

"Did you."

"I liked her before the cards."

"That was prudent."

"No."

The smile went away, but it left the room changed.

Night came hard once it started. Somewhere beyond the west wall a loose sheet of metal knocked itself slowly against stone. Wind worked the empty settlement like it was trying to remember the people. Vel kept the journal closed after that. There was no more route knowledge in it that mattered tonight. Only Maren's writing and hers sharing old paper like they still shared anything.

Kem slid down farther until he was almost lying on the floor. "If the commander has pushed patrols this far, does that mean she knows we're coming."

Vel considered before answering. "It means she knows somebody always comes, eventually. Maybe she knows about us. Maybe she just knows what the Scour is worth and doesn't believe in quiet weeks."

He folded an arm over his eyes. "I hate how often your best answer is maybe."

"Mine too."

A pause.

"Dren was right," Kem said.

"About what."

"That other doors would open if somebody could afford them." He lowered his arm and looked at the half roof above them. "Him having to keep shifting that pack. His girl hitting the floor and looking at him. He goes north with one bad arm and one hundred and twenty and that's the sensible journey. We go south because neither of us can leave a thing alone."

Vel listened to the wind move through the empty street.

"Yes," she said.

He let that answer stand. Then: "I still would have spent in the Margins."

"I know."

"I'm saying it because I don't want memory cleaning it up later."

"I'd have done it anyway." He shut the eye again. "Even if you'd told me clean. Maybe especially then. I don't know what that says about me."

"That you're not Hoarded enough to watch a child drown and call it prudence."

"That sounded almost kind."

"Don't get used to it."

"Too late."

They let the night thicken around that. No fire. No talking once full dark settled. Once, far to the south, a brief pale flash climbed the rim line and vanished. Signal plate or lantern shutter or moon on metal. Vel could not tell. She did not like not being able to tell.

Fourteen hummed at the edge of her awareness. Eighteen hummed at his. Thirty-two lay between them and a permanent garrison at the Scour. Dren was somewhere north with a ruined arm, one hundred and twenty levels, and an eight-year-old girl waiting for a healer to make her legs hold. Vel kept seeing his face when he'd said *I've been picking her up off the kitchen floor since spring.* She kept seeing Maren in the falling room. She kept seeing Kem in the hollow under the wall, finally asking the question with no place left to set it down unfinished.

Sometime after midnight Kem spoke into the dark without opening his eyes.

"No more surprises," he said.

Vel listened to the wind for a beat before answering.

"I'll do what I can," she said.

It wasn't a promise. He knew that. So did she. But in the room with the empty signature boxes on the wall and the journal between them, it was the straightest line she had left.


# Chapter 17

The wind hit first. It was not a gust but a condition, a constant gritty pressure coming off the Scour like breath from a furnace. It carried powdered rock and the flat mineral smell of laid-bare geology. Kem shielded his eyes with one hand, looked south, and felt the world drop out from under itself.

The Scour looked like a wound. It did not look like a valley or a canyon. It looked as if something had taken a bite from the earth and left the inside showing. The erosion basin stretched maybe two kilometers across, carved down through layered ground striped ochre, pale gray, and a deep oxidized red that looked like old blood in the summer sun. The walls fell away in terraces and shelves. Wind had cut them into overhangs, columns, and narrow sandstone fins left standing after centuries of grinding. There was no vegetation, no shade, and no cover. Sun bounced off the exposed rock so the heat came from everywhere at once.

Vel stood beside him at the ridgeline with one hand on her pack where the route journal sat. Her face did something Kem had learned to read over six weeks. It was not emotion exactly. It was the registration of a cost she had not budgeted for. She looked at the basin floor the way she looked at everything: measuring.

"There it is."

Somewhere down there Maren had reached zero. Vel went very still beside him.

Vel had fourteen levels left. Kem had eighteen. Thirty-two sat between them. He had spent more than that on a single defensive perimeter during the agent fight south of the Spent Towns. In the basin below, the number no longer felt large enough to be stupid. Somewhere north, Dren had done the sane thing and gone back to his life, and Kem hated how sensible that now looked.

"I count four," Vel said. She was looking through a gap in the ridgeline toward the basin's navigable descent, a switchback path cut into the wall and the only ordinary way down. Two figures stood at the top of it. Two more waited on the floor near a cluster of small garrison buildings.

These were not the untrained level-three-hundred agents who had accidentally detonated a grain silo and given themselves migraines. These were Red Reserve. These were the people who knew what abilities were for and had been permitted to use them.

"Two at the descent. Two on the floor. The descent pair are fresh. They're standing wrong. Too stiff. Too visible. They've been posted recently. The floor pair are permanent. Look at the camp."

Kem looked. The structures near the basin floor were not field shelters. They were low buildings of fitted block with canvas-and-leather roofing, built for years of use. A fire pit sat between them in a carbon-black ring. A gear rack leaned under an awning. Clothing hung on a line, bleached by sun and wind to a tired colorless gray. Someone had been living here long enough for boots to polish a path between doorways.

"Sable said permanent garrison," Kem said. "I was hoping she was being dramatic."

"Sable doesn't do dramatic."

"No. She does bureaucratic. Which is worse."

Vel's mouth moved as if a smile had started and then refused to build the rest of itself. "I need to map their positions properly. That's two levels."

That cost two levels out of fourteen, a seventh of everything she had left spent on looking. But squinting from a ridgeline was not a plan.

"Do it."

Vel closed her eyes and spent. The air around her shifted with the subtle pressure change that came before a storm. When she opened them again, her gaze moved with inhuman precision, sweeping the basin in hard clean arcs.

"The commander is near the threshold point. Woman. Compact build. She's been patrolling the same strip for years. I can see the wear in the ground. Same route, different hours. The other permanent agent runs a circuit between the camp and the east wall. He's covering the tunnel exit. The descent pair are young. One keeps touching her gear."

The sharpness left her eyes. Vel sat down hard on the ridgeline. "Twelve."

Kem let the number settle. Twelve and eighteen made thirty.

"I should mention," he said, "that at my current level I could perform the Conservatory Rite without anyone noticing. I'm finally discreet enough for a Hoarded ceremony. It took losing two hundred and eighty-two levels, but the form is improving."

Vel looked at him. This time the smile made it halfway. "Good. Maybe your father would be proud from a medically safe distance."

A voice rose from the basin floor. It was not shouted. The basin simply carried it.

"I can see you on the ridgeline. I've been watching you for thirty minutes."

Vel went still. Kem's hand moved toward nothing, an old reflex from when reaching meant options. He had twelve options left. Thirteen if he counted not using any of them.

"I know what you're here for," the voice called. "I'd like to talk before we do this the other way."

"She's alone down there," Vel said. The Perception was fading but not gone. "The circuit agent is at the eastern wall. The descent pair haven't moved."

Kem stood. "I'll go."

"If she-"

"Then you'll have twelve levels and no cascade partner and this will have been a very long walk for nothing." He kept his voice flat. Six weeks of approaching zero had burned the drama out of him. "But she announced herself. She asked to talk. That's a person trying not to spend."

"I hate procedure."

"No you don't. You hate other people's procedure."

He took the switchback descent. The turns were tight, and the footing had been worn smooth by thousands of patrols. His boots were held together by friction and optimism, and the soles had worn so thin he could feel the heat of each step through them. Dust slid away under his weight in little sighs. Every meter down made the basin walls rise higher around him until the ridgeline and Vel both became part of the sky.

The commander met him where the path leveled onto the basin floor. She was shorter than the voice suggested, compact and weathered, somewhere in her forties. Brown skin had gone darker under five years of Scour sun. Her hands were calloused. Her field clothes had been patched so many times the patches had their own repairs. She stood the way Vel stood, with her weight settled and her feet already where they needed to be.

At the edge of the camp, beside the gear rack, seven rock samples sat on a flat ledge. Kem saw a dark piece of basalt, a curl of pale shale, a block of iron-red sandstone, and four other scraps from the basin walls. They had been lined up by color and grain with the care of a person who had been alone with geology for a long time and had started arranging the company.

"Commander Osen," she said. "Red Reserve. Scour garrison."

"Kem Aldraste."

"I know. Your retrieval order has been sitting in my dispatch pouch for six weeks." She nodded toward the switchback. "Those two up there are for you. The Council sent them three weeks ago. They're competent enough."

"Just competent enough?"

"Nobody sends their best people to sit in a hole in the ground. The best people are back at the Vault not spending anything." She said it without bitterness. "I volunteered for this posting and renewed twice. People don't do that for promotion."

"Why do they do it?"

Osen looked past him at the featureless stretch of basin floor behind her. The threshold point, or what Kem assumed was the threshold point, looked like every other patch of stripped ground until he noticed the wear of her patrol strip and the way she never quite turned her back on it.

"The brief makes it sound easy," she said. "One patrol route. One camp. One classified nuisance in a place nobody wants to live. Then you see somebody hit zero and leave. After that you either file for transfer or you stay. Going home after that feels like lying."

"You saw it yourself."

"Three times." Her eyes came back to him. "So listen before you decide how much of yourself to spend."

"You came here to talk," he said. "Not about my retrieval."

"Your retrieval can wait. This can't." She tipped her chin toward the ridge. "The threshold has degraded."

Wind pushed dust between them. Kem felt the map shift under his feet.

"Three years ago the Council sent a specialist to damp the site. Natural erosion had already started changing the basin. The dampening finished the job. If the woman on the ridge is still using eight-year-old numbers, the two of you may buy a flicker and nothing else."

Kem's mouth went dry. Above him, on the ridgeline, Vel would be listening. Even with her Perception fading, the basin carried sound, and Vel had spent forty years listening for the piece of information that would kill a plan.

Vel's whole cascade calculation had been built on a threshold altered under her feet.

"What makes it hold?" Kem asked.

"More margin. More total spend. The report isn't generous about your odds." Osen spoke like a field memo stripped to essentials. "Two contributors are no longer reliable."

"And you're telling me this because?"

"Because ignorance isn't mercy." She pointed west, then north, then east without taking her eyes off him. "That shelf, the wash, the bright crust in the middle, the old mine exit. I know how long each route leaves a body in the open. If you keep coming, I keep you off the point until dark. People hear garrison and imagine a charge. Mostly this post is angles."

Kem followed the line of her hand. The basin stopped looking wide and started looking arranged.

"So that's it. You wait us out."

"Mostly." She rubbed dust off one thumb against the side seam of her trousers. "The young ones think this is a retrieval. It's erosion management with paperwork."

Kem thought of Sable and disliked how quickly the comparison arrived.

"That's bleak."

"It's accurate." She nodded toward the ridge. "Take the new arithmetic back to Vel. If she still wants it after that, at least she'll be choosing the real basin."

The threshold was here. That was the worst part. It had been turned into a posting and a report category. Someone had made a working life out of standing next to it.

"And if we keep coming?" he asked.

"Then I do my job."

He believed her. That was the problem. She was not a liar trying to scare him off. She was a competent person telling him exactly how little chance they had left and exactly how she planned to spend the rest of the daylight ruining what remained.

Kem turned and climbed back up the switchback. The basin wall threw heat at him like a second sun. By the time he reached the ridge, sweat had soaked his shirt and dust had turned to paste on the back of his neck.

Vel was still where he had left her, journal open on her knees. She had heard enough. Her face had gone blank in the specific way it did when feeling had been pushed off the surface so the work could continue underneath it.

"The site's still there," she said before he sat down.

"Yes."

"But the basin isn't the basin Maren mapped."

"No."

She looked down at the open page. The page held wind notes, rock notes, a small x at the cascade site, and the word *here*. Vel put two fingers on the page and then lifted them again.

"The x still lines up with Osen's patrol strip," she said. "The threshold point hasn't moved. The approach has."

"She told me how she holds it." Kem sat beside her. The ridge burned through his trousers. "She doesn't rush people. She keeps them off the point until dark. She knows every clean line across the basin and she breaks them one by one."

Vel shut her eyes for a moment. "That makes sense."

"You sound offended by it."

"I am. I spent eight years respecting the wrong problem."

Kem looked south again. Four Reserve agents. Thirty levels between him and Vel, and that was before the tunnel took its share. The basin floor did not look impossible. It looked administratively impossible, which was somehow worse.

"What do we do?" he asked.

Vel reopened the journal and set it flat on the ridge between them. The wind tried to lift the pages. She held them down with both hands.

"We don't give her the switchback. We don't give her the center line. We use the tunnel, come out east, and force her to solve our position in motion instead of watching us descend in order." She tapped the page with one fingernail. "Maren used the old mine because the eastern wall had better footing and less open exposure. If Osen's permanent circuit still covers that exit, then the gap isn't geography. It's timing."

"And if the threshold doesn't hold?"

Vel stared at the journal long enough that he thought she might not answer.

"Then I learn that from the threshold," she said. "Not from a commander who has spent five years standing next to it."

That was not hope. It was worse and truer than hope. It was Vel refusing to let somebody else be the final witness to her life.

"When do we move?"

"Two hours. Light behind us at the exit. In their eyes, not ours. Maybe that buys confusion. Maybe it buys nothing."

They spent the waiting time turning themselves into what could run.

The packs had made sense on a road. They made less sense now. Vel stripped hers down first with the blank efficiency of a person cutting dead weight off a cart axle. She set aside the blanket, the cook pan, the spare shirt, and the little packet of dried root Dren had insisted they buy in the last settlement because the road south carried no mercy and less food. She tucked the journal back into the pack, thought better of it, and pulled it free again. That stayed.

Kem copied her. His extra shirt came out. The cheap plate he had been carrying since the Spent Towns came out. The half bar of soap he had believed, with Hoarded optimism, would matter by the end of this journey came out. He touched the signet in his pocket by accident and left it there because taking it out would have required explaining to himself why he still had it.

They wedged the discarded gear under an overhang above the tunnel line and weighted it with broken shale so the wind would not scatter it across the ridge. Kem had the absurd feeling they were packing for two different futures. In one, they came back for the gear at dusk, bruised and furious and still alive in the old world. In the other, the ridge kept their things because people who crossed did not return for soap.

Below them, the garrison pattern went on.

The permanent circuit agent walked his eastern line with the patience of a clock. One of the retrieval pair at the switchback kept rolling her shoulders as if reminding herself she had joints to trust. Osen never strayed more than a certain distance from the threshold strip. Even when she bent to drink, she did it facing west.

Vel watched the rotations until the Perception spend had become mere memory and she was back to ordinary sight. Ordinary sight was enough. She counted steps.
