She tracked where the circuit agent slowed at a wash cut and where he sped up over bedrock. She muttered times under her breath and wrote two more marks in the margin of Maren's page.

"She leaves the east wall thin for forty breaths at a time," Vel said eventually.

"That doesn't sound generous."

"It isn't. It's just human." She snapped the journal shut. "That's what we've got."

The sun lowered. Heat changed character but did not lessen. Instead of hammering straight down it came sideways off the basin walls, baking one half of Kem's face and leaving the other dry with wind. He drank from his canteen and the water tasted like hot metal. Vel drank less. He noticed and said nothing because there was nothing to say that wasn't stupid.

"Do you think Osen's lying about the threshold?" he asked after a while.

"No."

"That was quick."

"Liars sell hope. She sold weather."

Kem looked at her.

"That was almost quotable," he said.

"Shut up."

The answer settled the last uncertainty in him. Osen had not offered a cleaner route, a hidden angle, a desperate maybe. She had laid out exactly how they would fail. That was why Vel believed her.

When the light had gone amber enough to knife sideways through the basin, Vel stood and rolled one shoulder. "Time."

---

Three hours behind them, Sable Orm stopped on a northern ridge and read the basin twice before trusting the first impression.

The Scour lay open below her in bands of exposed ground and hard light. She could see the garrison camp. She could see the permanent patrol lines worn into the basin floor. Farther east, two figures were moving below the skyline toward an old mine cut in the wall.

Sable had been tracking for nine days. Two Perception spends had brought her here. Two hundred and thirty-six levels remained, enough for almost any official purpose and not nearly enough to make this feel official.

At the farmhouse she had not yet opened the threshold files. She had the Council authorization, the permanent garrison brief, and enough certainty in her own procedure to mistake those for the urgent facts. The dampening assessment came later, during the third week of pursuit, after the farmhouse and after she had already sent her remaining agents back north. Pulling it from the last live relay office north of the basin had cost her nearly two days, enough to burn the road lead she should have kept from the farmhouse. An auditor's seal had opened cabinets no one thought needed hiding from an auditor.

The specialist report had not offered a clean answer. It had only stripped certainty from the old one. The site was unstable. The original crossing could not be treated as repeatable. Contributor load, energy margin, and failure behavior had all changed. The recommendation at the end was blunt: a stable opening now needed at least three contributors, and even that bought probability rather than promise.

Below her, the two figures cut for the mine entrance and the patrol pattern bent toward them. Sable watched the change happen and felt the lie in her own paperwork catch up with her. For nine days she had filed her movement under observation, intelligence gathering, and confirmation of a retrieval target's final disposition. Observation had been honest only while distance still counted as neutrality.

She opened her satchel. It held blank report forms, a retrieval warrant, spare pencils, seal wax, a second shirt, and other official weight with no downhill use. She kept the water, the bandages, the copied notes, and the knife she had learned to carry badly. The rest she wedged beneath an outcrop and weighted with a slab of shale.

The fastest descent was the north scree. It was also the stupid descent. It slid underfoot, skinned hands, and advertised every step with hissing gravel. It was the only route that cut time instead of preserving decorum.

She started down.

---

They moved laterally along the ridgeline, staying below the lip where rock met sky. Wind shoved grit into every seam of clothing. Kem's eyes watered until the tears cut clean tracks through the dust on his face. Up here the ground was sharper and less worn, with lichens and dead grass in the cracks. Thirty meters below, nothing lived.

The tunnel entrance was a horizontal scar in the wall, two meters high and three wide, its edges too clean for nature. Miners had cut it decades ago, extracting something the Hoarded had found profitable enough to spend on and unimportant enough to visit themselves. It was the usual arrangement. A rusted length of rail still jutted out of the dust near the entrance, half buried and too bent to serve any purpose now except accusation.

Cool air moved out of the dark carrying wet mineral rot and old dust. The ceiling sagged where ancient Reinforcement had long since faded. Rubble narrowed the passage to barely a meter. Drill marks still showed in places, neat lines of human labor preserved in rock long after the laborers' names had been spent out of any record that mattered.

Vel knelt at the entrance with the journal open. The pages rattled under her hands.

"Maren mapped the interior," she said. "Main shaft for two hundred meters, then down and east. Three partial collapses and one full blockage. That was eight years ago."

"So it's worse."

"Probably."

"Good. I was worried this might be straightforward."

She gave him a look and tucked the journal under her shirt, inside the remaining strap of her pack. Then they stepped inside.

The dark was immediate and total. It did not feel like night. Night at least admitted the existence of sky. This was underground dark, close and old and absolute. Kem lit his palm. Warm light opened over rough walls, broken debris, and a ceiling that seemed offended by the responsibility of holding itself up.

Sixteen.

The first stretch forced them low. Then lower. The miners had cut the main shaft straight, but time had not respected their intentions. One side had slumped inward where water had eaten a seam in the wall. Splintered timber braces stuck out of the debris like snapped ribs. Kem had to turn his shoulders to pass a section where collapsed marl had left the tunnel shaped like a throat.

The floor tilted unexpectedly under loose grit. Vel put one hand back without looking, and Kem took it long enough to keep himself from sliding face-first into the wall.

"This was a mine?" he whispered.

"It was. The Hoarded weren't interested in worker comfort."

"They continue to maintain brand consistency."

Her grip tightened once and released.

The passage pinched into a crushed crawl where a slab the size of a wagon leaned across the tunnel on cracked bedding lines. Every shift of Kem's weight made rock answer rock. Fine dust trickled down his sleeve and caught in the sweat on his forearms.

Vel flattened beside the gap and looked once. "If that settles while we're under it, we're done."

"That's very clarifying."

"It's your spend."

Three levels for structural Reinforcement. He had once spent more than that warming bathwater he had no intention of entering. Kem put both hands on the slab. It felt cool, damp at the seam, with the kind of patient weight that made human plans seem insulting. When he spent, the subtraction came in three clean absences. The Ledger's hum thinned around them.

Thirteen.

"Go."

Vel slid under first. She did not hesitate. She trusted the Reinforcement and, worse, she trusted him to hold it. Kem followed with the light bobbing ahead of his face and gravel grinding into both shoulders. The slab groaned above his spine. He had to let the breath out of his lungs to clear the tightest part. When he finally pulled himself through, his knees hit the far side hard enough to sting.

He dropped the hold.

The slab settled behind them with a heavy abrupt thud. Dust burst through the gap. The opening narrowed to a crack no wider than his wrist.

There was no way back through that crack.

For a few seconds they just listened to the tunnel reaccept its own weight.

Then Vel moved again.

The shaft dipped. Water had been working the rock for years. The floor ahead had split into a dark slot crossed only by a spine of intact bedrock along the left wall. Kem tested it with one foot. The ledge sheared under him at once. His stomach dropped. Vel caught a fistful of his shirt and hauled him back before the rest of the edge went with it. The falling debris hit somewhere below after a long enough pause to feel personal.

"Don't do that," she said.

"I'll add it to the list."

She spent on Navigation. There was no flare and no visible change, just a hard little tightening in the air and a change in her body, as if a second skeleton had briefly aligned itself inside her own. She took his wrist and put his hand on the wall.

"Left edge. Crack. High step. Don't trust the pale patch. It sounds solid and isn't."

Her voice stayed flat and exact while she fed him the route one placement at a time. He kept his palm against the wall and felt the tunnel sweat against his skin. More grit pattered into the darkness below. Somewhere water dripped and echoed like slow counting.

Ten.

The shaft forked twenty meters later. The right branch had collapsed into a choke of timber and rubble. The left slanted down under a low arch of rough-cut ceiling. Vel touched the left wall once, nodded to herself, and kept moving. Maren's map lived now in whatever part of Vel's head had once been reserved for safer long-term plans.

The only continuation after that was a constriction where ceiling and floor had shifted toward each other until the tunnel seemed to close into itself. Vel looked at it, swore once under her breath, and spent again. Evasion cost two more levels.

She went through like the gap had briefly remembered it was supposed to be a passage. Kem had to force his way after her, chest and back scraping opposite walls. Rock dust filled his mouth. The light in his palm smeared against the wall and made the space feel even smaller. There was not room to panic. Panic required room. His father's life rose up stupidly in his head: broad halls, wide doors, a body preserved from every pressure except the one that had finally hollowed him out.

When he shoved himself free on the far side, he lay on his back for one breath longer than he should have and stared at the ceiling two handspans above his nose.

"If this whole plan works," he said, breathing hard, "I do intend to complain about it at length."

"You can do that while running."

The tunnel widened just enough to stand bent instead of doubled. Vel was waiting with one hand braced on the wall. At eight levels her hands had started to shake. It was not fear. It was Ledger signal loss. The hum that had been with her for forty years was fading toward silence, and the body that had always lived inside that hum was being asked to improvise.

Heat replaced mineral cold. Wind touched the tunnel mouth ahead. Kem set a Ward at the exit, the lightest defensive shell he had left, enough to buy three seconds if someone was waiting outside and close enough to trigger on the first body through.

Twelve.

That was everything he owned in the world. Between them they had twenty. Enough for a mistake and a defense, maybe. Not enough for invention.

Vel adjusted the journal under her shirt and looked at him once. The look was not sentimental. It was inventory.

"If they catch us in the mouth," she said, "we turn around and die in a tunnel."

"Strong preference for the open air, then."

"Yes."

They stepped out into late-afternoon sun.

The basin floor hit like a fist made of heat and light. Red-gray bedrock, wind-scoured smooth, shimmered hard enough to hurt the eyes. Shallow erosion channels cut the floor into troughs and waist-high shelves of harder material. The threshold point lay somewhere eight hundred meters west, another patch of stripped ground blanched by sun and distance.

The garrison camp sat to the south-southwest. Two low buildings, a fire pit, a gear rack, and the line of washed-out clothes made an ugly little village out of duty. The circuit agent was between the camp and the eastern wall, closer than Kem wanted and moving on the line Vel had predicted.

The descent pair were still hidden behind the curve of the basin wall.

Commander Osen stood near the threshold strip as if she had known the exact minute they would appear.

"Eight hundred meters," Vel said. Her breathing had roughened now. Tunnel, heat, and spending had all started collecting in the same body.

"Open ground."

"Not quite." She pointed with two fingers. A shelf of wind-cut sandstone jutted from the floor twenty meters ahead. "That buys ten seconds."

The circuit agent turned. He saw them and shouted.

"Move," Vel said.

They ran.

The first stretch skimmed downhill across polished bedrock that wanted ankles more than footing. Kem heard the Ward at the tunnel mouth go off behind them with a flat hard crack and knew the spend had bought exactly what he had paid for. Dust burst from the entrance. Somebody swore. Vel hit the shelf first and dropped behind it with one knee down. Kem came in a heartbeat later, lungs burning.

"How many?" he asked.

"All of them if she has any sense."

"She does."

Osen did not charge. That was the worst part. She pointed once toward the north side of the basin and once toward the south. The permanent circuit agent cut away from the camp. One of the descent pair appeared at the top of the switchback and started down at a run. The other did not bother with the path. She vanished once and reappeared twenty paces lower, a short blink-step that made Kem's stomach flip.

"That seems unfair," he said.

"South line just got shorter," Vel said.

Osen herself stayed where she was, moving only enough to remain between them and the threshold.

The pattern snapped into place inside Kem. Osen had already described it, but seeing it happen made it uglier. She was not trying to meet them. She was cutting every straight line west and leaving only the longer, meaner routes across the basin's bad footing.

"She told you the truth," Vel said.

"She really did."

Osen's voice carried over the heat. "Keep them off the center. Don't force a spend unless you've got a body for it."

The order made Kem want to laugh and throw up together. They had become a scheduling problem.

Vel peered around the shelf and made her choice before the agents finished moving.

"North wash," she said. "Now."

She broke from cover first, not toward the threshold but northwest along an erosion trough that only pretended to be protection. Kem followed. Heat came up through his soles. The basin floor wore a bright mineral crust over softer grit, and twice it cracked under him with a nasty pottery sound that promised deeper collapse if he trusted it.

The trough bent around a low rise of pale shale. For five seconds they were hidden from Osen. On the sixth, the ground leveled and threw them back into open sight.

"Down," Vel snapped.

Kem dropped instinctively. A precise burst of shaped gravel hissed over where his head had been and shredded itself against the far lip of the trough. It was not enough to kill him. It was enough to teach him where the line was.

"Still unfair," he said, spitting dust.

"Get up."

The trough ended at a low step in the basin floor, not high enough to look dangerous until Kem tried to clear it at speed. Vel made it. Kem planted wrong. Grit rolled under his boot. His legs went out from under him and his chin smashed the edge hard enough to blank his vision.

He tasted copper immediately.

By the time he shoved himself upright, blood was already running down his throat and dripping off his jaw. Vel had doubled back three steps and was hauling him by the collar toward the next scrap of cover, a rib of sandstone no wider than a grave marker and not much more useful.

"Teeth?" she asked.

He tongued them fast, found all of them, and nearly laughed from relief. "I still have an unpleasant number."

"Good. Move."

From behind the rib, Kem saw the line settle wider. The permanent agent had taken the north angle. The blink-step specialist was arcing south in short efficient bursts that never looked flashy enough to be impressive and were therefore worse. The second descent agent held middle distance. Osen remained at the threshold strip, patient as drought.

She was not spending much. She did not need to. She had geography, training, and time, and any one of those would have been enough to ruin a person.

"How far to the wash?" Kem asked.

"Thirty meters and ugly."

"My favorite kind."

Vel looked once toward the threshold. It sat west of them in brutal plain sight, close enough that his body wanted to lie about it. Osen had somehow made eight hundred meters look shorter and farther at the same time.

"On me," Vel said.

They ran again.

This stretch dropped through a fan of loose gravel into a dry wash cut deeper than the first. The basin floor here had broken into layered steps of marl and basalt, each one hot enough to sting through worn soles. Kem heard the south agent blink-step again, heard the air pinch and release, and did not look because looking would have cost him footing.

The wash gave them fifteen good seconds.

Then it kinked west, opened, and offered a clean diagonal to the threshold that made every exhausted part of Kem want to believe in miracles. He saw Vel see it too. She leaned that way.

Osen had been waiting for exactly that.

The commander touched the ground with one hand.

The bright crust ahead of them cracked and sloughed away in a long sliding fan, a precise ugly failure that took the only clean diagonal west with it. Loose debris poured down the slope in a sound like dry applause. Vel stopped so hard her bad side dipped. Kem skidded into her shoulder.

"North," she said at once.

"That is farther from the point."

"Everything is farther from the point. Move."

They scrambled up the wash's northern lip. The climb was only chest high, but the marl broke under Kem's hands and sent him sliding back half a body length before he found purchase on a seam of harder rock. Vel got up first and turned to yank him the last bit. By the time he landed beside her, blood from his chin had dried tacky down his throat and the inside of his mouth tasted like a copper coin.

Osen's next order came over the basin, calm enough to make it feel personal.

"North and south. Make them tell you where they want the point."

Vel heard it too. He could tell by the flatness that came over her face. This was not a commander trying to end a fight fast. This was a commander trying to make the next several hours behave like policy.

The route north took them over a low shelf split by erosion channels. Vel judged the distance, chose speed over safety, and jumped the last drop instead of climbing it. She landed wrong. Her left foot folded under her on the slope. She caught herself on both hands, hissed once through her teeth, and stood anyway.

"What happened?" Kem asked.

"Ankle. It still works."

It worked with a hitch now. Her next step told the truth even if she didn't.

The threshold remained off to their left, white with heat and distance. The agents kept widening around them. The tunnel mouth behind them was gone as an option. The straight line west was gone too. Osen had taken the basin Kem first saw from the ridge and turned it into something administrative: not a battle, not a chase, but a long deliberate subtraction.

Vel wiped blood and dust off her hands onto her trousers, set her weight on the bad ankle as if daring it to argue, and looked north for the next piece of cover.

"Keep moving," she said.

Kem spat red into the grit and followed because the commander had already turned eight hundred meters into an evening's work.


# Chapter 18

The shelf bought them one breath.

Kem hit it first, both hands out, boots skidding on the polished stone. Vel came in after him hard enough to jar the old crack in her left ribs. The rock covered them from the waist down and almost nowhere that mattered above it.

The first Combat Pulse hit the top lip of the shelf and took a slab of sandstone off with it. Chips sprayed down the back of Vel's neck. Kem folded tighter against the rock on instinct.

"Was that supposed to be a warning?" he asked.

"No."

Another Pulse shaved the left side. Osen was not trying to dig them out by force. She was making the shelf smaller and waiting for the basin to finish the job.

Across the basin, Commander Osen did not raise her voice.

"You can't make it work," she called. "It's not enough anymore."

Vel kept her head low and looked anyway. The north descent agent had taken the shelf above them. The east descent agent was sliding off the switchback for a lower angle. The circuiting specialist was already moving south to turn the shelf into a pocket. Osen stayed by the point and let the basin do her talking. Nobody was rushing. That was the part that made it hard.

"She wants us paying out here," Vel said.

"I hate how simple that sounds."

"Move on three. Not west. North trough first."

He looked where she meant and saw it a moment later: a shallow erosion channel twelve meters off, hardly deep enough to hide a leg, but it bent out of sight behind a harder rib of stone. It was not safe. It was simply next.

Osen spoke again, still calm enough to make the words travel clean. "Walk back east and you keep what you've got."

Vel said, "One."

The east descent agent shifted his feet to cut the trough.

"Two."

Kem bent his knees.

On three they went.

Kem pushed off a half breath too early because the next Pulse had already started to build light in the corner of his eye. He came up high instead of low. The shot cracked the shelf where his head had been, and he had to throw himself sideways to keep the rest of himself attached. The move was ugly and a breath late, but it kept him alive. Six weeks earlier he would have taken it clean in the face and called the result surprising.

Vel hit the floor running. Stone heat drove up through her soles. Another shot tore a strip of ground open behind her heel. Kem landed out of that throw with no grace at all, stumbled, and smacked his chin on the basin floor hard enough that she heard tooth on stone over the alarm and the gun-crack echo.

"Still there?" she said.

"I hate this place."

That meant he was alive.

The north trough was narrower than it had looked from cover. Vel dropped into it and found herself running bent over in a cut in the stone no deeper than a cooking trench. Kem came after her with blood already sliding down the front of his chin. The north descent agent changed angle and forced them lower. Osen had the basin mapped in bodies now: the north descent agent above them, the east descent agent cutting the longer lane, the specialist still running south to threaten any southward break, herself at the point. There were no gaps, only places where a body might fit if it did not mind damage.

Vel chose damage.

The trough ended in a sloped spill of gravel. She took it fast anyway, boots skidding. Her left foot hit loose stone, rolled, and drove a bright hot wire up through her ankle.

She did not fall. She almost did. The ankle went soft and mean under her for two steps and then came back enough to be used badly.

Kem caught up beside her at the rib of harder stone and hit it shoulder-first.

"You all right?" he asked, looking at her ankle because he had gotten annoyingly better at reading bodies.

"No."

"Good. I was worried it was only me."

The line of gravel high on the north scree started up then, a narrow hiss that came down in a steady ribbon. Vel looked without lifting more than one eye above the rock. Wind did not make a ribbon like that. Osen looked too. Just once. Then she turned back to the basin and kept every agent where they were.

Nobody could spare a body for the ridge and still keep Vel off the threshold.

The east descent agent tried a suppression lane next, not a kill-shot but a sheet of pressure set low across the route ahead. Vel saw it coming in the shift of his shoulders and changed direction before it thickened, cutting deeper into the north curve where the footing went from polished to sharp. Kem followed and lost another patch of skin from one palm catching himself.

He said, not quite asking, "Is that her."

"Could be rock."

"That is not comforting."

"Wasn't meant to be."

They got thirty more meters west before the north descent agent on the shelf cracked the stone in front of them and forced them down into a kneeling scramble. The return lane from the east clipped the back of Vel's calf with a thick drag of force that did not cut flesh so much as tell muscle it belonged to someone else. She yanked the leg free before the effect could finish taking hold.

Kem saw the limp the instant it started and looked like he wanted to say something helpful. He had known her too long now to try.

The basin gave them another rib of cover, worse than the first. Vel went to one knee behind it, breathing through dust and the small ugly grinding in her ankle.

Osen was still visible beyond the heat-waver and late light, one hand resting at her side, not hurried, not wasting anything. Vel had spent enough years around people with levels to know the kind of vanity that came with big numbers. They liked to demonstrate. They liked to let power arrive in sentences. Osen did not have that disease. She was using superior resources the way a careful quartermaster used better stores: without romance, because she expected to need them later.

"You ever think," Kem said between breaths, "that there should be a procedure for this."

"There is."

"I meant one not written by the people shooting at us."

"No."

The specialist made the south curve then, not close enough to strike, just visible enough that their cover stopped being cover and turned into a trap. Vel saw the geometry settle into place. Osen was not trying to kill them on the next move. She was making the next move obvious.

South meant the specialist. North meant shelf fire. West meant Osen's line. East meant back the way they had come.

She said, "We go west anyway. Short run. Then cut north."

Kem took one look at Osen's position and said, "I assume you don't mean because it's wise."

"No."

"Good. I'd hate for my education to be complete at this point."

The west run bought them a different kind of trouble. The ground flattened, which let them move. It also gave Osen the clean line she had been building toward. Light gathered in the commander's hands with a flat, disciplined brightness, not theatrical, not rushed. Vel saw it and knew at once that this was not for warning. It was for correction.

"Down," she said.

She dropped before Kem could answer. He followed a beat later and still not fast enough. The beam took the space over them and peeled a black line out of the stone. Heat slapped the back of Vel's neck. Kem's sleeve caught the edge of it and smoked.

They rolled into the north cut harder than planned. Kem's shoulder hit first. Vel's ankle folded again. The pain came up white and immediate, then settled into something she could carry as long as she did not ask the foot for honesty.

They were farther west. It was not enough to matter. It was enough to keep doing.

By the time the first rush of movement ended, the alarm had become part of the basin the way wind was part of it, a continuous mechanical grief rattling off the walls. Dust had pasted itself to the sweat under Vel's shirt. Blood from Kem's chin had dried once and started again.

That was the first ten minutes.

The next hour taught them the rest of Osen's method.

She never let the line break clean. When Vel and Kem used stone, the east descent agent cut the next stone. When they hugged north, the north descent agent on the shelves drove them lower with ugly, exact shots that did not try to kill so much as place feet. When they tested south, the specialist blinked just far enough into view to make the route a lie. Every westward gain cost them twice in sideways distance, and sideways distance was what killed the old arithmetic. Vel could almost admire the cold bookkeeping of it when she hated her enough.

They learned which rocks rang before they split. They learned which layers of sandstone would hold a hand and which would slough off in sheets. They learned that the east descent agent liked low bindings at shin height and the north descent agent preferred force pulses aimed where a running body wanted its next step. They learned that Osen did not call many orders aloud because she did not need to. The Reserve were that well trained, or that well scared of getting it wrong in front of her.

Vel kept every level.

That part took work. At eight, Perception was useful enough to hurt. Every time a lane started to close or a shot built in a half-hidden hand, her body reached for the old answer before her mind could stop it. Spend one, see more, survive easier. Spend two, dirty the geometry, make the basin stupid instead of lethal. Spend now, feel better now.

She would not spend here. She would not spend eight hundred meters out, or six hundred, or four. The threshold had been weakened on purpose. Osen had told them that in the tunnel with the satisfaction of a person reciting maintenance history. Whatever had once answered eagerly to a two-person descent no longer did. Vel needed the point awake under her feet. She needed the release happening where the ground itself could take it. Anything she fed the basin early just taught the basin she was willing to pay.

So she paid in the old currencies instead.

She paid skin off her right palm hauling herself up a ledge that broke under Kem's weight anyway. She paid the cracked ribs in her left side every time she twisted too hard to keep him moving. She paid the ankle over and over until the joint stopped giving sharp reports and settled into a thick, hot wrongness.

Kem paid with his face first. The second time Osen cut a lane under them, he lost footing on a strip of stone polished smooth by old floodwater and went down chin-first again. This time the split opened for real. Blood ran over his throat and into his collar.

Vel hauled him up by the back of the shirt.

"You still with me?"

"I was about to ask you the same thing."

"Then ask after we cross."

"That is an irritatingly optimistic sentence."

Later he paid with breath. The north descent agent drove a force line into a narrow cleft just as Vel shoved him through it, and the rebound hit him full in the ribs. Not broken, Vel thought, because he did not make the right noise for broken. Bruised hard enough that every third breath turned cautious after that.

He noticed her noticing and said, "If you say you're fine, I'm allowed to be fine."

"You aren't."

"I don't enjoy that this is your affectionate register."

That almost got a laugh out of her. It did not, but it came close enough to be useful.

Once, with enough hard light left to cut the ridge edges clean, they got within five clean strides of a better west line. Vel saw it happen a second before the others did: a run of broken shelves that stepped downward toward the threshold side, enough cover between them that a fast body might stay half-hidden all the way to the last third of the basin.

She pointed.

Kem nodded.

Then Osen moved for the first time with anything resembling urgency.

She did not attack them. She put a clean, terrible beam into the second shelf in the sequence and collapsed the whole set in a coughing red slide. Stone went where their feet were about to go. Dust blew up. The line vanished under rubble before either of them could commit to it.

Kem stared at the ruin and said, "This feels targeted."

"It is."

"I don't mean generally."

Vel looked west, at Osen already still again. "Yes."

They spent the next stretch north because the south had become impossible. The specialist had learned their pace by then. He started appearing not where they were but where pain would make them want to be. Vel did not give him the clean kill he wanted. She made him work for the miss. That meant ugly routes, sharp climbs, using the body for leverage instead of speed. Kem kept up with a stubbornness that had lost its shine and become something heavier.

They crawled once, flat to the stone under a shelf barely high enough to take their ribs. Vel's shoulder blade scraped the rock chip lodged under it and sent a fresh current of nausea through her. Kem's split chin dripped on the stone between them.

"If I survive this," he said very quietly, because the shelf carried sound, "I'm going to have opinions about your definition of route fixing."

"Keep them for later."

"I have been keeping them for later for weeks."

"Keep harder."

The gravel ribbon on the north scree came again, lower than before.

Vel saw it while flat on her side under the shelf: a narrow run of dislodged stone moving in a controlled path, stopping, starting, correcting. Not a fall. A person making a bad descent on purpose and refusing to die of it. Osen saw it too. Her head turned a degree, no more.

Still she kept the basin floor staffed. That told Vel two things. First, the commander judged the immediate crossing threat as worse than whatever was on the ridge. Second, she was right.

The bad part was that Vel could not use the knowledge yet.

By then the heat coming out of the rock had turned from assault to punishment. It no longer struck. It sat on the basin and made every breath feel second-used. Kem's shirt was dark down the spine. Vel's hands had started slipping on stone unless she wiped them first.

They found a deeper fold in the ground at one point and got nearly a minute there before the east descent agent started shaving the lip off it shot by shot. Vel used the time to look, not rest. West still too open. South closed by the specialist. North possible if they accepted exposure from the shelf gun. She chose north.

Kem tipped his head back against the stone and closed his eyes for one second.

"Don't sleep," she said.

"That was not sleeping."

"Looked aspirational."

He opened one eye. "How far."

She measured against the threshold point, Osen, the old fault line running under the basin, the journal marks in her head, and the sheer amount of hell still visible between here and there.

"Six hundred."

He made a face. "That is extremely rude."

"I know."

"Do you want the useful lie or the useless truth."

"Neither. I want your legs under you."

He got them under him.

The north route cost them most of the next hour and bought them actual progress for the first time. The north descent agent had good placement but worse footing the farther he chased them along that side. Vel forced two changes of angle that made him expose himself to his own people. Osen corrected, of course. She always corrected. But correction took time, and time let bodies move.

They were not fast. Nothing about eight and twelve produced speed any longer. It produced refusal. It produced the choice to keep loading weight onto one bad ankle and one bruised side because lying down would be simpler in a way Vel could not permit herself to examine.

At one narrow break in the shelves she had to shove Kem up by the back of the thigh and then climb after him one-handed because the other wrist was already threatening treachery from how often she had caught herself on it. The stone edge bit into the meat of her palm. Kem rolled and caught her forearm before she slid back.

For a second they were both hanging on strain and leverage and nothing graceful.

"This," Kem said through his teeth, "is not my preferred form of trust exercise."

"Climb."

"That was not me refusing."

He got her up. They broke west again. A Pulse from the east hit the shelf behind them and sent chips into Vel's neck and ear. A fragment cut the shell of her ear and made the whole left side of her head feel wet. She kept moving.

The shelf route narrowed into a run of leaning slabs. They crossed it half sideways. The specialist appeared ahead of them in a blink, not close enough to strike but close enough to freeze the next choice. Vel feinted south. He bit on it and committed another blink to cut her off.

She did not spend to catch it. She did not have the level. She just changed her mind faster than he expected and went low north while he was still arriving south.

It bought them maybe forty meters.

Forty mattered.

Later, when the light started to flatten and the western ridge threw longer shadows across the basin floor, Osen spoke again.

"I've watched three people try to reach this point," she called. "One thought speed would solve it. One thought power would. One thought the site remembered mercy. It does not."

Kem, panting into a strip of shade that could not really be called cover, said, "Is she trying to comfort us."

"No."

"She's very consistent."

Vel did not answer because the line had lodged in her anyway. Three people had tried. She had known there were others, abstractly, in the way a person knew any secret worth killing for had already been tested on someone poorer. Hearing the number inside the basin made it smaller and fouler. It was not many. It was just enough.

It changed nothing tactical. It still made her angrier.

The next move went bad fast.

They had used a run of eroded columns to get farther west than Osen liked. Vel saw it in the first real adjustment the commander had made in hours. She sent the specialist hard south to north instead of north to south, reversing the pressure. The east descent agent set a suppression sheet not in front of them but behind, to prevent retreat. The north descent agent stopped trying to herd and simply fired for impact.

Vel said, "Down."

There was nowhere good to go down to. Kem chose a slit between two stones and got there first. Vel followed. A Pulse hit the lip above them and burst the seam open. Rock came in.

Kem twisted toward her without thinking. The falling slab missed her head and took his shoulder instead, driving him flat and forcing a sound out of him he would not have made on purpose.

Vel shoved the stone off before it settled. The slab was not heavy by quarry standards. It was heavy enough when you were doing it with a ruined ankle and a rib cage already sour from old damage.

Kem rolled over holding the shoulder close.

"Dislocated?" she asked.

He breathed once, twice, swallowed. "No. I don't think. It still belongs to me."

"Then get up."

"You're very encouraging."

"I know."

He got up, loose with pain and bad angles after that. That was when Vel started hauling him more often instead of assuming he would simply follow. She used the good side for it. The bad side already had enough complaints filed.

They crossed the next stretch almost hip to hip, not from tenderness but because separate bodies were easier to isolate. The two descent agents understood that as soon as Vel did. A low binding cracked under Kem's ankle and dropped him to one knee. He tore at it with both hands before it fully closed. Skin peeled under his nails. The east descent agent reloaded for a second attempt.

Vel looked at the distance still left, looked at the numbers she was hoarding, and said, "No."

Not to the binding. To the impulse.

She yanked Kem up by the elbow instead, and together they flung themselves sideways into a cut in the floor before the second binding landed. The line snapped shut on empty stone.

That evasion cost her the ankle again. She almost blacked out for a second from how quickly the pain climbed. Kem saw her mouth tighten and said nothing. That was how she knew he was actually worried.

The north scree gave another hiss of gravel, lower still.

By the time the sun had dropped harder toward the west rim, they had lost any sense of clean forward movement. Progress came in diagonals, in stolen shelves, in moments where Osen's perfect pattern was only good instead of perfect. Vel measured the basin now less by meters than by what remained between tactical shifts: the next rib, the next trough, the next bend where the specialist would have to show his hand, the next patch of shadow, the next place the ground might remember that it had once opened.

Kem had stopped asking how far except when he really needed the answer. That was growth, or exhaustion, or both.

At one bad pause behind a split column of sandstone, he put his head back against the rock and laughed once without humor when the sound of it turned into a cough.

"What's funny."

"I had an image. From the Vault."

"Bad time."

"Yes. That seemed true as I was having it. There was a man in our east court whose entire position existed because he knew how to arrange views. Not roads, not supply, not anything with a body in it. Views. He once spent six weeks on the question of whether a particular cypress made the conservatory feel too available."

Vel watched the specialist's line through a split in the stone.

"And."

"And I am thinking of him now while hiding behind a rock the size of a dead goat. I feel this says something unpleasant about how minds work."

"It does."

"Do you know what it is."

"No."

"Good. I would have hated for you to become interpretive at the end."

That time she did laugh, once and short, because the alternative was some worse use of air.

Then Osen took the column apart from the top down and the moment ended.

They reached the rib of sandstone with a little under two hundred meters left and nowhere decent to hide.

It was not a single run that got them there. It was the accumulation of bad choices that had been slightly better than the alternatives. By then Vel's left ankle hurt with every heartbeat. Dust had turned the inside of her mouth into old cloth. Kem lay three meters off her left knee, breathing through his mouth, chin bloodied from the earlier falls, shoulder held a fraction too carefully. Both of them were still carrying the same levels they had started with because there had been no honest place to spend.

The sun had slid far enough west that ridge shadows striped the basin floor. Heat still leaked out of the stone, but the flat white glare was gone. Osen had spent those hours doing the same thing over and over: cutting every clean line west and forcing them sideways, keeping any level they burned too far from the threshold to matter. Vel had answered with patience, bad footing, and blood instead of spending.

High on the north scree, gravel hissed down again in a narrow, steady ribbon. It was low enough now to be hopeful.

Osen looked that direction once and looked away. She kept every agent on the basin floor. If she thinned the line for even a few seconds, Vel would have the point.

Vel spat dust, found there was not enough spit for it, and said, "You hear me say spend, you spend."

Kem wiped blood off his mouth with the back of his hand. "And if you don't."

"You use your body."

He nodded once. "I can. I mean, I can for another minute or so."

"Only plan we've got."

She touched the rock, then went. Kem moved with her.

The first Combat Pulse cracked the stone where his head had been. He had already thrown himself sideways. The move was ugly and late, but it left him breathing. Six weeks ago he would have taken it full in the face.

Vel spent one level on Perception.

The basin snapped into hard focus. The north descent agent was behind a low shelf. The east descent agent was building the suppression lane she had been feeling for most of the chase. The specialist was closing from the south with another blink loaded in the back of his posture. Osen was holding back at the threshold, saving her own hand for the point.

"Left," Vel said.

Kem cut left without asking.

The suppression lane thickened a half second too slow and took only Vel's calf instead of her whole stride. It felt like running through wet sand while someone else owned the tendons. She tore free before it set. A force beam scorched past. Hot stone spat chips across her shoulders. One punched into the flesh under her shoulder blade and stayed there.

The specialist came in fast, arriving first as a gap in the basin and then as a body on her left with his shoulder already turned into the strike.

Vel got her forearm up. The hit ran through bone and tendon. Her wrist gave with a wet pop. Pain flashed white, then narrowed to something sharp enough to use. She let the blow turn her instead of trying to stop it. The agent overcommitted, skidded, and slammed shoulder-first into the rock.

"Keep moving," she said.

Another Pulse shattered the shelf behind them. Dust jumped. Through it Vel saw the specialist loading a second blink and the east descent agent closing to receive it.

She spent two levels together, one to catch the blink and one to kick its exit point backward and dirty.

The specialist vanished and reappeared twenty meters wrong, straight into the east descent agent. The collision burst bright and concussive. Both men went down in the dust.

Five.

Losing levels this low felt local. It was like a small useful organ shutting off and not promising return.

Kem was up again, running bent and quick with the body he had built mile by mile. He had learned to read a shoulder before an ability. He had learned to fall without dying. He had learned that a person could do a surprising amount with ordinary muscle if the alternative was ledger arithmetic performed by someone richer.

The commander chose that moment to stop waiting.

Vel felt the charge through her teeth before she saw the light gather in Osen's hands, a killing line tuned for this basin and this distance. The shot was not for Kem. It was for the route ahead of Vel, the exact strip of stone her body had to trust next.

She was off-angle and too far east. Her wrist was blown, her ankle half numb, and five levels did not buy clean geometry from there.

Kem saw it.

He stepped between them and spent five.

The shield he threw came up as a wall four meters wide and canted just enough to catch the beam and throw the worst of it away.

The impact tore rock out of the basin. Vel hit the ground on her bad shoulder. The buried chip drove deeper. Dust filled her mouth. When she looked up, Kem was on both knees, palms split open from bracing, down from twelve to seven.

"Up," Vel said.

He made a noise that might have been a laugh. "That was — shit. Was that five? I just spent five on one wall." He pressed his palms flat on the stone and stared at them. "Okay. I'm coming."

He came up loose with pain, hard to carry cleanly, all bad angles and sudden dead weight while Vel's own body was close to the end of its good behavior. She got under his arm with her good side and dragged him moving.

The two descent agents closed. These were Reserve professionals, not rich amateurs with too many levels and no sense. There was less flourish in them and better placement. The east descent agent caught Kem's ankle in a binding and dropped him face-first into the rock. His chin split wider. The north descent agent cracked the ground between them, opening a narrow crevice meant to keep their levels from touching in the same place.

Vel spent one level on misdirection and let the next binding catch an afterimage. Empty stone took the hit.

Four.

"Kem, move."

He tore the binding loose with both hands. Skin came off under his nails. He got up anyway, one side of him holding itself too carefully.

The commander called from the threshold, "I've watched three people try to reach this point. You can still walk away."

Vel kept running.

"Walk back to what," Kem said, not turning. Then he was moving too.

The specialist came again from the south, loaded for a kill. Kem waited until the blink took hold. Then he spent two levels and pinched the whole ability stack dark.

The specialist stumbled out of transit at ordinary speed and ordinary strength, suddenly just a man on bad footing. His punch clipped Kem's shoulder hard enough to hurt and nowhere near hard enough to end him.

Kem hit him back once, then twice. The man's head cracked on layered stone and stayed down.

Five.

High on the north wall, the gravel ribbon had become a figure now, tiny but human, coming down the ugly route on purpose. Vel saw the body slide, catch, keep coming. Osen saw it too. She still did not turn an agent to deal with it. If she thinned the line for even a few seconds, Vel would have the point.

Another binding started for Kem from the east descent agent. Vel spent one level and drove the caster's own frequency back through it. The line snapped sideways into his ribs. He folded where he stood, both hands clamped to his side.

Three.

The lane opened.

"Now," Vel said.

Kem caught her forearm and spent three levels on motion.

The basin disappeared under them. Stone blurred. Wind tore tears out of Vel's eyes. One heartbeat, then another, and the threshold point was under their feet.

Kem let go. He had two. She had three. Behind them the wounded agents were coming hard. In front of them Osen was close enough for Vel to see the threadbare places in her windscarf and the disbelief she was refusing to let become expression.

Vel did not waste the moment speaking.

She spent the first of her last three levels into the ground.

She did not send it at Osen or forward. She sent it down.

The buried seam under the threshold point answered with a deep vibration that ran up through the soles of her boots and into her bad ankle. That first spend did not open anything. It woke the site. The old cut in the world had to recognize a descent before it would take one.

She spent the second to hold a mouth in it.

Air thinned over the unmarked stone. The shape of the world started to slip, not yet torn but worrying loose where the seam ran weakest. That second spend was not for crossing either. It only kept the split from sealing the instant it formed.

Osen lunged then, building a containment field on instinct and training, trying to catch Vel above zero and freeze the whole attempt there.

Vel spent the last level before the field closed.

The first two levels had primed the place and held it open. The last one was the fall itself. Thresholds answered to nearby expenditure, yes, but they answered hardest to a number becoming none. Without zero there was force and light and waste. With zero there was a descent.

She had lived fifty-eight years under the Ledger and spent forty-one of them on purpose. Both counts ended here.

Zero.

The hum did not simply stop. It tore out of her.

Her jaw went loose. Pressure she had carried for so long it had stopped reading as pressure vanished from the roots of her teeth and the backs of her eyes. Her stomach dropped as if the ground had missed a stair. Her knees nearly followed. A cold flash ran over her scalp and down the inside of both arms. For one sick second she thought she had gone deaf because the constant internal tone was gone and her body still reached for it.

Then the rest of the world came back in pieces.

Wind still scraped the basin. Someone shouted. Loose stone hissed down the north scree. But the Ledger's background presence, the faint counting pressure that had sat under every hour since she was a child, was absent so completely it left shape behind, the way a pulled tooth left the tongue searching a hole.

Perception went with it.

For decades the world had never ended at her skin. Even when she was not actively using the ability, even when she was tired or sleeping or half drunk on bad road liquor, some part of her had always known where weight sat nearby, where people moved, where levels collected bright or thin in the dark. The basin had never needed to be seen all the way to be inhabited. Osen's field had been a pressure in the air before it was light. Kem had been a count to the left before he was a body. The wounded descent agent had remained present at the edge of awareness even while she ran the other direction.

That whole layer of the world dropped away.

Now there was only what her ordinary senses could manage. She had dust in her mouth. Pain throbbed in her wrist. The bad ankle threatened collapse. Osen's containment field existed only where light marked it and nowhere beyond that. Kem was visible only when she looked straight at him. The north wall stayed empty until the falling figure showed itself against it. Everything outside sight became guesswork again.

The loss struck a second time as nausea. Vel gagged and swallowed dust.

The threshold tore open.

It held badly, with ragged edges and fraying light, a mouth forced in bad cloth. Through it Vel saw hand-cut stone, packed-earth streets, people moving under a sky that had never carried the Ledger's hum. The sight came at her without the old overlay, with no counting pressure on the bodies beyond and no sense of levels because there were none to sense. There were only people and built stone and evening light.

She stepped through before the body could think too long about what had changed.

The crossing took one stride and all of it.

On the other side the air smelled of dust, herbs, and a cookfire somewhere beyond the first bend in the street. The silence was not true silence. Voices moved in it. Wind moved in it. A cart wheel knocked once on stone. But no hidden tally sat under any of it, and there was no second layer. The absence was so large it felt physical, like standing after years with a weight strapped to the spine and finding posture suddenly ungoverned.

Vel turned at once.

Kem was still in the basin, one knee at the edge of the tear, blood on his chin and hands. The opening was already shrinking around him.

Two people had not been enough.

Now the failure was visible. The mouth had force, but it did not have duration. Her zero had opened it. Kem's remaining two levels could finish only one job. He could spend them into the tear and keep it alive for a moment longer, but then he would be standing at zero in the basin while the frame died in front of him. Or he could spend them on himself and try to force the crossing, and the mouth would close while his body was still in it. The damped threshold wanted a third contributor, not another desperate guess at the old math.

Osen was not standing idle beside him.

The containment field she had thrown for Vel had changed shape with the failure. One band of it cinched the tear's edge tight, trying to choke width out of it. Another had hooked around Kem's calves and dragged him half a pace off the lip. Each time he leaned toward the opening, the band hauled him short again.

"Stay where you are," Osen said. "It's closing and you do not have enough."

She had one hand locked on the shrinking ring around the tear and the other feeding that clamp at Kem's legs. She was not sparing him. She was choosing the only interference that did not become self-damage. Any hard offensive spend from that position would go straight into the active threshold and feed the cascade she was trying to starve. A killing Pulse at the mouth would not seal it. It would widen it. So she pinned him short of the point and waited for the door to die.

Kem looked through the narrowing gap at Vel. "Two doesn't — it's not enough for both, is it." The flat concentration he got when the math was bad had already settled over his face.

The north-scree noise hit the basin floor at last as a rush of loose stone and hard breathing.

Osen half turned. Vel could see the exact second she understood who had made that descent.

Then seven fresh spends struck the cascade in rapid succession.

Sable Orm came off the scree with both hands up, gravel still running around her boots. Dust covered the front of her field coat. Blood shone at one palm where the rock had taken skin. She looked like a woman who had skidded three hours down bad stone and meant to spend more before night if that was the price of getting the answer in front of her.

Osen snapped the clamp at Kem harder and started to redirect the outer ring toward Sable's wrists.

She stopped halfway through the motion.

The threshold shuddered when her field shifted. Vel saw the tear flare wider for one ugly blink. Osen saw it too. That was the constraint made visible. If she abandoned the choke entirely for a clean attack, she would feed the opening with her own force and risk giving them the very breach she had spent years preventing. So she held the failing geometry with one hand and tried to spoil Sable from the edge of it instead.

Sable spent.

She was nowhere near zero. That was why the seven levels could help without taking her through. Thresholds answered to nearby expenditure, not only to terminal descent. Her body stayed anchored where it was because she was still miles above none. The energy went into the mouth instead of into a crossing.

The first two spends steadied the tear. Vel saw the shaking in the frame lessen and the ragged edge stop sawing inward.

The next ones widened it.

By the seventh the mouth was holding, ugly but real, no longer collapsing faster than the people around it could think.

Osen turned fully to her then, furious now because fury cost nothing.

"Auditor."

"I am," Sable said, and stopped there, breath still wrong from the descent. She looked once at the commander's hands on the containment lattice, once at Kem caught short of the lip, then through the opening at the stone street behind Vel. "Do not say anything right now."

She did not look back at Osen. She looked through the tear instead, at hand-cut stone, packed earth, and people moving under that quiet sky. Whatever question had dragged her out of the Vault and all the way south was no longer a question. The answer was standing there with blood on its face.

With Sable's seven levels in the frame, the mechanics changed.

Now the threshold was being held open by outside spend. Kem's last two no longer had to brace the mouth and make the crossing in the same instant. They only had to take him to zero while the mouth remained stable enough to accept a body.

Vel said, "Go."

Kem looked once at Sable, once at Osen's hands in the field, then at Vel.

He spent his last two levels.

There was no flare to it. Vel could not feel the Ledger leave him the way she would have an hour earlier. That was gone. She only saw the change in his face: the reflexive listening that had lived under his expression since she met him cut blank in an instant, as if someone had shut a window inside him.

The clamp at his calves failed when he did.

Whether Osen lost the leverage or the threshold rejected the geometry once his count hit none, Vel could not tell. Kem lurched forward. The mouth took him clean.

He crossed.

He hit the packed earth stumbling. Vel caught him with her good arm. He hissed around the bad rib and almost went down anyway. His hands were still trying to brace against a force that no longer existed.

"Hey," Vel said.

He looked at her, looked past her at the stone street, the built walls, the evening light, and then down at his own empty hands.

"It's so quiet," he said, not to her exactly.

Behind him, Sable stood on the basin floor still far above zero, hands lowered now, wind dragging at her ruined hair. She stayed where she was while the surviving agents regrouped. Osen looked from Sable to the place where the tear had been and said nothing. There was no useful sentence available to her that did not amount to witness.

The threshold folded in on itself. The Scour vanished. Silence stayed.

Vel felt the aftershock of its staying all the way through her body. No hum returned. No hidden pressure settled back into the bones. The world remained stubbornly ordinary in the frightening new way.

Somewhere in the stone streets behind her, a voice called out. Then another, closer. Footsteps started toward them.

Kem turned his head at the sound like a man relearning what distance meant.

Vel kept one hand on his arm and looked toward the nearest turn in the street.

For the first time in forty years, she had to wait for the corner to produce the body before knowing who was coming.

She waited for Maren to come around it.


# Chapter 19

Footsteps struck stone behind Vel before Kem had air enough to finish trying to laugh.

She kept hold of his sleeve and turned toward the sound. Three people came fast from the nearest bend: an older woman in a green-stained apron, a boy with a sloshing bucket, and behind them a woman carrying a rolled survey under one arm with a mason's hammer hooked through her belt.

Vel knew the hammer first. Maren had always carried tools as if they were part of the argument. Then the rest of her arrived. There was more gray at the temples now, a notch missing from one ear, new lines at the mouth. The hard line between her brows was the same. It was the look she wore when something hit her sideways and she had not decided what to do with it yet.

For one bad second Vel forgot Kem's weight entirely. He sagged against her, and the cracked rib under her left breast drove a knife through her breath.

The older woman reached them first. "Don't drop him on my road."

Maren stopped two paces away.

Dust moved between them. Somewhere farther down the street, somebody shouted for rope. Kem made a ragged sound low in his throat and tried to stand without using either of them.

Maren looked at Vel's face, the dirt dried across her shirt, the arm braced to her side. Then she looked at Kem and the clean blankness in both their expressions, the after-look of people who had just come out of the hum for good.

"Vel," she said.

The name came out rough, like she had not used it in a while and had not expected the first use to be this one.

Vel said, "Yeah."

That was what she had. Maren was alive, older, with stone dust on one boot and a council roll under her arm and both feet planted in a world that existed. Eight years of road, and Vel's mouth could only manage that.

Kem's knees buckled.

Maren moved before the older woman could swear again. She got under Kem's other arm with the same efficient turn of the body Vel remembered from riverbanks, collapsed camps, and one fight behind a tannery in Roth that had ended with both of them bleeding and laughing because neither of them had died. Her forearm pressed warm and solid against Vel's wrist. She was real enough to lean on.

"Intake house," the older woman said. "Boy, stop staring and bring the water. Both of them, now."

Vel said, "He spent the last two getting us through."

"I can see that he did something dramatic," the woman said. "He still has to lie down."

Maren's shoulder took more of Kem's weight. "Can you walk?"

Vel almost said yes out of habit. She took one step and the answer changed under her. "If nobody makes me run."

"Nobody's asking that much," Maren said.

They moved uphill together. The street under Vel's boots was packed earth laid between walls of cut stone. Tool marks caught the late light. A woman at a doorway flattened herself aside with an armful of split willow. Two children stopped carrying a basket between them long enough to stare, then kept going when the older woman snapped at them for standing in the path.

So this was Sera. The name flickered somewhere old and road-worn, but the pain under Vel's ribs was louder than memory.

Kem tried to lift his head. "Did we."

"We did," Vel said.

"Good," he muttered, and nearly went down again.

The intake house was two rooms of pale stone with a long bench under one wall and shelves of folded blankets on the other. Bundles of drying herbs hung from the rafters. A chipped blue bowl sat on the table beside a stack of clean bandages and an obsidian blade laid on cloth. The room held no shimmer of healing and no latent hum in the air. It was only a room built to hold hurt people until hurt stopped moving.

Sera pointed with her chin. "Bed. The one by the window. If either of you vomits on the blankets, you wash them yourselves."

Maren helped Vel get Kem onto the mattress. He hit it hard enough to grunt and then went slack in the alarming way zeroed people seemed to do, as if sleep and collapse were using the same door.

Vel stayed bent over him a moment longer than she needed to. When she straightened, black drifted in at the edge of her sight.

Sera caught it. "You too."

"I'm standing."

"You are briefly standing. Lie down before you argue yourself interesting."

Vel did not move.

Maren had set the survey roll on the table. She kept hold of it as if she had forgotten she was holding it. She looked worse for surprise than Vel did for pain, which ought to have been satisfying if Vel had room for anything that clean.

"You found it," Maren said.

Vel looked at the obsidian blade, the blankets, the herbs in the rafters, the wall somebody had built stone by stone without a single easy trick in it. Then she looked back at Maren. "Yeah."

Maren's mouth tightened once. It could have been anger. It could have been relief trying not to show itself in public. "Of course you did."

Sera cut across whatever would have come next. "If you two want to do this properly, do it after they've both stopped swaying."

Vel let herself be pushed to the second bed because resisting would have meant falling. Maren did not touch her again after that. She stood at the foot of the bed while Sera made Vel drink water, then said, "I was in council. I left in the middle of Pol being impossible, so he'll still be impossible when I get back to it."

Vel's laugh caught on the bad rib and turned into a hiss.

Maren glanced at her side. Her eyes stayed there a beat too long. "When you wake up, come down to the lower settlement."

Vel wanted to ask a dozen things and had no clean hold on any of them. She settled for, "All right."

Maren nodded once and took the survey roll with her.

Vel woke to a room gone quieter and darker.

For one blank instant she reached for the hum and found only the wrong loose silence under her skin. Then the rib announced itself, and the ache in her shoulder, and the memory came back in order: the threshold, the light, Kem's face going empty at zero, Maren standing two paces away with a hammer on her belt.

Sera was at the table crushing dried leaves into the grain of the board. "You lasted longer than the last voluntary one. He cried in his sleep and then tried to climb out the window."

"How long?"

"Long enough." Sera tipped the ground herbs into a cup and added hot water from a kettle hanging over a small brazier. "Your friend is still out. You were not meant to be up before him, but you look like the sort of person who hears 'not meant to' as an invitation."

Vel swung her feet to the floor. The room tilted, corrected, and held. "Where's Maren."

"Lower settlement. House by the well with too many herb pots in the window. She came back twice to check whether you'd woken, which I'm sure you'll both find a sensible thing to behave normally about."

Vel took the cup Sera held out. The steam smelled bitter and green. "You say that to everybody."

"No. Some people make normal faces." Sera looked toward Kem's bed, where he lay on his back with one arm curled over the blanket like he had been reaching for something in sleep and not found it. "Go on, then. Before I remember I'm supposed to be in charge here."

Outside, late light had turned the upper stones the color of old bread. Vel walked carefully, her forearm braced to her ribs when the slope steepened. Nobody stopped her. People made space when they had to and otherwise stayed in the business of finishing the day. A woman hauled two buckets from a cistern by rope and counterweight. At an open-sided infirmary, an apprentice washed red from a basin while an older man sharpened obsidian on a flat stone. She heard tool on tool, water on clay, and wind in leaves. The world kept arriving at her without the hum under it.

The lower settlement curved around a well and a cluster of cookfires. Maren's house sat a little off the ring, pale stone with a narrow garden pressed against the wall. Herbs crowded clay pots under the window. Somebody had argued them into rows and then let them go a little wild anyway. Vel could not tell whether the order or the disorder was more familiar.

The door stood open.

Inside, the room was warmer than the air outside and smelled of tea, damp wool, and stone that had kept a day's heat. A worktable took the center of it, scarred with knife marks and darkened where cups had been set down carelessly for years. Three rolled plans lay across one end under a river stone, beside a length of measuring cord and a little jar of nails. Shelves ran along the far wall holding bowls, seed packets, folded cloth, and a row of clay jars labeled in Maren's writing. A banked hearth sat to the left. Two chairs faced each other across its cold space as if people used them often enough to wear a habit into the floor.

Maren stood at the table with a cup held close. She had taken off the hammer. It hung from a peg near the door beside a work coat dusted white from stone.

She turned at the sound of Vel's step.

This close, the changes were worse and better than distance had allowed. The skin at her throat had loosened a little. There was a thin scar under her chin Vel had never seen before. Her forearms were stronger. Her face had not kept the road on it; it had taken on the slower wear of weather, labor, and years in one place. The old quickness was still there, but it had settled deeper. Nothing about her looked preserved. Everything looked used.

Vel stopped just inside the threshold. She had crossed half the world to get here and now had no idea what counted as far enough into the room.

Maren set the cup down.

Neither of them moved.

Wind touched the open door. Somewhere at the back of the house water ticked once into a basin. Vel saw the second cup already on the table, cooled enough that a skin had formed on the tea. She saw the extra blanket folded over the back of one chair. She saw a basket of onions under the window and a pair of mud-dried work boots by the hearth. Eight years had happened in objects before either of them said a word.

"You can close the door," Maren said.

Vel shut it. The latch clicked too loud.

"Sit down if you're staying upright," Maren said after a moment. "I don't want you hitting the floor and making me solve that first."

Vel lowered herself into the chair nearest the table. The rib punished her for pretending the movement did not matter. She kept her face as still as she could.

Maren pushed the waiting cup across to her and went back to the other chair. A whole table width sat between them. It was more than enough room for caution and not enough for distance.

Vel took the cup. It was still warm.

Maren said, "I used to do this stupid walk to intake every time somebody came through who might know your name."

Vel looked up.

Maren's eyes stayed on the table, on the measuring cord under the river stone. "I did it for five years. Then I got tired of coming home with the same answer."

Vel's first instinct was to say she had come as fast as she could. The sentence reached her mouth and died there. Eight years did not get smaller because the road had been difficult.

"I know," she said, and knew as soon as she said it that the line was useless.

Maren gave a short, airless laugh. "No. You don't."

That landed and stayed there.

Vel drank tea because it gave her a task. It had mint in it and something bitter underneath that kept pulling her back into her body.

Maren watched her over the cup's rim for a second and then said, "You look worse than I hoped and better than I expected."

"I can work with that."

"I wasn't offering work yet."

"No," Vel said. "I know."

Maren leaned back. The chair answered with a small wood complaint. "Do you."

Vel looked at the shelves instead of at her, at the labeled jars, the folded cloth, the nails, and the plans waiting under stone weights. All the ordinary things only existed because Maren had been here long enough to need them twice. "You built a life."

"Yes."

The word had no apology in it. Vel was glad of that and hurt by it in the same instant.

Another silence opened. This one held.

Maren broke it without looking away. "I'm glad you're alive."

Vel let out the breath she had been pinning behind her teeth. "All right."

"I'm not ready for the rest of it," Maren said. "Not yet. I left council in the middle of an argument and Pol's going to send somebody down with a map any minute because he thinks weather negotiates with stubbornness."

"All right," Vel said again.

Maren's mouth twitched once. It did not become a smile. "You still answer the smaller question when the bigger one is waiting right behind it."

"It's the one I can do sitting down."

That almost got a real laugh. It missed by very little.

A knock sounded once against the frame and then went away before either of them spoke. Some committee runner checking, deciding better of it. The house settled again.

Maren said, quieter now, "Did you come here to stay, or did you come here to prove you could reach the door."

Vel looked at the cup. Steam barely rose from it anymore. "I came here because you were here."

Maren's grip tightened on her own cup. No answer came, because none was easy enough for this early.

Vel did not press. She drank the tea. Maren sat across from her with both feet planted on the floor she had worn into habit. The room held them in the same square of light until the light shifted and the upper path began carrying other people's footsteps instead.

When Kem came back to himself, the first thing he noticed was the quiet.

It was not silence. Outside the window, a bird called, wind moved through leaves, and somebody said water and got an answer back. It was ordinary settlement noise. People were still awake, still moving through the last work of the day.

The quiet was inside him.

He lay on stone. The cloth under his back did not hide the grain of it. Above him, blocks fitted together without the seamlessness of Shaping. Every joint was visible. Every surface was scored by tools. He counted chisel marks in one block while his mind kept reaching for what was missing.

What was missing was the Ledger.

He reached for it before he knew he was doing it, the same stupid reflex as a tongue finding the gap where a tooth used to be. Nothing answered him. There was no hum, no tally, no number running at the edge of thought. For twenty-six years it had been there, first at 300, too high to feel like danger, then descending until every spend had a shape. He had felt it at 245 after the outriders, at 148 before the river, at 50 leaving the Margins, at 31 where Vel had started, at 12 in the tunnel, and at 2 with the threshold failing in front of him.

Now there was only the reach and the miss.

It felt less like losing a limb than losing a noise. His mind kept listening for it anyway. The listening became its own pressure.

He pushed himself upright. The room was small but not mean. A wooden bed frame held the straw mattress. A table stood under the single window with a clay cup on it. The walls were quarried stone, each block a slightly different shade. Nothing in the room tried to hide the work that had made it.

He drank the water. It tasted like water.

A woman filled the doorway, maybe sixty, with short gray hair and old burn scars from wrist to elbow. Her apron was stained dark green. A basket of dried herbs hung from her arm, sharp and peppery.

"You're the second one today," she said. "The woman woke about four hours before you did. Got to her feet faster, too."

"Vel."

"That her name? She didn't offer it. Just stood up and walked out of here like the room was slowing her down." The woman set the basket on the table and looked him over with practiced calm, weighing him for tears or vomiting or both. "I'm Sera. Intake house. Eat the bread. Your body is going to feel wrong for a while. Some people get dizzy. Some people cry. One man threw up for six hours."

The name hit a buried place all at once. He heard Fallow in the Margins, flat as weather, naming the people the Scour had taken and not returned. Sera and Hoster had gone in front of him and been talked about afterward as if the earth had eaten them.

"Sera from the Scour?" he said.

Her eyes sharpened. "If you met Fallow, yes. Is he still alive."

"He was when we left."

"Good." She pushed the loaf toward him. "Eat. We can all enjoy the cosmological revelation after you stop looking like you're about to slide out of your own skin."

The vanished were not vanished. They were old women with herb-stained aprons telling him to chew properly. Kem stared at the bread for a moment before picking it up.

"I'm not going to cry," he said.

"That's what the throwing-up man said. Then he cried and ruined my floor."

The bread was dense and dark, crust cracked, heat still trapped in the middle. He ate it at the window while looking over rooftops dropping into a valley. Stone and timber buildings stood with gardens between them. The rooflines were uneven without looking improvised, each building carrying the choices of the person who had made it. The place had not been built to prove anything. It had been built because people expected to stay.

"How do I get to the council hall?"

"Main path. Ten minutes. You won't miss it." Sera tucked a cloth over the basket. "If they're still on the cistern, don't step between Pol and the map unless you want your first day here to involve being shouted at by three practical people at once."

Outside, his legs felt wrong in a new way. Each step came a fraction off-center, as if the Ledger had been a weight hung somewhere inside him and the rest of his body had not yet learned to carry itself without it.

The main path was packed earth worn smooth by feet. People knelt in gardens, working actual soil that wanted rotation and rest and manure and time. A man carrying root vegetables nodded once and kept going. Two children ran past without slowing for the newcomer. An old woman on a bench watched him with patient curiosity and waited to see what he would do next.

At an open-sided building halfway down the path, he slowed.

Three people were bent over a table. A young apprentice held a sliver of dark volcanic glass while an older woman guided her wrist. On the table, a man sat with one leg out and a split in his calf shining wet in the light.

The older woman looked up and saw Kem watching. "If you're going to stand there, come hold his ankle."

He did. The man's skin was hot under his grip. Blood slid over Kem's knuckles while the apprentice worked the obsidian along the torn flesh and the older woman kept talking her through it.

"Shallower. Let the edge do the work. Good. Now wash it."

The apprentice poured from a clay jug. The man on the table flinched so hard his other heel banged the wood.

"Sorry," the apprentice said.

"Don't apologize," the older woman said. "You're helping him. He gets to flinch."

The man looked at Kem. "First day?"

"Yeah."

He jerked his chin toward the gash in his leg. "Temporary scaffold at the cistern. Ladder split under me." He took a breath through his teeth and let it out. "It hurts more than the abilities did. Better, though. At least I know where it is."

By the time the apprentice tied off the bandage, Kem was streaked red to the wrists. The older woman jerked her chin toward the door. "Wash at the cistern before you go up to council. Oriel hates blood on paper."

The council hall was bigger than the buildings around it and no grander than it had to be, two stories of stone with a lintel carved into two open gestures. Inside, the room smelled of damp clay, lamp oil, and the mud people had tracked straight in from work. A long table took the middle. Maps were weighted with river stones instead of seals. A clay cutaway of the upper hillside sat at one end, scored with terraces and channels, its basin dark from real water someone had poured through it to prove a point.
